sc-appeal-115-2014	21 January 2020	Attorney General v. Siripala and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; Priyantha Jayawardena, PC. J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Section 196 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act — Arraignment of Accused — Fundamental requirement — Failure to comply — Prejudice to substantial rights — Failure of justice — Trial by High Court — Plea of not guilty — Constitutional provisions	Facts The 1st and 2nd Accused-Appellants were indicted in the High Court of Embilipitiya alongside two others on charges of murder, causing grievous hurt, and theft. Following the conclusion of the trial, the High Court Judge convicted the Appellants on the counts of murder and grievous hurt. The Appellants subsequently appealed to the Court of Appeal, where a divisional bench dismissed the appeal by a majority decision. The Appellants then sought and were granted Special Leave to Appeal to the Supreme Court. The primary contention raised by the Appellants was that the trial judge failed to comply with Section 196 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act, which requires the court to read and explain the indictment to the accused and record a plea of guilty or not guilty before the commencement of the trial. The Appellants argued that this omission constituted a fundamental illegality that vitiated the conviction. The record of the trial proceedings did not contain an entry indicating that the indictment had been read and explained or that a plea had been formally taken. Throughout the proceedings, however, the Appellants were represented by counsel and participated in the trial process, including filing lists of defense witnesses and requesting a trial by judge without a jury. Issues Whether it is imperative for the High Court Judge to read and explain the indictment and record a plea under Section 196 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act before the commencement of a trial. Whether the failure to comply with Section 196 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act constitutes a fundamental illegality that automatically vitiates a conviction. Whether the omission of a formal arraignment in the record constitutes a curable irregularity under the proviso to Article 138(1) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 138(1) Code of Criminal Procedure Act No. 15 of 1979, Sections 182, 195, 196, 197, 436 Judicature Act No. 2 of 1978 Cases Referred to: David Perera v. The Attorney General - [1997/v1-sri-lr-390] Withanage Gunawardena v. The Attorney General - [ca-22/2002] Abdul Sameem v. The Bribery Commissioner - [1991/v1-sri-lr-76] B.S.H Kodithuwakku v. The Republic - [2010-blr-167] Rajakaruna v. The Attorney General - [ca-206-207/2010] Amaratunga Arachchige Nimal Sarathchandra v. The Attorney General - [2008/v2-sri-lr-35] Sunil Jayarathna v. The Attorney General - [sc-97/09] R v. Williams (Roy) - [1977-1-all-er-874] Analysis Issue 1: Imperative nature of Section 196 Rule applied: Section 196 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act. Court's reasoning: The court affirmed that Section 196 imposes a mandatory duty on the High Court to read and explain the indictment to the accused and to elicit a plea. This ensures the accused is fully aware of the charges. Finding: It is imperative under Section 196 to read and explain the indictment and ask for a plea. Issue 2: Effect of non-compliance Rule applied: Proviso to Article 138(1) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The court distinguished between Section 182 (Magistrates' Court) and Section 196 (High Court). In the High Court, the accused is served with the indictment and annexes well in advance, providing notice of the charges. Non-compliance with Section 196 does not automatically vitiate a conviction unless it causes prejudice to the accused's substantial rights or results in a failure of justice. Finding: Non-compliance with Section 196 does not per se vitiate a conviction. Issue 3: Curability of the irregularity Rule applied: Proviso to Article 138(1) of the Constitution; Section 205 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act. Court's reasoning: The court noted that the Appellants were represented by counsel, participated in the trial for years, and acted as if they had pleaded not guilty. Given the charge of murder, the court would have been required to proceed as if a "not guilty" plea was entered regardless of the actual plea. The omission was an irregularity that did not prejudice the Appellants. Finding: The omission is a curable irregularity that did not occasion a failure of justice. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected because the mandatory nature of Section 196 (Issue 1) is tempered by the constitutional threshold in Article 138(1) (Issue 2). The court determined that while the procedural requirement is mandatory, the constitutional proviso acts as a filter, preventing the reversal of convictions for irregularities that do not impact the fairness of the trial (Issue 3). Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. The court held that the failure to record a formal arraignment was a procedural irregularity that did not prejudice the Appellants or result in a failure of justice. Keywords Section 196 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act — Arraignment of Accused — Fundamental requirement — Failure to comply — Prejudice to substantial rights — Failure of justice — Trial by High Court — Plea of not guilty — Constitutional provisions		SC/APPEAL/115/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_115_2014.pdf
sc-appeal-39-2019	22 January 2020	Thiruganapillai Sivakumar v. Attorney-General	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R.Amarasekera, J	Identification of the accused — Evidence of witnesses — Culpable homicide not amounting to murder — Robbery — Reasonable doubt — Evaluation of dock statement — Burden of proof	Facts The Accused-Appellant, Thiruganapillai Sivakumar, was charged with the murder of Thilagaraja Jegath Janani under section 296 of the Penal Code and robbery under section 383 of the Penal Code. The prosecution alleged that a witness named Yesurasan heard a woman screaming and observed the Accused-Appellant grappling with her. Subsequently, a toddy tapper named Marrie and his son, Tiron Ranjith, arrived at the scene. Tiron Ranjith chased a person fleeing the scene and struck him with a club but could not identify him due to mud on the person's face. The Accused-Appellant was arrested eleven months after the incident. An identification parade was conducted where Yesurasan identified the Accused-Appellant, but Tiron Ranjith failed to do so. At trial, Tiron Ranjith testified that the Accused-Appellant present in court was not the person he had chased from the scene. The Accused-Appellant testified under oath, denying participation in the crime and asserting he was traveling by bus at the time. The High Court convicted the Accused-Appellant of culpable homicide not amounting to murder under section 297 of the Penal Code and robbery. The Court of Appeal affirmed these convictions. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal erred in law by failing to consider evidence regarding the identification of the Accused-Appellant. Whether the trial judgeâ€™s failure to explicitly accept or reject the sworn evidence of the Accused-Appellant warrants an acquittal. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code, Section 296 Penal Code, Section 297 Penal Code, Section 383 Cases Referred to: Ariyadasa v. Queen - [68-nlr-66] Martin Singho v. Queen - [69-clw-1] Queen v. Kularatne - [71-nlr-1] Analysis Issue 1: Identification of the Accused-Appellant Rule applied: The court applied the principle that prosecution evidence must be proven beyond reasonable doubt, particularly regarding witness identification. Court's reasoning: The court noted that Tiron Ranjithâ€™s testimony—that the Accused-Appellant was not the person he chased—directly contradicted the identification made by his father, Yesurasan. The court found that this contradiction created a reasonable doubt regarding the reliability of Yesurasanâ€™s identification. Furthermore, the court observed that the Court of Appeal failed to adequately evaluate whether the Accused-Appellant had been properly identified at trial. Finding: The identification evidence was insufficient to sustain the conviction, as the conflicting testimonies created a reasonable doubt. Issue 2: Evaluation of Accusedâ€™s Evidence Rule applied: The court applied the guidelines established in Ariyadasa v. Queen, Martin Singho v. Queen, and Queen v. Kularatne regarding the evaluation of an accused person's evidence. Court's reasoning: The court held that a trial judge has a duty to either accept or reject an accused's sworn evidence. If the evidence is believed, it must be acted upon; if it raises a reasonable doubt, the defense must succeed; and if it is neither accepted nor rejected, the defense must also succeed. In this case, the trial judge failed to perform this duty, leaving the Accused-Appellantâ€™s testimony regarding his alibi unaddressed. Finding: The failure of the trial judge to evaluate the Accused-Appellant's evidence entitles the Accused-Appellant to an acquittal. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues reinforce each other. The failure to properly identify the Accused-Appellant, combined with the trial judge's failure to properly evaluate the Accused-Appellant's sworn testimony, collectively demonstrate that the prosecution failed to meet the burden of proof required in a criminal trial. Conclusion The Supreme Court answered the question of law in the affirmative, holding that the prosecution failed to prove its case beyond reasonable doubt. The judgments of the Court of Appeal and the High Court were set aside, and the Accused-Appellant was acquitted of all charges. Keywords Identification of the accused — Evidence of witnesses — Culpable homicide not amounting to murder — Robbery — Reasonable doubt — Evaluation of dock statement — Burden of proof		SC/FR/39/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_39_2019.pdf
sc-appeal-31-2011	26 January 2020	Ramanayake v. Attorney General	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Burden of proof — Denial of offence — Balance of probability — Evaluation of accused's evidence — Reasonable doubt — Conviction — Penal Code — Criminal Procedure Code — Compensation	Facts The Accused-Appellant, Priyantha Lal Ramanayake, was charged with the robbery of a gold chain valued at Rs. 24,000, an offence punishable under Section 380 of the Penal Code. Following a trial in the Magistrate's Court, the Accused-Appellant was convicted and sentenced to one year of rigorous imprisonment, a fine of Rs. 1,500, and an order to pay Rs. 100,000 as compensation to the victim, Nandani. The Accused-Appellant appealed this conviction and sentence to the High Court of Hambantota. The High Court affirmed the Magistrate's judgment and dismissed the appeal. The Accused-Appellant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal on questions of law regarding the burden of proof, the evaluation of the defence, and the legality of the compensation order. Issues Whether the High Court erred in affirming the conviction despite the Magistrate's misdirection regarding the burden of proof and the evaluation of the defence. Whether the High Court erred in affirming the order for compensation without considering its legality and justification. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code High Court of Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 Criminal Procedure Code (Amendment) Act No. 47 Cases Referred to: Martin Singho v. Queen - [69-clw-21] Ariyadasa v. Queen - [68-nlr-169] Queen v. Kularatne - [71-nlr-529] Analysis Issue 1: Burden of Proof and Evaluation of Defence Rule applied: The court applied the principle that an accused person who does not rely on a general or special exception under the Penal Code bears no burden to establish any fact. Furthermore, the court established that if a court neither rejects nor accepts the evidence of an accused, the defence must succeed. Court's reasoning: The Magistrate erroneously held that the Accused-Appellant was required to prove his defence of denial on a balance of probability. The Supreme Court clarified that since the Accused-Appellant merely denied the charge and did not invoke any statutory exceptions, the burden remained entirely on the prosecution to prove guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. Additionally, the Magistrate failed to either accept or reject the Accused-Appellant's sworn testimony. Relying on Martin Singho v. Queen, Ariyadasa v. Queen, and Queen v. Kularatne, the Court held that if the evidence of an accused raises a reasonable doubt, or if the court remains neutral regarding the credibility of that evidence, the accused is entitled to an acquittal. Finding: The High Court erred in affirming the conviction, as the Magistrate committed a grave error in law regarding the burden of proof and failed to properly evaluate the Accused-Appellant's evidence. Issue 2: Legality of Compensation Rule applied: The court considered the propriety of the compensation order under the sentencing framework. Court's reasoning: Because the Court found that the prosecution failed to prove its case beyond a reasonable doubt, the underlying conviction was set aside. Consequently, the ancillary orders, including the order for compensation, became moot. Finding: The issue regarding the legality of the compensation order did not require further determination as the conviction was overturned. Interaction of Issues The resolution of the first issue regarding the burden of proof and the failure to properly evaluate the evidence was dispositive of the entire appeal. By finding that the prosecution failed to meet the standard of proof beyond a reasonable doubt, the Court rendered the remaining questions of law regarding the suspended sentence and the compensation order unnecessary for the final determination. Conclusion The Supreme Court set aside the judgments of the High Court and the Magistrate's Court. The Accused-Appellant was acquitted of all charges. Keywords Burden of proof — Denial of offence — Balance of probability — Evaluation of accused's evidence — Reasonable doubt — Conviction — Penal Code — Criminal Procedure Code — Compensation		SC/APPEAL/31/2011	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_31_2011.pdf
sc-fr-458-2012	27 January 2020	Arangallage Samantha and Others v. The Officer-in-charge of the Police Station and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC, J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; E. A. G. R. Amarasekera, J	Fundamental rights — Article 11 (freedom from torture) — Article 12(1) (equality before the law) — Article 13(1) (right to be informed of reason for arrest) — Police conduct — Assault — Medical evidence — Contradictions in testimony — Credibility of evidence — Burden of proof	Facts The Petitioner, a three-wheeler driver, alleged that his fundamental rights under Articles 11, 12(1), and 13(1) of the Constitution were violated by the Respondents. He claimed that on June 28, 2012, two unidentified persons in civilian clothing blocked his vehicle and forcibly took him to the Biyagama Police Station. He stated that he was detained there for half an hour without reason before being handed over to the 4th and 5th Respondents from the Mirihana Police Station. The Petitioner alleged that while he was driving his three-wheeler to the Mirihana Police Station under the supervision of the 4th and 5th Respondents, the 5th Respondent assaulted him on the head and ear, causing him to faint. He further claimed that he was detained at the Mirihana Police Station until the early hours of the following day. The Petitioner sought medical treatment at a private hospital, for which no records were provided, and was later admitted to the District General Hospital, Gampaha, on July 5, 2012. The medical evidence included a Medico-Legal Report noting no external injuries and an audiological evaluation suggesting hearing loss. The Respondents provided an excerpt from the Information Book of the Mirihana Special Crimes Investigation Unit, which stated that the Petitioner was stopped to assist in locating a suspect and was subsequently released after being produced before senior officers. Issues Whether the arrest and detention of the Petitioner constituted a violation of Article 13(1) of the Constitution. Whether the alleged assault by the 4th and 5th Respondents constituted a violation of the Petitioner's right to freedom from torture under Article 11 of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioner was denied the equal protection of the law under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 11, 12(1), 13(1), 126. Cases Referred to: Samanthilaka v. Ernest Perera - [1990/v1-sri-lr-318] Velmurugu v. The Attorney General - [1981/v1-sri-lr-406] Vivienne Goonewardene v. Hector Perera - [1983/v1-sri-lr-305] Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 13(1) Rule applied: Article 13(1) of the Constitution, which requires that any person arrested shall be informed of the reason for their arrest. Court's reasoning: The Petitioner failed to cite the officers from the Biyagama Police Station who initially arrested him. The 4th and 5th Respondents only took custody of the Petitioner after the arrest had already occurred. Furthermore, the Petitioner's account was contradicted by the Information Book excerpts, which suggested a different sequence of events. Given the lack of evidence regarding the initial arrest and the contradictions in the Petitioner's narrative, the court found the assertion of an illegal arrest unsubstantiated. Finding: The allegation of a violation of Article 13(1) was not established. Issue 2: Violation of Article 11 Rule applied: The test of a high degree of probability, as established in Velmurugu v. The Attorney General and Vivienne Goonewardene v. Hector Perera. Court's reasoning: The court applied the standard of a high degree of probability, noting that the gravity of the allegation requires clear evidence. The Petitioner's medical records showed no external injuries, and the medical reports failed to link the Petitioner's hearing loss to the alleged assault. Additionally, the court found the Petitioner's claim that he was assaulted while driving to be implausible, as it would have endangered the safety of the vehicle's occupants. The inconsistencies between the Petitioner's petition and his initial complaint regarding the number of officers involved further undermined his credibility. Finding: The Petitioner failed to satisfy the required standard of proof to establish a violation of Article 11. Issue 3: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution regarding the right to equal protection of the law. Court's reasoning: The court determined that the Petitioner failed to provide evidence indicating that he was treated differently or denied the equal protection of the law. As the allegations of torture and illegal arrest were not proven, there was no basis to conclude that the Petitioner's right to equality was infringed. Finding: The allegation of a violation of Article 12(1) was not established. Interaction of Issues The findings on the alleged violations are interconnected through the court's assessment of the Petitioner's credibility. The contradictions in the Petitioner's version of events, combined with the lack of corroborating medical evidence, led the court to reject the factual basis for all three alleged constitutional infringements. The failure to prove the underlying facts of the arrest and the assault rendered the claims under Articles 11, 12(1), and 13(1) unsustainable. Conclusion The court held that the Petitioner failed to prove the alleged violations of his fundamental rights to the required degree of probability. Consequently, the application is dismissed, with no order for costs. Keywords Fundamental rights — Article 11 (freedom from torture) — Article 12(1) (equality before the law) — Article 13(1) (right to be informed of reason for arrest) — Police conduct — Assault — Medical evidence — Contradictions in testimony — Credibility of evidence — Burden of proof		SC/FR/458/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_458_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-204-2015	28 January 2020	Officer-in Charge, Police Station, Sewanagala v. Impar and Others	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Appeal against sentence — Plea of guilty — Sentence bargaining — Suspended sentence — Previous conviction — Code of Criminal Procedure Act — Penal Code — Rehabilitation of offender — Deterrence	Facts The Appellant, Mohamed Irupan Impar, was charged before the Magistrate of Embilipitiya under Section 368(a) of the Penal Code for the theft of five cows and one buffalo. The Appellant pleaded guilty to the charge. The Magistrate sentenced the Appellant to six months of rigorous imprisonment and imposed a fine of Rs 1500, with a default sentence of one month of simple imprisonment. The Appellant appealed the sentence to the Provincial High Court of Sabaragamuwa, contending that the sentence was excessive and requesting a non-custodial sentence. The High Court dismissed the appeal. The Appellant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court, arguing that the Magistrate erred by failing to grant a suspended sentence despite the plea of guilt. During the proceedings, it was revealed that the Appellant had a prior conviction in the High Court of Hambantota, where he had received a suspended sentence that was still pending at the time of the current offense. Issues Whether the Magistrate erred in law by failing to grant a suspended sentence to the Appellant following his plea of guilt. Whether the Appellantâ€™s offer of compensation in exchange for a suspended sentence constitutes permissible plea bargaining or improper sentence bargaining. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code, Section 368(a) Judicature Act, Section 14(b)(i) Code of Criminal Procedure Act, Section 183(1) Code of Criminal Procedure Act, Section 303 Cases Referred to: State ex rel Clark v. Adams - [111 S.E.2d 336 (1959)] State of Gujarat v. Natwar Harchandji Thakor - [2005 CriLJ 2957] State of Uttar Pradesh v. Chandrika - [2000 Cr.L.J 384] S v. Rabie - [1975 (4) SA 855 (A)] S v. Karg - [1961 (1) SA 231 (A)] Attorney General v. Mendis - [1995/v1-sri-lr-138] Analysis Issue 1: Failure to grant a suspended sentence Rule applied: Section 303 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act. Court's reasoning: The court noted that sentencing is a discretionary judicial function that must balance the triad of the crime, the offender, and the interests of society. While suspended sentences are a progressive tool for rehabilitation, they are not mandatory. The court found that the Magistrate acted correctly by considering the Appellant's criminal record. Specifically, the Appellant was already subject to a suspended sentence from a prior conviction in the High Court of Hambantota. Imposing another suspended sentence under these circumstances would be inappropriate and contrary to the principles of deterrence and retribution. Finding: The Magistrate did not err in refusing to grant a suspended sentence. Issue 2: Sentence bargaining Rule applied: Attorney General v. Mendis - [1995/v1-sri-lr-138]. Court's reasoning: The court distinguished between a "plea of guilty" and "sentence bargaining." While a plea of guilt is a statutory process, the court emphasized that an accused cannot bargain for a reduced sentence by offering compensation. The court held that a trial judge must not surrender the "sacred right and duty" of sentencing to the accused or counsel. The Appellantâ€™s attempt to secure a suspended sentence by offering Rs. 50,000 in compensation was categorized as improper sentence bargaining, which the court explicitly frowned upon. Finding: The Appellantâ€™s attempt to bargain for a sentence was improper and did not entitle him to a reduction in punishment. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues are mutually reinforcing. The courtâ€™s determination that the Appellant engaged in improper sentence bargaining undermined his claim for leniency. Furthermore, the existence of a prior suspended sentence rendered the request for a new suspended sentence legally untenable, confirming that the Magistrateâ€™s exercise of discretion was sound and consistent with the requirements of justice. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. The Registrar is directed to implement the original sentence and to notify the High Court of Hambantota of this conviction to facilitate the activation of the Appellant's previous suspended sentence. Keywords Appeal against sentence — Plea of guilty — Sentence bargaining — Suspended sentence — Previous conviction — Code of Criminal Procedure Act — Penal Code — Rehabilitation of offender — Deterrence		SC/APPEAL/204/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_204_2015.pdf
sc-appeal-160-2010	4 February 2020	Ceylon Grain Elevators Limited v. Madihahewa and Others	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Gratuity — Salary determination — Commissioner of Labour's decision — Court of Appeal's review — Reasons for administrative decisions	Facts The Petitioner-Appellant, Ceylon Grain Elevators Limited, challenged a decision made by the Commissioner General of Labour regarding the payment of gratuity to the 4th Respondent, Ooi Eng Hooi. The Commissioner of Labour had directed the Petitioner-Appellant to pay gratuity based on the finding that the 4th Respondent was employed by the company from June 1, 1988, to July 25, 2004, at a monthly salary of US$ 5600. The Petitioner-Appellant filed a writ application in the Court of Appeal to contest this directive, but the Court of Appeal dismissed the application on August 28, 2009. Subsequently, the Petitioner-Appellant appealed to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal on specific questions of law regarding the salary calculation and the adequacy of the reasons provided by the Commissioner of Labour. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal erred in failing to consider the alleged illegality of the order regarding the 4th Respondent's salary of US$ 5600, which the Petitioner-Appellant claimed was unsupported by evidence. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in failing to consider the impact of the 1st and 2nd Respondents' failure to provide reasons for the impugned order despite a written request. Applicable Law Statutes: Not specified Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Salary Calculation Rule applied: The court applied an evidentiary review of the documents marked 'B' and 'D' to determine the factual basis of the salary and employment period. Court's reasoning: The Petitioner-Appellant argued that the 4th Respondent did not earn US$ 5600 per month. However, the court examined document 'B', which indicated that the 4th Respondent received a basic salary and a pensionable allowance totaling US$ 5600 per month. Additionally, document 'D' confirmed the employment period from June 1, 1988, to July 25, 2004. The court found the Petitioner-Appellant's contention to be contradicted by the documentary evidence. Finding: The court rejected the Petitioner-Appellant's argument and found that the salary calculation was supported by the evidence. Issue 2: Failure to Provide Reasons Rule applied: The court reviewed documents '2R1' and 'X17' to determine if the Commissioner of Labour had fulfilled the requirement to provide reasons for the decision. Court's reasoning: The Petitioner-Appellant contended that the Commissioner of Labour failed to provide reasons for the directive. Upon reviewing the documents marked '2R1' and 'X17', the court determined that the Commissioner of Labour had indeed provided sufficient reasons for the decision. Finding: The court rejected the contention that the Commissioner of Labour failed to provide reasons. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues are mutually reinforcing. By confirming that the documentary evidence supported the salary figure and that the Commissioner of Labour had provided adequate reasons for the decision, the court established that the administrative directive was both factually sound and procedurally compliant. Consequently, the resolution of these issues led to the conclusion that the Court of Appeal was correct in dismissing the writ application. Conclusion The Supreme Court answered both questions of law in the negative. The judgment of the Court of Appeal dated August 28, 2009, is affirmed, and the appeal is dismissed with costs. Keywords Gratuity — Salary determination — Commissioner of Labour's decision — Court of Appeal's review — Reasons for administrative decisions		SC/APPEAL/160/2010	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_160_2010.pdf
sc-appeal-138-2017	12 February 2020	Peiris v. Noratel International (Pvt.) Ltd.	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Industrial Disputes Act — Termination of employment — Unfair dismissal — Compensation — Equity jurisdiction of Labour Tribunal — Standard of proof — Balance of probabilities — Misconduct — Pilfering petrol — False evidence — Financial loss — Prospective employment	Facts The Employee-Respondent was employed as a technical supervisor in the production maintenance division of the Employer-Appellant. On 15th January 2001, a security officer observed the Employee-Respondent pouring petrol from a container into his motorcycle. Following a domestic inquiry into the incident, the Employee-Respondent was found guilty of pilfering company petrol and his services were terminated. The Employee-Respondent subsequently filed an application before the Labour Tribunal. The President of the Labour Tribunal determined that the termination was unreasonable and ordered compensation in lieu of reinstatement, amounting to Rs. 732,424/-. The Employer-Appellant appealed this decision to the Provincial High Court, which affirmed the Labour Tribunal's order and dismissed the appeal. The Employer-Appellant then appealed to the Supreme Court. Throughout the appellate proceedings, the Employee-Respondent remained absent and unrepresented despite receiving notice. Issues Whether the orders of the Provincial High Court and the Labour Tribunal regarding the calculation and award of compensation are consistent with the principles of industrial law. Whether the relief awarded to the Employee-Respondent is just and equitable considering the facts and circumstances of the case. Applicable Law Statutes: Industrial Disputes Act No. 43 of 1950 (as amended) Cases Referred to: Associated Battery Manufacturers (Ceylon) Ltd v. United Engineering Workers Union - [77-nlr-541] Richard Peiris and Co. Ltd v. D.J. Wijesiriwardena - [62-nlr-233] Moosajees Limited v. Eksath Engineru Saha Samanya Kamkaru Samithiya - [79/v1-nlr-285] Saleem v. Hatton National Bank - [1994/v2-sri-lr-379] The Caledonian (Ceylon) Tea and Rubber Estates Ltd. v. J.S. Hillman - [79/v1-nlr-421] Bank of America v. Abeygunasekara - [1991/v1-sri-lr-317] Jayasuriya v. Sri Lanka State Plantation Corporation - [1995/v2-sri-lr-379] Fentiman v. Fluid Engineering Products Ltd. - [1991-irlr-150] Associated Newspapers of Ceylon Ltd. v. Jayasinghe - [Not specified] Analysis Issue 1: Consistency of compensation calculation Rule applied: Section 33 of the Industrial Disputes Act No. 43 of 1950 and the principle that compensation is an indemnity for actual financial loss. Court's reasoning: The court noted that the Employee-Respondent provided false testimony under oath regarding his employment status, claiming he was unemployed when he had actually secured higher-paying employment. The court held that compensation must be based on actual financial loss rather than sentimental harm. Following the principles in Jayasuriya v. Sri Lanka State Plantation Corporation, the court determined that remuneration from fresh employment must be deducted from the loss. The Labour Tribunal failed to account for the Employee-Respondent's new employment, rendering the original compensation award unreasonable. Finding: The court answered the question of law negatively, finding the original calculation inconsistent with established principles of industrial law. Issue 2: Just and equitable nature of the relief Rule applied: Section 31C(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act No. 43 of 1950 and the equity jurisdiction of the Labour Tribunal. Court's reasoning: While the court affirmed the finding that the termination was unreasonable, it held that the Labour Tribunalâ€™s discretion must be exercised within the framework of the law and not as a philanthropic organization. The Employee-Respondentâ€™s dishonesty under oath regarding his post-termination employment status disqualified him from receiving an enhanced award. The court determined that a just and equitable award should be limited to the actual financial loss incurred during the period of unemployment. Finding: The court found the original award was not just and equitable and reduced the compensation to ten months' salary. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues are intrinsically linked. The determination that the compensation calculation was legally inconsistent (Issue 1) directly informed the court's conclusion that the original award failed to meet the "just and equitable" standard (Issue 2). By correcting the calculation method to reflect actual financial loss, the court ensured the final order aligned with the statutory requirements of the Industrial Disputes Act. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The order of the Labour Tribunal regarding the finding of unreasonable termination is affirmed, but the compensation award is varied to ten months' salary, totaling Rs. 262,580/-. Keywords Industrial Disputes Act — Termination of employment — Unfair dismissal — Compensation — Equity jurisdiction of Labour Tribunal — Standard of proof — Balance of probabilities — Misconduct — Pilfering petrol — False evidence — Financial loss — Prospective employment		SC/APPEAL/138/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_138_2017.pdf
sc-fr-21-2019	12 February 2020	Methanga and Prabath v. Herath and Others	Jayantha Jayasuriya PC, J	Jayantha Jayasuriya PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekera, J	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — School admissions — Residency requirements — Proximity category — Deeds of transfer — Deeds of gift — Marking scheme — Circular interpretation — Arbitrary decision	Facts The first petitioner is a minor child, and the second petitioner is his father. The second petitioner applied for the admission of the first petitioner to Grade 1 at Sirimavo Bandaranayake Vidyalaya for the year 2019 under the proximity category. The second petitioner had resided at the premises in question since childhood. His mother became the legal owner of the premises in 2004 and subsequently gifted the property to the second petitioner via a deed of gift attested on 18 October 2017. The interview board awarded the petitioners 3 marks for residency based on the deed of gift, representing the period of ownership from 2017 to the application date. The petitioners contended that they were entitled to additional marks for the 13-year period during which the second petitioner's mother held the title, arguing that the relevant circular permitted such aggregation. The petitioners appealed the decision to the Appeal and Objection Investigation Board, but they were not informed of the outcome. The petitioners subsequently filed this application alleging that the refusal to admit the child was arbitrary, unreasonable, and a violation of Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Issues Whether the failure to aggregate the period of ownership of the second petitioner's mother with that of the second petitioner when calculating residency marks under Circular 24/2018 was arbitrary, unreasonable, and a violation of Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1) Circular 24/2018 of the Ministry of Education Cases Referred to: S.M.N.S. Thilakaratne v. M.W.D.T.P. Wanasinghe - [sc-appeal-30/2018] Royster Guano Co v. Commonwealth of Virginia - [1920-253-US-412] Haputhantirige v. Attorney-General - [2007/v1-sri-lr-101] M.K. Wijethunga v. The Principal, Southlands College - [2005-b.l.r-19] Karunathilaka v. Jayalath de Silva - [2003/v1-sri-lr-35] Analysis Issue 1: Aggregation of residency marks Rule applied: Clause 7.2.1.1 of Circular 24/2018 regarding the marking scheme for residency based on title deeds. Court's reasoning: The Court examined the objective of the proximity category, which is to verify the residency of the applicant. The Court found that the circular recognizes both the applicant/spouse and the parents of the applicant/spouse as valid parties for establishing residency. The Court reasoned that the second petitioner's residency was continuous, and the transfer of ownership between the mother and the son did not diminish the residency status but rather enhanced the second petitioner's legal standing. Denying marks for the mother's period of ownership, which occurred within the relevant five-year window, was found to be an artificial interpretation that defeated the purpose of the scheme. The Court noted that the 2020 admission circular explicitly allowed such aggregation, suggesting that the previous denial was an oversight that caused grave prejudice and was inconsistent with the principle of equality. Finding: The failure to award marks for the deed of transfer in the name of the second petitioner's mother was arbitrary, unreasonable, and a violation of Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Conclusion The Court held that the petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) were violated. The first respondent was directed to admit the first petitioner to the second grade of Sirimavo Bandaranayake Vidyalaya forthwith. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — School admissions — Residency requirements — Proximity category — Deeds of transfer — Deeds of gift — Marking scheme — Circular interpretation — Arbitrary decision		SC/FR/21/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_21_2019.pdf
sc-appeal-79-2012	17 February 2020	Superintendent Udaweriya Estate and Agarapatana Plantations Limited and Lankem Tea and Rubber Plantation Limited v. Lanka Wathu Seva Sangamaya	P. Padman Surasena, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Industrial Disputes Act — Termination of service — Domestic inquiry — Rules of natural justice — Standard of proof — Labour Tribunal — High Court appeal — Question of law — Evidence — Stealing tea	Facts The Applicant, a trade union, filed an application in the Labour Tribunal on behalf of a workman, K. Jayaratne, seeking reinstatement, compensation, and back wages following the termination of his employment by the Employer. The Employer, comprising the Superintendent of Udaweriya Estate and two plantation companies, admitted the termination but justified it on the grounds that the workman, a Junior Assistant Factory Officer, was found guilty of stealing tea during a domestic inquiry. The workman denied the allegations and filed a replication. The Labour Tribunal conducted an inquiry and concluded that the termination was justifiable, ultimately dismissing the workman's application. The workman appealed this decision to the High Court of Uva Province. The High Court set aside the Tribunal's order and awarded the workman compensation equivalent to three years' salary, reasoning that the domestic inquiry was procedurally flawed, the rules of natural justice were not followed, and the workman had been acquitted of related criminal charges in the Magistrate's Court. The Employer subsequently appealed this High Court decision to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Provincial High Court erred in law and acted in excess of jurisdiction by disturbing the findings of fact made by the Labour Tribunal. Whether the Provincial High Court erred in law in awarding compensation when the circumstances did not warrant such relief. Whether the Provincial High Court erred in law by failing to correctly identify the petitioner against whom the order was made. Applicable Law Statutes: Industrial Disputes Act, Section 31 D (2) Industrial Disputes Act, Section 31 D (3) High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990, Section 9 Cases Referred to: K. B. D. Somawathie v. Baksons Textile Industries Ltd. - [79/v1-nlr-2014] Caledonian (Ceylon) Tea & Rubber Estates, Ltd. v. J. S. Hillman - [79/v1-nlr-421] Inland Revenue v. Fraser - [1942/v24-tax-cases-498] Edwards v. Baristow - [1956/v3-aer-57] Analysis Issue 1: Disturbance of findings of fact Rule applied: Section 31 D (3) of the Industrial Disputes Act and the principle that appellate courts should not interfere with findings of fact unless they are perverse or unsupported by evidence. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court found that the High Court failed to appreciate the limited scope of its appellate jurisdiction. The evidence presented before the Labour Tribunal, including testimony from witnesses who observed the workman moving tea bags and the workman's own admission of the act, provided a cogent basis for the Tribunal's findings. The High Court's conclusion that the evidence was insufficient was based on a misconception of facts and a failure to distinguish between the criminal standard of proof (beyond reasonable doubt) and the standard applicable to Labour Tribunal inquiries. Finding: The High Court erred in law by interfering with findings of fact that were supported by evidence and were not perverse. Issue 2: Awarding of compensation Rule applied: The principle that a Labour Tribunal must make a "just and equitable" order, which does not require a formal domestic inquiry or the production of a charge sheet as a mandatory prerequisite. Court's reasoning: The High Court's decision to award compensation was predicated on the erroneous belief that the absence of a formal domestic inquiry and the failure to follow strict procedural rules rendered the termination unjust. The Supreme Court clarified that there is no mandatory legal requirement for a formal domestic inquiry under the Industrial Disputes Act. Since the Employer successfully proved the misconduct before the Tribunal, the termination was a just and equitable step to maintain workplace discipline. Finding: The High Court erred in awarding compensation as the termination was found to be justified based on the evidence. Interaction of Issues The findings on the issues are intrinsically linked. The High Courtâ€™s erroneous legal interpretation regarding the necessity of a formal domestic inquiry and the standard of proof led it to incorrectly characterize the Tribunal's factual findings as perverse. Once the Supreme Court determined that the Tribunal's findings were supported by cogent evidence and that the procedural requirements imposed by the High Court were not mandatory, the basis for awarding compensation was effectively nullified. Conclusion The appeal is allowed, and the judgment of the High Court dated 13th December 2011 is set aside. The decision of the Labour Tribunal dated 05th August 2010 is restored. Keywords Industrial Disputes Act — Termination of service — Domestic inquiry — Rules of natural justice — Standard of proof — Labour Tribunal — High Court appeal — Question of law — Evidence — Stealing tea		SC/APPEAL/79/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_79_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-174-2014	17 February 2020	Cooray v. Cooray and Cooray	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Revocation of deed of gift — Ingratitude — Gross ingratitude — Irrevocable deed — Assault — Foul language — Intimidation — Roman Dutch Law — Donee's conduct — State of mind of the donee — Krishnaswamy Vs Thillaiyampalam — Podi Nona Ranaweera Menike Vs Rohini Senanayake	Facts The deceased plaintiff, Bulathsinhalage Edwin Cooray, gifted his dwelling house in Kirulapona to his youngest son, the defendant, Bulathsinhalage Harschandra Wasantha Cooray, via an irrevocable deed of gift while retaining a life interest for himself. Following the execution of the deed, the defendant engaged in a pattern of conduct characterized by demanding immediate possession of the house, using foul language, and intimidating the plaintiff. On one occasion, the defendant assaulted his sister with a stool and subsequently struck the plaintiff with the same object, causing injuries to the plaintiff's forehead and lips. The plaintiff initiated action in the District Court of Mount Lavinia to revoke the deed of gift on the grounds of gross ingratitude. The District Court ruled in favor of the plaintiff, holding that the defendant's conduct, including the verbal abuse and the physical assault, constituted sufficient grounds for the revocation of the gift. The Court of Appeal affirmed this decision, leading the defendant to appeal to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the lower courts erred in law by concluding that the plaintiff was entitled to revoke the irrevocable deed of gift based on the evidence of the defendant's conduct. Whether the trial court erred in law by concluding that the defendantâ€™s use of foul language and intimidation constituted gross ingratitude. Whether the lower courts erred in law by failing to consider the state of mind of the donee when determining that a single act of assault constituted gross ingratitude. Whether the lower courts failed to objectively analyze the evidence in holding that the respondent was entitled to revoke the deed of gift. Applicable Law Statutes: Not specified Cases Referred to: Krishnaswamy v. Thillaiyampalam - [59-nlr-265] Podinona Ranaweera Menike v. Rohini Senanayake - [1992/v2-sri-lr-180] Manuelpillai v. Nallamma - [52-nlr-221] Sivarasipillai v. Anthonypillai - [40-nlr-47] Stella Perera v. Margaret Silva - [2002/v1-sri-lr] Fernando v. Perera - [63-nlr-236] Hamine v. Gunawardene - [17-nlr-507] Analysis Issue 1: Entitlement to revoke the deed Rule applied: Roman Dutch Law principles regarding the revocation of gifts for gross ingratitude, specifically the five recognized causes including the laying of "manus impias" (impious hands) on the donor. Court's reasoning: The court held that ingratitude is a frame of mind inferred from conduct. The defendant's actions—persistent harassment, intimidation, and physical assault—constituted "manus impias" and atrocious injury. The court emphasized that there is no hard and fast rule requiring multiple acts, as a single act of sufficient gravity can justify revocation. Finding: The lower courts did not err; the plaintiff was entitled to revoke the gift. Issue 2: Foul language and intimidation Rule applied: Roman Dutch Law principles as interpreted in Krishnaswamy v. Thillaiyampalam. Court's reasoning: The court rejected the appellant's reliance on older precedents, noting that subsequent judicial pronouncements have established that verbal abuse and intimidation, when coupled with other acts of ingratitude, are relevant factors. Finding: The trial court correctly identified these actions as part of the defendant's ungrateful conduct. Issue 3: State of mind of the donee Rule applied: The principle that ingratitude is a frame of mind inferred from the donee's conduct after the execution of the gift. Court's reasoning: The court found that the defendant's state of mind was clearly evidenced by his persistent efforts to dispossess the donor of his life interest. This conduct demonstrated a lack of gratitude that the law does not condone. Finding: The lower courts did not err in their assessment of the defendant's state of mind. Issue 4: Objective analysis of evidence Rule applied: Standard of appellate review regarding the analysis of facts and circumstances. Court's reasoning: The court found that the lower courts thoroughly analyzed the evidence, including the uncontroverted testimony regarding the assault. The court dismissed the appellant's argument that the trial judge was hesitant, noting that the judgment clearly condemned the defendant's actions. Finding: The lower courts conducted a fair and objective analysis of the evidence. Interaction of Issues The findings on the specific acts of ingratitude (verbal abuse and physical assault) reinforced the court's conclusion regarding the defendant's overall state of mind. The resolution of the factual issues regarding the assault rendered the legal questions concerning the sufficiency of a "single act" moot, as the court affirmed that such conduct falls squarely within the established legal grounds for revocation. Conclusion The Supreme Court answered all questions of law in the negative and in favor of the plaintiff-respondent. The appeal is dismissed, and the judgments of the Court of Appeal and the District Court are affirmed. Keywords Revocation of deed of gift — Ingratitude — Gross ingratitude — Irrevocable deed — Assault — Foul language — Intimidation — Roman Dutch Law — Donee's conduct — State of mind of the donee — Krishnaswamy Vs Thillaiyampalam — Podi Nona Ranaweera Menike Vs Rohini Senanayake		SC/APPEAL/174/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_174_2014.pdf
sc-fr-291-2016	19 February 2020	Hewa Imaduwage v. De Silva and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; Priyantha Jayawardena PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Promotion to Lieutenant Colonel — Promotion to Colonel — Battle casualty — Medical board — Retirement on medical grounds — Disciplinary action — Court of Inquiry — Army Routine Order — Supernumerary cadre	Facts The Petitioner, an officer in the Sri Lanka Army, alleged the infringement of his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution regarding the denial of promotion to the rank of Lieutenant Colonel with his contemporary intake and the subsequent denial of promotion to the rank of Colonel. The Petitioner joined the Army in 1990 and was categorized as a battle casualty after sustaining injuries in 2000. Despite his injuries, he continued in service and was promoted to the rank of Lieutenant Colonel on a temporary basis in 2016. The Petitioner faced disciplinary investigations regarding the unauthorized use of military personnel and resources for the construction of his private residence. Consequently, the Petitioner was warned and placed on a one-year probationary period in 2014. During this period, the Army Board twice declined to confirm his rank of Lieutenant Colonel due to the pending investigations and his failure to meet selection criteria. The Petitioner was eventually informed that he would be retired from service. Although he appealed these decisions through the Redress of Grievance procedure and subsequently to the President, his requests for retroactive promotion to the rank of Lieutenant Colonel and further promotion to the rank of Colonel were not granted in the manner he sought. The Petitioner was ultimately retired on medical grounds. Issues Whether the failure to promote the Petitioner to the rank of Lieutenant Colonel with his contemporary intake and to the rank of Colonel constituted a violation of the Petitioner's fundamental right to equality under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the Respondents' decision to retire the Petitioner with effect from 02nd April 2016, despite his continued service until August 2016, was malicious and discriminatory. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 17, 126 Army Act No. 14 of 1949, Section 32 Cases Referred to: Gamaethige v. Siriwardena - [1988/v1-sri-lr-384] Wijesinghe v. Attorney General - [1978-1979-1980/v1-sri-lr-102] Snowden v. Hughes - [321-us-1] Analysis Issue 1: Entitlement to Promotion Rule applied: Regulation 12 of the Army Officers Service Regulations (Regular Force) 1992 and Army Routine Order No. 90/2009. Court's reasoning: The court observed that promotion to the rank of Colonel is based on selection and requires the officer to be best qualified. The Petitionerâ€™s disciplinary record, specifically the unauthorized use of military personnel and resources for private construction, provided a rational basis for the Army Boards to decline his confirmation and promotion. The court distinguished the Petitioner's situation from other officers he cited, noting that the Petitioner had been subjected to disciplinary proceedings, whereas the others had not. Finding: The Petitioner failed to establish that he was similarly situated to those who received promotions or that there was purposeful discrimination. Issue 2: Timing of Retirement Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The Respondents explained that the Petitionerâ€™s continued presence in service until August 2016, despite the official retirement date of 02nd April 2016, was due to routine administrative delays in processing retirement papers and the pendency of the Petitioner's own appeals. The court found no evidence of malice or intentional discrimination in this administrative process. Finding: The delay in the effective cessation of duties did not constitute a violation of the Petitioner's fundamental rights. Interaction of Issues The findings on the disciplinary record and the failure to meet promotion criteria directly informed the court's assessment of the retirement decision. Because the Petitioner was not entitled to the promotions he sought due to his conduct, the administrative handling of his retirement date did not result in a denial of a promotion he was otherwise qualified to receive. Conclusion The Petitioner failed to establish that his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution were violated by the Respondents. The application is dismissed. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Promotion to Lieutenant Colonel — Promotion to Colonel — Battle casualty — Medical board — Retirement on medical grounds — Disciplinary action — Court of Inquiry — Army Routine Order — Supernumerary cadre		SC/FR/291/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_291_2016.pdf
sc-appeal-74-2008	25 February 2020	Geekiyanage v. Vijithweera	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Priyantha Jayawardena, PC J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Ex parte trial — Default judgment — Application to purge default — Civil Procedure Code — Appeal against order refusing to purge default — Revisionary jurisdiction of Civil Appellate High Court — Section 88(1) of the Civil Procedure Code — Section 753 and 839 of the Civil Procedure Code	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant, Geekiyanage Viyantha Vijithweera, initiated an action against the Defendant-Respondent, Mohamed Thuwan, in the District Court of Nuwaraeliya. Upon the Defendant-Respondent failing to respond to summons, the District Judge fixed the matter for an ex parte trial. Following the trial, the District Judge entered judgment in favor of the Plaintiff-Appellant on 13.7.2000. The Fiscal reported that the decree was served on the Defendant-Respondent on 8.8.2000. The Defendant-Respondent subsequently filed an application to purge the default on 30.8.2000, claiming he only received the decree on 19.8.2000. The District Judge disbelieved this claim and refused the application to purge the default on 3.4.2002. The Defendant-Respondent appealed this specific order to the Civil Appellate High Court. The Civil Appellate High Court set aside the District Judgeâ€™s order of 3.4.2002 and, in doing so, also set aside the ex parte judgment of 13.7.2000. The Plaintiff-Appellant appealed this decision to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Civil Appellate High Court had the legal authority to set aside an ex parte judgment of the District Court when the appeal before it was limited to the refusal of an application to purge default. Whether the judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court dated 8.5.2008 is liable to be set aside. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code, Section 88(1) Civil Procedure Code, Section 753 Civil Procedure Code, Section 839 Cases Referred to: Sirimavo Bandaranayake v. Times of Ceylon - [1995/v1-sri-lr-22] Analysis Issue 1: Authority to set aside ex parte judgment Rule applied: Section 88(1) of the Civil Procedure Code, which states that no appeal shall lie against any judgment entered upon default. Court's reasoning: The Court observed that the Defendant-Respondentâ€™s appeal to the Civil Appellate High Court was strictly confined to challenging the District Courtâ€™s order refusing the application to purge default. There was no appeal or revision application filed against the ex parte judgment itself. The Court distinguished the present case from Sirimavo Bandaranayake v. Times of Ceylon, noting that in Bandaranayake, the court exercised revisionary jurisdiction in a specific application for revision. In the present case, the Civil Appellate High Court lacked the procedural basis to set aside the ex parte judgment while hearing an appeal regarding the refusal to purge default. Finding: The Civil Appellate High Court exceeded its authority by setting aside the ex parte judgment in the absence of a proper appeal or revision application against that judgment. Issue 2: Validity of the High Court judgment Rule applied: Section 753 and Section 839 of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The Court rejected the argument that the Civil Appellate High Court could invoke its inherent or revisionary powers under Sections 753 and 839 to set aside the ex parte judgment in the absence of a formal challenge to that judgment. The Court emphasized that a party cannot succeed in a matter where no appeal or revision application has been filed. Furthermore, the Defendant-Respondent failed to purge the default within the statutory 14-day period. Finding: The judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court was legally flawed and liable to be set aside. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court dated 8.5.2008 is set aside, and the order of the District Judge dated 3.4.2002 is affirmed. Keywords Ex parte trial — Default judgment — Application to purge default — Civil Procedure Code — Appeal against order refusing to purge default — Revisionary jurisdiction of Civil Appellate High Court — Section 88(1) of the Civil Procedure Code — Section 753 and 839 of the Civil Procedure Code		SC/APPEAL/74/2008	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_74_2008.pdf
sc-appeal-91-2013	25 February 2020	Heling and Heling v. Weerasinghe and Gunapala	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; Gamini Amarasekara, J	Property ownership — Deed of transfer — Consideration — Fraudulent transactions — Prescription Ordinance — Ejectment — Court of Appeal orders — Notaries Ordinance	Facts The Plaintiff-Respondents filed an action against the 1st and 2nd Defendants seeking a declaration of title, ejectment from the property in dispute, and damages. The Plaintiff-Respondents asserted ownership based on Deed No. 3128. The 1st and 2nd Defendants, who are husband and wife, admitted signing the deed but contended that the agreed consideration was higher than the amount stated in the deed and that they had not received the full payment. They argued that the deed was fraudulent and that the action was prescribed. The District Court held in favor of the Plaintiff-Respondents, finding them to be the owners of the property. The Court of Appeal subsequently dismissed the appeal filed by the Defendants, though it included an order requiring the Plaintiff-Respondents to pay the Defendants a balance sum of Rs. 300,000 upon the handing over of vacant possession. The 1st Defendant appealed to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal on several questions of law regarding the validity of the deed, the applicability of the Prescription Ordinance, and the nature of the action. Issues Whether the non-payment of the full consideration stated in a deed of transfer renders the deed invalid. Whether an action for ejectment is maintainable without a specific prayer for a declaration of title. Whether the action was barred by Section 6 of the Prescription Ordinance. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in ordering the payment of a balance sum as a condition for ejectment. Applicable Law Statutes: Prescription Ordinance, Section 6 Notaries Ordinance Cases Referred to: Jayawardena v. Amarasekara - [15-nlr-280] Mohamadu v. Hussim - [16-nlr-368] Jayasinghe v. Tikiri Banda - [1988/v2-calr-24] Dharmasiri v. Wickramatunga - [2002/v2-sri-lr-218] Pathirana v. Jayasundara - [58-nlr-169] Analysis Issue 1: Validity of the deed Rule applied: The principle that a notarial conveyance is complete upon execution, and the non-payment of consideration does not invalidate the deed absent fraud or misrepresentation (Jayawardena v. Amarasekara; Mohamadu v. Hussim). Court's reasoning: The court found no evidence of fraud regarding the consideration stated in Deed No. 3128. It held that even if the full consideration was not paid, the remedy for the seller is to initiate a separate action for recovery, rather than seeking the cancellation of the deed. Finding: The deed is valid, and the Plaintiff-Respondents are the rightful owners. Issue 2: Ejectment without declaration of title Rule applied: The principle that upon proof of title, a plaintiff is entitled to a decree for ejectment even without a specific prayer for a declaration of title (Jayasinghe v. Tikiri Banda; Dharmasiri v. Wickramatunga; Pathirana v. Jayasundara). Court's reasoning: The court noted that the Plaintiff-Respondents pleaded their title in the plaint and that the trial court correctly determined the issue of ownership in their favor. Finding: The absence of a specific prayer for a declaration of title does not preclude the relief of ejectment. Issue 3: Prescription Rule applied: Section 6 of the Prescription Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court determined that this was not a possessory action, as the Plaintiff-Respondents claimed ownership based on a deed. Furthermore, the action was not based on a contract or written security falling under Section 6. Finding: The action is not barred by Section 6 of the Prescription Ordinance. Issue 4: Conditional order for payment Rule applied: The principle that a completed sale transfers ownership, and the seller's remedy for unpaid consideration is a separate action. Court's reasoning: The court held that the Defendants could not use the current ejectment action to enforce payment of the alleged balance consideration. The Court of Appeal's order making ejectment conditional on the payment of Rs. 300,000 was legally impermissible in this context. Finding: The order for payment is set aside. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected: the validity of the deed (Issue 1) establishes the Plaintiff-Respondents' title, which in turn justifies the order for ejectment (Issue 2). Because the title is established, the Defendants' claim for payment (Issue 4) is relegated to a separate cause of action, and the rejection of the prescription argument (Issue 3) ensures the action remains maintainable. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed, and the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed subject to the variation that the order for the payment of Rs. 300,000 is set aside. The Plaintiff-Respondents are entitled to the ejectment of the Defendants and peaceful possession of the property. Keywords Property ownership — Deed of transfer — Consideration — Fraudulent transactions — Prescription Ordinance — Ejectment — Court of Appeal orders — Notaries Ordinance		SC/APPEAL/91/2013	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_91_2013.pdf
sc-appeal-205-2016	25 February 2020	Abeygunawardane v. Mahaththaya and Others	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Vijith Malalgoda PC, J; Gamini Amarasekara, J	Deed of Transfer — Deed of Gift — Validity of deeds — Signature comparison — Burden of proof — Notary Public — Evidence of donor — Fraudulent deeds	Facts The Plaintiff-Respondent filed an action in the District Court of Gampaha seeking a declaration that Deed of Transfer No. 2199 dated 18.9.1997 was null and void. The Plaintiff-Respondent claimed title to the property through Deed of Gift No. 27 dated 9.12.1997, executed by the same donor, Sathasivam Achalingam. The 2nd Defendant-Appellant, who is the son of the 1st Defendant, claimed title for his father through the earlier Deed of Transfer No. 2199. The District Court held that the Deed of Gift was genuine and the Deed of Transfer was null and void. The Civil Appellate High Court of Gampaha affirmed this decision. The 2nd Defendant-Appellant appealed to the Supreme Court. During the trial, an Examiner of Questioned Documents (EQD) testified that the signature on the Deed of Transfer did not match the known signatures of Sathasivam Achalingam, whereas the signature on the Deed of Gift did. The 2nd Defendant-Appellant had previously objected to the recording of the evidence of Sathasivam Achalingam, who was elderly and ill. The 2nd Defendant-Appellant failed to call the attesting witnesses of the Deed of Transfer, relying only on the testimony of the Notary Public. Issues Whether the Plaintiff-Respondent failed to discharge the burden of proof to vindicate her title. Whether the lower courts erred in failing to appreciate the evidence of the Petitioner and the Notary Public regarding the validity of Deed No. 2199. Whether the lower courts erred in awarding damages without evidence. Applicable Law Statutes: Not specified Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Burden of proof Rule applied: The burden of proof lies on the party asserting a claim to title, supported by evidence of the validity of the underlying deed. Court's reasoning: The court relied on the expert testimony of the EQD, who compared the disputed signature on the Deed of Transfer against multiple authentic documents, including a passport, an affidavit, a police statement, and a bank passbook. The EQD concluded the signature on the Deed of Transfer was not genuine. Furthermore, the Plaintiff-Respondent provided evidence from an attesting witness of the Deed of Gift, while the 2nd Defendant-Appellant failed to call the attesting witnesses of the Deed of Transfer. Finding: The Plaintiff-Respondent successfully discharged the burden of proof by providing expert forensic evidence and witness testimony, while the 2nd Defendant-Appellant failed to rebut this evidence. Issue 2: Evaluation of evidence Rule applied: The court must evaluate the credibility of deeds based on expert forensic analysis and the availability of attesting witnesses. Court's reasoning: The court noted that the 2nd Defendant-Appellant had actively prevented the testimony of the donor, Sathasivam Achalingam, by objecting to the application to record his evidence. The court found the EQDâ€™s analysis of the signatures to be conclusive in determining that the Deed of Transfer was not signed by the donor. Finding: The lower courts did not err in law; they correctly weighed the expert evidence and the failure of the 2nd Defendant-Appellant to produce attesting witnesses. Issue 3: Award of damages Rule applied: Damages must be supported by evidence placed before the court. Court's reasoning: The court reviewed the record and the findings of the lower courts regarding the validity of the deeds. Finding: The court answered the question of law regarding damages in the negative, effectively upholding the lower court's judgment on the merits of the title dispute. Interaction of Issues The findings on the validity of the deeds (Issue 1 and 2) directly informed the court's decision to dismiss the appeal. By confirming the Deed of Transfer was null and void, the court rendered the arguments regarding the validity of the competing title claims moot, leading to the dismissal of the appeal. Conclusion The Supreme Court affirmed the judgments of the District Court and the Civil Appellate High Court. The appeal was dismissed with costs. Keywords Deed of Transfer — Deed of Gift — Validity of deeds — Signature comparison — Burden of proof — Notary Public — Evidence of donor — Fraudulent deeds		SC/APPEAL/205/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_205_2016.pdf
sc-appeal-162-2010	27 February 2020	Tennakone Banda v. Menika and Others	E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, P.C., J; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Appeal under High Court of the Provinces Act — Erroneous finding on land identification — Failure to consider metes and bounds — Proof of title — Prescriptive title — Adverse possession — Conveyance of land — Deeds — Boundary disputes — Land ownership — Inheritance — Polyandry	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondent instituted an action in the District Court of Mawanella seeking a declaration of title to a land described in the schedule to the plaint, ejectment of the Defendants, and damages for trees felled. The Plaintiff claimed title through three deeds and asserted long, continuous, and uninterrupted possession. The Defendants-Respondents filed an amended answer claiming that the land described in the plaint was adjacent to their own land, which they inherited from their predecessor, Dasanayake Banda. The Defendants further alleged that the Plaintiff had no prescriptive title and that they had been in possession of the disputed land for over 25 years. The District Court dismissed the Plaintiff's action. Upon appeal, the Civil Appellate High Court allowed the appeal and granted the reliefs prayed for by the Plaintiff. The 2nd Defendant-Respondent-Petitioner-Appellant subsequently sought and was granted leave to appeal to the Supreme Court on questions of law regarding the identification of the land and the sufficiency of the Plaintiff's proof of title. Issues Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in determining that the main issue was whether Lot No. 1 in Plan 2V1 formed part of the land described in the plaint. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in failing to consider if the Plaintiff identified the land in dispute with reference to the metes and bounds in the plaint and the deeds relied upon. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court failed to consider if the Plaintiff proved his case by identifying the land and establishing title thereto. Applicable Law Statutes: Prescription Ordinance High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) (Amended) Act No. 54 of 2006 Cases Referred to: Gabriel Perera v. Agnes Perera - [43-clw-82] Ratnayake v. Kumarihamy - [2002/v1-sri-lr-65] Analysis Issue 1: Identification of the land Rule applied: The court applied the principle that where a deed clearly describes a land, minor inconsistencies in extent or boundary descriptions (such as the disappearance of a ditch) do not negate the identity of the land if the boundaries are otherwise compatible. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the 1st Defendant admitted in cross-examination that the land surveyed by the Plaintiff was the land bought from Muthubanda by Dasanayake Banda. The court found that the Defendants' claim that the land consisted of two separate lots was inconsistent with the physical evidence and the testimony of the 1st Defendant. The court concluded that the discrepancies in measurements were minor and consistent with regional variations in land measurement. Finding: The Civil Appellate High Court did not err; the main issue was correctly identified as the identity of the land. Issue 2: Metes and bounds Rule applied: The court applied the principle of falsa demonstratio, where a clear description of the land prevails over minor inconsistencies in extent or boundary descriptions. Court's reasoning: The court noted that while the High Court's judgment lacked elaborate reasoning, the final finding was correct. The court compared the survey plans and found that the boundaries in the Plaintiff's deeds were compatible with the land surveyed, despite the absence of a ditch on a 30-foot portion of the northern boundary. Finding: The High Court did not err in law; the identity of the land was sufficiently established. Issue 3: Proof of title Rule applied: The court applied the principles of the Prescription Ordinance and the law regarding co-ownership and permissive possession. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Plaintiff proved paper title through deeds. The Defendants' claim of prescriptive title failed because their possession, if any, was as licensees of the predecessor in title, Dasanayake Banda. The court held that the Defendants failed to prove an "overt act" of adverse possession required to defeat the title of a co-owner or the successor in title. Finding: The Plaintiff successfully proved his title, and the Defendants failed to establish title by prescription. Interaction of Issues The findings on identification and title are mutually reinforcing. Because the Plaintiff successfully identified the land as the one transferred to him by deed, and the Defendants failed to prove their own title or adverse possession, the court's conclusion that the Plaintiff is the rightful owner is robust. The resolution of the identification issue effectively undermined the Defendants' claim that they were in possession of a separate, distinct land. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed with costs. The Supreme Court answered all three questions of law in the negative, affirming the judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court. Keywords Appeal under High Court of the Provinces Act — Erroneous finding on land identification — Failure to consider metes and bounds — Proof of title — Prescriptive title — Adverse possession — Conveyance of land — Deeds — Boundary disputes — Land ownership — Inheritance — Polyandry		SC/APPEAL/162/2010	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_162_2010-1.pdf
sc-appeal-65-2014	2 March 2020	Fareeda and Shafi v. The Municipal Council Matale and Others	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Vijith Malalgoda PC, J; Gamini Amarasekara, J	Acquisition of land — Delegation of powers — Unauthorized construction — Demolition orders — Municipal Council Ordinance — Urban Development Authority Act — Planning permission — Injunction	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondents filed an action in the District Court of Matale seeking a permanent injunction to prevent the Defendant-Respondent-Appellants from demolishing a boundary wall constructed on their land. The land in question had been acquired by the Government for road widening purposes via a gazette notification, and this acquisition order remained valid and unchallenged. Although the Plaintiffs had obtained a permit to construct the wall, they breached the conditions of said permit. Consequently, the Magistrate of Matale issued an order for the demolition of the unauthorized structure, which had not been set aside by any appellate court. The District Court dismissed the Plaintiffs' action. Upon appeal, the Civil Appellate High Court of Kandy set aside the District Court's judgment and granted the permanent injunction. The Defendant-Respondent-Appellants subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal on several questions of law, primarily concerning the delegation of powers from the Urban Development Authority to the Municipal Council and the validity of the demolition order. Issues Whether the Urban Development Authority had the legal authority to delegate its powers to the Defendant-Respondent-Appellants under Section 23(5) of the Urban Development Authority Act No. 41 of 1978. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in granting a permanent injunction against the demolition of a structure situated on land acquired by the State. Applicable Law Statutes: Urban Development Authority Act No. 41 of 1978 Municipal Council Ordinance Civil Procedure Code Cases Referred to: Palligoda Vithanage Keerthi Wimal Withana v. Muniyandy Paneer Selvam - [sc-appeal-123/2019] Jayasinghe v. Seethawakapura Urban Council - [2003/v3-sri-lr-40] Analysis Issue 1: Delegation of Authority Rule applied: Section 23(5) of the Urban Development Authority Act No. 41 of 1978. Court's reasoning: The Court examined whether the Urban Development Authority (UDA) could delegate its planning and enforcement powers to local authorities. Relying on the precedent set in Palligoda Vithanage Keerthi Wimal Withana v. Muniyandy Paneer Selvam, the Court held that the powers under Section 28A(3) of the Act fall within the scope of "planning" and are therefore delegable. The Court further reviewed documents V44 and V44(a), confirming that the UDA had formally delegated its powers to the 2nd and 3rd Defendant-Respondent-Appellants. Finding: The UDA possesses the legal authority to delegate its powers, and such delegation was validly executed in favor of the Defendant-Respondent-Appellants. Issue 2: Validity of the Injunction Rule applied: Section 28A of the Urban Development Authority Act No. 41 of 1978. Court's reasoning: The Court noted that the boundary wall was constructed on land legally acquired by the State for road widening. Furthermore, a valid, subsisting order from the Magistrate of Matale mandated the demolition of the wall due to permit violations. Given that the Defendant-Respondent-Appellants held delegated authority to issue demolition orders, the Court reasoned that there was no legal basis to restrain them from exercising their statutory duty to remove an unauthorized structure on State land. Finding: The Civil Appellate High Court erred in granting the injunction, as the demolition order was legally sound and the structure was unauthorized. Interaction of Issues The resolution of the first issue regarding the validity of the delegation of power was foundational to the second issue. By confirming that the Defendant-Respondent-Appellants held the delegated authority of the UDA, the Court established that their actions were lawful. This finding rendered the injunction granted by the Civil Appellate High Court unsustainable, as it sought to prevent the exercise of a validly delegated statutory power. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court dated 25.9.2013 is set aside, and the judgment of the District Court dated 24.7.2009 is affirmed. Keywords Acquisition of land — Delegation of powers — Unauthorized construction — Demolition orders — Municipal Council Ordinance — Urban Development Authority Act — Planning permission — Injunction		SC/APPEAL/65/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_65_2014.pdf
sc-appeal-185-2014	3 March 2020	Re Subramaniam Asokan v. Siriwardana	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.
