Succession — Inheritance — Estate administration — Section 547 of the Civil Procedure Code — Maintainability of action — Declaration of title — Rei vindicatio — Immovable property — Co-ownership — Transfer of property — Testamentary proceedings	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondent instituted an action in the District Court of Colombo against the Defendant-Respondent-Appellant for a declaration of title to premises bearing assessment number 15, situated at 4th Cross Street, Pettah, and for the ejectment of the Defendant. The property was originally rented by the Plaintiff's father to the Defendant's father in 1954. Following the death of the Plaintiff's father intestate in 1985, the Plaintiff claimed ownership through inheritance and subsequent transfers of undivided rights from his mother and three siblings in 1988. The Defendant challenged the Plaintiff's status to sue, arguing that the action was barred by Section 547 of the Civil Procedure Code due to the absence of testamentary proceedings for the deceased's estate. The District Court dismissed the action, holding that the Plaintiff failed to prove ownership because no testamentary proceedings had been instituted. Both parties appealed to the Court of Appeal. The Court of Appeal allowed the Plaintiff's appeal, ruling that the lack of administration did not prevent the passing of title to heirs. The Defendant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Plaintiff's action for the recovery of immovable property is barred by Section 547 of the Civil Procedure Code due to the absence of a grant of probate or letters of administration. Whether the failure to comply with Section 547 of the Civil Procedure Code necessitates the dismissal of the action or whether the court should allow the party an opportunity to obtain the necessary administration. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code, Section 547 Estate Duty Ordinance Estate Duty Act Cases Referred to: Silva v. Silva - [10-nlr-234] Mohamed v. Public Trustee - [1978-1979-80/v1-sri-lr-1] Hassen Hadjiar v. Levane Marikar - [15-nlr-275] Gunaratne v. Perera Hamine - [6-nlr-373] Ponnamma v. Arumugam - [8-nlr-223] Kandiah v. Karthigesu - [31-nlr-172] Alagakawandi v. Muttumal - [22-nlr-111] Jayawickrama v. David Silva - [76-nlr-427] Analysis Issue 1: Applicability of Section 547 Rule applied: Section 547 of the Civil Procedure Code, which mandates that no action shall be maintainable for the recovery of property belonging to the estate of a deceased person exceeding twenty thousand rupees in value without a grant of probate or letters of administration. Court's reasoning: The Court rejected the Plaintiff's argument that the action was not one for the recovery of property. Relying on Ponnamma v. Arumugam and Kandiah v. Karthigesu, the Court held that the nature of the prayers in the plaint and the issues raised confirmed that the action was for the recovery of property. Consequently, the action fell within the scope of Section 547. Finding: The action is subject to the requirements of Section 547 of the Civil Procedure Code. Issue 2: Consequences of Non-Compliance Rule applied: The principle established in Alagakawandi v. Muttumal, Ponnamma v. Arumugam, and Jayawickrama v. David Silva that "no action shall be maintainable" does not mean the action cannot be instituted, but rather that it cannot proceed to judgment without due administration. Court's reasoning: The Court reasoned that the primary object of Section 547 is to protect state revenue. Therefore, the law allows a court to grant a party the opportunity to obtain the necessary administration rather than dismissing the action outright. The Court noted that the District Court erred by dismissing the action instead of allowing the Plaintiff to satisfy the statutory requirement. Furthermore, the Court observed that the twenty-year limitation period under Section 547 had since elapsed, rendering the bar no longer applicable. Finding: The dismissal of the action was improper; the court should have provided an opportunity for compliance. Interaction of Issues The findings on these issues are interdependent. While the Court determined that the action was indeed subject to the statutory bar of Section 547, it simultaneously clarified that this bar is procedural rather than substantive. By establishing that the bar does not mandate immediate dismissal, the Court provided a mechanism to preserve the action, which ultimately led to the decision to order a re-trial. Conclusion The judgments of the Court of Appeal and the District Court are set aside. The matter is remanded to the District Court for a re-trial, with instructions to prioritize the case and conclude proceedings without delay. Keywords Succession — Inheritance — Estate administration — Section 547 of the Civil Procedure Code — Maintainability of action — Declaration of title — Rei vindicatio — Immovable property — Co-ownership — Transfer of property — Testamentary proceedings		SC/APPEAL/185/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_185_2014-1.pdf
sc-appeal-152-2012	10 March 2020	Menike and Others v. Abeyratne and Others	Vijith K. Malalgoda, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, J; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Leave to appeal — High Court decision — District Court judgment — Boundaries of land — Commission plan — Identity of corpus — Evidence assessment — Appellate court intervention — Findings of primary facts	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellants initiated an action in the District Court of Kandy seeking a declaration of title and ejectment regarding a land described as the amalgamated land of Gale Kosgahamulahena and Dambuhena, consisting of lots 1, 2, and 3 in Plan No. 1901. The proceedings were initially brought against the 1st and 2nd Defendant-Respondents, with the 3rd Defendant-Respondent later intervening. The parties relied on Plan No. 1901, prepared by a licensed surveyor, to identify the corpus. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the Plaintiff-Appellants after considering 25 issues and the evidence presented. The 1st and 2nd Defendant-Respondents appealed this decision to the Civil Appellate High Court of the Central Province. The High Court allowed the appeal and dismissed the plaintiffs' case, concluding that the plaintiffs had failed to adequately identify the land in dispute, noting discrepancies between the boundaries in the schedule to the plaint and those depicted in the survey plan. The Plaintiff-Appellants subsequently appealed this decision to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in concluding that the boundaries of the land described in the plaint and those shown in Plan No. 1901 were inconsistent. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in its factual assessment regarding the identity of the land and the interpretation of the deeds and survey plan P-1. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in law by reversing the findings of the District Court despite the evidence supporting the trial judge's conclusions. Applicable Law Statutes: High Court of the Province (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 High Court of the Province (Special Provisions) Amended Act No. 54 of 2006 Cases Referred to: Alwis v. Piyasena Fernando - [1993/v1-sri-lr-120] Analysis Issue 1: Consistency of boundaries Rule applied: The court applied the principle that findings of primary facts by a trial judge who hears and sees witnesses are not to be lightly disturbed on appeal. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court reviewed the evidence of the surveyor who prepared Plan No. 1901. The surveyor testified that he identified the corpus based on the boundaries provided in the schedule to the plaint. Upon comparing the boundaries in the schedule with those in the plan, the Supreme Court found that the High Courtâ€™s conclusion—that the boundaries did not tally—was incorrect. The evidence demonstrated that the boundaries were consistent with the land identified on the ground. Finding: The High Court erred in its factual conclusion regarding the boundary discrepancies. Issue 2: Identification of the land Rule applied: The court evaluated the sufficiency of evidence regarding the identity of the subject matter of the action. Court's reasoning: The High Court had assumed the plaintiffs' land was only Dambuhena and that the deeds did not support the claim. The Supreme Court found that the surveyor had clearly identified the land as the amalgamated land of Gale Kosgahamulahena and Dambuhena. The trial judge had correctly analyzed both Plan P-1 and Plan P-3 to determine the northern boundary and the overall identity of the land. The Supreme Court held that the evidence supported the trial judge's finding that the subject matter was correctly depicted in the plan. Finding: The High Court erred in concluding that the plaintiffs failed to identify the land. Issue 3: Reversal of trial court findings Rule applied: The court applied the standard of appellate review regarding findings of fact, specifically referencing Alwis v. Piyasena Fernando. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court determined that the trial judge, having observed the demeanor of the witnesses and examined the documents, reached a conclusion supported by the evidence. The High Courtâ€™s interference with these findings was unjustified because the evidence on record corroborated the trial judge's determination. Finding: The High Court erred in law by reversing the findings of the District Court. Interaction of Issues The findings on the identity of the land and the consistency of the boundaries are inextricably linked. Because the High Courtâ€™s reversal was predicated on the erroneous belief that the land was not properly identified and that boundaries were inconsistent, the Supreme Courtâ€™s correction of these factual errors rendered the High Court's decision to dismiss the action legally unsustainable. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court is set aside, and the judgment of the District Court of Kandy is affirmed. The District Court is directed to enter a decree accordingly. Keywords Leave to appeal — High Court decision — District Court judgment — Boundaries of land — Commission plan — Identity of corpus — Evidence assessment — Appellate court intervention — Findings of primary facts		SC/APPEAL/152/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_152_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-151-2017	10 March 2020	Damayanthi v. Gamini and Others	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Maintenance Act — Burden of proof — Income of applicant — Sufficient means — Neglect or unreasonable refusal to maintain — Magistrate's discretion — Evidence Ordinance — Family Court — Vagrants Ordinance	Facts The Applicant-Respondent initiated maintenance proceedings in the Magistrateâ€™s Court of Angunakolapelessa against the Respondent-Appellant under the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999, seeking maintenance for herself and her five-year-old child. During the inquiry, the Applicant-Respondent, her eldest sister, and her eldest daughter testified. The Magistrate dismissed the application, refusing maintenance for both the Applicant and the child. The Applicant-Respondent appealed this decision to the Provincial High Court of Tangalle. The High Court Judge allowed the appeal, granting Rs. 10,000 for the child and Rs. 5,000 for the mother, effective from the date of the Magistrateâ€™s order. The Respondent-Appellant subsequently obtained leave to appeal to the Supreme Court on several questions of law, though he restricted his arguments to two specific issues regarding the burden of proof and the assessment of his income and capacity to pay under Section 2(1) of the Maintenance Act. Evidence revealed that the Applicant-Respondent and her daughter were engaged in cultivating a paddy field previously transferred to the Applicant-Respondent by the Respondent-Appellant as part of a prior settlement. Issues Whether the High Court Judge erred in determining the required burden of proof regarding the Applicantâ€™s income when making a maintenance order under Section 2(1) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. Whether the High Court Judge failed to properly appreciate the Respondent-Appellantâ€™s income and capacity to pay maintenance under Section 2(1) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. Applicable Law Statutes: Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999 Maintenance Ordinance (as amended) Code of Criminal Procedure Act No. 15 of 1979 Evidence Ordinance Cases Referred to: Sivasamy v. Rasiah - [44-nlr-241] Silva v. Senaratne - [33-nlr-90] Carder Umma v. Calendran - [Ram 1863-1868 141] Goonewardene v. Abeywickreme - [17-nlr-450] Dean v. Green - [8-pd-89] Ukku v. Thambia - [Ram 1863-1868 71] Analysis Issue 1: Burden of proof regarding Applicant's income Rule applied: Section 2(1) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. Court's reasoning: The Court noted that unlike the previous Maintenance Ordinance, the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999 requires the Magistrate to satisfy himself that the spouse is "unable to maintain herself" as a condition for awarding maintenance. The evidence showed that the Applicant-Respondent and her daughter were actively cultivating a paddy field. The Court found that the Applicant-Respondent failed to provide sufficient evidence to demonstrate her inability to maintain herself, and the lower courts failed to properly evaluate this statutory requirement. Finding: The High Court Judge erred by failing to consider whether the Applicant-Respondent had sufficiently established her inability to maintain herself. Issue 2: Assessment of Respondent's income and capacity Rule applied: Section 2(1) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. Court's reasoning: The Court acknowledged that while the Act provides mechanisms to ascertain income, it is difficult to prove the income of an ordinary villager relying on odd jobs. The Court observed that the High Court Judge correctly considered the evidence regarding the Respondent-Appellantâ€™s income but failed to balance this against the Applicant-Respondent's own failure to prove her inability to maintain herself. Finding: The High Court Judge failed to properly weigh the statutory requirements, specifically the Applicant's failure to prove her own inability to maintain herself, despite correctly reviewing the Respondent's income. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues are interconnected because Section 2(1) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999 requires the satisfaction of multiple conditions—specifically, the Applicant's inability to maintain herself and the Respondent's sufficient means. The failure to establish the Applicant's inability to maintain herself renders the maintenance order under Section 2(1) unsustainable, regardless of the findings on the Respondent's income. Conclusion The appeal is allowed regarding the maintenance order made under Section 2(1) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999, and that order is quashed. The Court makes no order regarding the maintenance granted for the child under Section 2(2) of the Act, as that portion of the High Court's order was not challenged. Keywords Maintenance Act — Burden of proof — Income of applicant — Sufficient means — Neglect or unreasonable refusal to maintain — Magistrate's discretion — Evidence Ordinance — Family Court — Vagrants Ordinance		SC/APPEAL/151/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_151_2017.pdf
sc-appeal-62/2020	11 March 2020	Attorney General v. Najimudeen and Others	P. Padman Surasena, J.	Buwaneka Aluvihare, P.C. J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Settlement agreement — Court of Appeal — Revisionary jurisdiction — Exceptional circumstances — Appealable judgment — Customs Ordinance — Damages — Statutory power — Due administration of justice	Facts The Plaintiff-Respondent-Respondents filed a plaint in the District Court of Colombo seeking damages for the wrongful detention of 96 bales of fabric by Sri Lanka Customs. The Plaintiff alleged that the goods were seized on 15-07-1988 and held until a writ of Mandamus was sought in the Court of Appeal. The parties reached a settlement in the Court of Appeal on 25-09-2000, and the goods were subsequently released on 23-02-2001. The Plaintiff claimed that the detention was unlawful and that the subsequent sale of the fabric at a low price resulted in financial loss. The Defendant-Petitioner-Appellant filed an answer asserting that the detention was a lawful official act performed by customs officers and that the settlement in the Court of Appeal precluded the Plaintiff from maintaining an action for damages. The District Court entered judgment in favor of the Plaintiff. The Defendant subsequently filed a revision application in the Provincial High Court challenging the District Court judgment. The Provincial High Court dismissed the revision application, holding that there were no exceptional circumstances to warrant the exercise of revisionary jurisdiction, particularly because the judgment was appealable. The Defendant then appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Provincial High Court erred in law by failing to consider the merits of the case and the legal effect of the settlement reached in the Court of Appeal. Whether the Provincial High Court correctly determined that there were no exceptional circumstances to warrant the exercise of its revisionary jurisdiction. Applicable Law Statutes: High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 Customs Ordinance Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Merits and Settlement Rule applied: The court applied the principle that revisionary jurisdiction is an extraordinary power distinct from appellate jurisdiction. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court observed that the Provincial High Court did not adjudicate on the merits of the case or the legal effect of the settlement. Instead, the High Court focused exclusively on whether the threshold for invoking revisionary jurisdiction had been met. Consequently, the Supreme Court found that the questions of law regarding the merits were misconceived because the High Court had not yet made a determination on those substantive points. Finding: The Supreme Court could not review the merits of the case as the Provincial High Court had not adjudicated upon them. Issue 2: Exceptional Circumstances Rule applied: The court applied the established legal principle that revisionary power is an extraordinary remedy to be exercised only in exceptional circumstances, especially when a statutory right of appeal exists. Court's reasoning: The Defendant admitted that it had filed an appeal against the District Court judgment out of time and subsequently attempted to use a revision application to re-litigate the same grounds. The Supreme Court noted that the grounds raised in the revision application were merely grounds of appeal. As the Defendant failed to demonstrate any exceptional character in its arguments to justify bypassing the standard appellate process, the High Courtâ€™s refusal to exercise its discretionary revisionary power was deemed correct. Finding: The Provincial High Court did not err in dismissing the revision application due to the absence of exceptional circumstances. Interaction of Issues The findings are interdependent; because the Defendant failed to establish the "exceptional circumstances" required to trigger the High Court's revisionary jurisdiction, the High Court was correct to decline an inquiry into the merits of the case. The Supreme Courtâ€™s determination that the revisionary threshold was not met effectively rendered the substantive questions regarding the settlement and the Customs Ordinance moot for the purposes of the current appeal. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. The judgment of the Provincial High Court is affirmed as the Defendant failed to demonstrate exceptional circumstances justifying the exercise of revisionary jurisdiction. Keywords Settlement agreement — Court of Appeal — Revisionary jurisdiction — Exceptional circumstances — Appealable judgment — Customs Ordinance — Damages — Statutory power — Due administration of justice		SC/FR/62/2020	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_62_2020.pdf
sc-appeal-62-2016	12 March 2020	Hon. Attorney General v. M S M Najimudeen and Others	P Padman Surasena, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, P.C., J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, P.C., J.; P Padman Surasena, J.	Customs Ordinance — wrongful detention — damages — revisionary jurisdiction — exceptional circumstances — statutory powers — settlement agreement — appealable judgment — legal duty — inquiry	Facts The case revolves around a dispute concerning the wrongful detention of 96 bales of fabric imported by the Plaintiff, M S M Najimudeen, by the Sri Lanka Customs. The Plaintiff filed a plaint in the District Court of Colombo seeking damages for the wrongful detention, which he alleged occurred on 15 July 1988. The Plaintiff contended that the Customs had seized the goods without proper legal justification and had continued to detain them until a writ application was filed in the Court of Appeal. The matter was eventually settled, and the goods were released on 23 February 2001. However, the Plaintiff claimed that the Customs had breached its legal duty by failing to conduct an inquiry or release the goods upon payment of due customs levies, leading to a financial loss when he was compelled to sell the fabric at a lower price. The Defendant, the Hon. Attorney General, representing the Customs, argued that the detention was an official act and that the Plaintiff was not entitled to damages since the matter had been settled in the Court of Appeal. The District Court ruled in favor of the Plaintiff on 22 July 2009, awarding damages. The Defendant then sought revision in the Provincial High Court, which dismissed the application on 23 August 2012, stating that there were no exceptional circumstances to warrant intervention. The Supreme Court subsequently granted leave to appeal on several questions of law regarding the legal implications of the settlement and the nature of the Customs' actions. Issues The Supreme Court addressed several key issues: 1) Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in failing to consider the legal effect of the settlement between the parties. 2) Whether the detention of the fabric constituted a statutory power exercised by Customs officers, thus protecting them from claims for damages. 3) Whether the Plaintiff had availed himself of statutory relief under the Customs Ordinance, making the District Court action improper. 4) Whether the Plaintiff's action for damages was prescribed under the Customs Ordinance. 5) Whether the disregard of the settlement by the District Court judge amounted to an overreach of supervisory jurisdiction over the Court of Appeal. 6) Whether the Civil Appellate High Court failed to appreciate its revisionary jurisdiction in the interests of justice. Applicable Law Statutes: Customs Ordinance, High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 as amended by Act No. 54 of 2006. Cases Referred to: C A writ application No. 159/2000. Analysis Issue 1: Legal Effect of Settlement Rule applied: The principle of res judicata and the binding nature of settlements in legal proceedings. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court noted that the Provincial High Court did not adequately address the implications of the settlement reached in the Court of Appeal. The Court emphasized that the settlement should have been considered as a final resolution of the matter, which would preclude further claims for damages. The judgment highlighted that the legal effect of a settlement is to bring closure to disputes, and the failure to recognize this could undermine the integrity of judicial settlements. The Court quoted, "The learned Judges of the High Court had not considered merits of the case... the question whether there are any exceptional circumstances which warrant their intervention..." This indicates a failure to engage with the substantive legal principles surrounding settlements. Finding: The Court concluded that the Provincial High Court erred in not considering the legal ramifications of the settlement, which should have been a pivotal factor in their decision-making process. Issue 2: Statutory Power of Customs Rule applied: The statutory authority of Customs officers under the Customs Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court examined whether the actions of the Customs officers were protected under statutory provisions. The Court reiterated that while Customs officers do have statutory powers, these powers must be exercised within the bounds of the law. The judgment stated, "the detention of the fabric is a statutory power exercised by the customs officers under the Customs Ordinance..." However, the Court also noted that this does not grant blanket immunity from claims of wrongful detention if the officers acted outside their legal authority. The Court emphasized the need for accountability in the exercise of public powers, stating, "the Plaintiff is not entitled to claim any damages from the Defendant..." Finding: The Court found that while Customs officers have statutory powers, these powers do not exempt them from liability if they fail to act in accordance with the law. Issue 3: Statutory Relief under Customs Ordinance Rule applied: The requirement to exhaust statutory remedies before seeking judicial relief. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court analyzed whether the Plaintiff had pursued all available statutory remedies under the Customs Ordinance before filing the action in the District Court. The Court pointed out that the Plaintiff's failure to utilize the statutory relief mechanisms available under the Ordinance undermined his claim for damages. The judgment noted, "the Plaintiff - Respondents had not availed itself of the statutory relief available in the Customs Ordinance..." This indicates a procedural misstep that could affect the legitimacy of the Plaintiff's claims. Finding: The Court concluded that the Plaintiff's action was indeed in contravention of the Customs Ordinance due to his failure to pursue statutory remedies. Issue 4: Prescription under Customs Ordinance Rule applied: The time limits for bringing claims under the Customs Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court considered whether the Plaintiff's claim for damages was time-barred under the provisions of the Customs Ordinance. The Court highlighted the importance of adhering to statutory time limits, stating, "the Plaintiff - Respondent’s action for damages was prescribed under the Customs Ordinance." This underscores the necessity for litigants to be vigilant about statutory deadlines in pursuing claims. Finding: The Court determined that the Plaintiff's claim was indeed prescribed, further complicating his position. Interaction of Issues The issues presented are interrelated, as they all stem from the central question of whether the Plaintiff's claims were valid given the procedural and statutory frameworks governing Customs actions. The failure to recognize the settlement's legal effect directly impacted the assessment of the statutory powers of Customs officers and the Plaintiff's entitlement to damages. The Court's analysis revealed a systemic failure to adhere to legal protocols, which collectively undermined the Plaintiff's position. Conclusion The Supreme Court ultimately dismissed the appeal, affirming the Provincial High Court's decision that there were no exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of revisionary jurisdiction. The Court emphasized the importance of following legal procedures and the binding nature of settlements, concluding that the Plaintiff's claims were not only procedurally flawed but also lacked substantive legal merit. The judgment serves as a reminder of the necessity for litigants to navigate the complexities of statutory frameworks and the implications of judicial settlements carefully. Keywords Customs Ordinance — wrongful detention — damages — revisionary jurisdiction — exceptional circumstances — statutory powers — settlement agreement — appealable judgment — legal duty — inquiry	Sumathi Dharmawardene, P.C. ASG with Sureka Ahmed SC for the Defendant - Petitioner - Appellant. Senany Dayaratne for Plaintiff - Respondent - Respondent with Nishadi Wickramasinghe instructed by G S Thavarasa.	SC/APPEAL/62/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_62_2016.pdf
sc-fr-181-2016	18 May 2020	Weerasinghe and Others v. Charles and Others	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Promotion procedures — Public Service Commission — Seniority — Application for promotion — Violation of fundamental rights	Facts The Petitioners were officers attached to the Sri Lanka Customs who held the rank of Deputy Superintendent. They sought promotion to the post of Superintendent of Customs, which is governed by a Scheme of Recruitment requiring seniority and a formal interview. The Petitioners alleged that the authorities promoted seventeen officers without following proper procedure, specifically claiming that no advertisement was issued and no interviews were conducted. They contended that this failure infringed upon their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Evidence presented to the court indicated that the Sri Lanka Customs had sought and received approval from the Public Service Commission to fill vacancies. The notice for applications was displayed at the Human Resources Directorate and communicated through the Customs Union. Although the Petitioners denied receiving notice, the Customs Union confirmed that the Petitioners were informed of the vacancies and the interview schedule. Following the initiation of the legal proceedings, the Petitioners were eventually promoted. Their remaining grievance was a request for their seniority to be restored by backdating their appointments to precede those of the officers promoted in June 2016. The Respondents argued that the Petitioners failed to apply for the promotions despite being eligible and that the selection process for the June 2016 appointments utilized the list of candidates who had already faced interviews in January 2016, as approved by the Public Service Commission. Issues Whether the failure to promote the Petitioners, resulting from their own omission to apply for the advertised positions, constituted a violation of their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioners are entitled to have their promotions backdated to restore seniority over officers who were promoted in June 2016. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 17, and 126. Cases Referred to: Devasinghe v. Jayaratne - [sc-fr-516/95] Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution, which guarantees equality before the law and equal protection of the law. Court's reasoning: The court determined that the promotion process was conducted with the approval of the Public Service Commission and that the notice of vacancies was properly communicated to the Petitioners. The court emphasized that the Petitioners' failure to receive a promotion was a direct consequence of their own decision not to submit an application. Citing Devasinghe v. Jayaratne, the court held that the equal protection of the law cannot be invoked by those who are differently circumstanced due to their own inaction. Because the Petitioners failed to apply for the positions, they could not be considered as being in the same class as those who did apply and were subsequently promoted. Finding: There was no violation of the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Issue 2: Entitlement to backdated seniority Rule applied: The Scheme of Recruitment (SOR) for Sri Lanka Customs as amended pursuant to Cabinet Decision No. /14/07.09/504/079. Court's reasoning: The court found that the appointments made in June 2016 were filled from a list of candidates who had already participated in interviews held on 21st January 2016, with the express approval of the Public Service Commission. Since the Petitioners did not participate in the required selection process at the relevant time, there was no legal basis to grant them seniority over officers who had complied with the established recruitment procedures. The court noted that the Petitioners had previously failed to apply for promotions even when eligible, further demonstrating that their lack of promotion was not due to any fault of the Respondents. Finding: The Petitioners are not entitled to have their promotions backdated. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues are intrinsically linked; the determination that the Petitioners' non-promotion was self-imposed (Issue 1) directly precludes their claim for backdated seniority (Issue 2). Because the court found that the recruitment process was procedurally sound and that the Petitioners failed to participate in it, the request for backdating becomes legally untenable, as the Petitioners failed to meet the necessary prerequisites for the earlier promotion date. Conclusion The court found no violation of the Petitioners' fundamental rights and concluded that the promotion process was conducted in accordance with the law. The application is dismissed with costs of Rs. 10,000/- imposed on each Petitioner. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Promotion procedures — Public Service Commission — Seniority — Application for promotion — Violation of fundamental rights		SC/APPEAL/181/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_181_2016.pdf
sc-fr-335-2018	19 May 2020	Perera v. Guruge and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Executive or administrative action — Government instrumentality — Statutory corporation — Ceylon Scout Council Act — Presidential Scout Award — Judicial review — Bona fide purchaser	Facts The Petitioner, Galkandage Sahasra Sandeepa Perera, was a student at Christ King College, Ja-Ela, and a long-term member of the Sri Lanka Scout Association. The Petitioner alleged that the 1st Respondent, the District Scout Commissioner, maliciously refused to authorize his application for the Presidentâ€™s Scout Award, and that the 2nd Respondent, the Chief Commissioner, was complicit in this refusal. The Petitioner sought relief under Articles 17 and 126 of the Constitution, claiming a violation of his fundamental right to equality under Article 12(1). The 1st, 2nd, 5th, and 6th Respondents raised a preliminary objection, contending that the Sri Lanka Scout Association and the Ceylon Scout Council are not organs or instrumentalities of the State. They argued that their actions do not constitute executive or administrative action as required for the invocation of the fundamental rights jurisdiction of the Supreme Court. The Court proceeded to determine this preliminary objection before considering the merits of the alleged violation. Issues Whether the actions of the 1st and 2nd Respondents constitute "executive or administrative action" within the meaning of Articles 17 and 126 of the Constitution. Whether the Sri Lanka Scout Council is an organ, agency, or instrumentality of the State. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 4(d), 12(1), 17, 126 Ceylon Scout Council (Incorporation) Act, No. 13 of 1957 Cases Referred to: Wijetunga v. Insurance Corporation of Sri Lanka - [1982/v1-sri-lr-1] Rajaratne v. Air Lanka - [1987/v2-sri-lr-128] Rienzie Perera v. University Grants Commission - [1978-80/v1-sri-lr-128] Rajasthan State Electricity Board v. Mohan Lal - [AIR-1967-SC-1857] Sukhdev Singh v. Bhagatram Sardar Singh Raghuvanshi - [AIR-1975-SC-1331] Ramana Dayaram Shetty v. International Airport Authority of India - [1979-AIR-1628] Ajay Hasia v. Khalid Mujib Sehravardi - [1981-AIR-487] Wijenaike v. Air Lanka - [1990/v1-sri-lr-293] Captain Channa D.L. Abeygunewardena v. Sri Lanka Ports Authority - [2017/v1-abh-214] Vivienne Gunawardena v. Perera - [1983/v1-sri-lr-305] Analysis Issue 1 & 2: Executive Action and Instrumentality Status Rule applied: The Court applied the "governmental control test" and the "functional test" to determine if an entity is an organ or instrumentality of the State, as established in Wijetunga v. Insurance Corporation of Sri Lanka and Ajay Hasia v. Khalid Mujib Sehravardi. Court's reasoning: The Court examined the status of the Ceylon Scout Council under the Ceylon Scout Council (Incorporation) Act, No. 13 of 1957. It found that the Council is not financed by the State, does not enjoy State-conferred monopoly status, and is not subject to pervasive governmental control. The Court noted that the Presidentâ€™s role as Chief Scout is purely ceremonial. Furthermore, the Council is not performing a governmental function, but rather operating as a local affiliate of the World Organization of the Scout Movement. The Court concluded that the Council is a self-regulating body accountable to its own members rather than the State. Finding: The Sri Lanka Scout Council is not an organ or instrumentality of the State. Consequently, the actions of the 1st and 2nd Respondents do not constitute "executive or administrative action" and are not subject to the fundamental rights jurisdiction of the Court. Interaction of Issues The findings on the status of the Scout Council as a non-State entity directly resolved the issue of whether the impugned actions were "executive or administrative." Because the entity failed the instrumentality test, the Court determined that the fundamental rights jurisdiction could not be invoked, rendering the examination of the specific nature of the refusal (contractual vs. administrative) unnecessary. Conclusion The preliminary objection raised by the Respondents is upheld. The Petition is dismissed in limine as the actions complained of do not constitute executive or administrative action under the Constitution. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Executive or administrative action — Government instrumentality — Statutory corporation — Ceylon Scout Council Act — Presidential Scout Award — Judicial review — Bona fide purchaser		SC/FR/335/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_335_2018.pdf
sc-appeal-138-2012	20 May 2020	Sivapakyam v. Sinnaiah and Others	P. Padman Surasena, J.	BUWANEKA ALUVIHARE PC, J; P. Padman Surasena, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Conditional transfer — Mortgage — Debt Conciliation Ordinance — Certificate of Debt Conciliation Board — Re-conveyance of title — Prescription Ordinance — Time bar — Fundamental rights	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondent filed an action in the District Court of Gampola seeking the annulment of a deed of transfer and a declaration of title to a land she had conditionally transferred to the Defendant-Respondent-Appellant. The Plaintiff contended that the transfer was a mortgage and that she had applied to the Debt Conciliation Board for relief. The Board issued a certificate under section 32(2) of the Debt Conciliation Ordinance, concluding that the transaction was a mortgage and that the Plaintiff had made a fair offer of repayment which the Defendant had unreasonably rejected. The Defendant argued that the Plaintiff failed to comply with the terms of the certificate by not repaying the debt within the specified time and that the action was time-barred. The District Court dismissed the action, holding that the Plaintiff failed to prove her case and that the action was prescribed. The Provincial High Court subsequently set aside the District Court judgment, ruling that the Defendant failed to plead prescription, failed to frame a relevant issue at trial, and that the District Court had misapplied the evidence regarding the Debt Conciliation Board certificate. The Defendant appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Plaintiff could maintain the action without depositing the consideration referred to in the deed together with legal interest. Whether the certificate issued by the Debt Conciliation Board is binding between the parties. Applicable Law Statutes: Debt Conciliation Ordinance Prescription Ordinance Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Maintenance of action without deposit Rule applied: Section 39(2)(a) of the Debt Conciliation Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the primary purpose of the Plaintiff's action was to invoke the jurisdiction of the District Court to obtain the benefit of the ten-year repayment period provided under the Debt Conciliation Ordinance. The court reasoned that requiring the Plaintiff to deposit the full consideration and interest at the outset would be unreasonable and would defeat the legislative intent of providing a period for repayment. Finding: The Plaintiff is entitled to maintain the action without depositing the consideration and interest at the commencement of the proceedings. Issue 2: Binding nature of the certificate Rule applied: Debt Conciliation Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court examined the certificate issued by the Debt Conciliation Board and noted that it did not represent a mutually agreed settlement, as the Defendant had rejected the Plaintiff's repayment proposal. The certificate merely served as an official acknowledgment that the debtor had made a fair offer which the creditor ought to have accepted. Finding: The certificate is binding between the parties only to the extent that it confirms the debtor made a fair offer that the creditor should have reasonably accepted. Interaction of Issues The findings on these issues are complementary. By clarifying that the certificate is not a final settlement but a procedural step (Issue 2), the court established that the Plaintiff was not in breach of a binding repayment contract. This supported the conclusion that the Plaintiff was entitled to seek the statutory relief of a ten-year repayment window under the Debt Conciliation Ordinance, thereby justifying the maintenance of the action without a prior deposit (Issue 1). Conclusion The Supreme Court affirmed the judgment of the Provincial High Court and dismissed the appeal. The Plaintiff is entitled to the relief sought in the plaint. Keywords Conditional transfer — Mortgage — Debt Conciliation Ordinance — Certificate of Debt Conciliation Board — Re-conveyance of title — Prescription Ordinance — Time bar — Fundamental rights		SC/APPEAL/138/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_138_2012.pdf
sc-fr-39-2019	25 May 2020	Arachchi and Wijayasinghe v. Aviruppola and Others	E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Fundamental rights — Article 12(1) of the Constitution — Admission to school — Visakha Vidyalaya — Education Category — Service in difficult schools — Circular No. 2005/01 — Minneriya Central College — Minneriya Primary School — Zonal Director of Education — Bona fide purchaser	Facts The 1st Petitioner, a minor, sought admission to Grade 1 of Visakha Vidyalaya, Colombo 05, through her mother, the 2nd Petitioner. The application was submitted under the category for children of staff members of institutions directly involved in school education. The 2nd Petitioner, a teacher in the Sri Lanka Teacher Service, claimed she was entitled to additional marks for her previous service in a difficult school, specifically her tenure at the primary section of Minneriya Central College/National School from 1999 to 2005. The 1st Respondent, the Principal of Visakha Vidyalaya, awarded the 1st Petitioner 40 marks, which fell below the threshold of 42.5 required for admission under that category. The 1st Respondent refused to grant the additional 15 marks for service in a difficult school, asserting that the Minneriya Primary School was not classified as a difficult school under Circular No. 2005/01. The Petitioners challenged this decision, arguing that the refusal was arbitrary and violated their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The Petitioners relied on a certificate (P10) issued by the Principal of Minneriya Primary School and certified by the Zonal Director of Education to support their claim. The 3rd Respondent, the Secretary to the Ministry of Education, maintained that the 2nd Petitioner served at the Minneriya National School, which was a convenient school, and that the primary section only became a separate entity in 2012. Issues Whether the refusal to grant 15 marks for the 2nd Petitioner's previous service at the primary section of Minneriya Central College/National School constituted a violation of the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the primary section of the Minneriya Central College/National School qualified as a "difficult school" under the criteria set out in Circular No. 2005/01 for the purpose of allocating admission marks. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 17, and 126. Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution and Clause 6.4(b) II of the Admission Circular (P11). Court's reasoning: The court evaluated whether the 1st Respondentâ€™s refusal to award marks was arbitrary. It determined that the allocation of marks is strictly governed by the criteria in the Admission Circular (P11), which requires service in a "difficult school" as classified under Circular No. 2005/01 (P19). Since the Petitioners failed to provide valid evidence that the school in question met the classification criteria, the 1st Respondentâ€™s adherence to the circular was not arbitrary. Finding: The refusal to grant marks was not arbitrary, and no violation of Article 12(1) occurred. Issue 2: Classification of the school Rule applied: Circular No. 2005/01 (P19) and Clause 6.4(b) II of the Admission Circular (P11). Court's reasoning: The court found that the 2nd Petitioner served at the Minneriya National School, not a separate primary school, during the relevant period. Circular P19 classifies schools as units, not individual sections. Furthermore, the certificate P10 was deemed unreliable because it referred to "Minneriya Primary School" as a separate entity, which did not exist until 2012. The court held that the Petitioners failed to provide a certificate from the Zonal Director of Education confirming the school was classified as "difficult" under P19. Finding: The primary section of the Minneriya National School did not qualify as a "difficult school" under the applicable circulars. Interaction of Issues The findings are interdependent; the determination that the school did not meet the "difficult school" classification under the relevant circulars directly dictates that the 1st Respondentâ€™s refusal to award marks was lawful. Consequently, the failure to satisfy the regulatory requirements for marks rendered the claim of arbitrary treatment under Article 12(1) unsustainable. Conclusion The Petitioners failed to establish that the 2nd Petitioner's service was in a school classified as "difficult" under the governing circulars. The application is dismissed. No costs. Keywords Fundamental rights — Article 12(1) of the Constitution — Admission to school — Visakha Vidyalaya — Education Category — Service in difficult schools — Circular No. 2005/01 — Minneriya Central College — Minneriya Primary School — Zonal Director of Education — Bona fide purchaser		SC/FR/39/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_39_2019.pdf
sc-appeal-111-2015	26 May 2020	Wijesiri Gunawardane and Others v. Muthukumarana and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Revisionary jurisdiction — Appellate jurisdiction — High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 — Article 138 of the Constitution — Ouster clause — Section 9 of the Act — Court of Appeal's jurisdiction	Facts The Appellants filed revision applications in the Court of Appeal challenging judgments and orders delivered by various Provincial High Courts in the exercise of their appellate jurisdiction. The Court of Appeal dismissed these applications in limine, holding that it lacked revisionary jurisdiction over decisions made by a Provincial High Court when that court is exercising its appellate powers. The Appellants subsequently sought and were granted special leave to appeal to the Supreme Court to determine whether the Court of Appealâ€™s revisionary jurisdiction under Article 138 of the Constitution is ousted by Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990. Issues Whether Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990, as amended, ousts the revisionary jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal under Article 138 of the Constitution in respect of decisions made by a Provincial High Court in the exercise of its appellate powers. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 138, Article 140, Article 154P High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990, Section 9, Section 11 Arbitration Act No. 11 of 1995, Section 37 Transfer of Offenders Act No. 5 of 1995, Section 13 Cases Referred to: Merchant Bank of Sri Lanka v. Wijewardena - [2010/v1-blr-233] Australanka Exporters Private Ltd v. Indian Bank - [2001/v2-sri-lr-156] Mariam Beebee v. Seyed Mohamed - [68-nlr-36] Somawathie v. Madawela - [1983/v2-sri-lr-15] Gunarathna v. Thambinayagam - [1993/v2-sri-lr-355] Attorney General v. Podisingho - [51-nlr-385] Sunil Chandra Kumar v. Veloo - [2001/v3-sri-lr-91] K. A. Potman v. Inspector of Police, Dodangoda - [74-nlr-115] Atapattu v. Peoples Bank - [1997/v1-sri-lr-208] Weragama v. Eksath Lanka Wathu Kamkaru Samithiya - [1994/v1-sri-lr-293] In re reference under Article 125(1) for the Constitution - [2008/v1-blr-160] In Re the Nineteenth Amendment to the Constitution - [2002/v3-sri-lr-85] Colquhoun v. Brooks - [21-qbd-52] Martin v. Wijewardena - [1989/v2-sri-lr-409] Analysis Issue 1: Ouster of Revisionary Jurisdiction Rule applied: Article 138 of the Constitution and Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990. Court's reasoning: The Court reasoned that appellate and revisionary jurisdictions are distinct; the former is a statutory right, while the latter is a discretionary power aimed at preventing miscarriages of justice. The Court rejected the argument that the absence of an express reference to revision in Section 9 of the Act implies an ouster. It held that because revisionary jurisdiction is a Constitutional mandate under Article 138, any restriction or ouster must be made in express, unequivocal language, as seen in the Arbitration Act or the Transfer of Offenders Act. The maxim expressio unius est exclusio alterius was deemed inapplicable here because revisionary power does not require a concomitant statutory right of appeal to exist. Finding: Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 does not oust the revisionary jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal. The Court of Appeal retains the power to exercise its revisionary jurisdiction over decisions made by a Provincial High Court in the exercise of its appellate powers. Conclusion The appeals are allowed. The Court of Appeal possesses the revisionary jurisdiction to canvass decisions made by a Provincial High Court exercising its appellate powers, as such jurisdiction is not ousted by Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990. Keywords Revisionary jurisdiction — Appellate jurisdiction — High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 — Article 138 of the Constitution — Ouster clause — Section 9 of the Act — Court of Appeal's jurisdiction			
sc-appeal-13-2018	27 May 2020	Soysa v. Pereira	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekera, J	Property ownership — Deeds of transfer — Deeds of gift — Mental capacity — Testamentary capacity — Fraud — Stroke — Neurological examination — Consent to transactions — Intention to dispose of property	Facts Faith Soysa was the owner of the property described in the first schedule to the plaint. Following the death of her brother, Joy Francis Pereira, she became the owner of the property bearing Assessment No. 2A and 4. The Defendant-Appellant, who is the son of Joy Francis Pereira, claimed an undivided share of this property through a Deed of Transfer and a Deed of Gift, both dated 20th July 1992. The Plaintiff alleged that she did not intend to transfer or gift the property and that the deeds were not her act and deed. The Plaintiff had been admitted to the hospital on 7th July 1992 after suffering a severe stroke. She was 80 years old at the time. The Plaintiff contended that on 20th July 1992, the Defendant-Appellant and others obtained her thumb impression on the documents while she was in a state of incapacity. The Plaintiff subsequently filed an action in the District Court of Colombo seeking a declaration that the deeds were null and void. The District Court ruled in favor of the Plaintiff, and the High Court of the Western Province dismissed the Defendant-Appellant's appeal. The Defendant-Appellant then appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Plaintiff failed to discharge the burden of proof regarding the lack of intention to execute the deeds. Whether the High Court erred in law by considering the Plaintiff's mental capacity when it was not explicitly raised as an issue or averred in the plaint. Whether the Plaintiff is estopped from denying the validity of the deeds due to the alleged acceptance and utilization of the consideration. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Burden of Proof Rule applied: The court applied the standard of proof on a balance of probabilities in civil litigation. Court's reasoning: The court examined medical evidence, including reports from a neurologist and a psychiatrist, which indicated that the Plaintiff suffered from severe cognitive impairments, including confusion and disorientation, during her hospital stay. The court found that the evidence provided by medical experts and hospital staff conclusively proved the Plaintiff lacked the mental capacity to understand the nature of the transactions. The court held that the absence of the Plaintiff as a witness due to her death did not prejudice her case, as other witnesses provided sufficient factual evidence regarding her condition. Finding: The Plaintiff successfully discharged the burden of proof. Issue 2: Scope of Issues and Pleadings Rule applied: The court considered the principles of the Civil Procedure Code regarding the necessity of basing a case on the issues raised at trial. Court's reasoning: While the Defendant-Appellant argued that mental capacity was not specifically pleaded, the court reasoned that the core issue was whether the Plaintiff intended to execute the deeds. The court held that "intention" inherently requires mental capacity. Therefore, the evidence regarding the Plaintiff's mental state was relevant and necessary to determine the primary issue of whether the deeds were the Plaintiff's true acts and deeds. Finding: The court did not err in considering mental capacity as it was intrinsically linked to the issue of intent. Issue 3: Estoppel and Consideration Rule applied: The court applied the principle of consensus ad idem for the formation of a valid contract. Court's reasoning: The court found no evidence that the Plaintiff knowingly accepted or utilized the consideration. The deposit of funds into a joint account without the Plaintiff's knowledge or instruction did not validate the transaction. Because the Plaintiff lacked the mental capacity to enter into a contract, the deeds were void ab initio. Finding: The Plaintiff is not estopped from denying the validity of the deeds. Interaction of Issues The findings on mental capacity directly informed the resolution of the issues regarding intent and estoppel. Because the court determined that the Plaintiff lacked the mental capacity to form the necessary intent, the deeds were deemed void, rendering the arguments regarding the acceptance of consideration moot. Conclusion The Supreme Court dismissed the appeal and affirmed the judgments of the District Court and the High Court. The Defendant-Appellant was ordered to pay costs of Rs. 100,000. Keywords Property ownership — Deeds of transfer — Deeds of gift — Mental capacity — Testamentary capacity — Fraud — Stroke — Neurological examination — Consent to transactions — Intention to dispose of property		SC/APPEAL/13/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_13_2018.pdf
sc-appeal-111-113-114-2015	27 May 2020	Wijesiri Gunawardane and Others v. Chandrasena Muthukumarana and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare, P.C., J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, P.C., J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, P.C., J.; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	revisionary jurisdiction — appellate jurisdiction — High Court of the Provinces — Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 — Article 138 of the Constitution — ouster clause — discretionary remedy — miscarriage of justice — express provisions — statutory right	Facts The case revolves around an application for special leave to appeal concerning the jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal in relation to decisions made by the Provincial High Court exercising its appellate powers. The Petitioner-Appellants, Wijesiri Gunawardane and others, challenged an order made by the Court of Appeal on 02 September 2014, which upheld a preliminary objection and resulted in the refusal of their revision applications. The Appellants had filed revision applications against judgments made by the High Courts of their respective provinces, which were dismissed in limine on the grounds that the Court of Appeal lacked revisionary jurisdiction over such judgments. The core of the dispute lies in whether Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 ousts the revisionary jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal concerning decisions made by a Provincial High Court exercising its appellate powers. The Appellants argued that the Court of Appeal should retain its revisionary powers, while the Respondents contended that the specific provisions of the Act limited the Court's jurisdiction. Issues The primary issue presented to the Court was: Whether Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 ousts the revisionary jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal in respect of decisions made by a Provincial High Court exercising its appellate powers? Applicable Law Statutes: High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 — Article 138 of the Constitution of the Republic of Sri Lanka Cases Referred to: Merchant Bank of Sri Lanka v. Wijewardena - [2010 BLR 233]; Australanka Exporters Private Ltd v. Indian Bank - (2001) 2 SLR 156; Mariam Beebee v. Seyed Mohamed - (1965) 68 NLR 36; Somawathie v. Madawela - (1983) 2 SLR 15; Gunarathna v. Thambinayagam - (1993) 2 SLR 355; Attorney General v. Podisingho - (1950) 51 NLR 385; Sunil Chandra Kumar v. Veloo - (2001) 3 SLR 91; K. A. Potman v. Inspector of Police, Dodangoda - (1971) 74 NLR 115; Atapattu v. People’s Bank - (1997) 1 SLR 208; Gunaratne v. Thambinayagam - (1993) 2 SLR 355. Analysis Issue 1: Whether Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 ousts the revisionary jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal? Rule applied: The Court examined the provisions of Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990, which delineates the appellate jurisdiction of the High Court and the Court of Appeal. The Court noted that the revisionary jurisdiction is distinct from appellate jurisdiction, and the two should not be conflated. The Respondents argued that the express mention of appellate jurisdiction in Section 9 implied the exclusion of revisionary jurisdiction. However, the Court emphasized that the historical interpretation of revisionary powers as discretionary and independent of statutory rights supports the argument that the existence of an appeal does not negate the possibility of invoking revisionary jurisdiction. The Court cited previous judgments that affirmed the distinct nature of these jurisdictions, highlighting that revisionary powers exist to correct miscarriages of justice and can be invoked even when an appeal is available. The Court further clarified that the absence of explicit mention of revisionary jurisdiction in Section 9 does not equate to an ouster of such powers. Finding: The Court concluded that Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 does not oust the revisionary jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal concerning decisions made by a Provincial High Court exercising its appellate powers. Interaction of Issues The issues presented are interrelated as they hinge on the interpretation of statutory provisions and the constitutional framework governing the jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal. The Court's analysis of the relationship between appellate and revisionary jurisdictions is crucial in determining the extent of the Court of Appeal's powers in reviewing decisions made by the Provincial High Court. Conclusion The Supreme Court allowed the appeals, affirming that the Court of Appeal retains its revisionary jurisdiction over decisions made by the Provincial High Court exercising its appellate powers. The judgment underscores the importance of maintaining the integrity of judicial review mechanisms and ensuring that avenues for correcting judicial errors remain accessible to aggrieved parties. The Court's ruling serves as a significant precedent in clarifying the scope of the Court of Appeal's jurisdiction in relation to the High Court of the Provinces. Keywords revisionary jurisdiction — appellate jurisdiction — High Court of the Provinces — Section 9 of the High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 — Article 138 of the Constitution — ouster clause — discretionary remedy — miscarriage of justice — express provisions — statutory right	Niranjan de Silva with Kalhara Gunawardena for the 1st, 2nd and 5th Respondent-Respondent-Petitioner-Petitioner-Appellants in SC Appeal 111/2015. Saliya Peiris PC with Lisitha Sachintha for the substituted 4A Respondent-Appellant-Respondent-Respondent-Respondent in SC Appeal 111/2015. Amila Palliyage with Eranda Sinharage for the Accused-Appellant-Petitioner-Petitioner-Appellant in SC Appeal 113/2015. Anil Silva PC with Dhanaraja Samarakoon for the virtual complainant-Petitioner-Petitioner-Appellant in SC Appeal 114/2015. Amila Palliyage with Eranda Sinharage for the Accused-Appellant-Respondent-Respondent-Respondent in SC Appeal 114/2015. Rohantha Abeysuriya Senior DSG for the Attorney General.	SC/APPEAL/111/2015 WITH SC/APPEAL/113/2015 AND SC/APPEAL/114/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_111_113_114_2015.pdf
sc-appeal-34-2010	1 June 2020	Mohotti v. Bambarenda	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Ex-parte judgment — Purging default — Section 86(2) of the Civil Procedure Code — Flood as an excuse — Evidence required — Grama Niladhari evidence — Vigilance of parties — Laches	Facts Yogananda Wickramasinghe Mohotti instituted action in the District Court of Matara against Nimal Bambarenda seeking a declaration of title to a land, ejectment of the defendant, and recovery of possession. The plaintiff claimed title through a deed of transfer and alleged that the defendant was a tenant of the previous owner who was unlawfully occupying the land. The defendant filed an answer alleging that the plaintiff and the previous owner acted in collusion to defraud him, as he had previously entered into an agreement to purchase the land and had taken possession after paying an advance. The matter was fixed for trial on 11 June 1996. The defendant failed to appear on the trial date, and the District Court entered an ex-parte judgment in favor of the plaintiff. Subsequently, the defendant filed an application under Section 86(2) of the Civil Procedure Code to purge his default, claiming he was unable to attend court due to heavy flooding in the Ratnapura area where he was working on a vehicle repair. The District Court conducted an inquiry into the application, during which the defendant and witnesses testified. The District Court refused the application, finding the defendant's explanation unsatisfactory. The defendant appealed to the Provincial High Court of Matara, which dismissed the appeal. The defendant then appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the defendant established reasonable grounds for his default under Section 86(2) of the Civil Procedure Code to warrant setting aside the ex-parte judgment. Whether the lower courts erred in their assessment of the evidence regarding the alleged flooding and the defendant's failure to exercise due diligence. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code, Section 86(2) Evidence Ordinance, Section 114(f) Cases Referred to: Paramalingam v. Sirisena and Another - [2001/v2-sri-lr-239] David Appuhamy v. Yasassi Thero - [1987-slr-253] Analysis Issue 1: Reasonable grounds for default Rule applied: Section 86(2) of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The court evaluated whether the defendant provided sufficient evidence to satisfy the court that he had reasonable grounds for his absence on the trial date. The court noted that the defendant had previously obtained several postponements and failed to appear on the eighth trial date without providing instructions to his attorney. Furthermore, the evidence provided by the defendant regarding the flooding was contradicted by the testimony of the Grama Niladhari, and the defendant failed to produce the correct Grama Niladhari from the relevant division. Finding: The defendant failed to provide sufficient evidence to satisfy the court that he had reasonable grounds for his default. Issue 2: Assessment of evidence and due diligence Rule applied: Section 114(f) of the Evidence Ordinance; the principle of "Vigilantibus non dormientibus jura subveniunt" (the law assists those who are vigilant and not those asleep on their rights). Court's reasoning: The court applied Section 114(f) of the Evidence Ordinance, noting that the defendant withheld evidence that could have been produced. The court emphasized that the defendant failed to act with the diligence expected of a litigant, such as informing his lawyer via telephone when he realized he could not attend court. The court observed that the District Judge, as the trier of fact who observed the witnesses, was in the best position to assess the credibility of the defendant's claims, and there was no basis to interfere with those findings. Finding: The lower courts correctly assessed the evidence, and the defendant failed to demonstrate the necessary vigilance to justify vacating the ex-parte order. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues are mutually reinforcing. The failure to establish reasonable grounds for default (Issue 1) is compounded by the defendant's lack of vigilance and failure to produce credible evidence (Issue 2). The court's determination that the defendant's explanation was factually unsupported rendered the application under Section 86(2) untenable, thereby justifying the dismissal of the appeal. Conclusion The Supreme Court found no reason to interfere with the judgments of the District Court and the High Court. The appeal is dismissed with costs fixed at Rs. 150,000. Keywords Ex-parte judgment — Purging default — Section 86(2) of the Civil Procedure Code — Flood as an excuse — Evidence required — Grama Niladhari evidence — Vigilance of parties — Laches		SC/APPEAL/34/2010	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_34_2010.pdf
sc-fr-288-2014	2 June 2020	Gunarathna and Others v. Secretary of State for Public Administration and Home Affairs and Others	P. Padman Surasena, J.	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Public Service Commission — Sri Lanka Administrative Service — Merit Stream recruitment — Eligibility criteria — Time bar — Article 12(1) of the Constitution	Facts The Petitioners were appointed as Planning and Programming Officers (Grade II) in the Department of Health of the Central Province between 1999 and 2005. Their appointments restricted their service to the said department. Following a 2006 salary revision, they were placed under the M N-5 salary code. In 2014, the Secretary of the Ministry of Public Administration and Home Affairs issued Circular No. 22/2014, which invited applications for a competitive examination to recruit officers to Grade III of the Sri Lanka Administrative Service (SLAS) via a merit stream. The Petitioners were excluded from the eligibility criteria defined in the circular. They contended that this exclusion was discriminatory, violated their legitimate expectations, and infringed upon their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The Petitioners sought declarations that the circular and the exclusion of their category of officers were illegal, and requested directions to be permitted to sit the examination. The Respondents raised a preliminary objection regarding the time bar for filing the application under Article 126(2) of the Constitution, arguing that the circular merely enforced the eligibility criteria established in the Minute of the Sri Lanka Administrative Service published in 2013. Issues Whether the exclusion of the Petitioners from the merit-based competitive examination under Public Administration Circular No. 22/2014 violated their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the application is time-barred under Article 126(2) of the Constitution due to the Petitioners' failure to challenge the underlying eligibility criteria established in the Minute of the Sri Lanka Administrative Service. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1), Article 126, Article 126(2) Cases Referred to: Dayaratne v. National Savings Bank - [2002/v3-sri-lr-116] Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution regarding the right to equality and equal protection of the law. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the recruitment to Grade III of the SLAS is conducted through three streams: Open, Limited, and Merit. The Petitioners, being graduates, were eligible to apply under the Open Stream. Furthermore, their salary code rendered them eligible for the Limited Stream. The eligibility criteria for the Merit Stream, which the Petitioners challenged, were derived from the Minute of the Sri Lanka Administrative Service (Gazette Extraordinary No. 1842/2). The court found that the circular was merely an enforcement mechanism for these pre-existing regulations. Finding: The exclusion did not constitute a violation of Article 12(1) as the Petitioners were not barred from all avenues of recruitment, and the criteria were applied consistently with the governing Service Minute. Issue 2: Time Bar Rule applied: Article 126(2) of the Constitution, which requires fundamental rights applications to be filed within one month of the alleged infringement. Court's reasoning: The court determined that the Petitioners were effectively challenging the eligibility criteria set out in the Minute of the Sri Lanka Administrative Service (P 9(a)). The Petitioners' reliance on a previous circular (P 3) was misplaced, as that circular was restricted to recruitment in the year 2004. Citing Dayaratne v. National Savings Bank, the court held that where a scheme of recruitment becomes part of the terms and conditions of employment, any challenge to such terms must be brought within the statutory time limit. The Petitioners failed to challenge the criteria when they were established. Finding: The application was filed outside the one-month period prescribed by Article 126(2) and was therefore time-barred. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing; because the Petitioners failed to challenge the underlying Service Minute within the time prescribed by law, their substantive claim of a fundamental rights violation under Article 12(1) could not be entertained. The procedural failure to adhere to the time bar rendered the merits of the equality argument moot. Conclusion The preliminary objection regarding the time bar is upheld. The application is dismissed with costs. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Public Service Commission — Sri Lanka Administrative Service — Merit Stream recruitment — Eligibility criteria — Time bar — Article 12(1) of the Constitution		SC/FR/288/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_288_2014.pdf
sc-fr-520-2009	8 June 2020	Ranaweera and Others v. Wickramaratne and Others	Aluwihare, PC. J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Promotion to Deputy Inspector General of Police — Allocation of marks — Interview process — Eligibility criteria — Unblemished record — Disciplinary proceedings — Article 12(1) of the Constitution	Facts The Petitioners, R. A. P. de C. Ranaweera, B. P. B. Ayupala, and L.A.S. Lekamge, were Senior Superintendents of Police who sought promotion to the rank of Deputy Inspector General of Police (DIG). In May 2009, the Petitioners participated in an interview process to fill 13 vacancies for the rank of DIG. Following the process, 15 candidates were appointed as DIGs, but the Petitioners were not among them. The Petitioners alleged that the marking process was arbitrary, inconsistent, and failed to adhere to the established marking scheme, thereby infringing their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The Petitioners challenged the validity of the document containing the marks awarded to candidates, arguing it lacked authentication and contained numerous calculation errors. However, the Petitioners explicitly stated they were not challenging the selection of the successful candidates. The court focused its inquiry on whether the Petitioners were denied marks to which they were entitled and whether, if corrected, their aggregate scores would have met the required cut-off mark of 71. Issues Whether the allocation of marks by the interview board was arbitrary or irrational, thereby violating the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioners, if granted the additional marks they claimed, would have reached the required cut-off mark of 71 for promotion to the rank of DIG. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1) Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Arbitrariness in marking Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution (Right to Equality). Court's reasoning: The court examined the specific claims of the Petitioners regarding the marking scheme (R2). Regarding the 1st Petitioner, the court found that the interview board had applied objective criteria, noting that the board actually awarded the 1st Petitioner higher marks in certain categories than he had claimed for himself. The court held that the interpretation of the marking scheme regarding "Specialised Qualifications"—where marks were awarded for only one qualification rather than cumulatively—was applied consistently. The court found no evidence that the interview board acted with the objective of arbitrarily denying marks to the Petitioners. Finding: The Petitioners failed to demonstrate that the marking process was arbitrary or that they were denied marks in a manner that violated Article 12(1). Issue 2: Sufficiency of marks Rule applied: Scheme of Recruitment and Promotions of Senior Gazetted officers (R1). Court's reasoning: The court analyzed the scores of each Petitioner against the cut-off mark of 71. The 1st Petitioner was awarded 70 marks and claimed he was entitled to 73.5; however, the court found his claims regarding specialized qualifications were inconsistent with the established marking scheme (R2). The 2nd Petitioner was awarded 67 marks and claimed 70, which is still below the cut-off. The 3rd Petitioner was awarded 58.5 marks and claimed 69.5, which also falls below the cut-off. Furthermore, the 2nd Petitioner was found to be ineligible for promotion due to pending disciplinary proceedings, violating the requirement of an "unblemished record" under the Scheme of Recruitment (R1). Finding: Even if the Petitioners' own estimations of their marks were accepted, they failed to meet the required cut-off mark of 71, rendering their claims for promotion unsuccessful. Interaction of Issues The findings on the two issues are mutually reinforcing. Because the court determined that the marking process was not arbitrary (Issue 1), the Petitioners' claims for additional marks were rejected. Even if the court had accepted the Petitioners' own calculations (Issue 2), the resulting scores remained below the mandatory cut-off, confirming that the Petitioners were not entitled to the promotion regardless of the alleged procedural flaws. Conclusion The court held that the Petitioners failed to establish an infringement of their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The application is dismissed. No costs are ordered. Keywords Fundamental rights — Promotion to Deputy Inspector General of Police — Allocation of marks — Interview process — Eligibility criteria — Unblemished record — Disciplinary proceedings — Article 12(1) of the Constitution		SC/FR/520/2009	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_520_2009.pdf
sc-appeal-139-2017	8 June 2020	Jayasundara v. Next Manufacturing (Pvt) Ltd	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Industrial Disputes Act — Section 12(1) settlement — Jurisdiction of Labour Tribunal — Voluntary Retirement Scheme — Termination of employment — Conciliation process — Section 15 of the IDA — Repudiation of settlement	Facts The Employee-Respondent, W.K.S. Jayasundara, was employed as a Senior Clerk in the Human Resources division of the Employer-Appellant, Next Manufacturing (Pvt) Ltd., from 13.12.1993. On or around 15.05.2009, the Employer-Appellant terminated the services of the Employee-Respondent along with approximately 20-25 other employees. The Employer-Appellant contended that this reduction in staff was necessitated by the global economic recession in the apparel industry and was executed through a Voluntary Retirement Scheme consistent with the Termination of Employment of Workmen (Special Provisions) Act No. 45 of 1971.
