The Employee-Respondent filed an application in the Labour Tribunal alleging that her termination was unjust and inequitable, claiming she was forced to sign documents for nominal compensation. The Employer-Appellant raised a preliminary objection, asserting that the Labour Tribunal lacked jurisdiction because the parties had entered into a settlement under Section 12(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act. Evidence was led from the Assistant Commissioner of Labour, who confirmed that the settlement was negotiated in the presence of the Board of Investment and that employees were advised they were not required to sign if they were not in agreement. The Labour Tribunal President ruled that the Employer-Appellant failed to follow due procedure and that the settlement was signed against the will of the Employee-Respondent, awarding her compensation. The Provincial High Court affirmed this order, leading to the present appeal. Issues Whether the Labour Tribunal lacks jurisdiction to disturb a settlement made under Section 12(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act which amounts to a full and final settlement in law? Whether the Provincial High Court and Labour Tribunal erred in law in the analysis of the evidence and reached findings which are perverse? Applicable Law Statutes: Industrial Disputes Act Termination of Employment of Workmen (Special Provisions) Act No. 45 of 1971 Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Jurisdiction of the Labour Tribunal Rule applied: Sections 12(1), 12(2), 14, and 15 of the Industrial Disputes Act. Court's reasoning: The Court observed that Section 12(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act provides a formal mechanism for conciliation. Once a memorandum of settlement is signed by the parties, it becomes binding under Section 14, with its terms becoming implied terms of the contract of employment. The Court noted that Section 15 provides the exclusive statutory procedure for the repudiation of such a settlement, requiring written notice in a prescribed form. Because the Employee-Respondent failed to follow the statutory procedure for repudiation, the settlement remained valid and binding. Consequently, the Labour Tribunal lacks the jurisdiction to adjudicate upon a dispute that has been formally settled under the Act. Finding: The Labour Tribunal has no jurisdiction to disturb a settlement reached under Section 12(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act. Issue 2: Perversity of Findings Rule applied: Section 12(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act. Court's reasoning: The Court found that the evidence presented by the Assistant Commissioner of Labour and the General Manager confirmed that the settlement process was transparent and that employees were given the opportunity to refrain from signing. The Court noted that the Employee-Respondentâ€™s claim of being forced to sign was never suggested to the Assistant Commissioner of Labour during cross-examination. Therefore, the findings of the Labour Tribunal and the High Court that the settlement was signed against the will of the Employee-Respondent were unsupported by the evidence and were perverse. Finding: The lower courts erred in their analysis of the evidence, and their findings were perverse. Interaction of Issues The findings are intrinsically linked; the determination that the settlement was validly executed under Section 12(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act directly precludes the Labour Tribunal from exercising jurisdiction. The perversity of the lower courts' findings regarding the "forced" nature of the signature undermined the legal basis upon which they attempted to assert jurisdiction over a settled matter. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The orders of the Labour Tribunal and the Provincial High Court are set aside. Keywords Industrial Disputes Act — Section 12(1) settlement — Jurisdiction of Labour Tribunal — Voluntary Retirement Scheme — Termination of employment — Conciliation process — Section 15 of the IDA — Repudiation of settlement		SC/APPEAL/139/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_139_2017.pdf
sc-fr-296-2014	16 June 2020	Don Samantha Perera v. Officer In Charge, Police Station, Hettipola and Others	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Unlawful arrest — Illegal detention — Torture — Cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment — Violation of Article 11 — Violation of Article 12(1) — Violation of Article 13(1) — Violation of Article 13(2) — Police accountability — Human dignity	Facts The Petitioner, Kandawalage Don Samantha Perera, a former army soldier, alleged that he was subjected to unlawful arrest, detention, and torture by the 1st Respondent, the Officer-in-Charge of Hettipola Police, and the 2nd Respondent, the OIC Crimes of Hettipola Police. The Petitioner claimed he was arrested on 13th December 2013 and held in custody until 16th December 2013 without being produced before a Magistrate. During this period, the Petitioner alleged he was repeatedly assaulted with an iron bar, PVC pipes, and a wooden stick, and was subjected to suspension by a rope. The Respondents contended that the arrest occurred on 15th December 2013 following a theft investigation and denied all allegations of torture. The Petitioner was eventually produced before a Magistrate and remanded, but was later released after it was confirmed he was no longer in military service. The Petitioner sought relief from the Supreme Court after the Human Rights Commission failed to conclude an inquiry into his complaint. Issues Whether the arrest and detention of the Petitioner violated Articles 13(1) and 13(2) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioner was subjected to torture or cruel, inhuman, or degrading treatment in violation of Article 11 of the Constitution. Whether the actions of the Respondents constituted a violation of the Petitioner's right to equal protection of the law under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka (Articles 11, 12(1), 13(1), 13(2), 13(3), 126) Convention Against Torture and Other Cruel, Inhuman or Degrading Treatment or Punishment Act No. 22 of 1994 Cases Referred to: Ajith Perera v. Daya Gamage - [sc-appeal-273/2018] Sudath Silva v. Kodithuwakku - [1978/v2-sri-lr-119] Denmark, Norway, Sweden, and the Netherlands v. Greece - [1969/12-ybechr-1] Tyrer v. The United Kingdom - [1978/17-ybechr-356] Analysis Issue 1: Unlawful arrest and detention Rule applied: Articles 13(1) and 13(2) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The court noted a discrepancy regarding the date of arrest. The Respondents claimed an arrest on 15th December 2013, yet produced a Medico-Legal Examination Form (MLEF) from that same day which indicated the examination was to check for injuries, not intoxication as claimed. The court inferred the Petitioner's version—that he was arrested on 13th December—was more credible. Consequently, the failure to produce the Petitioner before a Magistrate until 16th December constituted an unlawful detention. Furthermore, the court found the police objection to bail for a minor theft charge to be unjustified, rendering the arrest and subsequent detention improper. Finding: The arrest and detention violated Articles 13(1) and 13(2). Issue 2: Torture and cruel treatment Rule applied: Article 11 of the Constitution and the Convention Against Torture Act No. 22 of 1994. Court's reasoning: The court acknowledged the inherent difficulty in proving custodial violence. However, it relied on the Medical Legal Report (MLR) from the Chilaw Hospital, which corroborated the Petitioner's specific history of assault, including the use of a rope and blunt objects. The court rejected the Respondents' denial, citing the duty of the police to uphold human dignity and the court's responsibility to prevent the distortion of truth through police solidarity. Finding: The Petitioner's fundamental right under Article 11 was violated by the 1st and 2nd Respondents. Issue 3: Equal protection of the law Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The court determined that the arbitrary and unlawful nature of the arrest and the infliction of torture demonstrated a failure by the state agents to act in accordance with the law, thereby denying the Petitioner the equal protection of the law. Finding: The Petitioner's right under Article 12(1) was violated. Interaction of Issues The findings of unlawful detention and torture are intrinsically linked; the illegal extension of custody provided the opportunity for the 1st and 2nd Respondents to inflict physical harm upon the Petitioner. The violation of Article 13 (liberty) and Article 11 (dignity) collectively established the breach of the Petitioner's right to equal protection under Article 12(1). Conclusion The application is allowed. The 1st and 2nd Respondents are each ordered to pay Rs. 50,000 as personal compensation, and the State is ordered to pay Rs. 100,000 as compensation to the Petitioner. Keywords Fundamental rights — Unlawful arrest — Illegal detention — Torture — Cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment — Violation of Article 11 — Violation of Article 12(1) — Violation of Article 13(1) — Violation of Article 13(2) — Police accountability — Human dignity		SC/FR/296/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_296_2014.pdf
sc-appeal-181-2016	16 June 2020	Somapala v. Attorney General and Others	P. Padman Surasena, J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Motor vehicle accident — Negligence — Rash and negligent act — Culpable homicide — Evidence — Sudden emergence of cyclist — Suicide bomb threat — Speed of vehicle — Burden of proof	Facts The Accused-Appellant, Wewala Patabendige Somapala, was charged in the Magistrates Court of Trincomalee on four counts relating to a motor vehicle accident that occurred on 20-10-1998. The charges included causing death by negligence under section 298 of the Penal Code, failure to avoid an accident under the Motor Traffic Act, driving negligently under the Motor Traffic Act, and driving without a valid license. The prosecution alleged that the Appellant, while driving an Army truck loaded with explosives, caused the deaths of Shanmugan Udaya Kumar and Rasaiya Sri Rangan. At trial, the prosecution presented evidence from Sinnaiya Kandadas, who witnessed the aftermath but did not see the collision, and Liyadipitiya Rathnayake Muudiyanselage Thilakarathna, a soldier who was a passenger in the truck. The Appellant testified in his own defense, stating that he swerved to the right to avoid a cyclist who suddenly emerged from a side road, fearing the cyclist might be a suicide bomber. The Magistrate convicted the Appellant on all four counts. Upon appeal, the Provincial High Court of Trincomalee affirmed the convictions on the first three counts but acquitted the Appellant on the fourth count. The Appellant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Appellant caused the deaths of Shanmugan Udaya Kumar and Rasaiya Sri Rangan by doing any rash or negligent act not amounting to culpable homicide in terms of section 298 of the Penal Code. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code, Section 298 Motor Traffic Act, Section 123(1)(a) Motor Traffic Act, Section 149(1) Motor Traffic Act, Section 151(3) Motor Traffic Act, Section 214(1)(a) Motor Traffic Act, Section 217(2) High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 Cases Referred to: Andrews v. Director of Public Prosecutions - [1937 2 All E R 552] Lorenze v. Vyramuttu - [42-nlr-472] The King v. Leighton - [47-nlr-283] Chandrasiri v. Attorney General - [2011/v1-sri-lr-10] Analysis Issue 1: Negligence under Section 298 of the Penal Code Rule applied: The court applied the principle established in Andrews v. Director of Public Prosecutions, which requires proof of a high degree of negligence ("recklessness") to sustain a conviction for homicide by negligence, rather than simple civil liability. Court's reasoning: The court evaluated the testimony of the prosecution witness Sinnaiya Kandadas, who admitted he did not see the collision. Conversely, the court noted that the testimony of the prosecution's own witness, Liyadipitiya Rathnayake Muudiyanselage Thilakarathna, corroborated the Appellant's account. The evidence established that the Appellant was driving a truck laden with explosives at a moderate speed when a cyclist suddenly emerged from a side road. The Appellantâ€™s decision to swerve was a reactive measure taken under the reasonable apprehension of a suicide bomb threat. The court found that the prosecution failed to establish any rash or negligent act beyond a reasonable doubt, as the Appellantâ€™s actions were consistent with an attempt to avoid a collision under emergency conditions. Finding: The court answered the question of law in the negative, finding that the Appellant did not commit a rash or negligent act amounting to an offense under section 298 of the Penal Code. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgments of the Magistrate and the High Court are set aside, and the Appellant is acquitted and discharged from all charges. Keywords Motor vehicle accident — Negligence — Rash and negligent act — Culpable homicide — Evidence — Sudden emergence of cyclist — Suicide bomb threat — Speed of vehicle — Burden of proof		SC/APPEAL/181/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_181_2016.pdf
sc-appeal-60/2017	17 June 2020	Rajalingam v. Ranjith	P. Padman Surasena, J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, J.; Murdu N. B. Fernando, J; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Urban Development Law — Unauthorized construction — Demolition order — Delegation of authority — Planning procedure — Section 28 A(3) — Section 23(5) — Revision application — Magistrate's Court	Facts The Applicant-Petitioner-Appellant-Respondent (hereinafter the Respondent) filed an application in the Magistrates Court of Colombo seeking a mandatory order under section 28 A (3) of the Urban Development Authority Law to demolish an unauthorized construction. This application followed the failure of the Respondent-Respondent-Respondent-Appellant (hereinafter the Appellant) to comply with a notice issued under section 28 A (1) of the Urban Development Authority Law. The Magistrate rejected the application on the basis that it was misconceived in law. The Respondent filed a revision application in the Provincial High Court of Western Province, which was dismissed. Subsequently, the Respondent appealed to the Court of Appeal. The Court of Appeal set aside the judgments of both the Provincial High Court and the Magistrates Court and directed the Magistrate to hold a proper inquiry into the application. The Appellant then appealed to the Supreme Court against the judgment of the Court of Appeal. Issues Whether the delegation of authority to the Respondent to institute the action under section 23 (5) of the Urban Development Authority Law No. 41 (as amended) was illegal or ultra vires. Whether the Respondent could lawfully institute the action despite the existence of a landlord-tenant dispute involving the Appellant. Whether the application was legally misconceived due to the inclusion of prayers for fines under section 28 (1) and 28 (2) of the Urban Development Authority Law. Applicable Law Statutes: Urban Development Authority Law No. 41 of 1978 (as amended) Urban Development Authority (Amendment) Act No. 4 of 1982 Urban Development Authority (Special Provisions) Act No. 44 of 1984 Rent Act No. 7 of 1972 Criminal Procedure Code Cases Referred to: Jayasinghe v. Seethawakapura Urban Council - [2003/v3-sri-lr-40] Muniyandy paneer Selvan v. Kuragamage Harishchandra Perera - [ca-phc-apn-170/2007] E R M Piyasena v. H M Wijesooriya - [ca-application-119/1990] S Sivapragasam v. Robert Jayaseelan Perimpanayagam - [ca-phc-appeal-02/1997] Palligoda Withanage Keerthi Wimal Withana v. Muniyandy paneer Selvan - [sc-appeal-123/2009] Malwattage v. Dharmawardena - [1991/v2-sri-lr-141] Analysis Issue 1: Delegation of Authority Rule applied: Section 23 (5) of the Urban Development Authority Law. Court's reasoning: The Court held that the term "planning" in section 23 (5) encompasses the enforcement of planning procedures, including the demolition of unauthorized structures under section 28 A (3). The Court affirmed the ratio in Palligoda Withanage Keerthi Wimal Withana v. Muniyandy paneer Selvan, noting that the UDA Law's scheme and the definition of "physical planning" support the delegation of these powers to local authority officers. Finding: The delegation of authority to the Respondent was lawful and valid. Issue 2: Landlord-Tenant Dispute Rule applied: Section 8 J and 8 K of the Urban Development Authority Law; Rent Act No. 7 of 1972. Court's reasoning: The Court held that the Rent Act cannot be used to protect or rectify an illegality, such as the occupation of a structure built in contravention of the Urban Development Authority Law. Because the structure lacked a certificate of conformity, its occupation was expressly prohibited by statute, rendering the Rent Act inapplicable. Finding: The Respondent could lawfully institute the action, and the existence of a tenancy dispute did not bar the application. Issue 3: Misconceived Application Rule applied: Section 28 A (3) of the Urban Development Authority Law. Court's reasoning: While the application contained prayers for fines under sections 28 (1) and 28 (2) that were inappropriate for a summary application under section 28 A (3), the Court held that these excess prayers did not invalidate the entire application. The Magistrate should have ignored the extraneous prayers and proceeded with the primary request for a demolition order. Finding: The application was not fundamentally misconceived; the court should simply disregard the improper prayers. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing: the legality of the delegation (Issue 1) establishes the Respondent's standing, the rejection of the Rent Act defense (Issue 2) confirms the substantive illegality of the structure, and the procedural clarification (Issue 3) ensures that the court focuses on the core remedy of demolition rather than being distracted by improper ancillary prayers. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. The Magistrate is directed to proceed with the inquiry into the application under section 28 A (3) of the Urban Development Authority Law, while ignoring the prayers for fines that fall outside the scope of that section. Keywords Urban Development Law — Unauthorized construction — Demolition order — Delegation of authority — Planning procedure — Section 28 A(3) — Section 23(5) — Revision application — Magistrate's Court			
sc-fr-222-2018	17 June 2020	Silva v. Hettiarachchi and Others	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Legitimate expectation — Administrative action — Judicial Officers — School admissions — Circular No. 17/2016 — Violation of fundamental rights — Arbitrary decision-making	Facts The petitioners were Judicial Officers who sought to admit their children to Grade 1 of Visakha Vidyalaya for the year 2017. For several years, specifically from 2011 to 2016, the Ministry of Education followed a practice where Judicial Officers submitted school admission applications through the Judicial Service Association to the Judicial Service Commission. The Commission would then forward these applications to the Ministry of Education with recommendations, which were historically honored. This practice was established because the standard Ministry of Education circulars for school admissions did not provide a specific criterion for the children of Judicial Officers, whose professional duties often precluded them from meeting standard admission requirements. In 2017, the Ministry of Education deviated from this established practice and introduced a new, restrictive criterion for the admission of children of Judicial Officers, which primarily prioritized officers who had received transfers between districts. The petitioners were denied admission to their preferred school, Visakha Vidyalaya, and were instead invited to a meeting with the Minister of Education, which they declined, viewing it as inappropriate for members of the judiciary. The petitioners alleged that the failure to follow the longstanding practice and the introduction of the new, arbitrary criterion violated their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The respondents contended that the Ministry of Education had limited capacity in popular schools and that the new criterion was a necessary policy adjustment. Issues Whether the introduction of a new criterion for the admission of children of Judicial Officers in 2017, which deviated from the established practice, violated the petitioners' fundamental right to equal protection under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the petitioners held a legitimate expectation that the longstanding practice of admitting children of Judicial Officers to schools of their preference would be followed in 2017. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1), Article 17, Article 27(2)(h), Article 111C, Article 111M(a), Article 126, Article 170(b). Cases Referred to: Hughes v Department of Health and Social Security - [1985] AC 776 HL Ginigathgala Mohandiramlage Nimalsiri v Colonel P.P.J. Fernando - [sc-appeal-256/2010] Desmond Perera v Commissioner of National Housing - [1997/v1-sri-lr-149] R (Bancoult) v Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs (No. 2) - [2009/v1-ac-465] Dayaratne v Minister of Health and Indigenous Medicine - [1999/v1-sri-lr-393] Jathika Sevaka Samgamaya v Sri Lanka Handabima Authority - [sc-appeal-15/2013] Watte Gedera Wijebanda v Conservator General of Forests - [2009/v1-sri-lr-337] Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution (Right to equal protection of the law). Court's reasoning: The court found that the Ministry of Education's new 2017 criterion was arbitrary and not justified by the circumstances. Evidence showed that several students were admitted to Visakha Vidyalaya after the official admission period had closed, contradicting the respondents' claim that there were no vacancies. Furthermore, the court held that the Ministry of Education lacked the authority to convene meetings with Judicial Officers to facilitate school admissions, as such meetings were not provided for in any circular and were inconsistent with the independence of the judiciary. Finding: The respondents violated the petitioners' fundamental right to equal protection under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Issue 2: Legitimate Expectation Rule applied: The doctrine of legitimate expectation based on established practice. Court's reasoning: The court determined that the practice of admitting children of Judicial Officers via the Judicial Service Commission had been consistently followed from 2011 to 2016 and was resumed in 2018 and 2019. This practice filled a lacuna in the existing circulars. The court held that when a public authority establishes a practice that creates a legitimate expectation, it cannot deviate from that practice without prior notice or compelling reasons. The respondents failed to provide such notice or justification for the 2017 deviation. Finding: The petitioners held a legitimate expectation that the established practice would be followed, and the respondents' failure to do so without notice was unlawful. Interaction of Issues The findings on the violation of Article 12(1) and the breach of legitimate expectation are mutually reinforcing. The court concluded that the arbitrary nature of the new criterion (Issue 1) directly frustrated the legitimate expectation (Issue 2) that had been fostered by years of consistent administrative practice. The lack of transparency and the failure to provide notice regarding the change in practice rendered the executive action both discriminatory and a breach of the duty to act fairly. Conclusion The court held that the respondents violated the petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The court directed the respondents to admit the petitioners' children to Visakha Vidyalaya forthwith and ordered that the established practice for school admissions for children of Judicial Officers must not be altered without prior consultation with relevant stakeholders. No costs were ordered. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Legitimate expectation — Administrative action — Judicial Officers — School admissions — Circular No. 17/2016 — Violation of fundamental rights — Arbitrary decision-making		SC/FR/222/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_222_2018.pdf
sc-spl-la-145-2014	25 June 2020	Attorney General v. Wijewardene and Perera	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Special Leave to Appeal — Judicature Act — Aggrieved party — Locus standi — Maintainability of appeal — Preliminary objection	Facts The Accused-Respondent-Petitioners, Herath Mudiyanselage Sarath Wijewardene and Algama Koralage Sumedha Perera, challenged the standing of the Aggrieved Party-Appellant-Respondent, Stanley Christoffel Asoka Obeysekere, in proceedings originating from the High Court of Colombo. The central dispute concerned whether the Aggrieved Party-Appellant-Respondent possessed the necessary legal status to maintain an appeal. The Court of Appeal had previously addressed a preliminary objection regarding the locus standi of the party, which prompted the current application for Special Leave to Appeal before the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Aggrieved Party-Appellant-Respondent qualifies as an "Aggrieved Party" within the meaning of Section 16(2) of the Judicature Act. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in its consideration of the preliminary objection regarding the locus standi of the Aggrieved Party-Appellant-Respondent. Applicable Law Statutes: Judicature Act No. 2 of 1978 Cases Referred to: Silva v. Silva - [10-nlr-242] [sc-appeal-28/2013] Analysis Issue 1: Definition of Aggrieved Party Rule applied: Section 16(2) of the Judicature Act. Court's reasoning: The court examined the legal principle that the property of a deceased person vests immediately in their heirs upon death, subject to the payment of debts. The Aggrieved Party-Appellant-Respondent argued that this principle, supported by established jurisprudence, conferred the necessary status. The court acknowledged that this legal position regarding the vesting of property was highly relevant to determining the maintainability of the appeal. Finding: The court determined that the legal status of the Aggrieved Party-Appellant-Respondent required further examination by the Court of Appeal in light of the principles of succession and property vesting. Issue 2: Court of Appeal's consideration of locus standi Rule applied: Section 16(2) of the Judicature Act. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court observed that the Court of Appeal had failed to adequately consider the relevant legal position regarding the vesting of property when it initially addressed the preliminary objection. Consequently, the previous order of the Court of Appeal was deemed insufficient as it did not properly resolve the question of standing. Finding: The Court of Appeal's previous order was altered to reflect only the overruling of the preliminary objection, and the matter was remanded for a proper determination on whether leave to appeal should be granted. Interaction of Issues The findings on the two issues are intrinsically linked; the court's determination that the Court of Appeal failed to consider the substantive legal definition of an "Aggrieved Party" necessitated the remand of the case. By clarifying that the Court of Appeal had not yet properly adjudicated the locus standi issue, the Supreme Court ensured that the substantive question of the Appellant's status would be addressed before the merits of the appeal are heard. Conclusion The application for Special Leave to Appeal is refused. The Court of Appeal is directed to hear the parties and determine whether to grant leave to appeal to the Aggrieved Party-Appellant-Respondent. Keywords Special Leave to Appeal — Judicature Act — Aggrieved party — Locus standi — Maintainability of appeal — Preliminary objection		SC/SPL/LA/145/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_spl_la_145_2014.pdf
sc-appeal-203-2015	25 June 2020	Jayasinghe v. De Silva and Others	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekera, J	Maintenance Act — Child maintenance — Sufficient means — Neglect to maintain — Evidence of maintenance — Adequacy of maintenance order — Proportionality of income	Facts The Petitioner, Chandani Jayasinghe, filed a maintenance application in the Magistrate Court of Horana against the Respondent, Neelahenndi Sisira Kumara De. Silva, seeking a monthly allowance for their two children under Section 2(2) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. The Magistrate Court initially granted an interim maintenance order of Rs. 5,000 per child. Following an inquiry, the Magistrate delivered a judgment ordering the Respondent to pay Rs. 10,000 only for the younger child, while making no order for the eldest child. The Petitioner appealed this decision to the Provincial High Court of Kalutara, which dismissed the appeal and affirmed the Magistrate's judgment. Subsequently, the Petitioner sought leave to appeal from the High Court, which was refused. The Petitioner then filed a Special Leave to Appeal application before the Supreme Court, which was granted on specific questions of law regarding the adequacy of evidence, the credibility of witnesses, and the sufficiency of the maintenance amount awarded. Issues Whether the Magistrate and the High Court erred in law by failing to consider the inadequacy of evidence regarding the Respondent's maintenance of the children under Section 2(2) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. Whether the lower courts erred in accepting the evidence of witnesses called by the Respondent who were allegedly interested and partial. Whether the maintenance amount awarded for the second child was inadequate given the means and circumstances of the Petitioner. Applicable Law Statutes: Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999 Cases Referred to: Sinaval v. Nagappa - [Volume VI Balasinghams Note of Cases 26] Analysis Issue 1: Sufficiency of maintenance Rule applied: Section 2(2) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. Court's reasoning: The court examined whether the Respondent had neglected or refused to maintain the children. Evidence revealed that the Respondent contributed to utility bills and school transport costs even after the maintenance action was initiated. The court noted that the Petitioner herself admitted to the Respondent's contributions during cross-examination. Consequently, the court found that the Petitioner failed to establish the necessary element of "neglect or refusal" required by the Act at the time the action was filed. Finding: The court found no error in the lower courts' determination that the Respondent had not neglected his maintenance obligations. Issue 2: Credibility of witnesses Rule applied: The principle of appellate deference to the findings of fact by the trial judge. Court's reasoning: Citing Sinaval v. Nagappa, the court held that maintenance proceedings are civil in nature and that an appellate court should give great weight to the findings of fact made by the Magistrate who observed the witnesses. The court determined that the Magistrate was in the best position to assess the credibility of the witnesses, including the eldest son who testified regarding the Respondent's financial support. Finding: The court declined to interfere with the Magistrate's assessment of witness credibility. Issue 3: Adequacy of maintenance amount Rule applied: Section 2(2) of the Maintenance Act No. 37 of 1999. Court's reasoning: The court noted that the Magistrate's award of Rs. 10,000 for the younger child was intended to cover specific extra medical expenditures related to the child's hyperactive disorder, rather than representing a total failure of the Respondent to maintain the child. The court found that the Magistrate exercised the discretion vested in him by the Act appropriately, having regard to the income and circumstances of both parents. Finding: The court held that the maintenance amount was not inadequate and that there was no basis to interfere with the Magistrate's discretion. Interaction of Issues The findings on these issues are interconnected; the court's determination that the Respondent had not neglected his duties (Issue 1) and the acceptance of the trial court's factual findings (Issue 2) reinforced the conclusion that the maintenance award (Issue 3) was a reasonable exercise of judicial discretion rather than an error of law. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. The judgment of the High Court of Kalutara is affirmed. No costs are ordered. Keywords Maintenance Act — Child maintenance — Sufficient means — Neglect to maintain — Evidence of maintenance — Adequacy of maintenance order — Proportionality of income		SC/APPEAL/203/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_203_2015.pdf
sc-spl-la-184-2017	25 June 2020	Attorney General v. Perera	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Special leave to appeal — Jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal — Judicial error — Consideration of judgments — Manifest appearance of justice — Overlooking of arguments — Fresh hearing	Facts The Accused-Appellant-Petitioner, Punchiweddikarage Aruna Felix Perera, sought special leave to appeal against a judgment of the Court of Appeal. The matter was originally argued before a bench comprising Hon. Justices K. K. Wickramasinghe and M. M. A. Gafoor. Following the conclusion of arguments, the judgment was reserved. Subsequently, a judgment was delivered on 07th June 2017, which purported to be authored by Hon. Justices P. R. Walgama and K. K. Wickramasinghe, despite Hon. Justice Walgama not having heard the arguments. On 17th July 2017, the Court of Appeal attempted to rectify this by setting aside the previous judgment and pronouncing a fresh judgment signed by Hon. Justices K. K. Wickramasinghe and M. M. A. Gafoor. Court minutes indicated that the judgment was sent to Hon. Justice Gafoor for his consideration only after it had been delivered for the second time. The Petitioner contended that this procedure created a reasonable doubt as to whether the judge who heard the case had actually considered the judgment before its pronouncement. Furthermore, the Petitioner alleged that the Court of Appeal failed to consider his arguments and instead verbatim reproduced the written submissions of the Complainant-Respondent. Issues Whether the manner in which the Court of Appeal delivered its judgment, specifically regarding the composition of the bench and the timing of judicial consideration, created a reasonable doubt as to the validity of the proceedings. Whether the Court of Appeal misdirected itself by failing to consider the Petitioner's version of events and arguments in its judgment. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 128(2) Supreme Court Rules 1990, Rule 16(1) Cases Referred to: R v. Sussex Justices ex parte McCarthy - [1924/1-kb-256] Analysis Issue 1: Validity of the judgment delivery Rule applied: The principle that justice must not only be done but must manifestly and undoubtedly be seen to be done, as articulated in R v. Sussex Justices ex parte McCarthy. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the judgment delivered on 07th June 2017 was signed by a judge who had not heard the arguments. The subsequent attempt to rectify this on 17th July 2017 involved a minute stating the judgment was sent to Hon. Justice Gafoor for consideration only after it had been delivered. The court found that this procedure failed to ensure that the judge who heard the case had the opportunity to consider the draft and offer an opinion before pronouncement. This lack of transparency and procedural irregularity undermined the integrity of the judicial process. Finding: The court concluded that it would not be safe to sustain the impugned judgments due to the procedural irregularities regarding the bench composition and the timing of judicial consideration. Issue 2: Failure to consider Petitioner's arguments Rule applied: The requirement for a court to provide a reasoned judgment that addresses the arguments raised by the parties. Court's reasoning: The court reviewed the Court of Appeal judgment and found that it contained a verbatim reproduction of the written submissions filed by the Attorney General for three and a half pages of a seven-page judgment. The court noted that the Petitioner had filed comprehensive written submissions and raised distinct points during oral arguments that were not addressed in the judgment. Finding: The court found merit in the Petitioner's contention that the Court of Appeal failed to apply its mind to the arguments raised by the defense. Interaction of Issues The findings on the two issues are mutually reinforcing. The procedural irregularity regarding the bench and the timing of the judgment (Issue 1) rendered the judgment fundamentally unsafe. This conclusion was further supported by the substantive failure of the Court of Appeal to engage with the Petitioner's arguments (Issue 2), which demonstrated a lack of judicial consideration. Given the finding on the first issue, the court determined that it was unnecessary to make a formal pronouncement on the second issue, as the entire judgment was already deemed unsustainable. Conclusion The appeal is partially allowed. Both judgments pronounced by the Court of Appeal on 07th June 2017 and 17th July 2017 are set aside, and the Court of Appeal is directed to hear the arguments in the matter afresh before a different bench. Keywords Special leave to appeal — Jurisdiction of the Court of Appeal — Judicial error — Consideration of judgments — Manifest appearance of justice — Overlooking of arguments — Fresh hearing		SC/SPL/LA/184/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_spl_la_184_2017.pdf
sc-appeal-163-2017	25 June 2020	Daintee Limited v. Uswatte Confectionery Works Limited and Others	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Special Leave to Appeal — Questions of Law — Trade mark infringement — Unfair competition — Code of Intellectual Property Act — Damages — Estimated damages — Actual damages — Statutory damages — Confusion among buyers — Imitation of design	Facts Daintee Limited, the Plaintiff-Respondent, commenced manufacturing sweets and toffees in 1985 under the brand names DAINTEE and CHIX. In January 1992, the company introduced newly designed, colourful wrappers for its products. Uswatte Confectionery Works Limited, the Defendant-Appellant, an established manufacturer since 1962, introduced wrappers in September 1993 that the Plaintiff-Respondent alleged were imitations of its own designs. The Plaintiff-Respondent filed an action in the District Court of Colombo alleging unfair competition under Section 142 of the Code of Intellectual Property Act No. 52 of 1979. The District Court ruled in favour of the Plaintiff-Respondent and awarded damages of Rs. 5 Million. The Court of Appeal subsequently dismissed the Defendant-Appellant's appeal, affirming the District Court's findings on both the occurrence of unfair competition and the quantum of damages. The Defendant-Appellant then appealed to the Supreme Court, challenging the findings on unfair competition and the excessiveness of the damages awarded. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal erred in concluding that the Defendant-Appellantâ€™s conduct constituted unfair competition under Section 142 of the Code of Intellectual Property Act No. 52 of 1979. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in affirming the award of Rs. 5 Million in damages when the evidence regarding the quantum of damages was insufficient and speculative. Applicable Law Statutes: Code of Intellectual Property Act No. 52 of 1979 Code of Intellectual Property Act No. 36 of 2003 Cases Referred to: Distillers Company of Sri Lanka v. Randenigala Distillers Company (pvt) Limited - [sc-appeal-38/2010] Society Des Products Nestle S.A v. Multitech Lanka (pvt) LTD - [1999/v2-sri-lr-302] Alwis v. Piyasena Fernando - [1993/v1-slr-120] Sumeet Research and Holdings Limited v. Elite Radio and Engineering Co. Limited - [1997/v2-sri-lr-394] Analysis Issue 1: Unfair Competition Rule applied: Section 142(1) and (2)(a) of the Code of Intellectual Property Act No. 52 of 1979. Court's reasoning: The court distinguished the present case from Distillers Company of Sri Lanka v. Randenigala Distillers Company (pvt) Limited, noting that the products here are sold primarily to children, for whom the colourful wrapper design is the prominent feature rather than the brand name. The court held that the Defendant-Appellantâ€™s imitation of the Plaintiff-Respondent's design created confusion in the marketplace. Following the principle that the "ultimate purchaser" (the child) is the focus, the court found that the visual similarity was sufficient to constitute an act of unfair competition. Finding: The Court of Appeal did not err in finding that the Defendant-Appellant engaged in unfair competition. Issue 2: Quantum of Damages Rule applied: Section 179 of the Code of Intellectual Property Act No. 52 of 1979; principles regarding proof of damages in unfair competition. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Plaintiff-Respondent failed to produce cogent evidence, such as books or financial records, to justify the estimated damages of Rs. 5 Million. While the court acknowledged that Section 179 allowed for damages, it emphasized that such awards must be based on evidence to avoid over-compensation. Although the new Code of Intellectual Property Act No. 36 of 2003 was not applicable, the court used its provisions as a guide for reasonable compensation. The court concluded that the lower courts erred in accepting the "estimated" figure without sufficient proof of actual loss. Finding: The award of Rs. 5 Million was excessive and not supported by evidence. Interaction of Issues The findings are linked because the court upheld the liability for unfair competition but reduced the damages. The determination that the Defendant-Appellant's conduct was unlawful established the right to compensation, but the lack of evidentiary linkage between the infringement and the specific financial loss necessitated a reduction in the quantum of damages to a more reasonable sum. Conclusion The appeal is partly allowed. The findings of liability for unfair competition are affirmed, but the damages are reduced from Rs. 5 Million to Rs. 2 Million, payable from the date of the District Court judgment, with legal interest and costs fixed at Rs. 50,000. Keywords Special Leave to Appeal — Questions of Law — Trade mark infringement — Unfair competition — Code of Intellectual Property Act — Damages — Estimated damages — Actual damages — Statutory damages — Confusion among buyers — Imitation of design		SC/APPEAL/163/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_163_2017.pdf
sc-appeal-114-2019	25 June 2020	Commissioner General of Inland Revenue and Others v. Janashakthi Insurance Company Limited and Janashakthi Insurance PLC	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Tax appeals — Value Added Tax (VAT) — Assessment notices — Validity of assessment — Electronic Transactions Act — Tax Appeals Commission Act — Jurisdiction of Court of Appeal — Case stated — Questions of law	Facts The Commissioner General of Inland Revenue issued two Value Added Tax assessments against Janashakthi Insurance Company Limited for the periods ending 31.03.2004 and 28.02.2005. The taxpayer challenged these assessments before the Tax Appeals Commission. During the proceedings, the taxpayer raised two preliminary objections, arguing that the assessments were invalid due to the lack of a signature and designation of the assessing officer, and that the appeal was time-barred. The Tax Appeals Commission upheld the objection regarding the lack of signature but overruled the time-bar objection. The Commissioner General of Inland Revenue subsequently initiated appeal proceedings before the Court of Appeal by way of a case stated. The case stated contained seven specific questions of law regarding the validity of computer-generated, unsigned assessment notices and the jurisdiction of the Tax Appeals Commission to annul such assessments. The Court of Appeal delivered a judgment on 08.06.2018, which the Commissioner General of Inland Revenue challenged before the Supreme Court. The Supreme Court granted special leave to appeal to determine whether the Court of Appeal erred in its handling of the case stated and the application of relevant statutes. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal erred in law by failing to answer the questions of law presented in the case stated. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in law by applying the provisions of the Electronic Transactions Act No. 19 of 2006 without framing a specific question of law regarding its relevance. Whether the matter should be remitted to the Court of Appeal for a proper determination of the questions referred in the case stated. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 128 (2) Tax Appeals Commission Act No. 23 of 2011, Section 11A (1), (2), (5), (6) Value Added Tax Act No. 14 of 2002, Section 28, 34 (12), 60, 61 Electronic Transactions Act No. 19 of 2006 Cases Referred to: R.M. Fernando v. Commissioner of Income Tax - [Report of Ceylon Tax Cases Vol 1] Commissioner of Income Tax v. Sarverimuttu Ratty - [Report of Ceylon Tax Cases Vol 1 at 109] Analysis Issue 1: Failure to answer the case stated Rule applied: Section 11A (6) of the Tax Appeals Commission Act No. 23 of 2011. Court's reasoning: The Court observed that the Court of Appeal only provided answers to three of the seven questions submitted in the case stated. The Court of Appeal dismissed four questions by stating they depended on the facts of each case without applying them to the circumstances of the instant case. The Supreme Court held that the Court of Appeal is required to answer all questions presented in a case stated unless it determines that answering them would not result in the confirmation, reduction, increase, or annulment of the assessment. Finding: The Court of Appeal erred by failing to adequately address the questions of law presented. Issue 2: Application of the Electronic Transactions Act Rule applied: Section 11A (6) of the Tax Appeals Commission Act No. 23 of 2011. Court's reasoning: The Court noted that the Court of Appeal discussed the Electronic Transactions Act No. 19 of 2006 at length without framing a specific question of law regarding its applicability. Furthermore, the Court of Appeal failed to link this discussion to the specific question regarding computer-generated assessments. The Supreme Court held that while the Court of Appeal may consider additional questions of law, it must frame them clearly if they are to be the basis of the decision. Finding: The Court of Appeal erred by discussing the Act without properly integrating it into the legal framework of the case stated. Interaction of Issues The failure to answer the specific questions of law (Issue 1) and the improper introduction of extraneous legal material (Issue 2) collectively resulted in a failure by the Court of Appeal to exercise its statutory duty under Section 11A (6) of the Tax Appeals Commission Act. The resolution of these issues necessitates that the matter be remitted to the Court of Appeal to ensure a proper adjudication of the original questions of law. Conclusion The appeal is allowed and the judgment of the Court of Appeal dated 08.06.2018 is set aside. The Court of Appeal is directed to answer all questions raised in the case stated in accordance with the statutory powers vested in it. Keywords Tax appeals — Value Added Tax (VAT) — Assessment notices — Validity of assessment — Electronic Transactions Act — Tax Appeals Commission Act — Jurisdiction of Court of Appeal — Case stated — Questions of law		SC/APPEAL/114/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_114_2019.pdf
sc-appeal-120-2012	2 July 2020	Bank of Ceylon No.1 v. Flex Port (Pvt) Limited	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; P. Padman Suresena, J	Overdraft facilities — Prescription Ordinance — Statute of limitations — Loan — Demand for repayment — Cause of action — Dormant account — Written acknowledgement	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant Bank granted overdraft facilities to the Defendant-Respondent, who maintained a current account at the bank's Nugegoda branch. The Plaintiff-Appellant Bank subsequently instituted action in the District Court of Mount Lavinia to recover a sum of Rs. 1,232,642.57 along with interest, alleging default on the overdraft facilities. The District Court dismissed the action on the grounds that it was prescribed under the Prescription Ordinance. The Plaintiff-Appellant Bank appealed this decision to the Civil Appellate High Court, which affirmed the District Court's judgment, maintaining that the action was prescribed based on a default date of 1.8.1999. Evidence presented in the statement of account indicated that the Defendant-Respondent had made various payments into the account between 2000 and 2002, which reduced the outstanding balance. The Plaintiff-Appellant Bank issued a formal letter of demand to the Defendant-Respondent on 26.9.2003. The Defendant-Respondent responded via a letter dated 5.12.2003, acknowledging the overdraft and expressing an intention to settle the amount. The Plaintiff-Appellant Bank filed the action on 19.12.2003. Issues Whether the action for the recovery of money lent via an overdraft facility is prescribed under Section 7 of the Prescription Ordinance if the action is commenced within three years of the formal demand for repayment. Whether the account of the Defendant-Respondent had lain dormant, thereby triggering the prescriptive period from the date of the last advance. Applicable Law Statutes: Prescription Ordinance No. 22 (as amended) Cases Referred to: Seylan Bank Limited v. Intertrade Garments (Private) Limited - [2004/v2-blr-null] Hatton National Bank v. Helenluc Garments Ltd - [1999/v2-sri-lr-365] Analysis Issue 1: Prescription and Demand Rule applied: Section 7 of the Prescription Ordinance No. 22; principles regarding loans repayable on demand as established in Seylan Bank Limited v. Intertrade Garments (Private) Limited. Court's reasoning: The Court determined that an overdraft granted by a bank is a loan. Relying on the precedent in Seylan Bank Limited v. Intertrade Garments (Private) Limited, the Court reasoned that for a loan repayable on demand, the cause of action arises only when the demand is made. Since the formal demand was issued on 26.9.2003 and the action was filed on 19.12.2003, the action was commenced within the three-year prescriptive period. Furthermore, the Defendant-Respondent's letter dated 5.12.2003 acknowledging the debt served as additional confirmation that the claim was not prescribed. Finding: The action was not prescribed because the cause of action arose on the date of the demand, 26.9.2003. Issue 2: Dormancy of Account Rule applied: General principles of banking law regarding dormant accounts and the running of prescription. Court's reasoning: The Court examined the statement of account (P6) and observed that the Defendant-Respondent made multiple payments in 2000, 2001, and 2002, which reduced the overdraft balance. Consequently, the Court rejected the contention that the account had remained dormant since 1999. Finding: The account was not dormant, as active transactions occurred within the relevant period. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing. The determination that the account was not dormant (Issue 2) confirms that the relationship between the parties remained active, while the determination that the cause of action only accrues upon demand (Issue 1) provides the legal framework to calculate the prescriptive period correctly. Together, these findings negate the lower courts' conclusion that the action was time-barred. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgments of the District Court and the Civil Appellate High Court are set aside, and the District Court is directed to enter a decree in favor of the Plaintiff-Appellant Bank as prayed for in the plaint, with costs in all three courts. Keywords Overdraft facilities — Prescription Ordinance — Statute of limitations — Loan — Demand for repayment — Cause of action — Dormant account — Written acknowledgement		SC/APPEAL/120/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_120_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-20-2017	03 July 2020	Lansage Basil v. Hon. Attorney General	Sisira J. de Abrew, J.	Sisira J. de Abrew, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda PC, J; P. Padman Surasena J	bail — locus standi — revision application — Judicature Act — extraordinary jurisdiction — discretionary relief — error correction — supervisory nature — due administration of justice — statutory rights — appeal rights — criminal procedure — heroin possession — Government Analyst's report	Facts The case revolves around an appeal filed by Lansage Basil (the Petitioner-Appellant) against the judgment of the Court of Appeal dated 8 August 2016, which dismissed his application for revision concerning the denial of bail for his son, who was remanded on charges of heroin possession. The Petitioner-Appellant sought to have his son released on bail after the High Court of Panadura denied the bail application on 13 January 2016. The allegation against the son was possession of 42 grams of heroin; however, the Government Analyst's report indicated that the pure quantity was only 3.89 grams. The Petitioner-Appellant, aggrieved by the High Court's refusal, filed a revision application in the Court of Appeal, which was dismissed on the grounds that he lacked locus standi. The Court of Appeal relied on the precedent set in Senathilaka v. Attorney General, where it was held that a father of an accused does not have the standing to file a revision application. The Petitioner-Appellant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal on two questions of law regarding locus standi and the consideration of the Petitioner-Appellant's standing in the context of his role in the bail application. Issues The primary issues before the Supreme Court were: 1. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in law by deciding that the Petitioner-Appellant had no locus standi to maintain the application for revision. 2. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in law by not considering that the Petitioner-Appellant, as the applicant for bail in the High Court, had the locus standi to maintain the revision application against the High Court's order. Applicable Law Statutes: Judicature Act, Criminal Procedure Code Cases Referred to: Senathilaka v. Attorney General - [1998] 3 SLR 290; Sudharman de Silva v. Attorney General - [1986] 1 SLR 9; Rasheed Ali v. Mohamed Ali - [1981] 1 SLR 262; Attorney General v. Gunawardena - [1996] 2 SLR 149. Analysis Issue 1: Locus Standi Rule applied: The principle of locus standi in the context of revision applications. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court examined the Court of Appeal's reliance on the Senathilaka case, emphasizing that the facts of the current case were distinct. The Court noted that the father of the accused in Senathilaka was seeking to challenge a conviction, whereas in this case, the Petitioner-Appellant was contesting a refusal of bail for a suspect who had not yet been convicted. The Court highlighted that the Petitioner-Appellant's relationship to the suspect provided him with a legitimate interest in the proceedings, thus granting him locus standi. The Court further analyzed Section 16 of the Judicature Act, which outlines the rights of aggrieved persons to appeal and the powers of the Court of Appeal to act by way of revision. The Court concluded that the revisionary powers of the Court of Appeal were not limited by the definitions provided in Section 16(1) and (2), and that the Court had an obligation to consider the merits of the revision application. The Court referenced the judgment of Sharvananda CJ in Sudharman de Silva, emphasizing the duty of the court to ensure that statutory rights are not lost without due process. Finding: The Supreme Court found that the Court of Appeal had erred in dismissing the revision application based on locus standi and set aside the judgment of the Court of Appeal dated 8 August 2016, directing that the revision application be heard on its merits. Issue 2: The second question of law regarding the Petitioner-Appellant's standing was deemed unnecessary to address, given the affirmative finding on the first issue. Interaction of Issues The issues of locus standi and the consideration of the Petitioner-Appellant's role in the bail application are interrelated, as the determination of standing directly impacts the ability to seek revision. The Supreme Court's ruling clarified that the familial relationship and the nature of the application for bail provided sufficient grounds for the Petitioner-Appellant to pursue the revision application. Conclusion The Supreme Court ultimately set aside the Court of Appeal's judgment and directed that the revision application be reconsidered, affirming the importance of ensuring that individuals are not denied their rights to seek judicial review based on technicalities related to standing. The ruling reinforced the principle that the courts must administer justice according to law, ensuring that statutory rights are upheld. Keywords bail — locus standi — revision application — Judicature Act — extraordinary jurisdiction — discretionary relief — error correction — supervisory nature — due administration of justice — statutory rights — appeal rights — criminal procedure — heroin possession — Government Analyst's report	J.P. Gamage for the Petitioner-Appellant; Suharshi Herath SSC for the Attorney General	SC/APPEAL/20/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_20_2017.pdf
sc-fr-322-2011	5 July 2020	Randeniya and Others v. Fernando and Others	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, CJ	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, CJ; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Public Service Commission — Salaries and Cadres Commission — Career Guidance Officers — Salary scales — Legitimate expectation — Administrative action	Facts The thirty-four Petitioners were recruited in 1999 as Graduate Trainees and subsequently appointed as Career Guidance Officers in 2000. They were confirmed in their posts in 2003. In 2006, their salaries were converted to the MN4 scale pursuant to Public Administration Circular No. 6/2006. The Petitioners sought to be placed on the MN6 scale and later requested the creation of Supra Grade positions to be placed on the MN7 scale. In 2010, the Petitioners were absorbed into the newly established Department of Manpower and Employment. The Secretary of the Ministry of Labour Relations sought approval to suppress thirty-eight existing positions and create thirty-eight Supra Grade positions to facilitate the Petitioners' placement on the MN7 scale. The National Salaries and Cadres Commission declined this request, observing that the officers could be absorbed into a structured grading system in compliance with Public Administration Circular 06/2006, which would allow for increased responsibilities. The Petitioners challenged this decision, alleging it was arbitrary, unreasonable, and discriminatory, thereby infringing their fundamental right to equality under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Issues Whether the decision of the National Salaries and Cadres Commission not to create Supra Grade positions for the Petitioners constituted an infringement of the fundamental right to equality under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioners had a legitimate expectation to be placed on a higher salary scale (MN6 or MN7). Whether the Petitioners complied with the time bar requirements under Article 126(2) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1), Article 126(1), Article 126(2) Human Rights Commission of Sri Lanka Act No. 21 of 1996, Section 31 Cases Referred to: Dayarathna v. Minister of Health and Indigenous Medicine - [1999/v1-sri-lr-393] G.M. Nimalasiri v. Colonel P.P.J. Fernando - [sc-fr-256/2010] Ariyaratne v. Illangakoon - [sc-fr-444/2012] Akarawita v. Dr. Nanda Wickramasinghe - [sc-fr-320/2007] Siriwardane v. Seneviratne - [2011/v2-sri-lr-1] Tuan Ishan Raban v. Members of the Police Commission - [2007/v2-sri-lr-351] Ananda Dharmadasa v. Ariyaratne Hewage - [2008/v2-sri-lr-19] Ram Krishna Dalmia v. Justice Tendolka - [AIR 1985 S.C. 538] H.K. Subasinghe v. The Inspector General of Police - [sc-spl-16] Ranaweera v. Sub-Inspector Wilson Siriwardane - [2008/v1-sri-lr-260] Analysis Issue 1: Infringement of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution, which guarantees equality before the law but permits reasonable classification based on intelligible differentia. Court's reasoning: The court held that the Commission's decision was rational and based on the need to restructure the public service in line with overall policy. The court noted that different departments have unique needs, and the fact that other graduate recruits were placed on different salary scales does not constitute discrimination, as salary scales depend on specific job requirements and qualifications. Finding: The Petitioners failed to establish that the decision was arbitrary, capricious, or discriminatory. Issue 2: Legitimate Expectation Rule applied: The doctrine of legitimate expectation, which requires a specific, unambiguous assurance or an established, unambiguous practice. Court's reasoning: The court found that the documents relied upon by the Petitioners were merely proposals or recommendations and did not constitute a promise or assurance by the Respondents. A mere hope or expectation does not satisfy the legal threshold for a legitimate expectation. Finding: The Petitioners failed to establish a substantive legitimate expectation. Issue 3: Time Bar Rule applied: Article 126(2) of the Constitution, requiring applications to be filed within one month of the alleged infringement, subject to the exclusion of time spent in pending Human Rights Commission inquiries under Section 31 of Act No. 21 of 1996. Court's reasoning: The Petitioners failed to provide evidence that an inquiry was actually pending before the Human Rights Commission at the time they invoked the Supreme Court's jurisdiction. Finding: The application was time-barred, though the court proceeded to determine the matter on its merits. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected as the failure to establish a legitimate expectation or an arbitrary classification (Issue 1 and 2) reinforces the conclusion that the administrative decision was reasonable. Furthermore, the failure to meet the procedural time bar (Issue 3) provided an independent ground for dismissal, though the court's substantive analysis confirmed the lack of merit in the Petitioners' claims. Conclusion The Petitioners failed to establish an infringement of their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The application is accordingly dismissed. No costs are ordered. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Public Service Commission — Salaries and Cadres Commission — Career Guidance Officers — Salary scales — Legitimate expectation — Administrative action		SC/FR/322/2011	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_322_2011.pdf
sc-fr-446-2016	5 July 2020	Batalanda and Others v. Jayantha and Others	Vijith K. Malalgoda, J	Priyantha Jayawardena, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Provincial Revenue Service — Deputy Commissioner appointment — Seniority — Public Administration Circular 6/2006 — Legitimate expectation — Service minute — Promotion	Facts The Petitioner in SC FR 227/2016 was a Senior Assessor in the Provincial Revenue Service of the Western Province. In 2015, a vacancy arose for the post of Deputy Commissioner. The Petitioner applied for this position, but the 1st Respondent informed him that the appointment would be made based on seniority under the existing service minute, rather than through an interview process. The Petitioner challenged this, arguing that the existing service minute was outdated and failed to comply with Public Administration Circular 6/2006, which required specific qualifications and competitive selection processes. He contended that he had a legitimate expectation that the recruitment process would be amended to reflect these national policy requirements. The Petitioner in SC FR 446/2016 was the most senior officer in Class II Grade I of the same service. She was recommended by the 1st Respondent for the Deputy Commissioner vacancy based on the existing service minute, which prioritized seniority. She was subsequently appointed to the post on an acting and covering-up basis. She later filed her application alleging that the failure to promote her to the permanent cadre violated her fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1) of the Constitution. The two applications presented conflicting positions regarding the legality of the promotion criteria. While the Petitioner in SC FR 227/2016 sought to invalidate the seniority-based promotion, the Petitioner in SC FR 446/2016 sought to enforce it to secure a permanent appointment. Issues Whether the failure to amend the existing service minute to incorporate the requirements of Public Administration Circular 6/2006 violated the Petitioner's fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution in SC FR 227/2016. Whether the Petitioner in SC FR 446/2016 had a legitimate expectation to be appointed to the permanent cadre of Deputy Commissioner based on the service minute in operation at the time the vacancy occurred. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 14(1), 17, 126 Provincial Councils Act No. 42 of 1987 Cases Referred to: Kamalawathie v. The Provincial Public Service Commission, North-Western Province - [2001/v1-sri-lr-1] Siriwardana v. Seneviratne - [sc-fr-589/2009] Kurukulasooriya v. Edirisinghe - [sc-fr-577/2009] R (Bancoult) v. Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs - [2009/v1-ac-465] Dayarathne v. Minister of Health and Indigenous Medicine - [1999/v1-sri-lr-393] Analysis Issue 1: Legitimate expectation of policy change Rule applied: The doctrine of legitimate expectation as established in Siriwardana v. Seneviratne and Kurukulasooriya v. Edirisinghe. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the Petitioner in SC FR 227/2016 relied on a draft scheme of recruitment that had not been finalized or implemented at the time the vacancy occurred. The court held that a mere hope or expectation of a policy change does not constitute a legitimate expectation. Furthermore, the court noted that while national policy must be followed, the delay in implementing the circular did not equate to an arbitrary or invidious exercise of power by the Respondents, as the existing service minute was the only legally operative document at the time. Finding: The Petitioner failed to establish a violation of Article 12(1) as his claim was based on a non-implemented policy. Issue 2: Right to permanent appointment Rule applied: The principles of legitimate expectation and the requirement of acting in accordance with the prevailing service minute. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Petitioner in SC FR 446/2016 was recommended for the post based on the existing service minute. However, the court noted that the vacancy could not be filled permanently due to the stay order issued in the connected case (SC FR 227/2016). The court concluded that the Respondents did not act arbitrarily or unreasonably, as they followed the existing, albeit outdated, service minute in recommending the Petitioner. Finding: The Petitioner failed to establish that the Respondents violated her fundamental rights by failing to grant her a permanent appointment while the matter was sub judice. Interaction of Issues The resolution of the two issues is interdependent. The court's finding that the existing service minute remained the valid governing document for the vacancy in SC FR 227/2016 directly impacted the claim in SC FR 446/2016. Because the court determined that the Respondents were bound by the existing service minute, the Petitioner in SC FR 446/2016 could not claim a violation of rights when the Respondents were legally prevented from finalizing her appointment due to the ongoing challenge to that very service minute. Conclusion Both applications are dismissed. The Petitioners are ordered to bear their own costs. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Provincial Revenue Service — Deputy Commissioner appointment — Seniority — Public Administration Circular 6/2006 — Legitimate expectation — Service minute — Promotion			
sc-fr-446-2016	06 July 2020	K.N. Kanthi Silva v. G. A. N. Jayantha and Others	Priyantha Jayawardena, P.C., J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, P.C., J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, P.C., J.; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Fundamental Rights — Article 12(1) — Article 14(1)(g) — Promotion — Seniority — Public Administration Circular 6 of 2006 — Legitimate Expectation — Provincial Revenue Service — Service Minute — Administrative Law — Judicial Review	Facts The case revolves around two petitions filed under Article 17 and 126 of the Constitution of Sri Lanka, concerning alleged violations of fundamental rights. The first petitioner, K.N. Kanthi Silva, was a Senior Assessor in the Provincial Revenue Service and claimed that she was not promoted to the vacant post of Deputy Commissioner, which she argued was a violation of her rights under Article 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. The second petitioner, Dissanayake Mudiyanselage Udayasiri, also claimed a similar violation regarding the same post. The background of the case indicates that a vacancy arose in the Deputy Commissioner position on 30 April 2015, and both petitioners contended that they had legitimate expectations to be appointed based on their seniority and qualifications. The court had to consider the procedural aspects of promotions within the Provincial Revenue Service and the implications of the Public Administration Circular 6 of 2006, which outlined the promotion criteria. The court noted that both petitioners had different positions regarding their claims, which complicated the matter. Issues Whether the failure to promote K.N. Kanthi Silva to the post of Deputy Commissioner constituted a violation of her fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the failure to consider Dissanayake Mudiyanselage Udayasiri's application for the same post violated his rights under Article 12(1) and 14(1)(g). Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1), Article 14(1)(g); Public Administration Circular 6 of 2006. Cases Referred to: Kamalawathie and Others Vs. The Provincial Public Service Commission, North-Western Province and Others - [2001] 1 Sri LR 1; Siriwardana Vs. Seneviratne and Others - SC FR 589/2009; Kurukulasooriya Vs. Edirisinghe and Others - SC FR 577/2009; Dayarathne Vs. Minister of Health and Indigenous Medicine - [1999] 1 Sri LR 393. Analysis Issue 1: Whether the failure to promote K.N. Kanthi Silva to the post of Deputy Commissioner constituted a violation of her fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution guarantees equality before the law and the equal protection of the law. Court's reasoning: The court examined the procedural history surrounding the promotion to the Deputy Commissioner post, noting that K.N. Kanthi Silva was the most senior officer in Class II Grade I at the time the vacancy arose. The court highlighted that the promotion process was governed by the existing service minute, which stipulated that promotions were to be based on seniority. The court also referenced the Public Administration Circular 6 of 2006, which aimed to standardize promotion criteria across the public service. The court found that the respondents had not acted in accordance with the established procedures, as they failed to promote Silva despite her qualifications and seniority. The court emphasized that the principle of legitimate expectation was crucial in this context, as Silva had a reasonable expectation to be promoted based on her position and the existing rules. The court also noted that the respondents had not provided sufficient justification for their failure to promote her, which constituted an arbitrary exercise of power. Finding: The court concluded that the failure to promote K.N. Kanthi Silva violated her fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Issue 2: Whether the failure to consider Dissanayake Mudiyanselage Udayasiri's application for the same post violated his rights under Article 12(1) and 14(1)(g). Rule applied: Article 12(1) and Article 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The court analyzed Udayasiri's claim, noting that he had applied for the Deputy Commissioner position based on his seniority and qualifications. However, the court found that Udayasiri's expectation of promotion was based on a draft scheme of recruitment that had not been implemented at the time the vacancy arose. The court emphasized that legitimate expectation must be grounded in a clear promise or established procedure, which was lacking in Udayasiri's case. The court concluded that Udayasiri's claim was based on a mere expectation of future policy changes rather than a right established by existing rules. Finding: The court determined that Udayasiri had failed to establish a violation of his rights under Article 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Interaction of Issues The issues presented by both petitioners were interrelated, as they both concerned the same vacancy and the application of the same legal principles regarding promotions within the Provincial Revenue Service. However, their claims diverged in terms of the basis for their expectations of promotion, with Silva relying on established procedures and Udayasiri on anticipated changes to policy. This divergence highlighted the complexities of administrative law and the necessity for clear and consistent application of promotion criteria. Conclusion The Supreme Court dismissed both applications, concluding that K.N. Kanthi Silva's rights had been violated, while Dissanayake Mudiyanselage Udayasiri's claims were unfounded. The court ordered that the petitioners bear their own costs, reflecting the mixed outcome of the judgment. The decision underscored the importance of adhering to established procedures in public service promotions and the need for clarity in the application of administrative policies. Keywords Fundamental Rights — Article 12(1) — Article 14(1)(g) — Promotion — Seniority — Public Administration Circular 6 of 2006 — Legitimate Expectation — Provincial Revenue Service — Service Minute — Administrative Law — Judicial Review	Manohara de. Silva, PC, with Hirosha Munasinghe and Imalka Abeyratne for the Petitioner in SC FR 446/2016; Saliya Peiris, PC, with Thanuka Nandasiri for the Petitioner in SC FR 227/2016; Uditha Egalahewa, PC, with Sachinthana Rajamuni for the Intervenient Petitioners in SC FR 227/2016; Ms. Sureka Ahmed SC for the 1st to 7th and 9th Respondents in SC FR 227/2016 and for all the Respondents in SC FR 446/2016.	SC/FR/446/2016 with SC/FR/227/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_446_227_2016.pdf
sc-appeal-26-2016	9 July 2020	Indrawansha v. Rupasiri and Others	Aluwihare, PC. J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; Priyantha Jayawardena, PC. J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	High Court of Civil Appeal — Revisionary jurisdiction — Partition action — Right of way — Servitutal right — Prescription — Necessity — Final decree — Fundamental rights — District Court — Civil Procedure Code	Facts The Plaintiff-Respondent-Respondent-Appellant instituted a partition action in the District Court of Gampaha regarding a land described in the third schedule of the plaint. The District Court entered an interlocutory decree on 24.07.2001 and a final decree on 30.09.2002. Following the entry of the final decree, the Plaintiff attempted to execute the writ of possession on multiple occasions. These attempts were met with resistance from various parties, leading to the initiation of contempt of court proceedings. These proceedings were eventually settled, and the Plaintiff was placed in possession. During the pendency of these events, the Respondent, who was not a party to the original partition action, filed an application in the District Court under Section 325(4) of the Civil Procedure Code. The Respondent sought to set aside the writ of possession, claimed a right of way by prescription or necessity, and requested possession of the land. The District Court dismissed this application in limine. The Respondent subsequently moved the High Court of Civil Appeals by way of revision. The High Court set aside the partition judgment and final decree and granted the reliefs sought by the Respondent. The Plaintiff then appealed this decision to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the High Court erred in law by granting final reliefs, including prescriptive and servitutal rights, without leading evidence. Whether the High Court erred in law by granting both main and alternative reliefs (right of way by prescription and necessity) simultaneously. Whether the High Court erred in setting aside the judgment and final decree of the partition action, thereby violating the finality of the Partition Law No. 21 of 1977. Whether the High Court erred in its finding that the writ of possession was executed after the expiration of the ten-year period prescribed by Section 337 of the Civil Procedure Code. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 138 High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) (Amendment) Act No. 54 of 2006 Civil Procedure Code, Section 325(4) Civil Procedure Code, Section 337 Partition Law No. 21 of 1977, Section 48 Cases Referred to: Sikander Abdul Samadh v. M. M. Musajee - [1998/v2-ca-lr-147] Setunga v. Fernando - [1982/v2-sri-lr-584] Andradie v. Jayasekera Perera - [1985/v2-sri-lr-204] Kamanie Alles de Silva v.
