Wijewardane - [2002/v3-sri-lr-236] De Soysa v. Fonseka - [58-nlr-501] Maddumage Sulochana Perera v. Maddumage Nimal Gunasiri Perera - [sc-appeal-59/2012] Somawathie v. Madawela - [1983/v2-sri-lr-15] Gunatillake v. Muriel Silva - [79-nlr-481] Laxman Senevirathne v. Sri Jayewardenepura Kotte Municipal Council - [ca-1212/2003] Rustom v. Hapangama & Co. - [1978/79/v2-sri-lr-225] Rasheed Ali v. Mohamed Ali - [1981/v2-sri-lr-29] Mariam Beebee v. Seyed Mohamed - [68-nlr-36] Ukku v. Sidoris - [59-nlr-90] Analysis Issue 1: Granting relief without evidence Rule applied: The burden of proof for servitudes and prescriptive rights lies with the claimant, requiring clear and satisfactory evidence. Court's reasoning: The High Court granted prescriptive and servitutal rights based solely on a bare affidavit without conducting an inquiry or allowing for cross-examination. The Respondent only acquired title in 2012, making a claim of prescriptive rights impossible. Finding: The High Court erred in law; the issue is answered in the affirmative. Issue 2: Granting alternative reliefs Rule applied: Right of way by prescription and right of way by necessity are distinct legal concepts with different requirements. Court's reasoning: Prescription requires proof of long-term, adverse, and uninterrupted use, whereas necessity is based on the geographical impossibility of accessing a property without traversing the servient tenement. They are mutually exclusive in their evidentiary requirements. Finding: The High Court erred in law; the issue is answered in the affirmative. Issue 3: Finality of partition decree Rule applied: Section 48 of the Partition Law No. 21 of 1977. Court's reasoning: A partition decree is final and conclusive. While revisionary jurisdiction exists to prevent a miscarriage of justice, it must be exercised cautiously. The Respondent was a stranger to the partition action and had no valid claim to the corpus at the time of the decree. Finding: The High Court erred in law; the issue is answered in the affirmative. Issue 4: Execution of writ Rule applied: Section 337(2) of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The Plaintiff sought the writ within one year of the final decree. Subsequent delays were caused by resistance and contempt proceedings, which toll the limitation period under Section 337(2). Finding: The High Court erred in law; the issue is answered in the affirmative. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing. The High Court's failure to conduct an inquiry (Issue 1) led to an erroneous factual finding regarding the execution of the writ (Issue 4). Furthermore, the lack of evidence (Issue 1) and the misapplication of the nature of servitudes (Issue 2) directly undermined the statutory finality of the partition decree (Issue 3), resulting in an improper exercise of revisionary jurisdiction. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The order of the High Court of Civil Appeal dated 13.01.2015 is set aside, and the Plaintiff-Respondent-Petitioner-Appellant is awarded costs. Keywords High Court of Civil Appeal — Revisionary jurisdiction — Partition action — Right of way — Servitutal right — Prescription — Necessity — Final decree — Fundamental rights — District Court — Civil Procedure Code		SC/APPEAL/26/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_26_2016.pdf
sc-appeal-134-2014	13 July 2020	Fernando and Munasinghe v. Jayasiri and Others	E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Debt Conciliation Ordinance — Debt Conciliation (Amendment) Act No. 29 — Section 19A(1A) — Transfer of immovable property — Mortgage — Possession of property — Retrospective effect — Interpretation of statutes — Case stated to Court of Appeal	Facts The Original Debtor, Handuwala Devage Simon Fernando, entered into a loan agreement with the Original Creditor, Ranepura Devage Hector Jayasiri, on 01.02.1993. As security for the loan of Rs. 75,000, the Original Debtor transferred his land to the Original Creditor via a notarially executed transfer deed. The Original Debtor subsequently passed away, and his interest was pursued by the Substituted Debtor Applicant. On 02.02.2006, the Original Debtor filed an application with the Debt Conciliation Board seeking relief under the Debt Conciliation (Amendment) Act No. 29 of 1999. The Board faced a question regarding its jurisdiction to entertain applications based on transfer deeds executed prior to the certification of the Amending Act on 17.09.1999. Consequently, the Board stated a case to the Court of Appeal for its opinion. The Court of Appeal concluded that the amendments applied to transfer deeds executed on or after 17.09.1996, three years prior to the certification of the Act. The Substituted Debtor Applicant appealed this decision to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal misconstrued the proviso to Section 19A(1A) of the Debt Conciliation (Amendment) Act No. 29 of 1999 regarding its applicability to deeds executed prior to 17.09.1999. Whether the Amending Act has retrospective effect on transfer deeds executed between 17.09.1996 and 17.09.1999. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 128 Debt Conciliation Ordinance Debt Conciliation (Amendment) Act No. 29 of 1999 Evidence Ordinance Prevention of Frauds Ordinance Interpretation Ordinance, Section 2 Cases Referred to: R v. Smith - [1910/v1-kb-17] R v. Weston - [1910/v1-kb-17] Ismalebbe v. Assistant Commissioner of Agrarian Services - [1991/v2-sri-lr-332] Organo Chemical Industries v. Union of India - [AIR-1979-SC-1803] Lloyds and Scottish Finance, Ltd. v. Modern Cars and Caravans (Kingston), Ltd - [1966/v1-qb-779] In re. Tabrisky, Ex. parte The Board of Trade - [1947/ch-565] Colombo Apothecaries Limited v. E.A. Wijesooriya - [73-nlr-05] Grocock v. Grocock - [1920/v1-kb-1] R v. Chandra Dharma - [1905/v2-kb-335] Director Public Prosecution v. Lamb - [1941/v2-all-er-499] Sunshine Porcelain Potteries Pty. Ltd. v. Nash - [1961/ac-927] Re Athlumney - [1898/v2-qb-547] Attorney General v. Vernazza - [1960/v3-all-er-97] Colonial Sugar Refining Co. v. Irving - [1905/ac-369] Kanagasabai v. Seevaratnam - [76-nlr-517] Analysis Issue 1: Construction of the Proviso Rule applied: Section 19A(1A) of the Debt Conciliation (Amendment) Act No. 29 of 1999. Court's reasoning: The Court held that a proviso cannot be read as a standalone provision but must be interpreted in the context of the main subsection it qualifies. The proviso to Section 19A(1A) serves to qualify the three-year limitation period for filing applications. It does not grant independent jurisdiction to the Board to entertain applications for deeds executed at any time in the past. The Court affirmed that the scope of a proviso is limited to the ambit of the section it qualifies. Finding: The Court of Appeal correctly identified that the proviso is linked to the main subsection, but erred in extending the application of the Act to deeds executed prior to the date of certification. Issue 2: Retrospective Operation Rule applied: The presumption against retrospective operation of statutes as established in the Interpretation Ordinance and common law principles. Court's reasoning: The Court noted that the Amending Act contains no express provision for retrospective effect. Applying the principle that statutes should not be construed to impair substantive rights or create new obligations for past transactions unless clearly intended, the Court found that the Court of Appealâ€™s interpretation created a retrospective burden on vendees of deeds executed between 17.09.1996 and 17.09.1999. Since the legislature did not manifest a clear, unambiguous intent to affect these antecedent rights, the Act must be construed as prospective, applying only to deeds executed on or after 17.09.1999. Finding: The Amending Act is not applicable to debts secured by transfers made prior to 17.09.1999. Interaction of Issues The findings are interdependent; the determination that the proviso is not a standalone provision (Issue 1) reinforces the conclusion that the Act cannot be applied retrospectively to deeds executed before the Act's certification (Issue 2). By linking the proviso to the main section, the Court effectively limited the Board's jurisdiction to the timeframe established by the Act's commencement. Conclusion The appeal is allowed in part, and the opinion of the Court of Appeal is amended to state that Section 19A(1A) of the Debt Conciliation (Amendment) Act No. 29 of 1999 is not applicable to debts secured by transfers made prior to 17.09.1999. Consequently, the Court cannot grant the relief prayed for by the Substituted Debtor Applicant. No costs are ordered. Keywords Debt Conciliation Ordinance — Debt Conciliation (Amendment) Act No. 29 — Section 19A(1A) — Transfer of immovable property — Mortgage — Possession of property — Retrospective effect — Interpretation of statutes — Case stated to Court of Appeal		SC/APPEAL/134/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_134_2014.pdf
sc-fr-244-2012	16 July 2020	Namal Sanjeewa v. Gunawardena and Others	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Public service recruitment — Structured interview — Marking scheme — Achievements in sports — District level competition — Natural justice — Equal protection of the law	Facts The Petitioner, a graduate and an unsuccessful applicant for the post of Assistant Superintendent of Customs Grade II, challenged the recruitment process initiated by a gazette notice dated 07-01-2011. The selection process involved a written examination followed by a structured interview where marks were awarded for sports achievements. The Petitioner sat for the examination and was subsequently called for a structured interview. Following the interview, the Petitioner was awarded four marks for sports achievements, which he contended was insufficient. The Petitioner alleged that he should have received nine marks based on his weightlifting achievements at the district level across multiple years. He argued that the marking scheme was arbitrary, capricious, and discriminatory, and that the terminology used in the interview invitation letter regarding the levels of sports competition was inconsistent with the original gazette notice. The Petitioner sought a declaration that his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution had been violated and requested a re-evaluation of his marks and subsequent appointment. The Respondents maintained that the marking scheme was rational, that marks were awarded for the highest level of achievement in a single sport, and that no additional marks were granted for repeated success in the same sport over multiple years. Issues Whether the marking scheme for the structured interview was arbitrary, capricious, or unlawful, thereby violating the Petitioner's fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the inconsistency in terminology regarding sports competition levels between the gazette notice and the interview invitation letter rendered the selection process discriminatory or unlawful. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 14(1)(g), 126 Cases Referred to: Gamaethige v. Siriwardena - [1988/v1-sri-lr-384] Analysis Issue 1: Validity of the marking scheme Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution, which guarantees equality before the law and equal protection of the law. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the marking scheme awarded marks based on the highest level of achievement (Zonal, District, or National) in a single sport. The court found this to be a rational criterion designed to ensure a level playing field among candidates. The court reasoned that awarding marks for every year of success in the same sport would lead to absurd results, potentially exceeding the maximum allotted marks and creating injustice for other candidates. The Respondents' policy of limiting marks to the highest level of achievement was deemed a reasonable classification. Finding: The marking scheme was not arbitrary or capricious, and the Respondents acted within the established guidelines. Issue 2: Inconsistency in terminology Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The court noted that while the invitation letter (P2) contained a minor discrepancy by using the word "provincial" instead of "district" in one instance, the gazette notice (P1) clearly and specifically set out the criteria for selection. The court held that the gazette notice is the primary document governing the recruitment process and that the minor typographical error in the invitation letter did not invalidate the entire marking scheme or render it unlawful. Finding: The disparity in terminology did not constitute a violation of the Petitioner's fundamental rights or render the process discriminatory. Interaction of Issues The court's findings on the rationality of the marking scheme reinforced the conclusion that the process was fair. Because the marking scheme itself was found to be based on a legitimate and intelligible differentia, the minor procedural inconsistency in the invitation letter was insufficient to establish a breach of equal protection. The resolution of the substantive marking issue rendered the procedural complaint immaterial to the claim of fundamental rights infringement. Conclusion The Petitioner failed to establish that his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution were violated by the Respondents. The application is dismissed. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Public service recruitment — Structured interview — Marking scheme — Achievements in sports — District level competition — Natural justice — Equal protection of the law		SC/FR/244/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_244_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-32-2015	16 July 2020	Chandi v. Attorney General	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekera, J	Murder — Attempted murder — Culpable homicide not amounting to murder — Grave and sudden provocation — Cumulative provocation — Penal Code — Exception 1 to Section 294 — Evidence — Trial judgeâ€™s discretion — Court of Appealâ€™s decision — Intention to kill	Facts The Accused-Appellant-Petitioner was indicted in the High Court of Polonnaruwa along with his father for the murder of a woman and the attempted murder of her brother-in-law. The trial judge convicted the accused on both counts, while the father was acquitted. The accused was sentenced to death for murder and fifteen years of rigorous imprisonment for attempted murder. The Court of Appeal subsequently dismissed the appeal filed by the accused. The incident occurred around 7:30 in the morning. Earlier that day, the accused had an altercation with the victim regarding employees who had left the accused to work for the victim. During this initial scuffle, the accused assaulted the victim and pushed his bicycle aside. The victim fled the scene, met his brother and sister-in-law, and they proceeded toward their paddy field, passing the house of the accused. As they approached, the accused emerged armed with a gun and opened fire, killing the woman and injuring the victim. The accused claimed in a dock statement that he was attacked by a crowd and the gun went off accidentally, but the trial judge rejected this version, relying on the testimony of independent eyewitnesses. Issues Whether the judgment of the Court of Appeal was contrary to law and against the weight of evidence. Whether the accused was entitled to the benefit of the plea of grave and sudden provocation under Exception 1 to Section 294 of the Penal Code to reduce the conviction to culpable homicide not amounting to murder. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code, Section 294 Cases Referred to: Attorney-General v John Perera - [54-nlr-265] Jamis v The Queen - [53-nlr-401] K.D.J. Perera v The King - [53-nlr-193] Samithamby v The Queen - [75-nlr-49] Gamini Silva v Attorney-General - [1998/v3-sri-lr-248] Premlal v Attorney-General - [2000/v2-sri-lr-403] Jan Muhammed v Emperor - [AIR 1929 Lahore 861] W.A. Gamini v Attorney-General - [ca-142/2009] Marimuthu v State - [crl.a.(md)-no-29] Poovammal v State - [2012/v2-mlj-(crl.)-482] K.M. Nanavathi v State of Maharashtra - [AIR 1962 SC 605] Suresh Kumar v State of Delhi - [crl.-a-182/2002] Analysis Issue 1: Weight of evidence and legality of conviction Rule applied: The court applied the standard of appellate review regarding the weight of evidence and the trial judge's assessment of witness credibility. Court's reasoning: The trial judge accepted the evidence of three eyewitnesses, including the injured victim and a neighbor, who provided a consistent account of the shooting. The court noted that the nature of the injuries, including multiple gunshot wounds to the face and chest, demonstrated a clear murderous intention. The accused's dock statement claiming accidental discharge was rejected as inconsistent with the evidence. Finding: The Court of Appeal did not err in affirming the conviction, as the evidence clearly supported the trial judge's findings. Issue 2: Plea of grave and sudden provocation Rule applied: Exception 1 to Section 294 of the Penal Code, which requires the accused to prove on a balance of probability that they were deprived of self-control by grave and sudden provocation. Court's reasoning: While the court acknowledged that jurisprudence has expanded to include "cumulative" or "sustained" provocation, the facts of this case did not meet the threshold. There was a clear "cooling off" period between the initial scuffle and the subsequent shooting. Furthermore, the deceased woman had no prior interaction or provocative conduct toward the accused. The accused failed to demonstrate a state of continued mental stress or agony resulting from the victim's actions. Finding: The plea of grave and sudden provocation was not established, and the conviction for murder was justified. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing; the rejection of the provocation plea confirms that the accused acted with the requisite murderous intent, which in turn validates the trial court's assessment of the evidence regarding the nature of the attack and the lack of credibility in the accused's defense. Conclusion The question of law is answered in the negative. The appeal is dismissed, and the convictions and sentences imposed by the High Court are affirmed. Keywords Murder — Attempted murder — Culpable homicide not amounting to murder — Grave and sudden provocation — Cumulative provocation — Penal Code — Exception 1 to Section 294 — Evidence — Trial judgeâ€™s discretion — Court of Appealâ€™s decision — Intention to kill		SC/APPEAL/32/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_32_2015.pdf
sc-fr-214-2017	16 July 2020	Badathuruge and Others v. Pathiranawasam and Others	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Priyantha Jayawardene, PC, J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Admission to school — Proximity category — Appeals Board — Reduction of marks — Legitimate expectation — Due process of law — Violation of constitutional rights — Admission Circular	Facts The 1st and 2nd Petitioners, being the child and father respectively, applied for the admission of the 1st Petitioner to Grade One at Southlands College, Galle, for the year 2017 under the proximate category. The Petitioners were initially awarded 80 marks at the interview. Subsequently, an objection was raised regarding the application, and the 2nd Petitioner was summoned before the Appeals and Objections Board. At this inquiry, the Petitioners were informed that the marks would not be altered. However, the 1st Petitioner was later excluded from the final admission list, having been moved to a waiting list. Upon inquiry through the Human Rights Commission, it was revealed that the Appeals Board had reduced the 1st Petitioner's marks by five points because there were seven schools in closer proximity to the residence than Southlands College, bringing the total to 75, which was below the cut-off mark. The Petitioners challenged this decision, alleging that the Respondents violated the Admission Circular and their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The Petitioners further contended that other students were admitted despite living further away and that the Appeals Board's failure to follow procedural requirements, such as endorsing the mark sheet, constituted an abuse of process and a breach of legitimate expectation. The Respondents maintained that the marks were correctly adjusted based on the proximity rule and that the Petitioners had provided inaccurate information in their application. Issues Whether the reduction of the 1st Petitioner's marks by the Appeals Board, which resulted in the 1st Petitioner falling below the cut-off mark, violated the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the alleged procedural irregularities in the admission process and the admission of other students under similar circumstances entitle the 1st Petitioner to admission to Southlands College. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 17, 126 Cases Referred to: K.J.A. Chathumi Sehasa v. Pathiranawasam - [sc-appeal-201/2017] C.W. Mackie and Co. Ltd v. Hugh Molagoda - [1986/v1-sri-lr-300] Gamaethige v. Siriwardena - [1988/v1-sri-lr-384] Jayasekera v. Wipulasena - [1988/v2-sri-lr-237] Seelawansa Thero v. Tennakoon - [2004/v2-sri-lr-241] Samaraweera v. Peoples Bank - [2007/v2-sri-lr-362] Analysis Issue 1: Reduction of marks and Article 12(1) Rule applied: The principle that the Constitution guarantees equal protection of the law, not equal violation of the law, and that applicants must provide accurate information to be eligible for relief. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Petitioners provided inaccurate information in their application regarding the number of schools in closer proximity to their residence. The Survey General's map confirmed the existence of seven schools in closer proximity to the residence than Southlands College. The Appeals Board acted within its authority to verify facts and adjust marks accordingly. The court held that the Petitioners failed to establish that the reduction of marks was contrary to the law or the Admission Circular. Finding: The reduction of marks was legally justified, and no violation of Article 12(1) occurred. Issue 2: Procedural irregularities and legitimate expectation Rule applied: The principle that "two wrongs do not make a right" and that the court cannot compel a public authority to act illegally or in excess of its powers. Court's reasoning: The court determined that even if the Respondents had acted irregularly in admitting other students, this does not grant the Petitioners a right to be admitted through an illegal act. Regarding legitimate expectation, the court held that the Petitioners' case failed on its own merits due to the misrepresentation of facts in their application. The court further noted that while procedural prudence is desirable, the Admission Circular does not strictly mandate that all mark variations occur in the presence of the applicant. Finding: Procedural irregularities, even if proven, do not entitle the Petitioners to relief when the underlying claim for admission is not supported by law. Interaction of Issues The findings on the two issues are mutually reinforcing. The determination that the Petitioners provided false information and that the mark reduction was substantively correct renders the procedural arguments moot. Because the court cannot sanction an illegal act, the failure to establish a valid entitlement to admission based on merit precludes the court from granting relief based on alleged procedural lapses or the treatment of other applicants. Conclusion The application of the 1st and 2nd Petitioners is dismissed. The court held that the Petitioners failed to establish a violation of their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Admission to school — Proximity category — Appeals Board — Reduction of marks — Legitimate expectation — Due process of law — Violation of constitutional rights — Admission Circular		SC/FR/214/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_214_2017.pdf
sc-fr-29-2018	16 July 2020	Locomotive Assistants Union and Others v. Abeywickrema and Others	Aluwihare, P.C., J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, P.C., J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Scheme of Recruitment — Railway Driver Assistants — Non-implementation of SOR — Public service employment — Trade union action — Railway safety — Administrative action	Facts The Petitioners are office bearers and members of the Locomotive Assistants Union, employed as Railway Driver Assistants in the Sri Lanka Railways Department. The Added-Respondents are members of the Railway Locomotive Operating Engineers Union, representing Railway Engine Drivers. The Petitioners challenge the non-implementation of the 2016 Scheme of Recruitment (SOR) for Railway Driver Assistants, which was approved by the Public Service Commission. Following a 2006 salary restructuring, Railway Driver Assistants and Engine Drivers (Shunting) were categorized under labour grades, but a 2010 SOR placed them in the Management Assistant Technical-Segment 3 category. In 2016, a new SOR was approved, increasing the cadre of Railway Driver Assistants to 540 and revising recruitment qualifications. Despite this, the 1st to 3rd Respondents repeatedly suspended the recruitment process, citing trade union actions and protests by the 6th Added-Respondent Union. The Petitioners contend that the failure to implement the SOR has left the department severely understaffed, with over 300 vacancies. This has forced the department to rely on untrained labour grade workers and retired employees to cover essential duties. The Petitioners argue that this inaction violates their right to equality before the law and equal protection of the law under Article 12(1) of the Constitution, as they have been denied promotions and subjected to excessive workloads while the department acquiesces to the demands of the 6th Added-Respondent Union. Issues Whether the non-implementation of the 2016 Scheme of Recruitment by the 1st to 3rd Respondents constitutes a violation of the Petitioners' right to equality before the law and equal protection of the law under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether there exist any legal impediments preventing the 1st to 3rd Respondents from implementing the 2016 Scheme of Recruitment. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 55(1), 55(3), 61B, 58(1) Railways Ordinance, No. 1, Section 37 Establishments Code, Chapter II, Section 2:1:1 Cases Referred to: Abeywickrama v. Pathirana - [1986/v1-sri-lr-120] C.W. Mackie and Co. Ltd v. Hugh Molagoda - [1986/v1-sri-lr-300] The Public Services United Nurses Union v. Montague Jayewickrema - [1988/v1-sri-lr-229] Best Footwear (Pvt) Ltd. v. Aboosally - [1997/v2-sri-lr-137] Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution; the criteria for discrimination established in C.W. Mackie and Co. Ltd v. Hugh Molagoda. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Petitioners were treated differently from other railway employees by being denied promotions and subjected to excessive workloads due to the failure to fill vacancies. The Respondents' decision to repeatedly suspend the recruitment process to appease the 6th Added-Respondent Union, while relying on untrained labour to perform technical duties, was found to be arbitrary and unreasonable. The court noted that the wage structure and grade of the Petitioners were legally established, and the Respondents' failure to maintain this structure by implementing the SOR resulted in a disadvantageous position for the Petitioners compared to their peers. Finding: The Petitioners' right to equality before the law and equal protection of the law under Article 12(1) was violated. Issue 2: Legal Impediments to Implementation Rule applied: Article 55(3) of the Constitution; Public Service Commission Procedural Rules; Establishments Code, Chapter II, Section 2:1:1. Court's reasoning: The court examined whether the 2016 SOR remained in force. It found that the SOR was duly signed by the Head of Department, the Secretary to the line Ministry, and the Secretary of the Public Service Commission. No subsequent amendment or replacement had been approved by the Public Service Commission. The court held that under the Establishments Code, the SOR continues to be in force until formally amended or replaced. The court concluded that there were no legal impediments to its implementation, and the previous challenges to the SOR by the Added-Respondents had been withdrawn. Finding: There were no legal impediments to the implementation of the 2016 Scheme of Recruitment. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing: the absence of legal impediments (Issue 2) confirms that the failure to implement the SOR was a discretionary, arbitrary act by the Respondents. This arbitrariness directly establishes the violation of the Petitioners' constitutional rights (Issue 1), as the Respondents prioritized the demands of one trade union over the legal requirements of the service and the rights of the Petitioners. Conclusion The application is allowed. The court declares that the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) have been violated and directs the 1st to 3rd Respondents to implement the 2016 Scheme of Recruitment forthwith, subject to budgetary constraints, and to report progress to the court by 30th November 2020. No compensation or costs are awarded. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Scheme of Recruitment — Railway Driver Assistants — Non-implementation of SOR — Public service employment — Trade union action — Railway safety — Administrative action		SC/FR/29/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_29_2018.pdf
sc-fr-288-2017	19 July 2020	Weerasinghe v. University of Colombo and Others	P. Padman Surasena, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, P.C., J; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Sexual harassment — Disciplinary proceedings — University Establishments Code — Interdiction — Reinstatement — Administrative action — Continuing infringement	Facts The Petitioner, Dr. W. M. P. N. Weerasinghe, served as the Head of the Department of Mass Media at the Sri Palee Campus of the University of Colombo and held the substantive post of Senior Lecturer (Grade II). In mid-February 2016, several academic staff members, including the 22nd and 23rd Respondents, submitted written complaints against the Petitioner alleging sexual harassment and abuse of authority. Following a preliminary investigation, the 1st Respondent University interdicted the Petitioner on 10-03-2016. A formal charge sheet was subsequently issued on 19-09-2016, alleging sexual harassment, discriminatory practices, and the creation of a hostile work environment. The Petitioner challenged his interdiction and the subsequent disciplinary proceedings, asserting that the complaints were fabricated, the interdiction violated the University Establishments Code, and the University failed to conclude the disciplinary inquiry within three months. Furthermore, the Petitioner contended that the University failed to reinstate him after one year of interdiction, as required by the Government Establishments Code. The Respondents raised a preliminary objection, arguing that the application was time-barred under Article 126(2) of the Constitution. Issues Whether the Petitionerâ€™s claims regarding his interdiction, the fabrication of complaints, and the failure to conclude the disciplinary inquiry within three months are time-barred under Article 126(2) of the Constitution. Whether the failure of the 1st Respondent University to reinstate the Petitioner after one year of interdiction constitutes an infringement of his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1), Article 126, Article 126(2) Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Time-bar of claims Rule applied: Article 126(2) of the Constitution, which requires fundamental rights applications to be filed within one month of the alleged infringement. Court's reasoning: The Court determined that the Petitionerâ€™s grievances regarding the alleged fabrication of complaints and the initial interdiction occurred on 10-03-2016. The grievance regarding the failure to conclude the disciplinary inquiry within three months matured upon the expiry of that period. As the application was filed on 15-08-2017, significantly beyond the one-month limit from the date of these events, these specific claims were not brought within the mandatory statutory timeframe. Finding: The preliminary objection was upheld; the claims regarding the interdiction, the alleged fabrication of evidence, and the delay in the disciplinary inquiry are time-barred. Issue 2: Failure to reinstate Rule applied: Public Administration Circular No. 06/2004 (1) dated 30-12-2011 [P 6 (c)] and Clause 18.3 of the University Grants Commission Establishments Code. Court's reasoning: The Court held that while the University Establishments Code was silent on the procedure for prolonged disciplinary inquiries, the Public Administration Circular No. 06/2004 (1) applied. However, this circular grants the disciplinary authority discretion to reinstate an officer after one year, rather than imposing an imperative duty. The Court noted that the University had a duty to protect the 22nd and 23rd Respondents, who were probationary lecturers testifying against the Petitioner. Given the nature of the allegations and the ongoing disciplinary inquiry, the Universityâ€™s decision not to reinstate the Petitioner was a reasonable exercise of its discretion. Finding: The failure to reinstate the Petitioner did not constitute an infringement of Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Interaction of Issues The Courtâ€™s finding that the majority of the Petitioner's claims were time-barred narrowed the scope of the judicial review to the singular issue of non-reinstatement. The resolution of the time-bar issue reinforced the Court's focus on the merits of the discretionary power exercised by the University, ultimately leading to the conclusion that no fundamental rights violation occurred. Conclusion The application is dismissed with costs. The Court found that the Petitioner failed to file his application within the prescribed time limit for most claims and failed to establish that the Universityâ€™s discretionary decision not to reinstate him violated his fundamental rights. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Sexual harassment — Disciplinary proceedings — University Establishments Code — Interdiction — Reinstatement — Administrative action — Continuing infringement		SC/FR/288/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_288_2017.pdf
sc-fr-459-2019	22 July 2020	Kasthuriarachchi v. Sri Lanka Medical Council and Others	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, CJ; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Medical registration — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Provisional registration — Medical Ordinance — ERPM examination — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Administrative action — Ultra vires — Foreign medical degrees	Facts The Petitioner, Denushi Vindya Kasthuriarachchi, obtained a medical degree from a foreign university recognized by the Sri Lanka Medical Council. Upon returning to Sri Lanka, the Petitioner sought approval from the 1st Respondent, the Sri Lanka Medical Council, to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine. The 1st Respondent refused to grant this approval, citing the Petitioner's failure to meet a pre-entry qualification requiring specific G.C.E. Advanced Level results, which the 1st Respondent had adopted in February 2010. The Petitioner challenged this refusal, arguing that the imposition of such a pre-entry qualification was ultra vires, unreasonable, and a violation of her fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. The 1st Respondent contended that it acted within its powers under the Medical Ordinance and further argued that the application was time-barred. Issues Whether the 1st Respondent acted ultra vires by imposing a pre-entry G.C.E. Advanced Level qualification for foreign medical graduates to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine. Whether the refusal to permit the Petitioner to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine constitutes a violation of the Petitioner's fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioner's application is time-barred under the rules governing Fundamental Rights jurisdiction. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 14(1)(g), 17, 126 Medical Ordinance, Section 29(2)(iii)(bb)(i), Section 29(2)(iii)(cc) General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018, Section 2(a), 2(c), 4 Cases Referred to: S.F. Zamrath v. Sri Lanka Medical Council - [sc-appeal-119/2019] S.M. Halpe v. Dr. Anil Jayasinghe - [sc-appeal-54/2019] Analysis Issue 1: Ultra Vires Nature of Pre-entry Qualification Rule applied: Section 29(2) of the Medical Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court reasoned that the Medical Ordinance empowers the 1st Respondent to recognize foreign universities and prescribe a special examination (the ERPM) for graduates. However, the statute does not grant the 1st Respondent the authority to prescribe pre-entry qualifications for foreign medical schools. The court noted that the Parliament, through the General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018, established a lower threshold for basic qualifications, indicating that the 1st Respondent cannot override legislative intent by imposing stricter, unauthorized requirements. Finding: The imposition of the pre-entry qualification was ultra vires and exceeded the 1st Respondent's statutory powers. Issue 2: Violation of Fundamental Rights Rule applied: Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Petitioner possessed the requisite degree from a recognized foreign university, satisfying the statutory requirements for provisional registration. By arbitrarily denying the Petitioner the opportunity to sit for the ERPM based on an ultra vires rule, the 1st Respondent infringed upon the Petitioner's right to equal protection of the law and her right to engage in a lawful profession. Finding: The 1st Respondent's conduct violated the Petitioner's fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g). Issue 3: Time-bar Argument Rule applied: Fundamental Rights jurisdiction time limits. Court's reasoning: The court determined that the refusal to grant approval for the ERPM constitutes a continuing infringement of the Petitioner's rights. Because the infringement persists as long as the Petitioner is denied the opportunity to practice, the argument that the application was filed out of time is invalid. Finding: The application is not time-barred. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected because the determination that the 1st Respondent acted ultra vires (Issue 1) directly establishes the basis for the fundamental rights violation (Issue 2). Furthermore, the characterization of the refusal as a "continuing infringement" (Issue 3) is predicated on the ongoing nature of the unlawful restriction imposed by the 1st Respondent. Conclusion The court held that the 1st Respondent acted arbitrarily and exceeded its legal authority. The 1st Respondent is ordered to permit the Petitioner to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Medical registration — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Provisional registration — Medical Ordinance — ERPM examination — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Administrative action — Ultra vires — Foreign medical degrees		SC/FR/459/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_459_2019.pdf
sc-fr-400-2019	22 July 2020	Rushdhaan and Others v. Sri Lanka Medical Council and Others	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Medical education — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Provisional registration — Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine (ERPM) — Medical Ordinance — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Arbitrary action — Ultra vires	Facts The Petitioners, A.L.M Rushdhaan, R.W.D.L.H. Rajasekara, M.L.M. Nasly, Dulanjana Nishamali de Silva, W.I. Madhushani, and Francis Vijitharan, are medical graduates from foreign universities. Upon returning to Sri Lanka, the Petitioners sought approval from the 1st Respondent, the Sri Lanka Medical Council (SLMC), to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine (ERPM). The SLMC refused to grant this approval, citing the Petitioners' failure to meet a pre-entry qualification requiring specific G.C.E. Advanced Level passes, a requirement adopted by the SLMC in February 2010. The Petitioners challenged this refusal, arguing that the SLMCâ€™s imposition of a pre-entry qualification was ultra vires, unreasonable, and a violation of their fundamental rights. They contended that they had complied with the requirements set forth in the Medical Ordinance and that the SLMC lacked the statutory authority to impose additional entry criteria for foreign medical education. The 1st Respondent maintained that it acted within its powers to regulate medical standards and argued that the applications were time-barred under the one-month rule for fundamental rights petitions. Issues Whether the imposition of a pre-entry G.C.E. Advanced Level qualification by the SLMC for foreign medical graduates is ultra vires the Medical Ordinance. Whether the refusal to allow the Petitioners to sit for the ERPM constitutes a continuing infringement of the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioners' applications are time-barred under the one-month rule for fundamental rights petitions. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 14(1)(g), 17, 126 Medical Ordinance, Section 29(2) General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018, Sections 2(a), 2(c), 4 Cases Referred to: S.F. Zamrath v. Sri Lanka Medical Council - [sc-appeal-119/2019] S.M. Halpe v. Dr. Anil Jayasinghe - [sc-appeal-54/2019] Analysis Issue 1: Ultra Vires Nature of Pre-entry Qualification Rule applied: Section 29(2) of the Medical Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Court held that the Medical Ordinance empowers the SLMC to recognize foreign medical degrees and prescribe a special examination (ERPM) for registration. However, the Ordinance does not grant the SLMC authority to dictate pre-entry educational qualifications for foreign universities. The Court noted that the Parliament, through the General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018, established different minimum standards, indicating that the SLMC cannot override legislative intent or the autonomy of foreign institutions regarding admission criteria. Finding: The imposition of the pre-entry qualification is ultra vires and beyond the powers granted to the SLMC by the Medical Ordinance. Issue 2: Infringement of Fundamental Rights Rule applied: Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The Court reasoned that the right to engage in a profession is protected under Article 14(1)(g), and the arbitrary denial of the opportunity to sit for the ERPM violates the principle of equal protection under Article 12(1). By imposing unauthorized criteria, the SLMC effectively usurped the legislative power of Parliament and infringed upon the Petitioners' legitimate expectations and professional prospects. Finding: The SLMCâ€™s conduct constitutes a violation of the Petitioners' fundamental rights. Issue 3: Time-bar Argument Rule applied: Fundamental Rights jurisdiction time limits. Court's reasoning: The Court rejected the Respondents' argument that the petitions were time-barred. It held that the refusal to grant approval for the ERPM constitutes a "continuing infringement" of the Petitioners' rights. As the infringement persists as long as the Petitioners are denied the right to sit for the examination, the one-month rule does not preclude the Court from granting relief. Finding: The applications are not time-barred. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected: because the SLMC acted ultra vires (Issue 1), its refusal to permit the Petitioners to sit for the ERPM was inherently arbitrary and unreasonable, directly leading to the infringement of fundamental rights (Issue 2). The determination that the infringement is "continuing" (Issue 3) is predicated on the fact that the SLMC's ultra vires policy remains in effect, thereby sustaining the violation of the Petitioners' rights. Conclusion The Court held that the SLMC acted arbitrarily and exceeded its statutory authority by imposing pre-entry qualifications not provided for in the Medical Ordinance. The 1st Respondent is ordered to permit the Petitioners to sit for the ERPM. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Medical education — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Provisional registration — Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine (ERPM) — Medical Ordinance — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Arbitrary action — Ultra vires		SC/FR/400/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_400_2019.pdf
sc-fr-399-2019	22 July 2020	Silva and Others v. Sri Lanka Medical Council and Others	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Medical registration — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Provisional registration — Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine (ERPM) — Medical Ordinance — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Arbitrary action — Ultra vires	Facts The Petitioners are medical graduates from various foreign universities who returned to Sri Lanka and sought approval from the Sri Lanka Medical Council to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine. The Sri Lanka Medical Council refused to grant this approval, citing the failure of the Petitioners to meet a pre-entry qualification requiring specific G.C.E. Advanced Level passes, which the Council had adopted in February 2010. The Petitioners challenged this refusal, arguing that the imposition of such a pre-entry qualification was ultra vires, unreasonable, and a violation of their fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. The Sri Lanka Medical Council maintained that it acted within its powers under the Medical Ordinance to regulate medical standards. The Petitioners contended that they had complied with all statutory requirements under the Medical Ordinance and that the Councilâ€™s decision effectively barred them from their chosen profession without legal authority. Issues Whether the imposition of a pre-entry G.C.E. Advanced Level qualification by the Sri Lanka Medical Council for foreign medical graduates is ultra vires the Medical Ordinance. Whether the refusal to permit the Petitioners to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine constitutes a violation of the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioners' application is time-barred under the rules governing Fundamental Rights jurisdiction. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 14(1)(g) Medical Ordinance, Section 29(2) General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018 Cases Referred to: S.F. Zamrath v. Sri Lanka Medical Council - [sc-fr-119/2019] S.M Halpe v. Dr. Anil Jayasinghe - [sc-fr-54/2019] Analysis Issue 1: Ultra vires nature of pre-entry qualifications Rule applied: Section 29(2) of the Medical Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Court reasoned that the Medical Ordinance empowers the Sri Lanka Medical Council to recognize foreign universities and prescribe a special examination (the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine) for graduates. However, the statute does not grant the Council the authority to impose pre-entry educational qualifications for admission into foreign universities. The Court noted that the Parliament, through the General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018, established different standards for basic qualifications, indicating that the Council cannot override legislative intent or impose restrictions not stipulated by the Medical Ordinance. Finding: The imposition of the pre-entry qualification is ultra vires the powers of the Sri Lanka Medical Council. Issue 2: Violation of Fundamental Rights Rule applied: Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The Court found that the Petitioners possess the requisite degrees from recognized foreign universities as per Section 29(2) of the Medical Ordinance. By arbitrarily imposing an unauthorized pre-entry requirement, the Sri Lanka Medical Council impeded the Petitioners' right to engage in their chosen profession and denied them the equal protection of the law. The Court emphasized that the Council cannot take away an entitlement granted by the law of the land. Finding: The conduct of the Sri Lanka Medical Council constitutes a violation of the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g). Issue 3: Time-bar argument Rule applied: Fundamental Rights jurisdiction time limits. Court's reasoning: The Court rejected the argument that the application was time-barred. It held that the refusal to grant approval for the examination constitutes a "continuing infringement" of the Petitioners' fundamental rights, as the denial of their right to practice persists as long as they are barred from the examination. Finding: The application is not time-barred. Interaction of Issues The findings are interdependent; because the imposition of the pre-entry qualification was found to be ultra vires (Issue 1), the resulting denial of the Petitioners' right to sit for the examination was necessarily arbitrary and unconstitutional (Issue 2). The determination that the infringement is "continuing" (Issue 3) reinforces the conclusion that the Councilâ€™s ongoing reliance on an invalid regulation maintains the violation of the Petitioners' rights. Conclusion The Court held that the Sri Lanka Medical Council acted arbitrarily and exceeded its legal authority. The Court ordered the 1st Respondent to permit the Petitioners to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Medical registration — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Provisional registration — Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine (ERPM) — Medical Ordinance — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Arbitrary action — Ultra vires		SC/FR/399/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_399_2019.pdf
sc-fr-442-2019	22 July 2020	Jayathilaka Dissanayake v. Sri Lanka Medical Council and Others	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Medical Ordinance — Provisional registration — Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine (ERPM) — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Ultra vires — Pre-entry qualification — Foreign medical graduates	Facts The Petitioner, Shamini Jayathilaka Dissanayake, obtained a medical degree from a foreign university recognized by the 1st Respondent, the Sri Lanka Medical Council. Upon returning to Sri Lanka, the Petitioner sought approval from the 1st Respondent to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine. The 1st Respondent refused to grant this approval, citing the Petitioner's failure to meet a pre-entry qualification requiring specific G.C.E. Advanced Level results, a requirement adopted by the 1st Respondent in February 2010. The Petitioner challenged this refusal, arguing that the 1st Respondent acted in an arbitrary, capricious, and ultra vires manner by imposing a pre-entry qualification not supported by the Medical Ordinance. The Petitioner contended that this conduct violated her fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. The 1st Respondent maintained that it possessed the authority to set such standards and further argued that the application was time-barred under the rules governing fundamental rights jurisdiction. Issues Whether the 1st Respondentâ€™s imposition of a pre-entry G.C.E. Advanced Level qualification for foreign medical graduates is ultra vires the Medical Ordinance. Whether the 1st Respondentâ€™s refusal to permit the Petitioner to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine constitutes a continuing infringement of the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitionerâ€™s application is time-barred under the rules of the Fundamental Rights jurisdiction. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 14(1)(g), 17, 126 Medical Ordinance, Section 29(2) General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018, Sections 2(a), 2(c), 4 Cases Referred to: S.F. Zamrath v. Sri Lanka Medical Council - [sc-fr-119/2019] S.M. Halpe v. Dr. Anil Jayasinghe - [sc-fr-54/2019] Analysis Issue 1: Ultra vires nature of pre-entry qualifications Rule applied: Section 29(2) of the Medical Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Court reasoned that the Medical Ordinance empowers the 1st Respondent to recognize foreign universities and prescribe a special examination (the ERPM) for graduates. However, the statute does not grant the 1st Respondent the power to dictate pre-entry qualifications for foreign medical schools. The Court noted that the Parliament, through the General Sir John Kotelawala Defence University (Special Provisions) Act No. 17 of 2018, indicated that the minimum qualification for medical education is a simple pass in three subjects, and the 1st Respondent cannot override this legislative intent by imposing stricter, unauthorized requirements. Finding: The imposition of the pre-entry qualification is ultra vires the powers of the 1st Respondent. Issue 2: Infringement of Fundamental Rights Rule applied: Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The Court found that the 1st Respondentâ€™s arbitrary refusal to allow the Petitioner to sit for the ERPM, based on an ultra vires rule, directly impeded the Petitionerâ€™s right to engage in her chosen profession. By usurping legislative power and creating barriers not found in the Medical Ordinance, the 1st Respondent denied the Petitioner the equal protection of the law. Finding: The 1st Respondentâ€™s conduct constitutes a violation of the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental rights. Issue 3: Time-bar argument Rule applied: Fundamental Rights jurisdiction time limits. Court's reasoning: The Court rejected the 1st Respondentâ€™s argument that the application was time-barred. It held that the refusal to grant approval for the ERPM constitutes a "continuing infringement" of the Petitioner's rights. As the infringement persists as long as the Petitioner is denied the opportunity to sit for the examination, the application is not out of time. Finding: The application is not time-barred. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected; the determination that the 1st Respondent acted ultra vires (Issue 1) serves as the foundation for the conclusion that the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental rights were infringed (Issue 2). Furthermore, the characterization of the infringement as "continuing" (Issue 2) directly resolves the procedural objection regarding the time-bar (Issue 3), as a continuing wrong prevents the expiration of the limitation period. Conclusion The Court held that the 1st Respondent infringed the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental rights under Articles 12(1) and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. The 1st Respondent is ordered to permit the Petitioner to sit for the Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Sri Lanka Medical Council — Medical Ordinance — Provisional registration — Examination for Registration to Practise Medicine (ERPM) — Legitimate expectation — Natural justice — Ultra vires — Pre-entry qualification — Foreign medical graduates		SC/FR/442/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_442_2019.pdf
sc-appeal-59A-2006	28 July 2020	Karunanayake v. Weerawansa	E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Motor vehicle possession — Entitlement to possession — Bona fide purchaser	Facts The Plaintiff-Respondent-Petitioner-Respondent (Plaintiff) instituted a defamation action against the Defendant-Petitioner-Respondent-Petitioner (Defendant) in the District Court of Colombo. The Defendant filed an answer and subsequently moved to amend it. The District Court fixed the inquiry regarding the amended answer for 06.01.2003. The Defendant failed to appear or tender written submissions on that date, claiming his counsel had erroneously noted the date as 06.02.2003. Consequently, the District Judge rejected the amended answer and fixed the matter for trial on the original answer. The Defendant filed an application in the District Court to vacate the order of 06.01.2003, citing the mistake of counsel. The District Judge, acting under Section 839 of the Civil Procedure Code, set aside the order and re-fixed the inquiry. The Plaintiff appealed this decision to the Court of Appeal. The Court of Appeal allowed the Plaintiff's appeal, setting aside the District Judge's order on the basis that the court lacked jurisdiction to vacate its own order under Section 839 in these circumstances and that the evidence provided by the Defendant was insufficient and legally flawed. The Defendant then appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the District Court has the jurisdiction under Section 839 of the Civil Procedure Code to vacate an order made in default of appearance where no specific provision in the Code provides for such relief. Whether the affidavits submitted by the Defendant to explain the default were legally valid. Whether the Defendant established that the default was due to a bona fide mistake rather than negligence. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 128 Civil Procedure Code, Sections 86(2), 87(3), 93(1), 93(2), 168, 189, 403, 437, 438, 439, 666, 839 Oaths and Affirmation Ordinance, Section 5 Cases Referred to: Leechman & Company Ltd. v. Rangalla Consolidated Limited - [1981/v2-sri-lr-373] Victor de Silva v. Jinadasa de Silva - [68-nlr-45] Ramasamy Pulle v. De Silva - [12-nlr-298] Mohideen v. Carder - [3-c.l.r.13] Deonis v. Samarasinghe - [15-nlr-39] Caldera v. Santiagopillai - [22-nlr-155] Sayadoo Mohamado v. Maula Abubakkar - [28-nlr-58] Muttiah v. Muttusamy - [1-nlr-25] Gargial v. Somasundaram Chetty - [9-nlr-26] Lokumenika v. Selenduhamy - [48-nlr-353] Andradie v. Jayasekara Perera - [1985/v2-sri-lr-204] Galigamuwa v. Air Lanka Ltd. - [1993/v1-sri-lr-411] De Fonseka v. Dharmawardena - [1994/v3-sri-lr-49] Senaviratne v. Francis Fonseka Abeykoon - [1986/v2-sri-lr-1] Sirinivaso Thero v. Sudassi Thero - [63-nlr-31] Ittepana v. Hemawathie - [1981/v1-sri-lr-476] Paulusz v. Perera - [34-nlr-438] Hukum Chand Boid v. Kamalanand Singh - [33-cal-927] Karunaratne v. Mohideen - [43-nlr-102] Kamala v. Andris - [41-nlr-71] Jeyaraj Fernandopulle v. Premachandra de Silva - [1996/v1-sri-lr-70] Hettiarachchi v. Senaviratne - [1994/v3-sri-lr-293] Wijesinghe v. Uluwita - [34-nlr-362] Huddersfield Police Authority v. Watson - [1947/1-all-e-r-193] Alasupillai v. Yavetpillai - [39-clw-107] All Ceylon Commercial and Industrial Workers Union - [1995/v2-sri-lr-295] Gunasena v. Bandarathilake - [2000/v1-sri-lr-292] The King v. Baron Silva - [4-times-of-ceylon-reports-3] The Police Officer of Mawalla v. Galapatha - [1-c-w-r-197] V.A. Ranmenika v. B.A.S. Tissera - [65-nlr-214] Kariyawasam v. Priyadarshani - [2004/v1-sri-lr-189] De Silva v. L.B. Finance Ltd. - [1993/v1-sri-lr-371] Clifford Ratwatte v. Thilanga Sumathipala - [2001/v2-sri-lr-55] Pan Asia Bank v. Kandy Multipurpose Co-operative Society - [2005/v2-sri-lr-211] Multi-Purpose Co-operative Society, Madawachchiya v. Kirimudiyanse - [2011/v1-sri-lr-135] Chandrawathie v. Dharmaratne - [2001-blr] Inaya v. Orix Leasing Co. Ltd. - [1993/v3-sri-lr-197] Facy v. Sanoon - [2003/v3-sri-lr-8] Mohomad v. Jayaratne - [2002/v3-sri-lr-181] Kumarasiri v. Rajapaksha - [2006/v1-sri-lr-359] Weeraman v. Sadacharan - [2002/v3-sri-lr-222] Mark Rajendran v. First Capital Ltd. - [2010/v1-sri-lr-60] Fernando v. Ceylon Breweries Ltd. - [1998/v3-sri-lr-61] The Ceylon Breweries Ltd. v. Jax Fernando - [2001/v1-sri-lr-270] Punchihamy v. Rambukpotha - [16-times-law-reports-119] Kathiresu v. Sinnaiah - [71-nlr-450] Pakir Mohideen v. Mohammadu Casim - [4-nlr-299] Daniel v. Chandradeva - [1994/v2-sri-lr-1] Rankira v. Silindu - [10-nlr-376] Julius v. Hodgson - [11-nlr-25] Sanjeewa v. Piyatissa - [2006/v1-sri-lr-241] Packiyanathan v. Singarajah - [1991/v2-sri-lr-205] Analysis Issue 1: Jurisdiction under Section 839 Rule applied: Section 839 of the Civil Procedure Code (Inherent powers). Court's reasoning: The court held that Section 839 is not limited by the expressio unius est exclusio alterius rule. While the Code is not exhaustive, Section 839 allows courts to exercise inherent powers to meet the ends of justice and prevent abuse of process where no specific provision exists. The court clarified that while a court cannot create new jurisdiction, it can use inherent powers to vacate ex parte orders or orders made per incuriam to rectify injustice. Finding: The District Court possesses the jurisdiction to vacate its own orders under Section 839 in appropriate circumstances, notwithstanding the absence of specific statutory provisions for such defaults. Issue 2: Validity of Affidavits Rule applied: Sections 437, 438, and 439 of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The court reviewed conflicting precedents regarding the validity of jurats. It concluded that the absence of the word "affirm" in the jurat is not fatal if the affidavit as a whole, including the recital, demonstrates the deponent's intention to solemnly declare the truth. The court found that the affidavits in question sufficiently manifested this intention. Finding: The affidavits were legally valid. Issue 3: Proof of Bona Fide Mistake Rule applied: Principles of professional diligence and burden of proof. Court's reasoning: The court held that the burden of proving a "genuine mistake" rested on the Defendant. The Defendant failed to produce diary entries for the correct date (06.01.2003) and failed to show that the Registered Attorney acted with due diligence. The court emphasized that a client must suffer for the negligence of their legal representatives. Finding: The Defendant failed to prove that the default was a bona fide mistake; it was instead a result of negligence. Interaction of Issues The findings on the first two issues (jurisdiction and affidavit validity) favored the Defendant, but the failure to prove the factual basis for the default (Issue 3) rendered the relief under Section 839 unavailable. The court's inherent power is discretionary and contingent upon the applicant demonstrating that the default was excusable. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. Although the Court of Appeal misstated the law regarding the District Court's inherent jurisdiction and the validity of the affidavits, its final conclusion to set aside the District Court's order was correct because the Defendant failed to prove that the default was a bona fide mistake. Keywords Motor vehicle possession — Entitlement to possession — Bona fide purchaser		SC/APPEAL/59A/2006	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_59a_2006.pdf
sc-fr-451-2016	28 July 2020	Hemakumara v. Gunasekara and Others	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Article 12(1) of the Constitution — Police reservist — Back wages — Active service — Disciplinary inquiry — Time bar — Re-instatement in service — Violation of fundamental rights	Facts The Petitioner, Batuwana Dewage Lionel Hemakumara, joined the Sri Lanka Police Reserve as a Reserve Sub-Inspector in 1990. In 1999, he was arrested in connection with an alleged murder, leading to the immediate suspension of his services. He was indicted before the High Court in 2007 and subsequently acquitted in 2011. Following his acquittal, the Petitioner requested reinstatement. The police conducted a preliminary investigation and served a charge sheet on the Petitioner in 2013. A formal disciplinary inquiry was held, but the Petitioner was not informed of the outcome. In 2015, the Petitioner was summoned for another disciplinary inquiry regarding the same charge sheet, which was later postponed indefinitely. The Petitioner filed a previous fundamental rights application, SC/FR 01/2016, challenging the second inquiry. While that matter was pending, the Petitioner was informed by a letter dated 22-08-2016 that he was reinstated in service effective 19-08-2016, subject to conditions. The Petitioner reported for duty and subsequently appealed for back wages for the period of his suspension. This appeal was rejected by a letter dated 27-09-2016. The Petitioner then filed the instant application, alleging that the denial of back wages violated his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The Respondents raised a preliminary objection that the application was time-barred and argued that, as a reservist, the Petitioner was not entitled to wages for the period he was not in active service. Issues Whether the Petitionerâ€™s application is time-barred under Article 126(2) of the Constitution. Whether the denial of back wages to the Petitioner, a police reservist, constitutes a violation of his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1), Article 126(2) Termination of Employment of Workmen (Special Provisions) Act Cases Referred to: Muthuweeran v. The State - [5-srikanthas-law-reports-123] Namasivayam v. Gunawardene - [1989/v1-sri-lr-394] Edirisuriya v. Navaratnam - [1985/v1-sri-lr-100] Gamaethige v. Siriwardene - [1988/v1-sri-lr-384] Demuni de Zoysa v. Dissanayake - [sc-fr-206/2008] Kegalle Plantations Ltd. v. Silva - [1996/v2-sri-lr-180] Hapuarachchi v. Commissioner of Elections - [2009/v1-sri-lr-1] Coats Thread Lanka (Pvt) Ltd v. Samarasundara - [2010/v2-sri-lr-1] Hanley v. Pease and Partners - [1915/v1-kb-698] Seneviratne v. Inspector General of Police - [sc-fr-396/2010] Analysis Issue 1: Time-bar under Article 126(2) Rule applied: Article 126(2) of the Constitution, which mandates that a fundamental rights application must be filed within one month of the alleged infringement. Court's reasoning: The Court held that the one-month rule is mandatory and that pursuing administrative appeals does not interrupt the running of this time limit. The Petitioner became aware of the denial of back wages upon receipt of the reinstatement letter (P12), which explicitly stated he was not entitled to payments for the period of suspension. The Petitionerâ€™s subsequent appeal (P13) and the response (P14) did not reset the time limit. Finding: The application is time-barred. Issue 2: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution (Right to Equality). Court's reasoning: The Court distinguished the Petitionerâ€™s status as a police reservist from that of a regular police officer. Reservists were governed by specific circulars (e.g., 1044/92) and were paid based on active service, unlike the regular force. The Court found that the Petitioner was not in active service during his suspension and that the Respondents provided valid reasons for the denial of back wages based on the definition of "active service" provided by the Public Service Commission. The Court distinguished the cited precedents (Coats Thread and Seneviratne) on the basis that they involved different employment terms or different procedural contexts. Finding: The Petitioner failed to establish discrimination or a violation of Article 12(1). Interaction of Issues The Courtâ€™s finding that the application was time-barred provided a sufficient basis to dismiss the petition in limine. However, the Court addressed the merits to ensure justice, ultimately finding that the Petitionerâ€™s substantive claims regarding his entitlement to back wages were also without merit, thereby reinforcing the dismissal. Conclusion The preliminary objection regarding the time-bar is upheld, and the Petitioner has failed to establish a violation of his fundamental rights. The application is dismissed. Keywords Fundamental rights — Article 12(1) of the Constitution — Police reservist — Back wages — Active service — Disciplinary inquiry — Time bar — Re-instatement in service — Violation of fundamental rights		SC/FR/451/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_451_2016.pdf
sc-fr-551-2012	30 July 2020	De Silva v. Public Service Commission and Others	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Time bar — Article 126 of the Constitution — Public Service Commission — Sri Lanka Engineering Service — Acting appointment — Promotion — Circular No. 31 — Engineering Service	Facts P.U.P.K. De Silva, the Petitioner, was initially employed as a Technical Officer in the Local Government Service and was later absorbed into the Sri Lanka Technical Services. Following a previous fundamental rights application, the Petitioner was reinstated with seniority and eventually promoted to the special grade of the Sri Lanka Technical Services. In 2006, the Petitioner was assigned to perform acting duties as an electrical engineer within the Southern Provincial Council. The Petitioner sought to be made permanent in this engineering position, claiming entitlement under Engineering Service Circular No. 31. In August 2012, the Petitioner was notified that the acting allowance for his cover-up duties would be terminated, as a permanent engineer had been appointed to the post. The Petitioner filed the present application in September 2012, alleging that the failure to grant him a permanent appointment and the subsequent termination of his acting allowance violated his fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The Respondents raised preliminary objections, asserting that the application was time-barred, that necessary parties were absent, and that the petition failed to comply with Supreme Court rules. Issues Whether the application was filed within the mandatory one-month time limit prescribed by Article 126(2) of the Constitution. Whether the Petitioner possessed the requisite qualifications under Engineering Service Circular No. 31 to be appointed to the Sri Lanka Engineering Service Class II Grade II. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1), 12(2), 14(1)(g), 17, 126(2). Cases Referred to: Demuni Sriyani De Soyza v Dharmasena Dissanayake - [sc-appeal-206/2008] Edirisuriya v Navaratnam - [1985/v1-sri-lr-100] Lewla Thiththapajjalage Illangaratne v Kandy Municipal Council - [1995-balj-vol-vi-part-1] Siriwardene v Rodrigo - [original citation as in decision_text] Sri Lanka Nidahas Rubber Inspectors Union v R. B. Premadasa - [sc-appeal-109/2005] Analysis Issue 1: Time bar under Article 126(2) Rule applied: Article 126(2) of the Constitution, which mandates that an application for the infringement of fundamental rights must be filed within one month of the alleged violation. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the Petitionerâ€™s grievance regarding the termination of his acting allowance was linked to the appointment of a permanent engineer who assumed duties on August 8, 2012. The Petitioner filed his application on September 19, 2012, which exceeded the one-month period. The court rejected the Petitioner's attempt to rely on the date of the notification letter (August 20, 2012) in isolation, noting that the Petitioner should have been aware of the infringement when the permanent appointee assumed duties on August 8, 2012. Finding: The application was filed out of time and is therefore unmaintainable. Issue 2: Qualification for permanent appointment Rule applied: Engineering Service Circular No. 31 dated 5/8/1997. Court's reasoning: The court examined the mandatory requirements for promotion from the Sri Lanka Technical Services to the Sri Lanka Engineering Service as set out in Circular P7. The Petitioner failed to provide evidence that he had successfully completed the required examinations, specifically the 2nd examination for Technical Officers. The court further noted that acting appointments and associated allowances are privileges, not rights, and the Petitioner had no legal entitlement to be made permanent without fulfilling the prescribed criteria. Finding: The Petitioner failed to establish that he met the mandatory qualifications for the permanent position. Interaction of Issues The court's finding that the application was time-barred precluded it from exercising jurisdiction to grant the substantive relief sought. However, the court addressed the merits of the qualification issue to demonstrate that, even if the application had been timely, the Petitioner lacked the necessary credentials to succeed in his claim for a permanent appointment. Conclusion The preliminary objection regarding the time bar is upheld, and the application is dismissed for being unmaintainable under Article 126 of the Constitution. No costs are ordered. Keywords Fundamental rights — Time bar — Article 126 of the Constitution — Public Service Commission — Sri Lanka Engineering Service — Acting appointment — Promotion — Circular No. 31 — Engineering Service		SC/FR/551/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_551_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-25-2017	31 July 2020	K. Sivasamy v. D.L.Hema Malini de Silva	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J.; L. T. B. Dehideniya, J.; Murdu N. B. Fernando, PC, J.	Industrial Disputes Act — unlawful termination — compensation — Labour Tribunal — burden of proof — evidence evaluation — just and equitable — reinstatement — misconduct — age of employee — alternative employment — financial loss — judicial discretion — appeal	Facts The case revolves around an appeal concerning the unlawful termination of K. Sivasamy, who had been employed by D.L.Hema Malini de Silva since 1977. His employment was terminated on December 10, 2004, based on allegations of arson and theft. Following his termination, Sivasamy filed a complaint with the Labour Tribunal, asserting that his dismissal was unjustified. The Labour Tribunal initially suspended proceedings pending the outcome of criminal charges against him, which resulted in his acquittal in 2011. The Tribunal later ruled in favor of Sivasamy, awarding him Rs. 390,000 as compensation, equivalent to five years' salary. The employer appealed this decision to the High Court, which upheld the Tribunal's ruling. The employer subsequently sought special leave to appeal to the Supreme Court, questioning whether the Labour Tribunal's compensation award was contrary to the evidence presented. Issues Whether the Labour Tribunal erred in awarding compensation contrary to the evidence on record? Whether the compensation awarded was excessive? Applicable Law Statutes: Industrial Disputes Act, No. 43 of 1950 (as amended) Cases Referred to: Ceylon Transport Board v. Wijeratne [1975] 77 NLR 481; Merril J. Fernando & Co. v. Deiman Singho [1988] 2 SLR 242; Ceylon University Clerical and Technical Association v. University of Ceylon [1968] 72 NLR 84; Hatton National Bank v. Perera [1996] 2 SLR 231; Silva v. Kuruppu (SC 182/69, SC Minutes dated 14/10/71). Analysis Issue 1: Whether the Labour Tribunal erred in awarding compensation contrary to the evidence on record? Rule applied: The Labour Tribunal's duty to evaluate evidence and determine compensation under the Industrial Disputes Act. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court examined the evidence presented before the Labour Tribunal, particularly focusing on the daily wage of Rs. 250 claimed by Sivasamy. The employer contended that the Tribunal had erred in accepting this figure, citing discrepancies in Sivasamy's testimony regarding his wages. However, the Court noted that the employer failed to challenge Sivasamy's claims during cross-examination and did not provide any documentation to substantiate their assertions about his wages. The Court emphasized that the burden of proof lies with the party making the claim, and in this case, Sivasamy's consistent testimony regarding his daily wage was deemed credible. The Tribunal's acceptance of this evidence was justified, as it was not contradicted by the employer. Furthermore, the Court highlighted the importance of considering the context of the evidence, especially given the significant time lapse since the termination. Finding: The Supreme Court concluded that the Labour Tribunal did not err in its assessment of the evidence and that the compensation awarded was based on a proper evaluation of the facts. Issue 2: Whether the compensation awarded was excessive? Rule applied: The criteria for determining compensation under the Industrial Disputes Act, considering factors such as the nature of employment, length of service, and the circumstances of termination. Court's reasoning: The Court analyzed the factors that should be considered when determining the quantum of compensation. It acknowledged the employer's argument referencing the Ceylon Transport Board v. Wijeratne case, which suggested that compensation should not exceed three years' salary.
