However, the Supreme Court clarified that while this precedent exists, it does not impose a strict ceiling on compensation. The Court noted that each case must be evaluated on its own merits, taking into account the specific circumstances surrounding the termination. In this instance, the Court found that the Labour Tribunal had appropriately considered Sivasamy's age, length of service, and the impact of the unlawful termination on his future employment prospects. The Court also recognized the adverse effects of the pending criminal proceedings on Sivasamy's ability to secure alternative employment. Finding: The Supreme Court determined that the compensation awarded was not excessive, given the unique circumstances of the case. Interaction of Issues The issues of whether the Labour Tribunal erred in its compensation award and whether the amount was excessive are interrelated. The Court's analysis of the evidence directly influenced its determination of the appropriateness of the compensation awarded. The credibility of Sivasamy's claims and the employer's failure to provide counter-evidence were pivotal in establishing the legitimacy of the Tribunal's decision. Conclusion The Supreme Court dismissed the appeal, affirming the Labour Tribunal's ruling and the High Court's decision. The Court ordered the employer to pay Rs. 100,000 in costs within three months, emphasizing the need for just and equitable treatment in employment disputes. The judgment underscored the importance of thorough evidence evaluation and the necessity of considering the broader implications of unlawful termination on employees' livelihoods. Keywords Industrial Disputes Act — unlawful termination — compensation — Labour Tribunal — burden of proof — evidence evaluation — just and equitable — reinstatement — misconduct — age of employee — alternative employment — financial loss — judicial discretion — appeal	Ranga Dayananda for the Respondent-Appellant-Appellant; Kamal Suneth Perera for the Applicant-Respondent-Respondent	SC/APPEAL/25/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_25_2017.pdf
sc-fr-40-2019	3 August 2020	Dangolla and Wijenayaka and Others v. Aviruppola and Others	L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — School admission — Difficult school categorization — Zonal Director of Education certification — Documentary evidence — Compliance with circulars — Administrative action — Negligence in submitting documents	Facts Iresha Dulashini Dangolla and Kandabadage Don Nadeera Wijenayaka, the parents of a minor child, applied for the child's admission to Visaka Vidyalaya, Colombo. The application was submitted under the category for children of staff members in institutions involved in school education. The Petitioners sought additional marks based on the 1st Petitionerâ€™s service as a teacher at A/ Habarana Maha Vidyalaya, which they claimed was a difficult school under circular 24/2018. The Petitioners initially submitted a document from the Zonal Director of Education, Kekirawa, which certified the 1st Petitioner's service but only described the school's geographical location as a difficult division rather than certifying the school itself as a difficult school. The interview board rejected this as insufficient proof. The Petitioners subsequently appealed, submitting a second letter from the same Zonal Director of Education that explicitly categorized the school as a difficult school. The appeal board refused to consider this new document, citing a policy that prohibits the consideration of documents not tendered at the original interview. The Petitioners alleged that the denial of admission constituted an infringement of their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Issues Whether the refusal of the interview board and the appeal board to grant additional marks for service in a "difficult school" violated the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the document marked P4, submitted during the appeal stage, constituted valid proof of the school's status as a "difficult school" under the relevant circular. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 17, and 126. Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Fundamental Rights Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution regarding the right to equality and the requirements of circular 24/2018. Court's reasoning: The court reasoned that the 1st Petitioner, being a teacher, was expected to be familiar with the requirements of the Education Department's circulars. The Petitioners failed to provide the necessary certification at the initial interview. The court found that the 1st Respondent, as Principal, acted in accordance with the established procedure by relying on the official list of difficult schools (1R3) provided by the Ministry of Education, which did not include A/ Habarana Maha Vidyalaya. Finding: There was no violation of the Petitioners' fundamental rights under Article 12(1) as the school authorities acted reasonably and in accordance with the circular. Issue 2: Validity of Document P4 Rule applied: The procedural requirement under the school admission circular that limits the appeal board's consideration to documents tendered at the original interview. Court's reasoning: The court noted that document P4 lacked a reference number and the Zonal Director of Education could not trace its issuance in official records. Conversely, official records (R3 and R3A) confirmed that A/ Habarana Maha Vidyalaya was categorized as a "more convenient school" rather than a "difficult school." The court held that the appeal board correctly excluded P4 because it was not produced at the initial interview and because its contents were contradicted by official Ministry records. Finding: Document P4 was unreliable and insufficient to establish the school's status as a "difficult school." Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing. The failure to produce a valid document at the initial interview (Issue 1) was compounded by the fact that the document later produced (Issue 2) was substantively inaccurate and procedurally inadmissible. The courtâ€™s determination that the school was not officially categorized as "difficult" rendered the Petitioners' claim for additional marks legally unsustainable, thereby negating the allegation of a fundamental rights violation. Conclusion The court found no merit in the Petitioners' application as they failed to comply with the mandatory documentation requirements and the school was not categorized as a difficult school. The application is dismissed. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — School admission — Difficult school categorization — Zonal Director of Education certification — Documentary evidence — Compliance with circulars — Administrative action — Negligence in submitting documents		SC/FR/40/2019	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_40_2019.pdf
sc-appeal-123-2012	6 August 2020	Nanayakkara v. Kyuma and Others	P. Padman Surasena, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, P.C. J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; P. Padman Surasena, J.	Appeal against acquittal — Manifestly erroneous in law — Medical evidence — Assessment of evidence — Appreciation of evidence — Discrepancies in witness statements — Assault — Wrongful confinement — Robbery — Mischief	Facts The Accused-Appellant, Dehigaspe Patabandige Nishantha Nanayakkara, was charged in the Magistrates Court of Kesbewa with seven counts, including assault, wrongful confinement, criminal force, robbery, and abetment to commit mischief. These charges arose from an incident on 08-12-1999 at the office of Ceylon M. K. N. Ecopower (Pvt) Ltd, where the Appellant and the complainant, Yukinara Kyuma, were directors. The prosecution alleged that the Appellant assaulted Yukinara Kyuma, abetted the assault of Kyoko Kyuma, wrongfully confined Yukinara Kyuma, used criminal force, and abetted the robbery of a camera and mischief to a door. The prosecution led evidence from Yukinara Kyuma, Kyoko Kyuma, and two police officers. The Appellant testified in his own defense, admitting that he used force to remove Yukinara Kyuma from the door of a locked room but denied the specific allegations of assault and robbery. Two defense witnesses also testified, confirming that the Appellant instructed others to open the door and that force was used during the incident. The Magistrate acquitted the Appellant of all charges. The Aggrieved Party appealed to the Provincial High Court of Panadura, which set aside the acquittal and ordered a re-trial. The Appellant appealed this decision to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the judgment of the High Court setting aside the order of acquittal was manifestly erroneous in law, particularly regarding the evaluation of medical evidence and the history provided to the Medical Officer. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 Cases Referred to: Bandaranaike v. Jagathsena - [1984/v2-sri-lr-397] State of Uttar Pradesh v. M K Anthony - [A I R (1985) SC 48] Analysis Issue 1: Perversity of the Magistrate's acquittal Rule applied: The court applied the principle that a trial judge must evaluate evidence as a whole, considering whether discrepancies are due to dishonesty or limited powers of observation, rather than adopting a hyper-technical approach. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court found that the Magistrateâ€™s acquittal was perverse because he failed to assess the evidence in its entirety. The Magistrate ignored the Appellantâ€™s own admissions regarding the use of force and the corroboration provided by medical evidence (MLR P4), which documented injuries consistent with the prosecution's account. The Magistrateâ€™s rejection of the evidence based on minor discrepancies between the witnesses was inconsistent with the legal standard that minor variations in testimony do not necessarily render evidence unworthy of belief. Furthermore, the Magistrateâ€™s conclusion that the prosecution failed to prove assault was contradicted by his own finding that an incident occurred where the victim could have been injured. Finding: The High Courtâ€™s decision to set aside the acquittal was not perverse; the Magistrateâ€™s original judgment was based on an unrealistic and flawed evaluation of the evidence. Conclusion The Supreme Court answered the question of law in the negative, finding that the High Courtâ€™s judgment was not erroneous. The appeal is dismissed, and the order for a re-trial stands. Keywords Appeal against acquittal — Manifestly erroneous in law — Medical evidence — Assessment of evidence — Appreciation of evidence — Discrepancies in witness statements — Assault — Wrongful confinement — Robbery — Mischief		SC/APPEAL/123/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_123_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-52-2014	6 August 2020	Sascon Knitting Company (Pvt) Ltd v. Commissioner General of Labour and Others	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Priyantha Jayawardene, PC, J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Termination of employment — Transfer of employees — Associate company — Commissioner General of Labour — Jurisdiction of Commissioner — Writ application — Delay — Fundamental rights — Contract of employment	Facts The Appellant, Sascon Knitting Company (Pvt) Ltd., operated textile factories in Ekala and Colombo. The 3rd to 53rd Respondents were employed as machine operators at the Appellant's Ekala factory. In 2006, the Appellant directed these employees to report for work at a premises on Baseline Road, Colombo, where an entity named San Fashions operated. The employees refused the transfer, asserting that the Appellant did not operate a factory at that location and that they were not willing to accept employment at a different company. The Appellant maintained that the transfer was permitted under the terms of the employees' contracts, which allowed for attachment to associate companies. The employees, through the 2nd Respondent trade union, complained to the 1st Respondent, the Commissioner General of Labour, alleging that their services had been terminated. The 1st Respondent conducted inquiries and an on-site inspection, which confirmed that the Appellant did not have a factory at the Baseline Road premises and that San Fashions was a separate legal entity. Consequently, the 1st Respondent ordered the Appellant to pay compensation to the employees for unlawful termination under the Termination of Employment of Workmen (Special Provisions) Act. The Appellant challenged these orders in the Court of Appeal via a Writ Application, which was dismissed. The Appellant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Appellant had the contractual right to transfer employees from one company to another (specifically from Sascon Knitting to San Fashions). Whether the Commissioner General of Labour had jurisdiction under the Termination of Employment of Workmen (Special Provisions) Act No. 45 of 1971 to make orders regarding the employees' separation. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in determining that the Appellantâ€™s application for judicial review was barred by undue delay. Applicable Law Statutes: Termination of Employment of Workmen (Special Provisions) Act No. 45 of 1971 (as amended by Law No. 04 of 1976) Cases Referred to: Hassan v. Fairline Garments International Ltd. - [1989/v2-sri-lr-137] Ceylon Estate Staffs Union v. The Superintendent Meddecombra Estate, Watagoda - [73-nlr-278] Salomon v. Salomon and Co. Ltd. - [1897] AC 22 Trade Exchange (Ceylon) Ltd. v. Asian Hotels Corporation - [1981/v1-sri-lr-67] Biso Manika v. Cyril de Alwis - [1982/v1-sri-lr-368] PS Bus Co. Ltd v. Members and Secretary of the Ceylon Transport Board - [61-nlr-491] Analysis Issue 1: Contractual right to transfer Rule applied: The principle of separate legal personality of companies (Salomon v. Salomon and Co. Ltd.) and the scope of employment contracts. Court's reasoning: The Court held that a company is a distinct legal entity. An employer cannot transfer an employee from one legal entity to another unless explicitly provided for in the contract. The Appellantâ€™s contract only permitted transfers between sections or departments of the same company. The Appellant failed to prove that the contract allowed for transfers to associate companies or that such a transfer was bona fide. Finding: The Appellant had no legal right to transfer the employees to a separate company (San Fashions) without their consent. Issue 2: Jurisdiction of the Commissioner Rule applied: Section 2(1), 2(4), and 5 of the Termination of Employment of Workmen (Special Provisions) Act No. 45 of 1971. Court's reasoning: The Act defines "termination" to include non-employment resulting from the closure of a business or any other reason. By attempting to force employees into a new, separate company, the Appellant effectively terminated their original employment. As this was done without the Commissioner's prior approval, the 1st Respondent acted within his statutory authority to declare the termination illegal and order compensation. Finding: The 1st Respondent had jurisdiction, and the orders were not nullities. Issue 3: Undue delay Rule applied: Principles of judicial review regarding the discretionary nature of prerogative writs. Court's reasoning: The Appellant waited six months to file the writ application after the initial order. The Court of Appeal found this explanation insufficient and exercised its discretion to refuse the writ based on inordinate delay. The Supreme Court affirmed that the Court of Appeal correctly applied the law regarding the discretionary nature of writs. Finding: The Court of Appeal did not err in dismissing the application due to delay. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing: because the transfer was legally invalid (Issue 1), the resulting non-employment constituted a termination under the Act (Issue 2), thereby validating the Commissioner's intervention. The failure on the merits, combined with the procedural failure regarding delay (Issue 3), confirms that the Appellant had no grounds for relief. Conclusion The Supreme Court affirmed the judgment of the Court of Appeal and dismissed the appeal. The Appellant is ordered to pay costs fixed at Rs. 100,000.00. Keywords Termination of employment — Transfer of employees — Associate company — Commissioner General of Labour — Jurisdiction of Commissioner — Writ application — Delay — Fundamental rights — Contract of employment		SC/APPEAL/52/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_52_2014.pdf
sc-fr-73-2009	7 September 2020	Fonseka and Others v. National Police Commission and Others	Priyantha Jayawardena, P.C., J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, J.; Priyantha Jayawardena, P.C., J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Promotions in the Police Service — Merit promotions — Seniority — Alteration of marking scheme — Separate cadre for female officers — Unblemished record of service — Delay in promotions	Facts The petitioners, serving as Chief Inspectors of Police, challenged the denial of their promotions to the rank of Assistant Superintendent of Police. The promotion process was governed by an Amended Scheme of Promotions approved in 1998, which required candidates to be confirmed in their rank and possess an unblemished service record for five years. The Inspector General of Police initially called for applications in 2007, but subsequently issued a circular in 2008 calling for fresh applications and introducing a new marking scheme. This marking scheme was further amended twice before the interviews were conducted. The petitioners participated in the selection process but were not promoted. They alleged that the process was arbitrary and discriminatory, noting that two female candidates, the 17th and 18th respondents, were promoted despite scoring lower marks than the petitioners. The respondents contended that these female candidates were promoted under a separate cadre for women. During the proceedings, it was revealed that several officers were promoted even after they had retired from the service. The petitioners sought relief for the infringement of their Fundamental Rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Issues Whether the promotion of the 17th and 18th respondents over the petitioners, despite the respondents scoring lower marks, violated Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the repeated calling for fresh applications and the continuous alteration of the marking scheme after the closing date of applications constituted an arbitrary and capricious process in violation of Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 17, 126, 17th Amendment, 18th Amendment, 19th Amendment. Cases Referred to: Liyanage v. Ratnasiri - [2013/v1-sri-lr-6] Ramupillai v. Festus Perera - [1991/v1-sri-lr-11] Guneratne v. Sri Lanka Telecom - [1993/v1-sri-lr-109] Maharashtra State Road Transport Corporation v. Rajendra Bhimrao Mandve - [air-2002-supreme-court-224] Perera v. Cyril Ranatunga - [1993/v1-sri-lr-39] [sc-fr-600/2003] [sc-fr-121/2009] [sc-fr-68/2009] Analysis Issue 1: Gender-based promotion and cadre validity Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution regarding the right to equality and the principle that once integrated into a common class, employees cannot be treated differently for promotion. Court's reasoning: The court found that the respondents failed to produce evidence of a validly created separate cadre for female Assistant Superintendents of Police. The Department of Management Services lacked the authority to create such a cadre, as that power resided with the National Police Commission or Public Service Commission. Since all candidates were serving in the same cadre as Chief Inspectors, the respondents could not justify the promotion of lower-scoring female candidates over higher-scoring petitioners. Finding: The promotion of the 17th and 18th respondents over the petitioners constituted an infringement of the petitioners' Fundamental Rights under Article 12(1). Issue 2: Procedural fairness in selection Rule applied: The principle that administrative authorities must not alter selection criteria after the process has commenced, and the requirement for reasonable notice when changing established procedures. Court's reasoning: The court determined that changing the marking scheme and calling for fresh applications after the initial closing date created an unfair advantage for some candidates. The lack of compelling reasons for these changes, combined with the inordinate delay in the promotion process, undermined the fairness and transparency of the selection. Furthermore, the court noted the unexplained promotion of retired officers, which further evidenced the arbitrary nature of the process. Finding: The administrative actions in altering the marking schemes and delaying the process were arbitrary and violated the petitioners' rights under Article 12(1). Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues reinforce the conclusion that the promotion process was fundamentally flawed. The lack of a valid separate cadre (Issue 1) combined with the arbitrary manipulation of the marking scheme (Issue 2) created a cumulative effect that denied the petitioners equal protection of the law. The court's finding that the process was arbitrary and lacked transparency applies to the entire selection exercise, rendering the petitioners' exclusion discriminatory. Conclusion The court declared that the Fundamental Rights of the 1st to 3rd petitioners under Article 12(1) were violated. The Inspector General of Police and the National Police Commission were directed to backdate the petitioners' promotions to 1st January 2008 and adjust their seniority and increments accordingly. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Promotions in the Police Service — Merit promotions — Seniority — Alteration of marking scheme — Separate cadre for female officers — Unblemished record of service — Delay in promotions		SC/FR/73/2009	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_73_2009.pdf
sc-appeal-12-2012	8 September 2020	De Silva and Others v. De Silva	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Section 218 of the Civil Procedure Code — Residential house seizure — Actual residence — One and only residential house — Condominium units — Assessment number — Electricity meter — Water meter	Facts The 4th Defendant-Respondent, Anthony Joseph Mahinda De Silva, filed a petition in the Commercial High Court seeking to release his residential property located at 27/3, Chandralekha Mawatha, Colombo 8, from a seizure executed pursuant to a court decree. The 4th Defendant-Respondent contended that the property was his sole residential house and was therefore exempt from seizure under Section 218 of the Civil Procedure Code. The Plaintiff-Appellant, Benedict Raja Philip De Silva, argued that the property comprised two separate condominium units, specifically Unit 3 and Unit 4, and that one of these units could be seized independently. The Commercial High Court Judge determined that the property constituted a single residential house and ordered its release from seizure. The Plaintiff-Appellant subsequently appealed this order to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal. During the proceedings, evidence was presented regarding the physical configuration of the property, including the existence of a single assessment number, a single electricity meter, and a single water meter. Testimony from a technical officer of the Municipal Council confirmed that the property functioned as a single unit without separate entrances for the upper and lower levels. Issues Whether the property at 27/3, Chandralekha Mawatha, Colombo 8, constitutes a single residential house or two separate, seizable condominium units. Whether the property is exempt from seizure under Section 218(n) of the Civil Procedure Code. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code, Section 218 High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 10 of 1996, Section 5(2) Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Characterization of the property Rule applied: The court applied the factual criteria regarding the physical unity of the premises, specifically the presence of shared utilities (electricity and water meters), a single assessment number, and the lack of independent access between the upper and lower floors. Court's reasoning: The court evaluated evidence provided by the 4th Defendant-Respondent and a technical officer from the Municipal Council. The evidence established that the property shared a single assessment number, one electricity meter, and one water meter. Furthermore, the court noted that the upstairs area lacked a separate entrance, requiring occupants to use the ground floor entrance. The court rejected the Plaintiff-Appellant's argument that the property consisted of two distinct condominium units, finding that the physical configuration and utility arrangements supported the conclusion that the premises functioned as a single house. Finding: The property at 27/3, Chandralekha Mawatha, Colombo 8, is a single residential house. Issue 2: Exemption from seizure Rule applied: Section 218(n) of the Civil Procedure Code, which prohibits the seizure of a house that serves as the actual residence of a judgment-debtor, provided it is not mortgaged as security for the decree. Court's reasoning: Having determined that the property was a single residential house, the court applied the statutory protection afforded by Section 218(n). The court found that the 4th Defendant-Respondent had established that the house was his one and only residence. As the property met the criteria for exemption under the Civil Procedure Code, it could not be subject to seizure in execution of the decree. Finding: The property is exempt from seizure under Section 218(n) of the Civil Procedure Code. Interaction of Issues The findings are interdependent; the determination that the property is a single residential unit (Issue 1) is the necessary factual predicate for applying the legal exemption under Section 218(n) (Issue 2). Once the court concluded the property was a single, indivisible residence, the statutory protection against seizure followed as a matter of law. Conclusion The Supreme Court affirmed the order of the Commercial High Court dated 1.7.2011. The appeal is dismissed. Keywords Section 218 of the Civil Procedure Code — Residential house seizure — Actual residence — One and only residential house — Condominium units — Assessment number — Electricity meter — Water meter		SC/CHC APPEAL/12/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_chc_appeal_12_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-179-2014	8 September 2020	Keerthisheeli and Others v. Upasena Niriella	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; P. Padman Suresena, J; Gamini Amarasekera, J	Constructive trust — Locus standi — Trust Ordinance — Gift of property — Intention of donor — Evidence of Notary Public — Irrevocable gift — Beneficial interest	Facts Niriellage Upasena, the father of the three Plaintiff-Respondents and the Defendant-Appellant, executed an irrevocable Deed of Gift No. 529 on 11.6.2001, transferring the property in question to the Defendant-Appellant. The donor did not reserve a life interest in the property and passed away in June 2002. Following his death, the Plaintiff-Respondents initiated an action in the District Court of Mount Lavinia, seeking a declaration that the Defendant-Appellant held the property in trust for them. The Plaintiff-Respondents contended that the property was transferred solely to facilitate the Defendant-Appellant's visa application for Canada and that the donor never intended to relinquish the beneficial interest. The Notary Public who attested the deed testified that the donor had expressed a clear intention to gift the property to his youngest daughter and did not indicate any desire to reclaim it. The District Court ruled in favor of the Plaintiff-Respondents, and the Civil Appellate High Court subsequently dismissed the Defendant-Appellant's appeal. Issues Whether the District Court and the Civil Appellate High Court erred in law by holding that a constructive trust was established in favour of the Plaintiff-Respondents. Whether the alleged cause of action falls within the categories of constructive trusts contemplated in Chapter IX of the Trust Ordinance. Whether the lower courts failed to evaluate the evidence regarding the donor's intention to transfer the beneficial interest. Applicable Law Statutes: Trust Ordinance Cases Referred to: Not specified Analysis Issue 1: Existence of a Constructive Trust Rule applied: The principles governing the creation of a constructive trust under the Trust Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court examined the evidence provided by the Notary Public, which remained unchallenged during cross-examination. The Notary confirmed that the donor intended to gift the property to the Defendant-Appellant and did not express any intention to reclaim it or hold it in trust. Consequently, the court determined that no trust existed in favour of the donor, and by extension, no trust could exist in favour of the Plaintiff-Respondents. Finding: The lower courts erred in concluding that a constructive trust was established. Issue 2: Applicability of Chapter IX of the Trust Ordinance Rule applied: Chapter IX of the Trust Ordinance. Court's reasoning: Given the court's finding that no trust was established in the first instance, the court determined that it was unnecessary to address whether the facts satisfied the specific statutory categories of constructive trusts. Finding: This issue did not arise for consideration. Issue 3: Evaluation of Donor's Intention Rule applied: Principles of evidence regarding the intent of a donor in a deed of gift. Court's reasoning: The court found that the lower courts failed to properly weigh the testimony of the Notary Public. The evidence demonstrated that the donor had a clear, unencumbered intent to gift the property to the Defendant-Appellant. The Plaintiff-Respondents failed to provide evidence of attendant circumstances that would suggest the creation of a trust. Finding: The court answered this question in the affirmative, confirming that the lower courts failed to correctly evaluate the evidence of the donor's intent. Interaction of Issues The findings on the donor's lack of intent to create a trust (Issue 3) directly negated the legal basis for the existence of a constructive trust (Issue 1). Because the court concluded that no trust was created, the technical legal question regarding the classification of the trust under the Trust Ordinance (Issue 2) became moot. Conclusion The appeal is allowed, and the judgments of the District Court and the Civil Appellate High Court are set aside. The action of the Plaintiff-Respondents is dismissed, and the Defendant-Appellant is awarded costs in all three courts. Keywords Constructive trust — Locus standi — Trust Ordinance — Gift of property — Intention of donor — Evidence of Notary Public — Irrevocable gift — Beneficial interest		SC/APPEAL/179/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_179_2014.pdf
sc-appeal-208-2017	8 September 2020	Jinadasa v. Waduge Bandusoma and Hewage Seetha	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.	K.S.J. De Abrew, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Gamini Amarasekara, J	Property ownership — Lease agreement — Life interest — Deed of gift — Evidence Ordinance — Notary Public — Proof of execution — Renunciation of life interest — Validity of lease — Transfer of property	Facts The Plaintiff-Respondent filed an action seeking a declaration of title and the ejectment of the Defendant-Appellants from the property in question. Princy Samarawickrama originally gifted the property to her daughter Anulawathi via Deed of Gift No. 3647 dated 3.5.1988, while reserving a life interest for herself. Subsequently, Anulawathi transferred the property back to Princy Samarawickrama by Deed No. 2831 dated 3.4.1997. The Defendant-Appellants claimed rights to the property based on a Lease Agreement No. 2396 dated 28.10.1992, allegedly executed by Princy Samarawickrama. Princy Samarawickrama later gifted the property to her daughter Manic Pura Hewage Chureen via Deed of Gift No. 123 dated 2.12.1998, without reserving a life interest. Manic Pura Hewage Chureen subsequently gifted the property to her husband, the original Plaintiff-Respondent, via Deed of Gift No. 14523 dated 20.8.2003. The District Court ruled in favor of the Plaintiff-Respondent, and the Civil Appellate High Court affirmed this decision. The Defendant-Appellants appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Lease Agreement No. 2396 dated 28.10.1992 was proved in accordance with Section 68 of the Evidence Ordinance. Whether the Deed of Gift No. 123 dated 2.12.1998 was validly executed by Princy Samarawickrama. Whether the Defendant-Appellants are entitled to the benefit of the lease despite the subsequent transfer of the property. Applicable Law Statutes: Evidence Ordinance, Section 68 Notaries Ordinance, Section 31(9) Cases Referred to: Wijegoonetilleke v. Wijegoonetilleke - [60-nlr-560] Marian v. Jesuthasan - [59-nlr-348] Ramen Chetty v. Assen Najna - [1909/current-law-reports-of-ceylon-256] Analysis Issue 1: Proof of Lease Agreement Rule applied: Section 68 of the Evidence Ordinance and Section 31(9) of the Notaries Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court held that a Notary Public is an attesting witness only if they personally knew the executant or the attesting witnesses and can testify to the signature. In this case, the Notary admitted he did not know the executant personally, and the Defendant-Appellants failed to call the attesting witnesses. Furthermore, the court found the 99-year lease term inherently improbable given the life interest holder's age. Finding: The Lease Agreement No. 2396 was not proved and cannot be used as evidence. Issue 2: Validity of Deed of Gift Rule applied: Section 68 of the Evidence Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Notary who attested Deed of Gift No. 123 testified that he knew the donor and donee, and that all parties signed in his presence. An attesting witness also confirmed her signature and the execution of the deed. Finding: The Deed of Gift No. 123 was validly proved as the act and deed of Princy Samarawickrama. Issue 3: Entitlement to Lease Rule applied: Principles of life interest and property transfer. Court's reasoning: Because the lease was never proved, it created no valid interest. Furthermore, when Princy Samarawickrama gifted the property via Deed of Gift No. 123 without reserving a life interest, she effectively renounced any prior interest in the property. Finding: The Defendant-Appellants have no valid claim to the property. Interaction of Issues The failure to prove the lease agreement (Issue 1) rendered the Defendant-Appellants' claim legally void. This, combined with the valid proof of the subsequent Deed of Gift (Issue 2), confirmed that the Plaintiff-Respondent held clear title, thereby resolving the dispute in favor of the Plaintiff-Respondent. Conclusion The Supreme Court affirmed the judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court and dismissed the appeal with costs. The Plaintiff-Respondent is confirmed as the owner of the property. Keywords Property ownership — Lease agreement — Life interest — Deed of gift — Evidence Ordinance — Notary Public — Proof of execution — Renunciation of life interest — Validity of lease — Transfer of property		SC/APPEAL/208/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_208_2017.pdf
sc-appeal-224-2017	9 September 2020	Nadumali v. Bamunusinghe and Others	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; Yasantha Kodagoda, PC, J.	Promissory note — Debt recovery — Affidavit requirements — Civil Procedure Code — Section 705(1) — Section 704(2) — Section 706 — Oaths and Affirmations Ordinance — Technical irregularities — Judicial discretion	Facts The Plaintiff-Respondent-Respondent-Respondent (Plaintiff) instituted an action in the District Court of Homagama under Chapter LIII of the Civil Procedure Code against the Defendant-Petitioner-Petitioner-Appellant (Defendant) to recover a sum of Rs. 3,000,000/- based on a promissory note. The District Court issued summons, and the Defendant appeared to seek leave to defend the action. The Defendant contended that the Plaint was not supported by an affidavit at the time of filing as required by Section 705(1) of the Civil Procedure Code, noting that the Plaint was dated 09/11/2015 while the affidavit was dated 20/11/2015. The Defendant further alleged that he had already repaid Rs. 1,000,000/- and that the promissory note was provided as security for a loan obtained from a third party. The District Court judge, noting the Defendant's admission of the debt in his affidavit, ordered the Defendant to deposit the full sum of Rs. 3,000,000/- as a condition for filing an answer. The Defendant challenged this order via a revision application in the Provincial High Court. The Provincial High Court dismissed the revision application, holding that the Defendant failed to explain why he did not pursue the alternative remedy of an appeal and that the technical irregularity regarding the affidavit date did not prejudice the Defendant. The Defendant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the failure to file an affidavit simultaneously with the Plaint under Chapter LIII of the Civil Procedure Code renders the proceedings invalid. Whether the Court has jurisdiction to issue summons under Section 705 of the Civil Procedure Code if the supporting affidavit is technically irregular. Whether the District Court judge erred in exercising discretion to impose a condition of depositing the full sum before allowing the Defendant to file an answer. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code, Chapter LIII Civil Procedure Code, Section 704 Civil Procedure Code, Section 705(1) Civil Procedure Code, Section 706 Civil Procedure Code, Section 754(2) Civil Procedure Code, Section 757 Oaths and Affirmations Ordinance No. 9, Section 9 Oaths and Affirmations Ordinance No. 9, Section 13 Presidential Elections Act No. 15, Section 96(d) Cases Referred to: Kobbekaduwa v. Jayewardene - [1983/v1-sri-lr-419] Kumarasinghe v. Ratnakumara - [1983/v2-sri-lr-1] Distilleries Company Ltd v. Kariyawasam - [2001/v3-sri-lr-1] Mohamed Facy Mohamed v. Mohamed Azath Sanoon Sally - [sc-appeal-4/2004] Rasheed Ali v. Mohamed Ali - [1981/v1-sri-lr-1] K.H.S. Pushpadeva v. Senok Trade Combine Ltd - [sc/hc/la-02/2014] Fernando v. Sybil Fernando - [1997/v3-sri-lr-1] Analysis Issue 1: Validity of the Plaint and Affidavit Rule applied: Section 705(1) of the Civil Procedure Code and Section 9 of the Oaths and Affirmations Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Court observed that while Section 705(1) requires an affidavit to support the Plaint, the object of the Civil Procedure Code is to regulate proceedings and prevent frustration by technical irregularities that cause no prejudice. The Court applied Section 9 of the Oaths and Affirmations Ordinance, which provides that irregularities in form do not invalidate proceedings. The Court found the discrepancy in dates to be a technicality that did not prejudice the Defendant. Finding: The irregularity regarding the affidavit date does not invalidate the Plaint. Issue 2: Jurisdiction to issue summons Rule applied: Section 705(1) of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The Court held that the primary purpose of the affidavit is to provide prima facie evidence of the debt. Since the affidavit was present in the record and the irregularity was merely technical, the court's jurisdiction to issue summons was not ousted. Finding: The court had the necessary jurisdiction to issue summons. Issue 3: Discretion to impose conditions Rule applied: Sections 704(2) and 706 of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The Court noted that the trial judge has discretion to impose conditions if the defence is not prima facie sustainable or if there is doubt regarding the Defendant's good faith. The Defendant provided contradictory statements regarding the debt, justifying the trial judge's exercise of discretion. Finding: The trial judge did not act arbitrarily or perversely in requiring the deposit. Interaction of Issues The findings on the technical validity of the affidavit (Issues 1 and 2) reinforce the court's decision to uphold the trial judge's discretionary order (Issue 3). By determining that the procedural irregularities were not fatal, the Court affirmed that the trial judge's focus on the merits of the defence and the Defendant's conduct was the correct approach to the administration of justice. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. The orders of the Provincial High Court and the District Court are affirmed, and the Defendant-Appellant is ordered to pay costs of Rs. 50,000/-. Keywords Promissory note — Debt recovery — Affidavit requirements — Civil Procedure Code — Section 705(1) — Section 704(2) — Section 706 — Oaths and Affirmations Ordinance — Technical irregularities — Judicial discretion		SC/APPEAL/224/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_224_2017.pdf
sc-chc-appeal-12-2012	09 September 2020	Benedict Raja Philip De Silva v. Anthony Joseph Mahinda De Silva and Others	Sisira J. de Abrew, J.	Sisira J. de Abrew, J.; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J; S. Thurairaja PC J	Civil Procedure Code — Seizure of property — Residential house — Execution of decree — Judgment-debtor — Condominium units — High Court — Appeal — Legal principles — Evidence — Assessment number — Electricity meter — Water meter	Facts The case revolves around an appeal concerning the seizure of a residential property belonging to the 4th Defendant-Petitioner-Respondent, Anthony Joseph Mahinda De Silva. The property in question is located at 27/3, Chandralekha Mawatha, Colombo 8, and is claimed to be his one and only residential house. The Plaintiff-Respondent-Petitioner-Appellant, Benedict Raja Philip De Silva, contested this claim, arguing that the property consists of two condominium units (Unit No. 3 and Unit No. 4) that could be treated as separate residences. The 4th Defendant-Respondent filed a petition in the Commercial High Court to release his house from seizure, which was executed based on a decree against him. The High Court ruled in favor of the 4th Defendant-Respondent, stating that the house could not be seized under Section 218 of the Civil Procedure Code, which protects the judgment-debtor's residential property from execution. The Plaintiff-Appellant appealed this decision, leading to the current Supreme Court proceedings. Issues The primary issues in this case are: Whether the property at 27/3, Chandralekha Mawatha, Colombo 8, constitutes a single residential house or two separate condominium units. Whether the seizure of the property is permissible under Section 218 of the Civil Procedure Code, given that it is claimed to be the only residential house of the judgment-debtor. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code, Section 218 Cases Referred to: None explicitly mentioned in the judgment. Analysis Issue 1: Whether the property at 27/3, Chandralekha Mawatha, Colombo 8, constitutes a single residential house or two separate condominium units. Rule applied: Section 218 of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court, led by Justice Sisira J. de Abrew, meticulously examined the evidence presented regarding the nature of the property. The 4th Defendant-Respondent asserted that the house is indeed a single residential unit, supported by several key points: it has only one assessment number, one electricity meter, and one water meter, indicating that it functions as a single household. Furthermore, the layout of the house was described in such a way that the upstairs and downstairs are interconnected without separate entrances, reinforcing the argument that it is a single dwelling. The Plaintiff-Appellant's contention that the property consists of two separate condominium units was dismissed by the court, which noted that if this were true, there would logically be separate utility meters for each unit. The court highlighted that the evidence provided by the municipal technical officer corroborated the 4th Defendant-Respondent's claim, stating that the building plan indicated a single residential house. The court also pointed out that the Plaintiff-Appellant failed to contradict this evidence effectively. The judgment emphasized the importance of the residential nature of the property, as outlined in Section 218(n) of the Civil Procedure Code, which protects the judgment-debtor's actual residence from seizure. Finding: The court concluded that the property is indeed a single residential house and cannot be seized under the provisions of the Civil Procedure Code. Interaction of Issues The issues are interrelated as the determination of whether the property is a single house or two separate units directly impacts the applicability of Section 218 of the Civil Procedure Code. If the property is deemed a single house, the protections afforded to the judgment-debtor under the law would apply, preventing its seizure. Conversely, if it were found to be two separate units, the legal protections might not apply in the same manner. Conclusion The Supreme Court upheld the decision of the High Court, affirming that the property at 27/3, Chandralekha Mawatha, Colombo 8, is the one and only residential house of the 4th Defendant-Respondent and cannot be seized in execution of the decree. The appeal was dismissed, reinforcing the legal protections afforded to residential properties under the Civil Procedure Code. Keywords Civil Procedure Code — Seizure of property — Residential house — Execution of decree — Judgment-debtor — Condominium units — High Court — Appeal — Legal principles — Evidence — Assessment number — Electricity meter — Water meter	Romesh de Silva PC with Manjuka Fernandopulle for the Plaintiff-Respondent-Petitioner-Appellant. Harsha Soza PC with R. Weerasinghe for the 4th Defendant-Petitioner-Respondent-Respondent.	SC/CHC APPEAL/12/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_chc_appeal_12_2012.pdf
sc-chc-appeal-34-2011	09 September 2020	Benedict Raja Philip De Silva v. Anthony Joseph Mahinda De Silva and Others	Sisira J. de Abrew, J.	Sisira J. de Abrew, J.; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J; S. Thurairaja PC, J.	Civil Procedure Code — Section 218 — Section 343 — Section 349 — High Court of the Provinces (Special Provinces) Act No.10 of 1996 — Leave to Appeal — Dismissal of Appeal — Jurisdiction — Agreement of Parties — Legal Representation	Facts The case revolves around a civil dispute involving Benedict Raja Philip De Silva, the Plaintiff-Respondent-Petitioner-Appellant, and Anthony Joseph Mahinda De Silva along with other defendants. The Plaintiff, residing in the United States, initiated legal proceedings in Sri Lanka concerning matters that were presumably related to property or civil rights, as indicated by the references to the Civil Procedure Code. The Plaintiff sought to appeal a decision made by the High Court, which had dismissed his earlier appeal. The context of the case is further complicated by the involvement of multiple defendants, including Chris Peiris, Chamalee Deepthika Jayawardena, and Haritha Munasinghe, who were all named in the proceedings. The Plaintiff's legal representation was led by Romesh de Silva, PC, while the 4th Defendant was represented by Harsha Soza, PC. The case was argued on 16 June 2020 and the judgment was delivered on 9 September 2020. The Supreme Court noted that the parties had agreed to abide by the judgment in another related case, SC/CHC/12/2012, which had a direct bearing on the outcome of this appeal. The Supreme Court ultimately dismissed the appeal, aligning its decision with the findings in the aforementioned case. Issues The primary issues in this case can be framed as follows: Whether the Supreme Court has the jurisdiction to hear the appeal based on the agreements made by the parties. Whether the dismissal of the appeal in the related case SC/CHC/12/2012 affects the outcome of this appeal. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code — Section 218 — Section 343 — Section 349 — High Court of the Provinces (Special Provinces) Act No.10 of 1996 Cases Referred to: SC/CHC/12/2012 - [citation not provided] Analysis Issue 1: Jurisdiction of the Supreme Court Rule applied: The Supreme Court's jurisdiction to hear appeals under the Civil Procedure Code and related statutes. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court examined the procedural aspects of the appeal, particularly focusing on the agreements made by the parties involved. The Court emphasized that the parties had consented to abide by the judgment in the related case, SC/CHC/12/2012. This agreement was pivotal as it indicated the parties' acceptance of the legal outcomes that would arise from that case. The Court noted that such agreements are significant in civil proceedings, as they reflect the parties' willingness to resolve disputes based on mutual consent. The judgment in SC/CHC/12/2012 had dismissed the appeal of the Plaintiff-Respondent-Petitioner-Appellant, which directly impacted the current appeal. The Supreme Court reiterated that the principle of res judicata applies, meaning that once a matter has been adjudicated, it cannot be re-litigated. The Court further stated that the dismissal of the appeal in the related case effectively barred the Plaintiff from pursuing the same claims in this appeal. The Court also highlighted the importance of judicial efficiency and the need to prevent the same issues from being litigated multiple times, which could lead to conflicting judgments and undermine the integrity of the judicial process. Finding: The Supreme Court found that it had no jurisdiction to entertain the appeal due to the binding nature of the prior judgment and the agreement of the parties. Interaction of Issues The issues of jurisdiction and the impact of the related case's dismissal are interlinked. The agreement by the parties to abide by the judgment in SC/CHC/12/2012 created a binding precedent that the Supreme Court was compelled to follow. This interaction underscores the principle that parties cannot selectively choose which judgments to follow based on their preferences; rather, they must adhere to the legal consequences of their agreements and the judgments rendered in related cases. Conclusion The Supreme Court dismissed the appeal, affirming the decision made in the related case SC/CHC/12/2012. The dismissal was based on the parties' agreement to abide by that judgment, which effectively precluded the Plaintiff from pursuing his appeal in this instance. The Court's ruling reinforces the importance of adherence to judicial decisions and the agreements made by parties in civil litigation. The outcome serves as a reminder of the binding nature of legal agreements and the principle of res judicata in maintaining the integrity of the judicial process. Keywords Civil Procedure Code — Section 218 — Section 343 — Section 349 — High Court of the Provinces (Special Provinces) Act No.10 of 1996 — Leave to Appeal — Dismissal of Appeal — Jurisdiction — Agreement of Parties — Legal Representation	Romesh de Silva PC with Manjuka Fernandopulle for the Plaintiff-Respondent-Petitioner-Appellant. Harsha Soza PC with R. Weerasinghe for the 4th Defendant-Petitioner-Respondent-Respondent.	SC/APPEAL/34/2011	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_34_2011-1.pdf
sc-appeal-88-2012	10 September 2020	Mendis v. Attorney General	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; Preethi Padman Surasena, J; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Criminal breach of trust — Excessive sentence — Sentence variation — Time elapsed since the offence — Mitigating factors — Aggravating factors — Judicial discretion — Suspended sentence — Financial fraud — Position of trust	Facts The Accused-Appellant-Petitioner-Appellant, Udugamaralalage Walter Mendis, was employed as an Accounts Executive at Freudenberg Air Service Ltd. He was charged with and convicted of Criminal Breach of Trust under Section 391 of the Penal Code regarding a sum of Rs. 527,496.00. Following his conviction in the High Court, he was sentenced to a term of rigorous imprisonment and a fine of Rs. 700,000.00. The Accused appealed this conviction and sentence to the Court of Appeal, which dismissed the appeal. The Accused subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court, specifically challenging the excessiveness of the custodial sentence imposed. During the proceedings before the Supreme Court, the Accused highlighted that thirty years had elapsed since the commission of the offence and that he had maintained a clean record during the interim. Furthermore, the Accused submitted medical evidence indicating a history of myocardial infarction and ongoing treatment for cardiac conditions. The prosecution did not object to the removal of the custodial sentence but requested that the fine be enhanced if the court opted for a suspended sentence. Issues Whether the custodial sentence imposed on the Accused for the offence of Criminal Breach of Trust under Section 391 of the Penal Code was excessive. Whether the court should exercise its discretion to substitute the custodial sentence with a suspended sentence under Section 303 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code, Section 391 Code of Criminal Procedure Act, Section 303 Cases Referred to: Karunanayake v. The State - [1978-nlr-413] Ananda v. The Attorney General - [1995/v2-sri-lr-315] Wimalaratne v. The State - [CA Application 46/58-CA minutes dated 24.11.2000] Ranawaka Arachchige Priyanka Ruwanari Perera v. The Attorney General - [sc-appeal-99/2006] Analysis Issue 1: Whether the custodial sentence was excessive Rule applied: The court applied the principle that judicial discretion in sentencing must balance the gravity of the offence and societal deterrence against mitigating factors, including the lapse of time since the offence and the health of the accused. Court's reasoning: The court acknowledged that while the delay in the administration of justice is not a standalone reason to avoid incarceration, it must be weighed alongside other factors. The court considered the thirty-year lapse since the offence, the Accused's clean record during that period, and his documented medical condition (myocardial infarction). The court concluded that these factors, in conjunction with the prosecution's lack of objection to removing the custodial sentence, rendered the original sentence excessive. Finding: The custodial sentence was found to be excessive in light of the specific mitigating circumstances presented. Issue 2: Whether to substitute with a suspended sentence Rule applied: Section 303 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act. Court's reasoning: The court determined that while the offence of Criminal Breach of Trust by an employee is serious and requires a deterrent sentence, the specific circumstances of the Accused warranted a non-custodial approach. To maintain the deterrent effect, the court decided to suspend the term of imprisonment for ten years and significantly increase the fine to Rs. 2.5 million. Finding: The court exercised its discretion to set aside the original custodial sentence and substitute it with a suspended sentence and an enhanced fine. Interaction of Issues The findings are intrinsically linked; the determination that the original sentence was excessive necessitated the exercise of judicial discretion under Section 303 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act. The court's decision to suspend the sentence was contingent upon the ability to impose a higher fine, thereby balancing the need for leniency regarding the Accused's health and the passage of time with the need for societal deterrence regarding financial fraud. Conclusion The appeal is partially allowed. The original sentence is set aside and substituted with a two-year rigorous imprisonment term, suspended for ten years, and a fine of Rs. 2.5 million with a default sentence of one year and nine months rigorous imprisonment. Keywords Criminal breach of trust — Excessive sentence — Sentence variation — Time elapsed since the offence — Mitigating factors — Aggravating factors — Judicial discretion — Suspended sentence — Financial fraud — Position of trust		SC/APPEAL/88/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_88_2012.pdf
sc-appeal-65-2016	10 September 2020	Kushan Ediriweera and Chandra Ediriweera v. Sivagankan and Sundhararajan	E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Motor Traffic Act — Third-party insurance — Exclusion of liability — General exceptions — Named drivers — Public policy — Statutory limitations — Intoxicated drivers	Facts The Plaintiff, Kushan Ediriweera, instituted an action in the District Court of Colombo against the 1st Defendant, the driver, and the 2nd Defendant, the registered owner, seeking damages for injuries sustained in a motor vehicle accident on 19th July 2005. The 3rd Defendant, Union Assurance General Limited (now Fairfirst Insurance Limited), was subsequently added as an insurer. It was admitted that the 1st Defendant was driving the vehicle after consuming intoxicating liquor at the time of the accident and that the 3rd Defendant had issued a notice of non-liability under Section 109 of the Motor Traffic Act. The insurance policy contained a general exception clause stating that the company was not liable for any accident or loss arising while the vehicle was driven by any person who had consumed intoxicating liquor. The 3rd Defendant moved to dismiss the action against it in limine, arguing that this clause effectively named the 1st Defendant as an excluded driver under Section 102(4)(c)(i) of the Motor Traffic Act. The District Court Judge rejected this argument, holding that the clause lacked the necessary precision to qualify as an exclusion under the Act and that such clauses could not override the public policy objective of mandatory third-party insurance. The High Court of the Western Province (Civil Appellate) subsequently set aside the District Court order, concluding that the policy clause was sufficiently clear to exclude the 1st Defendant. The Plaintiff appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the term in the insurance policy excluding liability for accidents caused by drivers who have consumed intoxicating liquor constitutes a valid naming of an "excluded driver" under Section 102(4)(c)(i) of the Motor Traffic Act No. 14 as amended. Whether an insurer is entitled to a declaration of non-liability under Section 109 of the Motor Traffic Act based on a policy condition that conflicts with the legislative intent of mandatory third-party insurance. Applicable Law Statutes: Motor Traffic Act No. 14 as amended (Sections 99(1), 100(1)(b), 102(1), 102(4), 105(1), 109) High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) (Amendment) Act No. 54 Civil Procedure Code (Section 757) Cases Referred to: Gardner v. Moore - [1984/v1-all-e-r-1100] White v. White - [2001/v2-all-e-r-43] Royal Insurance Co. Ltd. v. Ven. J. A. R. Navaratnam - [60-nlr-520] The Ceylon Insurance Co. Ltd. v. Richard - [53-nlr-64] Ruby General Insurance Co. Ltd. v. S. Yasapala De Silva - [71-nlr-54] Emjay Insurance Co. Ltd. v. P. S. William - [70-nlr-566] Central Inland Water Transport Corporation Ltd. v. Borja Nath Ganguly - [air-1986-sc-1571] Shri Parsar v. Municipal Board - [1997/v1-wlc-443] Oil and Natural Gas Company Ltd. v. Saw Pipes Ltd. - [air-2003-sc-2629] Analysis Issue 1: Validity of the exclusion clause Rule applied: Section 102(4)(c)(i) of the Motor Traffic Act, which permits the exclusion of "any person or persons named in the policy." Court's reasoning: The Court reasoned that the power to "name" a driver under Section 102(4)(c)(i) requires specificity and identification. The impugned clause did not name a specific individual but rather described a future contingent situation (consumption of liquor). Allowing insurers to use broad, imprecise descriptions to exclude drivers would enable them to circumvent the mandatory third-party insurance requirements. The Court held that the legislature did not intend to allow parties to defeat public interest by drafting illusory or overly broad exclusion clauses. Finding: The clause in the insurance policy does not satisfy the requirements of Section 102(4)(c)(i) and is not a valid naming of an excluded driver. Issue 2: Entitlement to declaration of non-liability Rule applied: Sections 99(1), 100(1)(b), and 102(1) of the Motor Traffic Act, which establish the mandatory nature of third-party insurance to protect the public. Court's reasoning: The Court emphasized that the primary legislative intent behind mandatory third-party insurance is to ensure that victims of accidents are compensated, regardless of the financial status of the wrongdoer. Because the insurance system is fault-based, excluding liability for the very acts that constitute the fault (such as negligent or intoxicated driving) would render the statutory protection redundant. The Court concluded that any exclusion clause that conflicts with this public safety objective is of no effect against third parties. Finding: The 3rd Defendant is not entitled to a declaration of non-liability under Section 109, as the policy condition is contrary to the public interest protected by the Act. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing. The determination that the exclusion clause is invalid under Section 102(4)(c)(i) (Issue 1) directly supports the conclusion that the insurer cannot rely on that clause to obtain a declaration of non-liability (Issue 2). Both issues hinge on the principle that private contractual terms cannot override the statutory mandate to provide third-party coverage for the protection of the public. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgment of the High Court is vacated, and the order of the District Court refusing to dismiss the action against the 3rd Defendant is restored. Keywords Motor Traffic Act — Third-party insurance — Exclusion of liability — General exceptions — Named drivers — Public policy — Statutory limitations — Intoxicated drivers		SC/APPEAL/65/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_65_2016.pdf
sc-appeal-70-2018	10 September 2020	Attorney General v. Fernando	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Culpable homicide not amounting to murder — Intention to cause death — Knowledge likely to cause death — Plea of guilty — Revision application — Sentence reduction — Delay in trial — Right to speedy trial — Intoxication as a defence	Facts The Accused-Appellant, Badde Liyanage Wasantha Kumara Fernando, was indicted for the murder of Anthonilage Pradeep Gamini Fernando under Section 296 of the Penal Code. Following a non-summary inquiry, the indictment was forwarded in 2009. During the trial, which commenced in 2015, the Accused-Appellant withdrew his plea of not guilty and pleaded guilty to an amended charge of culpable homicide not amounting to murder under Section 297 of the Penal Code. The High Court sentenced him to 15 years rigorous imprisonment and a fine. The Accused-Appellant subsequently filed a revision application in the Court of Appeal. The Complainant-Respondent, the Attorney General, raised preliminary objections regarding the propriety of invoking revisionary jurisdiction when a statutory right of appeal existed and argued that the High Court order was not irregular or illegal. The Court of Appeal affirmed the conviction but reduced the sentence to 10 years rigorous imprisonment without explicitly addressing the preliminary objections or providing detailed reasoning for the reduction. The Accused-Appellant appealed to the Supreme Court on a question of law regarding whether the sentence was excessive given the lapse of time since the commission of the offence. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal erred in failing to address the preliminary objections raised by the State regarding the maintainability of the revision application. Whether the sentence imposed was excessive due to the lapse of time between the commission of the offence and the conclusion of the trial. Whether the Appellate Court should consider new defenses, such as intoxication, that were not pleaded before the trial court. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 13(3) Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 128 Penal Code, Section 79 Penal Code, Section 296 Penal Code, Section 297 Code of Criminal Procedure Act No. 15 of 1979, Section 13 Judicature Act No. 2 of 1978, Section 14(b) Cases Referred to: Ananda v. Attorney General - [1995/v2-sri-lr-315] Attorney General v. Dewapriya Walgamage - [1990/v2-sri-lr-212] Liyana Mendis Gunadasa v. Attorney General - [ca-141/2006] Attorney General v. Segulebbe Latheef - [2008/v1-sri-lr-1] Niranjan Hemachandra Sashittal v. State of Maharashtra - [2013/v4-scc-642] Abdul Rehman Antulay v. R.S. Nayak - [AIR 1992 SC 1701] Rex v. Bazely - [1969/clw-107] Dhananjoy Chaterjee v. State of West Bengal - [1994/v1-scr-37] Attorney General v. H.N. de Silva - [57-nlr-121] Analysis Issue 1: Preliminary Objections Rule applied: The duty of an appellate court to adjudicate upon preliminary objections raised by the State. Court's reasoning: The Court observed that the Court of Appeal failed to indicate whether it accepted or rejected the State's preliminary objections. The Supreme Court emphasized that when serious legal objections are raised, the court is duty-bound to provide reasons for its decision. Finding: The Court of Appeal erred by failing to address the preliminary objections. Issue 2: Delay and Sentencing Rule applied: Principles of fair trial and the impact of delay on sentencing as established in Ananda v. Attorney General and Liyana Mendis Gunadasa v. Attorney General. Court's reasoning: The Court found that the trial was concluded within one year of its commencement. It held that while delay is a factor, it must be weighed against the gravity of the crime and the rights of the victim. The Court noted that the deceased was killed while attempting to defend the Accused-Appellant, and the medical evidence confirmed the fatal nature of the injuries. Finding: The delay was not inordinate or prejudicial to the extent of warranting a further reduction in sentence. Issue 3: New Defenses on Appeal Rule applied: The principle that appellate courts should not entertain new defenses or evidence not presented at the trial court. Court's reasoning: The Accused-Appellant attempted to raise the defense of intoxication for the first time on appeal. The Court held that the trial judge, having seen and heard the witnesses, is in the best position to evaluate the evidence. Allowing new defenses on appeal undermines the trial process and is unfair to the trial judge. Finding: The defense of intoxication was rejected as it was not pleaded at trial. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected; the Courtâ€™s rejection of the new defense of intoxication reinforced the validity of the original conviction. Furthermore, the determination that the trial process was efficient and the sentence was proportionate to the gravity of the offense rendered the arguments regarding delay and the Court of Appeal's procedural failures insufficient to justify altering the sentence. Conclusion The Supreme Court answered the question of law negatively and dismissed the appeal. The conviction and the sentence of 10 years rigorous imprisonment imposed by the Court of Appeal are affirmed. Keywords Culpable homicide not amounting to murder — Intention to cause death — Knowledge likely to cause death — Plea of guilty — Revision application — Sentence reduction — Delay in trial — Right to speedy trial — Intoxication as a defence		SC/APPEAL/70/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_70_2018.pdf
sc-appeal-04/2015	14 September 2020	Peopleâ€™s Bank v. Kularatne and Others	Priyantha Jayawardena PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; Priyantha Jayawardena PC, J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Debt Recovery Act — Section 4(1) — Instrument, agreement or document sued upon — Written agreement — Overdraft facility — Definition of debt — Decree nisi — Summary procedure — Fundamental rights	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondent (Peoples Bank) instituted an action in the District Court of Tissamaharama against the Defendant-Respondent-Appellant (Mahavidanage Simpson Kularatne) under the Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990. The bank sought a decree nisi for the recovery of an alleged debt arising from an overdraft facility. Along with the plaint, the bank filed copies of two cheques, a statement of account, and a letter of demand. The District Court initially entered a decree nisi, but subsequently granted the defendant unconditional leave to appear and show cause. The bank attempted to amend the plaint to introduce new documents, but the District Court rejected this application. The parties later agreed to proceed under the regular procedure of the Civil Procedure Code. Following the trial, the District Court dismissed the action, holding that the bank failed to comply with the mandatory requirements of Section 4(1) of the Debt Recovery Act by not filing the instrument or agreement sued upon, and that the claim did not constitute a "debt" under Section 30 of the Act. The Civil Appellate High Court reversed this decision, holding that the overdraft fell within the definition of a debt and that the bank's documents were sufficient. The defendant appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the requirement in Section 4(1) of the Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990 to file the instrument, agreement, or document sued upon or relied on along with the plaint is mandatory. Whether the Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondent complied with the requirements of Section 4(1) of the Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990 Civil Procedure Code, Ordinance No. 02 Evidence Ordinance, No. 14 of 1895 Cases Referred to: Dharmarathne v. Peoples Bank - [2003/v3-sri-lr-307] Kiran Atapattu v. Pan Asia Bank Corporation - [2005/v2-sri-lr-276] [sc-sd-1/90] [sc-sd-23/2003] Analysis Issue 1: Mandatory nature of Section 4(1) Rule applied: Section 4(1) of the Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990. Court's reasoning: The Court examined the legislative intent, noting that the Act provides a special, expedited procedure that must be balanced against the rights of the debtor. The Court held that the requirement to file the instrument or agreement sued upon is a condition precedent to invoking the court's jurisdiction. This is supported by Section 4(2), which mandates that the court must be satisfied of the validity of the document before entering a decree nisi. Failure to comply with this requirement deprives the defendant of the ability to present a proper defense. Finding: The provisions of Section 4(1) are mandatory. Issue 2: Compliance with Section 4(1) Rule applied: Section 4(1) and Section 30 of the Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990. Court's reasoning: The bank failed to produce a written agreement or promise for the overdraft facility. The bank's reliance on a general mandate, cheques, and a statement of account was rejected. The Court held that a statement of account under Section 90C of the Evidence Ordinance does not constitute an "agreement" or "instrument" under the Debt Recovery Act. Furthermore, the bank admitted in its submissions that no written agreement existed for the overdraft. Finding: The bank failed to comply with the mandatory requirements of Section 4(1). Interaction of Issues The findings are intrinsically linked: because Section 4(1) is mandatory, the failure to produce a written agreement as required by that section meant the bank could not establish a "debt" as defined in Section 30. Consequently, the court lacked the jurisdictional basis to enter a decree nisi, rendering the subsequent proceedings and the High Court's reversal legally unsustainable. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court is set aside, and the judgment of the District Court dismissing the action is affirmed. Keywords Debt Recovery Act — Section 4(1) — Instrument, agreement or document sued upon — Written agreement — Overdraft facility — Definition of debt — Decree nisi — Summary procedure — Fundamental rights		SC/APPEAL/4/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_4_2015_1.pdf
sc-appeal-4-2015	15 September 2020	People’s Bank v. Mahavidanage Simpson Kularatne	Priyantha Jayawardena PC, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J.; Priyantha Jayawardena PC, J.; Murdu N. B. Fernando PC, J.	Debt Recovery Act — Overdraft Facility — Written Agreement — Decree Nisi — Compliance with Statutory Requirements — Judicial Interpretation — Procedural Law — Substantive Law — Natural Justice — Appeal Process — Banking Regulations — Legal Obligations — Court Jurisdiction — Legislative Intent — Rights of Defendants	Facts This case revolves around an appeal filed by Mahavidanage Simpson Kularatne (the appellant) against the judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court of the Southern Province, which had favored the People’s Bank (the respondent bank). The respondent bank initiated action in the District Court of Tissamaharama under the Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990, claiming that the appellant had overdrawn his current account and owed a substantial sum of money. The appellant contended that the respondent bank failed to comply with the mandatory provisions of the Debt Recovery Act, particularly section 4(1), which requires the filing of a written instrument or agreement along with the plaint. The District Court initially dismissed the respondent bank's action, agreeing with the appellant's objections regarding the lack of a written agreement. However, the Civil Appellate High Court reversed this decision, leading to the current appeal. The Supreme Court was tasked with determining whether the Civil Appellate High Court had erred in its judgment by not addressing the fundamental issue of compliance with the Debt Recovery Act. Issues The primary issues for consideration were: (a) Whether it is mandatory or directory to file the instrument, agreement, or document sued upon or relied on by the institution along with the plaint under section 4(1) of the Debt Recovery Act? (b) If mandatory, did the respondent bank fail to comply with the requirement to file the instrument, agreement, or document along with the plaint? Applicable Law Statutes: Debt Recovery (Special Provisions) Act No. 2 of 1990; Civil Procedure Code Ordinance No. 02 of 1889; Evidence Ordinance No. 14 of 1895. Cases Referred to: Dharmarathne v. Peoples Bank (2003) 3 SLR 307; Kiran Atapattu v. Pan Asia Bank Corporation (2005) 2 SLR 276. Analysis Issue 1: Compliance with Section 4(1) of the Debt Recovery Act Rule applied: Section 4(1) of the Debt Recovery Act mandates that any institution suing for debt recovery must file the relevant instrument, agreement, or document along with the plaint. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court analyzed the legislative intent behind the Debt Recovery Act, emphasizing that the requirement to file a written instrument is not merely procedural but a condition precedent for the court's jurisdiction. The Court noted that the respondent bank had filed certain documents, including cheques and a statement of account, but these did not constitute a written agreement as required by the Act. The Court highlighted that the definition of 'debt' under section 30 of the Act explicitly excludes sums owed under promises or agreements that are not in writing. The Court further stated that the absence of a written agreement meant that the respondent bank could not establish a prima facie case for the recovery of the claimed amount. The Court also referenced previous determinations regarding the necessity of written agreements in debt recovery actions, reinforcing the mandatory nature of compliance with section 4(1). Finding: The Court concluded that the respondent bank failed to comply with the mandatory requirements of the Debt Recovery Act, and thus the appeal should be allowed. The judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court was set aside, and the District Court's dismissal of the plaint was affirmed. Interaction of Issues The issues were interrelated as the determination of whether the filing requirement was mandatory directly impacted the court's ability to adjudicate the case. The failure to comply with the statutory requirement meant that the respondent bank could not pursue its claim, thereby rendering the appeal valid. Conclusion The Supreme Court allowed the appeal, affirming the District Court's judgment and emphasizing the importance of adhering to statutory requirements in debt recovery actions. The Court's decision underscored the necessity for lending institutions to provide written evidence of debts claimed, thereby protecting the rights of defendants in such proceedings.	W. Dayaratne, PC with D. N. Dayaratne for the defendant – respondent – appellant; Rasika Dissanayake for the plaintiff – appellant – respondent	SC/APPEAL/4/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_4_2015_1.pdf
sc-appeal-118-2013	16 September 2020	Dayananda v. Ceylon Electricity Board and Ceylon Electricity Board	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Interpretation of statutes — Sinhala and English texts — Applicability of regulations — Trade Tax — Service Charge — Municipal Councils Ordinance — Commercial purpose — Industry vs. trade — Regional Engineers Office	Facts The complainant, a Revenue Inspector for the Dehiwala-Mt. Lavinia Municipal Council, initiated proceedings against the Ceylon Electricity Board in the Magistrates Court of Mt. Lavinia. The complainant alleged that the appellant failed to pay annual Trade Tax and Service Charges for the year 2008 regarding premises used for commercial purposes, pursuant to section 247B of the Municipal Councils Ordinance No. 29 as amended by the Municipal Councils (Amendment) Act No. 42. The appellant objected, arguing that the premises functioned solely as a Regional Engineers Office and did not constitute a trade or industry subject to taxation under the Ordinance. The Magistrate overruled these objections and ordered the appellant to pay the taxes. The appellant appealed to the High Court, which affirmed the Magistrateâ€™s judgment, finding that the premises were liable for tax under the Regulations issued via Gazette No. 1542. The appellant subsequently sought special leave to appeal to the Supreme Court.
