Issues Whether the Sinhala text of section 247B of the Municipal Councils Ordinance prevails over the English text, and whether there is an inconsistency between them regarding the term "trade" versus the Sinhala term used. Whether the term used in the Sinhala text of section 247B restricts the scope of the tax to manufacturing industries only. Whether the Regulations published in Gazette No. 1542 are ultra vires section 247B of the Municipal Councils Ordinance. Whether the appellant is liable to pay Trade Tax and Service Charges for the premises in question. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka (Articles 18, 23(1)) Municipal Councils Ordinance No. 29 Municipal Councils (Amendment) Act No. 42 Cases Referred to: Crest Gems Ltd v The Colombo Municipal Council - [2003/v1-sri-lr-370] Ceylon Electricity Board v A.D.A. Wijesuriya - [sc-minutes-5th-november-2011] Analysis Issue 1: Consistency of Statutory Texts Rule applied: Article 23(1) of the Constitution. Court's reasoning: The court determined that in the event of an inconsistency between the English and Sinhala texts of a statute, the Official Language (Sinhala) text prevails. Upon examining the dictionary definitions, the court found that the term used in the Sinhala text is a broad term encompassing trade, business, and industry. The court concluded that the English word "trade" is a translation of the Sinhala term, and there is no substantive inconsistency between the two texts. Finding: The Sinhala text prevails, but there is no conflict as both texts intend to cover commercial activities. Issue 2: Interpretation of the Sinhala Term Rule applied: Principles of literal and purposive interpretation; Maxwell on The Interpretation of Statutes. Court's reasoning: The court rejected the appellant's argument that the term refers exclusively to manufacturing industries. By applying purposive interpretation and examining section 247C of the Ordinance, the court noted that the legislature distinguished between general business activities and manufacturing. Restricting the term to manufacturing would render other provisions of the Ordinance redundant and defeat the legislative intent. Finding: The term is broad and includes service-oriented commercial activities, not just manufacturing. Issue 3: Validity of Regulations Rule applied: Section 289(1) of the Municipal Councils Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court held that the Minister is empowered under section 289 to promulgate regulations to give effect to the Ordinance. Gazette No. 1542 was issued to implement the tax collection powers granted by section 247B. Therefore, the regulations are within the scope of the parent statute. Finding: The Regulations are intra vires and valid. Issue 4: Liability for Tax Rule applied: Section 247B of the Municipal Councils Ordinance; Gazette No. 1542. Court's reasoning: Evidence established that the premises were used not only as an office but also to generate income by collecting electricity bills and processing new connections. This constitutes a commercial purpose falling under the items specified in the Gazette. Finding: The appellant is liable for the Trade Tax and Service Charges. Interaction of Issues The court's finding that the Sinhala term is broad and inclusive (Issue 2) directly supports the validity of the Regulations (Issue 3), as the Regulations merely categorize specific commercial activities under the umbrella of the Ordinance. This, in turn, confirms the appellant's liability (Issue 4), as the premises clearly fall within the scope of these categorized activities. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed. The High Court did not err in its interpretation of the law or its finding that the appellant was engaged in taxable commercial activity. Keywords Interpretation of statutes — Sinhala and English texts — Applicability of regulations — Trade Tax — Service Charge — Municipal Councils Ordinance — Commercial purpose — Industry vs. trade — Regional Engineers Office		SC/APPEAL/118/2013	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_118_2013.pdf
sc-spl-la-159-2017	16 September 2020	Wijesinghe v. Tenderlea Farms (Pvt) Limited	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Special leave to appeal — Industrial Disputes Act — Termination of employment — Labour Tribunal Order — Bona fide termination — Supreme Court Rules — Rule 2 — Rule 6 — Rule 10 — Material breach — Due diligence	Facts The applicant-respondent-petitioner was employed as a Farm Manager by the respondent-appellant-respondent. The workman filed an application in the Labour Tribunal seeking compensation for unlawful termination and gratuity. The employer contended that the termination resulted from the frustration of the employment contract due to the closure of the farm. The Labour Tribunal ruled in favor of the workman, awarding compensation and gratuity. The employer appealed this order to the Provincial High Court, which set aside the Labour Tribunal's order. The workman subsequently filed an application for special leave to appeal to the Supreme Court. During the proceedings in the Supreme Court, the employer raised a preliminary objection regarding the maintainability of the application. The employer argued that the workman failed to comply with Rule 2 and Rule 6 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990 by not annexing all documents produced before the High Court. The workman contended that the documents provided were sufficient to address the questions of law and that the employer failed to follow the correct procedure for raising such an objection. Issues Whether the failure to annex all documents produced before the High Court constitutes a material breach of Rule 2 and Rule 6 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990. Whether the employer followed the proper procedure under Rule 10 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990 to raise a preliminary objection regarding non-compliance. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 128 High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 Industrial Disputes Act, Section 31DD(1) Supreme Court Rules 1990, Rule 2, Rule 3, Rule 6, Rule 10 Cases Referred to: Wimalasiri Perera v. Lakmali Enterprises Diesel and Petrol Motor Engineers - [2003/v1-sri-lr-62] Ceylon Electricity Board v. Ranjith Fonseka - [2008/v1-sri-lr-337] Aaron Senerath v. The Manager, Moray Estate - [sc-spl-la-231/2015] Kiriwanthe v. Navaratne - [1990/v2-sri-lr-393] S. J. Sirisena v. A. A. Gunawardane - [sc-spl-la-133/2015] Analysis Issue 1: Material breach of Supreme Court Rules Rule applied: Rule 2 and Rule 6 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990. Court's reasoning: The court noted that Rule 2 and Rule 6 require the filing of documents that cannot be verified by the judgment alone. The workman had filed the High Court appeal brief and the impugned judgment. The court determined that these documents were sufficient to address the questions of law raised. Citing Kiriwanthe v. Navaratne, the court held that non-compliance does not necessitate automatic dismissal and that the court has the discretion to determine if the documents are necessary for the administration of justice. The court found that the missing documents (written submissions and objections) were not material to the questions of law. Finding: There was no material breach of the Supreme Court Rules, and the workman demonstrated substantial compliance. Issue 2: Procedure for raising preliminary objections Rule applied: Rule 10 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990. Court's reasoning: The court examined whether the employer followed the correct procedure for challenging the maintainability of the application. Citing S. J. Sirisena v. A. A. Gunawardane, the court held that Rule 10(1) stipulates that a party seeking to challenge an application for non-compliance must move the court by filing a motion for rejection. The employer failed to do so, instead raising the objection orally at a later stage after previously participating in the proceedings without objection. Finding: The employer failed to follow the mandatory procedure under Rule 10, and therefore, the preliminary objection was not properly raised. Interaction of Issues The findings on these issues are mutually reinforcing. The court determined that the application was substantively compliant with the filing requirements, rendering the employer's objection meritless. Furthermore, the procedural failure of the employer to invoke Rule 10 provided an independent basis for overruling the objection, ensuring that the merits of the appeal could be addressed without being defeated by technicalities. Conclusion The preliminary objection raised by the employer is overruled. The court ordered the employer to pay costs of Rs. 25,000/- to the workman on or before 30th September, 2020. Keywords Special leave to appeal — Industrial Disputes Act — Termination of employment — Labour Tribunal Order — Bona fide termination — Supreme Court Rules — Rule 2 — Rule 6 — Rule 10 — Material breach — Due diligence		SC/SPL/LA/159/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_spl_la_159_2017.pdf
sc-appeal-148-2012	20 September 2020	Chandrasena v. The Monetary Board	Aluwihare PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Labour Tribunal — Termination of services — Domestic inquiry — Evidence at inquiry — Review of Labour Tribunal order — Questions of law — Justification for termination — Industrial Disputes Act — Wrongful termination — Relief ordered by High Court	Facts The Applicant, R. Chandrasena, was employed by the Central Bank of Sri Lanka as a Senior Analyst Programmer. In 2000, it was discovered that the Applicant had manipulated the payroll software to credit unauthorized sums of money into his personal bank account. Following a domestic inquiry, the Applicantâ€™s services were terminated in 2002. The Applicant challenged this termination before the Labour Tribunal. The Labour Tribunal President found that the Applicant had committed a fraudulent act and that the termination was justified. The Applicant appealed this decision to the High Court. The High Court Judge set aside the Labour Tribunalâ€™s order, concluding that the termination was wrongful. The High Court Judge based this decision primarily on the procedural history of the domestic inquiries, noting that the Applicant had been exonerated in an initial inquiry before a second inquiry was conducted. The High Court Judge awarded the Applicant salary up to retirement age, compensation of one million rupees, and pension rights. The Monetary Board of the Central Bank subsequently appealed this judgment to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the High Court Judge erred in placing reliance on the evidence and proceedings of the domestic inquiries without considering the nature of the proceedings before the Labour Tribunal. Whether the High Court Judge exceeded his appellate role by reviewing the Labour Tribunalâ€™s order on matters other than questions of law. Whether the High Court Judge erred in granting compensation, back-wages, and pension rights after finding the termination wrongful. Applicable Law Statutes: Industrial Disputes Act No. 43 of 1950 Cases Referred to: St. Andrews Hotels Ltd. v. Ceylon Mercantile Union - [CA-138/85] The Batticaloa Multi-Purpose Co-operative Societies Union Ltd. v. Velupillai - [76-nlr-60] Ceylon Transport Board v. W. A. D. Gunasinghe - [72-nlr-76] Jayasuriya v. Sri Lanka State Plantations Corporation - [1995/v2-sri-lr-379] Analysis Issue 1: Reliance on domestic inquiry proceedings Rule applied: Section 31(C)(1) of the Industrial Disputes Act. Court's reasoning: The Court held that while domestic inquiries are desirable to establish employer bona fides, they are not a statutory requirement. Citing The Batticaloa Multi-Purpose Co-operative Societies Union Ltd. v. Velupillai, the Court noted that while a Labour Tribunal may use evidence from a domestic inquiry for corroboration or to evaluate witness credibility, it should not be treated as substantive evidence to determine the justification of termination. The High Court Judge focused exclusively on the perceived unfairness of the domestic inquiry process rather than evaluating the evidence presented before the Labour Tribunal. Finding: The High Court Judge erred in placing undue reliance on the domestic inquiry proceedings to overturn the Labour Tribunal's order. Issue 2: Scope of appellate review Rule applied: Section 31D(2) of the Industrial Disputes Act. Court's reasoning: The Court reiterated that appeals from a Labour Tribunal are limited to questions of law. Citing Jayasuriya v. Sri Lanka State Plantations Corporation, the Court noted that appellate courts may only intervene if a finding is wholly unsupported by evidence, ignores material evidence, or involves a misdirection in law. The Court found that the Labour Tribunal President had properly evaluated the evidence and that the High Court Judge failed to identify any valid legal error that would justify interfering with the Tribunalâ€™s findings of fact. Finding: The High Court Judge erred by reviewing the Labour Tribunalâ€™s order beyond the permissible scope of a question of law. Interaction of Issues The findings on the first two issues are intrinsically linked. Because the High Court Judge improperly prioritized the domestic inquiry process over the evidence presented at the Labour Tribunal, he failed to adhere to the statutory limitations of his appellate jurisdiction. By treating the domestic inquiry's procedural history as a basis for overturning the Tribunal's factual findings, the High Court Judge effectively engaged in a re-evaluation of facts rather than a review of law, rendering the entire appellate intervention invalid. Conclusion The Supreme Court allowed the appeal and set aside the judgment of the High Court. The order of the Labour Tribunal dated 16th October 2009, which held that the termination of the Applicant was justified, was affirmed. Keywords Labour Tribunal — Termination of services — Domestic inquiry — Evidence at inquiry — Review of Labour Tribunal order — Questions of law — Justification for termination — Industrial Disputes Act — Wrongful termination — Relief ordered by High Court		SC/APPEAL/148/2012	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_148_2012.pdf
sc-rule-08-2014	24 September 2020	Banda v. Wijewardena	P. Padman Surasena, J.	Vijith K Malalgoda, J; P. Padman Surasena, J.; S Thurairaja, J	Rule issued against Attorney-at-Law — Disciplinary proceedings — Professional misconduct — Absence from court — Failure to file re-listing application — Deceit — Malpractice — Fundamental Rights Application — Attorney-at-Law's duties — Due diligence	Facts The Complainant, R M Karunaratne Banda, retained the Respondent, Wasantha Wijewardena, an Attorney-at-Law, to file a Fundamental Rights application in the Supreme Court. The Complainant paid the full professional fees requested by the Respondent. The Respondent filed the application, which was listed for support on 17-06-2009. The Respondent failed to appear on that date, though he arranged for another attorney to request a postponement, and the court re-fixed the matter for 20-07-2009. The Respondent failed to appear on 20-07-2009, resulting in the dismissal of the application for non-prosecution. Following the dismissal, the Respondent failed to take timely steps to restore the application. He eventually filed a motion on 19-10-2009 to re-list the case, which was rejected by the Supreme Court due to the delay. The Complainant alleged that the Respondent misled him by providing a motion document that appeared to be filed in court when it had not been. A disciplinary committee of the Bar Association of Sri Lanka inquired into the matter and concluded that the Respondent failed to exercise due diligence. The Supreme Court subsequently issued a Rule against the Respondent under the Judicature Act. Issues Whether the Respondentâ€™s failure to appear in court on 17-06-2009 and 20-07-2009, resulting in the dismissal of the client's application, constitutes professional misconduct under the Judicature Act and the Supreme Court (Conduct of and Etiquette for Attorneys-at-Law) Rules 1988. Whether the Respondentâ€™s failure to file a re-listing application in a timely manner and his act of misleading the client regarding the filing of a motion constitute deceit or malpractice under Section 42 of the Judicature Act. Applicable Law Statutes: Judicature Act No. 2 of 1978, Section 42(2) and 42(3) Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 136 Cases Referred to: Daniel v. Chandradewa - [1994/v2-sri-lr-1] Piyadasa v. Kurukulasuriya - [1997/v2-sri-lr-410] Ranaweera v. Jinadasa - [sc-appeal-41/1991] Analysis Issue 1: Failure to appear Rule applied: Rule 16 of the Supreme Court (Conduct of and Etiquette for Attorneys-at-Law) Rules 1988, which mandates that an attorney must appear in proceedings unless prevented by circumstances beyond their control. Court's reasoning: The Respondent admitted that it was his responsibility to ensure representation on the dates fixed for support. The court noted that the Respondent failed to appear or arrange for counsel on 20-07-2009, leading to the dismissal of the case. Relying on Daniel v. Chandradewa, the court emphasized that a registered attorney has a personal duty to track case dates and ensure representation. The court rejected the Respondent's excuses, noting that he had been informed of the date by his client and failed to act with due diligence. Finding: The Respondent was in default of his professional obligations, and this conduct is established as professional misconduct. Issue 2: Failure to restore and deceit Rule applied: Rule 15 of the Supreme Court (Conduct of and Etiquette for Attorneys-at-Law) Rules 1988, requiring an attorney to exercise skill with due diligence and care in the best interests of the client. Court's reasoning: The Respondent failed to file a re-listing application for three months after the dismissal. Furthermore, the court found that the Respondent provided the Complainant with a signed and sealed motion (P1) that was never filed in court, effectively deceiving the client into believing the matter was being pursued. The court rejected the Respondent's claim that this was merely a draft, noting that the document was complete and signed. The court held that this conduct constitutes deceit and malpractice. Finding: The Respondent's failure to act and his deceptive conduct toward the client are proven, amounting to malpractice and deceit under Section 42 of the Judicature Act. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues reinforce the conclusion that the Respondent demonstrated a persistent lack of professional diligence. The failure to appear (Issue 1) initiated the harm to the client, while the subsequent failure to restore the case and the act of deceiving the client (Issue 2) aggravated the breach of trust, demonstrating a pattern of conduct that renders the Respondent unfit to practice. Conclusion The Respondent is found guilty of deceit and malpractice. He is suspended from practice for a period of seven years and ordered to pay compensation of Rs. 300,000 to the Complainant within one month. Keywords Rule issued against Attorney-at-Law — Disciplinary proceedings — Professional misconduct — Absence from court — Failure to file re-listing application — Deceit — Malpractice — Fundamental Rights Application — Attorney-at-Law's duties — Due diligence		SC/APPEAL/8/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_08_2014-1.pdf
sc-hcca-la-404-2013	24 September 2020	Preethi Reeta Bastian and Others v. Pattiyage Leelawathie Gomes	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J.	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J.; Murdu N.B. Fernando, PC, J.; Yasantha Kodagoda, PC, J.	leave to appeal — Supreme Court Rules — time limit — jurisdiction — mandatory compliance — civil appellate jurisdiction — declaration of title — ejectment — procedural rules — substantial question of law	Facts The case revolves around an application for leave to appeal concerning a judgment delivered by the Civil Appellate High Court affirming a decision made by the District Court of Mount Lavinia. The Plaintiffs, who are the substituted parties in this case, initiated legal proceedings in the District Court seeking a declaration of title and the ejectment of the Defendants from a specified property. The District Court ruled in favor of the Plaintiffs, leading the Defendants to appeal to the Civil Appellate High Court. However, the Civil Appellate High Court dismissed the appeal on 28th August 2013, prompting the Defendants to file an application for leave to appeal to the Supreme Court on 10th October 2013. A preliminary objection was raised by the Plaintiffs regarding the timeliness of this application, asserting that it was filed beyond the six-week period stipulated in the Supreme Court Rules. Issues The primary issues in this case can be framed as follows: Whether the application for leave to appeal was filed within the six-week time limit as required by the Supreme Court Rules? Whether the non-compliance with the time limit is fatal to the maintainability of the application? Applicable Law Statutes: Supreme Court Rules of 1990; High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990; High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Amendment Act No. 54 of 2006. Cases Referred to: L. A. Sudath Rohana and another v. Mohamed Cassim Mohemmed Zeena - SC/HCCA/LA/111/2010; George Stuart and Co. Ltd. v. Lankem Tea and Rubber Plantations (Pvt.) Ltd. - (2004) 1 SLR 246; Priyanthi Chandrika Jinadasa v. Pathma Hemamali and 4 Others - (2011) 1 SLR 337; Mahaweli Authority of Sri Lanka v. United Agency Construction (Pvt) Ltd - (2002) 1 SLR 8; Nirmala de Mel v. Seneviratne - (1982) 2 SLR 569; The Ceylon Brewery Ltd. v. Jax Fernando, Proprietor, Maradana Wine Stores - (2001) 1 SLR 270; Edward v. De Silva - (1945) 46 NLR 342. Analysis Issue 1: Whether the application for leave to appeal was filed within the six-week time limit as required by the Supreme Court Rules? Rule applied: Supreme Court Rules of 1990, Rule 7. Court's reasoning: The Supreme Court examined the timeline of events leading to the filing of the application for leave to appeal. The judgment of the Civil Appellate High Court was delivered on 28th August 2013, and the Defendants filed their application on 10th October 2013. The Court noted that Rule 7 of the Supreme Court Rules mandates that applications for leave to appeal must be filed within six weeks of the judgment. The Court emphasized that the first day of the judgment must be excluded from the calculation of the six-week period. Therefore, the six-week period commenced on 29th August 2013 and concluded on 9th October 2013. Since the application was filed on 10th October 2013, it was deemed to be out of time. The Court further referenced previous rulings, including George Stuart and Co. Ltd. v. Lankem Tea and Rubber Plantations (Pvt.) Ltd., which established that when no specific time frame is provided in the relevant Act, the Supreme Court Rules apply. The Court concluded that the Defendants failed to comply with the mandatory six-week time limit stipulated in Rule 7. Finding: The application for leave to appeal was filed out of time. Issue 2: Whether the non-compliance with the time limit is fatal to the maintainability of the application? Rule applied: Supreme Court Rules of 1990, Rule 7. Court's reasoning: The Court considered the implications of the Defendants' failure to adhere to the time limit. The learned Counsel for the Defendants argued that the Court could exercise discretion to waive the time limit based on precedents. However, the Court distinguished the cited case of Nirmala de Mel v. Seneviratne, noting that it was decided before the current Supreme Court Rules were established and thus was not applicable. The Court reiterated that procedural rules regarding time limits are essential for maintaining uniformity and efficiency within the legal system. The Court emphasized that compliance with Rule 7 is mandatory and that failure to comply with such rules affects the jurisdiction of the court. The Court referenced several cases to support this position, asserting that the time limits are not mere technicalities but fundamental to the court's jurisdiction. Finding: The non-compliance with the time limit was fatal to the maintainability of the application. Interaction of Issues The issues are interrelated as the determination of the timeliness of the application directly impacts its maintainability. The Court's analysis of the time limit established the foundation for its conclusion regarding the application’s viability. The strict adherence to procedural rules ensures the integrity of the judicial process and the efficient functioning of the legal system. Conclusion The Supreme Court upheld the preliminary objection raised by the Plaintiffs, concluding that the Defendants had not complied with the mandatory time limit set forth in Rule 7 of the Supreme Court Rules. Consequently, the application for leave to appeal was dismissed, and no costs were ordered. The ruling underscores the importance of adhering to procedural timelines in legal proceedings, reinforcing the principle that compliance with established rules is essential for the exercise of judicial authority. Keywords leave to appeal — Supreme Court Rules — time limit — jurisdiction — mandatory compliance — civil appellate jurisdiction — declaration of title — ejectment — procedural rules — substantial question of law	Venura Cooray for the Defendants-Appellants-Petitioners; J.A.J Udawatta for the 1st, 3rd and 4th substituted Plaintiffs-Respondents-Respondents	SC/HC CALA/404/2013	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_hc_cala_404_2013.pdf
sc-rule-8-2014	25 September 2020	R M Karunaratne Banda v. Wasantha Wijewardena	P Padman Surasena, J.	Vijith K Malalgoda PC J; P Padman Surasena J; S Thurai Raja PC J	Judicature Act — Attorney-at-Law — Disciplinary Committee — Fundamental Rights Application — Professional Misconduct — Due Diligence — Malpractice — Deceit — Suspension from Practice — Legal Ethics — Court Proceedings — Client Representation — Legal Fees — Inquiry Process — Supreme Court Rules	Facts The case revolves around a complaint made against Mr. Wasantha Wijewardena, an Attorney-at-Law, by R M Karunaratne Banda, the Complainant. The complaint was based on allegations of professional misconduct, specifically regarding the handling of a Fundamental Rights Application (No. SC FR 445/2009) that the Respondent was retained to file on behalf of the Complainant. The Complainant had paid a total fee of Rs. 100,000, of which Rs. 50,000 was paid upfront. The Respondent failed to appear in court on two scheduled dates (17 June 2009 and 20 July 2009), resulting in the dismissal of the application due to the Complainant being unrepresented. The Respondent also failed to file a timely re-listing application after the dismissal, which was not submitted until 19 October 2009, and was subsequently rejected by the Supreme Court. The disciplinary committee of the Bar Association of Sri Lanka conducted a preliminary inquiry and concluded that the Respondent had not shown due diligence in his professional obligations, leading to the referral of the case to the Supreme Court for further action. Issues The primary issues in this case can be framed as follows: Whether the Respondent Attorney-at-Law failed to inform the Complainant about the time bar concerning the Fundamental Rights Application? Whether the Respondent Attorney-at-Law's failure to appear in court on the scheduled dates constituted professional misconduct? Whether the Respondent Attorney-at-Law's delay in filing a re-listing application amounted to malpractice? Applicable Law Statutes: Judicature Act No. 2 of 1978; Supreme Court (Conduct of and Etiquette for Attorneys-at-Law) Rules 1988. Cases Referred to: Daniel vs Chandradewa - [1994 2 Sri LR 1]; Piyadasa Vs Kurukulasuriya Attorney-at-Law - [1997 2 Sri LR 410]. Analysis Issue 1: Whether the Respondent Attorney-at-Law failed to inform the Complainant about the time bar concerning the Fundamental Rights Application? Rule applied: The relevant provisions of the Judicature Act and the Supreme Court Rules regarding the duties of Attorneys-at-Law. Court's reasoning: The Court refrained from making a definitive ruling on this issue, allowing the Respondent to benefit from any doubt regarding the alleged failure to inform the Complainant about the time bar. The Court acknowledged that there were ongoing proceedings before the Human Rights Commission, which could imply a continuing infringement of rights. Finding: The Court did not make a conclusive finding on this issue. Issue 2: Whether the Respondent Attorney-at-Law's failure to appear in court on the scheduled dates constituted professional misconduct? Rule applied: Rule 16 of the Supreme Court (Conduct of and Etiquette for Attorneys-at-Law) Rules 1988. Court's reasoning: The Respondent admitted his responsibility to ensure representation on the scheduled dates. The Court cited previous cases where Attorneys-at-Law were held accountable for failing to appear in court, emphasizing the duty of diligence owed to clients. The Respondent's failure to appear resulted in the dismissal of the Complainant's application, which was deemed a serious breach of professional conduct. Finding: The Court found the Respondent guilty of professional misconduct for failing to appear in court. Issue 3: Whether the Respondent Attorney-at-Law's delay in filing a re-listing application amounted to malpractice? Rule applied: Rule 15 of the Supreme Court (Conduct of and Etiquette for Attorneys-at-Law) Rules 1988. Court's reasoning: The Respondent's delay in filing the re-listing application was scrutinized, and it was established that he failed to act promptly after the dismissal of the application. The Court noted that the Respondent had admitted to his responsibility but offered inadequate explanations for his inaction. The Court highlighted the importance of timely action in legal proceedings and the detrimental impact of the Respondent's delay on the Complainant's case. Finding: The Court found the Respondent guilty of malpractice due to the delay in filing the re-listing application. Interaction of Issues The issues are interrelated as they all stem from the Respondent's failure to fulfill his professional obligations towards the Complainant. The failure to inform about the time bar, the absence in court, and the delay in filing the re-listing application collectively demonstrate a pattern of negligence and malpractice that undermines the integrity of the legal profession. Conclusion The Supreme Court concluded that the Respondent Attorney-at-Law, Mr. Wasantha Wijewardena, was guilty of deceit and malpractice as defined under the Judicature Act. The Court ordered a suspension from practice for seven years and mandated the Respondent to pay Rs. 300,000 to the Complainant as compensation for the damages incurred due to his professional misconduct. The Court emphasized the need to uphold the standards of the legal profession and protect the interests of clients. The Registrar was instructed to take necessary steps to enforce the order. Keywords Judicature Act — Attorney-at-Law — Disciplinary Committee — Fundamental Rights Application — Professional Misconduct — Due Diligence — Malpractice — Deceit — Suspension from Practice — Legal Ethics — Court Proceedings — Client Representation — Legal Fees — Inquiry Process — Supreme Court Rules	Dr. Avanthi Perera SSC with Sureka Ahamad SC for Attorney General; Rohan Sahabandu PC for the Bar Association of Sri Lanka; Respondent Wasantha Wijewardena Attorney-at-Law appeared in person.	SC/APPEAL/8/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_08_2014-1.pdf
sc-appeal-54-2018	29 September 2020	Nestle Lanka PLC v. Rajapakshe	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Industrial Disputes Act — High Courts of Provinces (Special Provisions) Act — Leave to Appeal — Supreme Court Rules — Due diligence — Preliminary objection — Compliance with rules — Special Leave to Appeal Applications	Facts Nestle Lanka PLC, the Petitioner, filed an application for leave to appeal against a judgment of the High Court of Negombo, which had dismissed the Petitioner's appeal against an order of the Labour Tribunal. The Petitioner filed the petition and affidavit on 10 May 2018 but failed to include the certified copy of the appeal brief at that time. The Petitioner sought permission to tender the brief later, claiming it was being obtained. The certified copy of the appeal brief was only filed on 24 July 2018. The Respondent, Gamini Rajapakshe, filed an affidavit on 27 August 2018, moving for the dismissal of the application in limine due to non-compliance with the Supreme Court Rules 1990. The Respondent contended that the Petitioner failed to comply with Rules 2, 6, and 8, specifically regarding the timely filing of necessary documents and the service of materials upon the Respondent. The Petitioner argued that the Supreme Court Rules 1990 were inapplicable to this application and that any procedural lapses were excusable. Issues Whether the Supreme Court Rules 1990 are applicable to an application for leave to appeal against a judgment of the High Court arising from an order of a Labour Tribunal. Whether the Petitioner failed to comply with Rules 2 and 8 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990, and if so, whether such non-compliance warrants the dismissal of the application. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka (Article 136) Industrial Disputes Act No. 43 of 1950 (as amended) High Courts of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 Cases Referred to: Rambanda v Peoples Bank - [sc-appeal-229/2011] Aaron Senarath v The Manager Moray Estate - [sc-spl/la-231/2015] Sudath Rohana v Mohamed Zeena - [2011/v2-sri-lr-134] Udaya Shantha v Jeevan Kumarathunga - [2012/v1-blr-129] A.D. Bandara v H.M.L. Menike - [sc-appeal-172/2011] K. Reaindran v K. Velusomasunderan - [sc-spl-la-298/99] N.A. Premadasa v The Peoples Bank - [sc-spl-la-212/99] Hameed v Majibdeen - [sc-spl-la-38/2001] K.M. Samarasinghe v R.M.D. Ratnayake - [sc-spl-la-51/2001] Soong Che Foo v Harosha K. De Silva - [sc-spl-la-184/2003] C.A. Haroon v S.K. Muzoor - [sc-spl-la-158/2006] Woodman Exports (Pvt) Ltd v Commissioner General of Labour - [sc-spl-la-335/2008] Tissa Attanayake v Commissioner General of Election - [sc-spl-la-55/2011] Samantha Niroshana v Senarath Abeyruwan - [sc-spl-la-145/2006] A.H.M. Fowzie v Vehicles Lanka (Pvt) Ltd - [sc-spl-la-286/2007] Wickramatillake v Marikkar - [2-nlr-9] Attanayake v Commissioner General of Elections - [2011/v1-sri-lr-220] Peiris v Peiris - [sc-hc-ca/la-137/12] Analysis Issue 1: Applicability of Supreme Court Rules Rule applied: Article 136 of the Constitution and the Supreme Court Rules 1990. Court's reasoning: The Court held that the 1990 Rules govern the practice and procedure of the Supreme Court. While the Petitioner argued that Part IA of the Rules applies only to Court of Appeal judgments, the Court noted that the Rules do not explicitly exclude High Court judgments. The legislative change in 1990 merely shifted the forum of the first appeal from the Court of Appeal to the High Court; it did not alter the final appellate jurisdiction of the Supreme Court or the procedural requirements for invoking it. Finding: The Supreme Court Rules 1990 are applicable to the instant application. Issue 2: Compliance with Rules 2 and 8 Rule applied: Rules 2, 6, and 8 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990. Court's reasoning: Rule 2 requires a petitioner to file all necessary documents or, if unable, to demonstrate due diligence and seek permission. The Petitioner failed to disclose why the appeal brief was not filed initially and failed to prove that the delay was due to circumstances beyond their control. Furthermore, Rule 8 requires the Petitioner to ensure the Respondent is served with all materials "forthwith." The Petitioner delayed service of the necessary case record on the Respondent for eight months, which prejudiced the Respondent's right to enjoy the benefit of the Labour Tribunal order. Finding: The Petitioner failed to exercise due diligence and failed to comply with mandatory procedural rules. Interaction of Issues The finding that the Supreme Court Rules are applicable (Issue 1) establishes the mandatory framework that the Petitioner failed to satisfy (Issue 2). The procedural failures regarding the filing of documents and service of notice were not mere technicalities but were breaches of rules designed to ensure the efficient administration of justice, thereby necessitating the dismissal of the application. Conclusion The preliminary objection is upheld. The application for leave to appeal is dismissed for non-compliance with Rules 2 and 8 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990. Keywords Industrial Disputes Act — High Courts of Provinces (Special Provisions) Act — Leave to Appeal — Supreme Court Rules — Due diligence — Preliminary objection — Compliance with rules — Special Leave to Appeal Applications		SC/APPEAL/54/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_54_2018.pdf
sc-fr-230-2018	29 September 2020	Rumy and Samaraweera v. Athukorale and Others	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Inquirer into sudden deaths — Colombo City Coroner — Qualifications for appointment — Scheme of recruitment — Gazette notifications — Judicial function — Attorney-at-Law — Public service	Facts The Petitioners, M. Ashroff Rumy and Ms. Iresha Deshani Samaraweera, are Attorneys-at-Law appointed as Inquirers into Sudden Deaths for the Colombo City division. They challenged the administrative decision of the Ministry of Justice, specifically the letter marked X6, which assigned the 4th and 5th Respondents to perform duties as Additional Colombo City Coroners alongside the Petitioners. The Petitioners contended that the 4th and 5th Respondents were unqualified for these positions because they are not Attorneys-at-Law. They argued that the post of Colombo City Coroner requires legal qualifications based on a historical scheme of recruitment and the nature of the functions performed. The 2nd Respondent, the Secretary to the Ministry of Justice, disputed the existence of a permanent, salaried post of Colombo City Coroner, asserting that the position is an honorary one for a fixed term with an allowance per inquiry. The Respondents maintained that the 4th and 5th Respondents were appointed following a valid recruitment process initiated by a Gazette notification. The Court noted that the recruitment criteria for Inquirers had varied significantly over time, ranging from G.C.E. Ordinary Level qualifications to professional legal qualifications. The 4th and 5th Respondents were appointed in 2012, predating the Petitioners, and were subsequently reassigned to share the workload in the Colombo City division to mitigate delays in conducting inquests. Issues Whether the appointment and assignment of the 4th and 5th Respondents as Additional Colombo City Coroners violated the Petitioners' right to equality under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether there exists a mandatory, legally binding scheme of recruitment requiring an Inquirer into Sudden Deaths for Colombo City to be an Attorney-at-Law. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 12(1) Code of Criminal Procedure Act No. 15 of 1979, Section 108 Cases Referred to: Ananda Dharmadasa v. Ariyaratne Hewage - [2008/v2-sri-lr-19] Tuan Ishan Raban v. Members of the Police Commission - [2007/v2-sri-lr-351] Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution, which guarantees equality before the law and equal protection of the law. Court's reasoning: The Court held that classifications are permissible if they are not arbitrary and are founded upon an intelligible differentia. The evidence showed that the 4th and 5th Respondents were appointed through a standard recruitment process and were reassigned to address administrative delays and workload issues. The Court found no evidence that the distinction in qualifications or the assignment of duties was based on arbitrary criteria or resulted in unequal treatment of similarly situated individuals. Finding: No violation of Article 12(1) was established. Issue 2: Existence of a mandatory recruitment scheme Rule applied: Section 108 of the Code of Criminal Procedure Act No. 15 of 1979. Court's reasoning: The Court examined the documents provided by the Petitioners (X4) and compared them with the actual Gazette notifications (X5). The discrepancies between the alleged scheme of recruitment and the actual terms of appointment—specifically regarding salary and the nature of the post—led the Court to conclude that no such approved, permanent scheme existed as claimed. The Court noted that the Minister has the discretion under Section 108 to appoint persons as Inquirers, and the qualifications for such roles have historically fluctuated based on administrative needs. Finding: The Court concluded that no mandatory scheme of recruitment requiring an Attorney-at-Law for the post exists. Interaction of Issues The findings are intrinsically linked; because the Petitioners failed to prove the existence of a mandatory legal requirement for the post (Issue 2), their claim that the appointment of non-lawyers constituted a violation of their constitutional right to equality (Issue 1) necessarily failed. The absence of a fixed, objective scheme meant the administrative action taken by the Ministry was within the scope of its discretionary power under the Code of Criminal Procedure Act. Conclusion The Court held that the Petitioners failed to establish an infringement of their fundamental rights under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. The application is dismissed. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Inquirer into sudden deaths — Colombo City Coroner — Qualifications for appointment — Scheme of recruitment — Gazette notifications — Judicial function — Attorney-at-Law — Public service		SC/FR/230/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_230_2018.pdf
sc-hc-la-54-2018	30 September 2020	Nestle Lanka PLC v. Gamini Rajapakshe	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, CJ	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, CJ; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Industrial Disputes Act — High Court of Provinces (Special Provisions) Act — Leave to Appeal — Supreme Court Rules — Procedural Compliance — Labour Tribunal — Judicial Delay — Due Diligence — Preliminary Objection — Appellate Jurisdiction — Legal Representation — Affidavit Submission — Certified Copies — Notification Requirements — Jurisprudence	Facts The case revolves around an application for Leave to Appeal filed by Nestle Lanka PLC (the Petitioner) against the judgment of the High Court of Negombo dated 29 March 2018, which dismissed their appeal against a Labour Tribunal's decision that had ruled in favor of Gamini Rajapakshe (the Respondent). The Respondent had initially challenged his dismissal from service by the Petitioner before the Labour Tribunal, which found that his termination was unjust and awarded him compensation. Following the Tribunal's ruling, the Petitioner appealed to the High Court, which upheld the Tribunal's decision. The Petitioner subsequently sought Leave to Appeal to the Supreme Court, citing sections of the Industrial Disputes Act and the High Court of Provinces (Special Provisions) Act. However, the application faced preliminary objections regarding procedural compliance with the Supreme Court Rules. Issues Whether the Supreme Court Rules are applicable to the application for Leave to Appeal filed by the Petitioner. Whether the Petitioner complied with the requirements of the Supreme Court Rules, particularly Rules 2, 6, and 8, regarding the submission of necessary documents and timely notification to the Respondent. Applicable Law Statutes: Industrial Disputes Act No. 43 of 1950 (as amended) — High Court of Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19 of 1990 Cases Referred to: Rambanda v People's Bank - [SC Spl. LA No. 229/11]; Aaron Senarath v The Manager Moray Estate - [SC SPL/LA 231/2015]; Udaya Shantha v Jeevan Kumarathunga - [SC/Spl/LA 49/2010]; Attanayake v Commissioner General of Elections - [2011] 1 SLR 220; Peiris and another v Peiris - [SC/HC CA/LA137/12]. Analysis Issue 1: Applicability of Supreme Court Rules Rule applied: Supreme Court Rules 1990 Court's reasoning: The Petitioner contended that the Supreme Court Rules, particularly those pertaining to Special Leave to Appeal, were not applicable to their application since it was not an appeal from the Court of Appeal but rather a Leave to Appeal from a High Court judgment. The Court examined the legislative framework and the historical context of the Supreme Court Rules, noting that the Rules were established under Article 136 of the Constitution to regulate the practice and procedure of the Supreme Court. The Court found that the absence of explicit exclusion of High Court judgments from the applicability of the Rules indicated that they were indeed applicable to the current case. The Court referenced previous judgments that highlighted the importance of procedural compliance and the need for clarity in the application of the Rules. Finding: The Court concluded that the Supreme Court Rules 1990 were applicable to the application for Leave to Appeal. Issue 2: Compliance with Supreme Court Rules Rule applied: Rules 2, 6, and 8 of the Supreme Court Rules 1990 Court's reasoning: The Respondent raised a preliminary objection, asserting that the Petitioner failed to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in the Supreme Court Rules, particularly regarding the timely submission of necessary documents and the notification of the Respondent. The Court scrutinized the timeline of events, noting that the Petitioner had filed their application without the required certified copies of the appeal brief and had delayed in serving the Respondent with the necessary documents. The Court emphasized that the Rules were designed to ensure that all parties were adequately notified and had the opportunity to respond without undue delay. The Petitioner’s failure to demonstrate due diligence in obtaining and submitting the required documents was highlighted, as well as the lack of a valid explanation for the delays. Finding: The Court upheld the preliminary objection and dismissed the application for Leave to Appeal due to non-compliance with the Supreme Court Rules. Interaction of Issues The issues of applicability and compliance with the Supreme Court Rules are interrelated, as the determination of whether the Rules apply directly influences the assessment of the Petitioner’s compliance. The Court's analysis of the applicability of the Rules set the stage for evaluating the Petitioner’s actions and the subsequent procedural shortcomings. Conclusion The Supreme Court dismissed the Petition for Leave to Appeal, ruling that the Petitioner had failed to comply with the Supreme Court Rules, particularly Rules 2 and 8, which resulted in a denial of the Respondent's right to a fair opportunity to contest the matter. The judgment underscored the importance of procedural adherence in the judicial process, emphasizing that parties must act with due diligence to uphold the integrity of the legal system. Keywords Industrial Disputes Act — High Court of Provinces (Special Provisions) Act — Leave to Appeal — Supreme Court Rules — Procedural Compliance — Labour Tribunal — Judicial Delay — Due Diligence — Preliminary Objection — Appellate Jurisdiction — Legal Representation — Affidavit Submission — Certified Copies — Notification Requirements — Jurisprudence	Manohara de Silva, PC with Hirosha Munasinghe for the Respondent-Appellant-Petitioner instructed by D.L. & F. de Saram. Dilip Obeysekera with Sanjeewa Dissanayake for the Applicant-Respondent-Respondent.	SC/HCLA/54/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_hcla_54_2018.pdf
sc-appeal-228-2017	1 October 2020	Rodrigo v. Central Engineering Consultancy Bureau	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Industrial Disputes Act — Labour Tribunals — Fundamental rights application — Section 31B(5) — Jurisdiction of Labour Tribunal — Res judicata — Equality before the law — Termination of employment — Just and equitable	Facts W.K.P.I. Rodrigo was employed by the Central Engineering Consultancy Bureau as a Civil Engineer. Following a disciplinary inquiry, his services were terminated on 14th October 2013. The employee subsequently filed a fundamental rights application in the Supreme Court alleging violations of Articles 12(1), 12(2), and 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Thereafter, he filed an application in the Labour Tribunal of Colombo challenging the termination of his services. The employer raised a preliminary objection in the Labour Tribunal under Section 31B(5) of the Industrial Disputes Act, contending that the employee was precluded from maintaining the Labour Tribunal application because he had previously resorted to a fundamental rights application in the Supreme Court. The Labour Tribunal upheld the objection and dismissed the application. The employee appealed this decision to the High Court of Colombo, which affirmed the Labour Tribunal's order. The employee then appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the learned High Court Judge erred in law in the interpretation and application of Section 31B(3) and Section 31B(5) of the Industrial Disputes Act No. 43. Whether the learned High Court Judge erred in holding that two cases cannot be filed regarding the same incident. Whether the learned High Court Judge erred in law in upholding the dismissal of the employee's application. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 12(2), 14(1)(g), 17, 126(4). Industrial Disputes Act No. 43 (as amended), Sections 3, 4, 31A, 31B, 31C, 31DD, 33. Industrial Disputes (Amendment) Act No. 62. High Courts of the Provinces (Special Provisions) Act No. 19. Cases Referred to: Colombo Appothecaries Co. Ltd v. Wijesooriya - [70-nlr-481] The United Engineering Workers Union v. Devanayagam - [69-nlr-289] Richard Pieris and Co. Ltd. v. Wijesiriwardena - [62-nlr-233] Mendis v. RVDB - [80-clw-49] Ceylon Tobacco Co. Ltd v. Illangasinghe - [1986/v1-sri-lr-1] Construction Liason Ltd v. Fernando - [1988/ii-calr-122] Independent Newspapers Ltd v. Commercial and Industrial Workers Union - [1997/v3-sri-lr-197] Fernando v. Standard Chartered Bank - [2011-balr-242] Tri-Star Apparels Exports (Pvt) Ltd v. Gajanayake - [sc-appeal-85/2003] Sri Lanka State Plantations Corporation v. Dharmawansa - [2006/v1-sri-lr-346] Ceylon Estate Staff Union v. The Superintendent, Sunderland Estate - [2012-balr-9] Colombo Muncipal Council Employees Co-Operative Thrift and Savings Society v. Hettiarachchi - [sc-appeal-136/2009] Palihawadana v. Attorney General - [1978/v1-sri-lr] Gamaethige v. Siriwardene - [1988/ii-calr-62] Analysis Issue 1: Interpretation of Section 31B(5) Rule applied: Section 31B(5) of the Industrial Disputes Act No. 43. Court's reasoning: The court held that Section 31B(5) must be interpreted restrictively. The second limb of the section, which bars a remedy if a workman has "first resorted to any other legal remedy," requires that the previous proceedings be "taken and concluded." Mere commencement of proceedings in another forum does not trigger the bar. Furthermore, the bar only applies if the previous remedy and the Labour Tribunal application share the same subject matter, substantive relief, and core issue of "good cause" for termination, and if there is no significant procedural disparity. Finding: The High Court erred by interpreting the section as an automatic bar upon the mere filing of a fundamental rights application. Issue 2: Filing of multiple cases Rule applied: Section 31B(3)(b) and Section 31B(5) of the Industrial Disputes Act. Court's reasoning: The court distinguished between public law fundamental rights applications and private law Labour Tribunal applications. Fundamental rights applications focus on state action and constitutional violations, whereas Labour Tribunals focus on the equity of termination in an employer-employee relationship. Because these forums serve different purposes and apply different principles, they do not constitute the "same matter" for the purposes of res judicata or Section 31B(5). Finding: The court found that filing a fundamental rights application does not preclude a Labour Tribunal application. The appropriate procedure is for the Labour Tribunal to suspend its proceedings under Section 31B(3)(b) until the conclusion of the other proceedings. Issue 3: Dismissal of the application Rule applied: Section 31B(3)(b) and Section 31B(5) of the Industrial Disputes Act. Court's reasoning: The court concluded that the Labour Tribunal and High Court incorrectly dismissed the application. The correct legal approach was to suspend the Labour Tribunal proceedings pending the outcome of the Supreme Court fundamental rights application, rather than dismissing the claim entirely. Finding: The dismissal was legally erroneous. Interaction of Issues The findings are interdependent; the restrictive interpretation of "resorted to" in Section 31B(5) (Issue 1) necessitates that the Labour Tribunal should not dismiss claims prematurely. This reinforces the court's conclusion that the proper mechanism for managing concurrent proceedings is the suspension of the Labour Tribunal application under Section 31B(3)(b) (Issue 2), thereby rendering the dismissal of the application (Issue 3) an error of law. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The judgment of the High Court and the order of the Labour Tribunal are set aside, and the Labour Tribunal is directed to rehear the application. Keywords Industrial Disputes Act — Labour Tribunals — Fundamental rights application — Section 31B(5) — Jurisdiction of Labour Tribunal — Res judicata — Equality before the law — Termination of employment — Just and equitable		SC/APPEAL/228/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_228_2017.pdf
sc-fr-357-2018	13 October 2020	Unawatuna v. Sirisena and Others	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J	Priyantha Jayawardena, PC, J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Dissolution of Parliament — Presidential Proclamation — Article 126 of the Constitution — Article 24(3) of the Constitution — Translation of court records — Interpretation of constitutional provisions — Restriction on translation requests	Facts The petitioner, an Attorney-at-Law, filed a fundamental rights application challenging the Proclamation issued by the former President dissolving Parliament and calling for a General Election. The petitioner alleged that the Proclamation violated several articles of the Constitution. The petitioner subsequently filed a motion requesting the court to provide a Sinhala translation of the judgment delivered in a separate, similar fundamental rights application, SC/FR/351/2018. The petitioner claimed he had previously requested this translation from the former President without success. The court considered whether the petitioner was entitled to such a translation under the provisions of the Constitution. Issues Whether the petitioner is entitled under Article 24(3) of the Constitution to obtain a Sinhala translation of a judgment delivered in a case to which he was not a party. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 10, 12(1), 12(2), 14(1)(a), 14(1)(c), 14(1)(f), 24(3), 24(5), 35. Cases Referred to: [sc-fr-351/2018] Analysis Issue 1: Entitlement to translation of court records Rule applied: Article 24(3) and Article 24(5) of the Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka. Court's reasoning: The court examined Article 24(3), which entitles a judge, juror, party, applicant, or their legal representative to obtain translations of court records if they are not conversant with the language used in court. Article 24(5) defines "record" to include judgments. The court interpreted these provisions as restricting the right to obtain translations specifically to those individuals who are parties or legal representatives in the proceedings of the case in question. The court noted that the petitioner was neither a party nor an attorney representing a party in the referenced case, SC/FR/351/2018. Consequently, the court held that the petitioner did not fall within the category of persons entitled to request such a translation under the Constitution. Finding: The petitioner is not entitled to obtain a Sinhala translation of the judgment in SC/FR/351/2018 because he was not a party or legal representative in that matter. Conclusion The motion filed by the petitioner on 31st December 2018 requesting a Sinhala translation of the judgment in SC/FR/351/2018 is rejected. Keywords Fundamental rights — Dissolution of Parliament — Presidential Proclamation — Article 126 of the Constitution — Article 24(3) of the Constitution — Translation of court records — Interpretation of constitutional provisions — Restriction on translation requests		SC/FR/357/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_357_2018.pdf
sc-appeal-125-2018	16 October 2020	Dharmalingam v. Sivalingam	Aluwihare PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; V. K. Malalgoda PC, J; Preethi Padman Surasena J, J.	Prescriptive title — Adverse possession — Section 41 of the Civil Procedure Code — Cause of action — High Court of Civil Appeal — District Court — Section 3 of the Prescription Ordinance — Right of way	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondent and the Defendant-Respondent-Petitioner are brothers who resided in the premises at No. 23, Sedawatte, Wellanpitiya. The property was originally rented by their parents and subsequently vested with the Commissioner of National Housing under the Ceiling on Housing Property Law. Following the death of their father, the mother of the parties was granted the property by the Commissioner. The mother later transferred the property to the Defendant via a Deed of Gift. The Plaintiff instituted action in the District Court seeking a declaration of prescriptive title to a half share of the premises, asserting that he had occupied a distinct portion of the house and managed household expenses after his father's death. The District Court dismissed the Plaintiff's action, holding that he failed to identify the specific portion of the corpus through a plan, rendering a decree unexecutable. The Plaintiff appealed to the High Court of Civil Appeal, which set aside the District Court judgment and ruled in favor of the Plaintiff. The Defendant subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal on questions regarding the identification of the cause of action and the validity of the prescriptive claim. Issues Whether the High Court of Civil Appeal failed to consider that the District Court properly identified the cause of action. Whether the High Court of Civil Appeal erred in concluding that the Plaintiff had established a prescriptive right to the property. Applicable Law Statutes: Ceiling on Housing Property Law No. 1 of 1973 Prescription Ordinance Civil Procedure Code Cases Referred to: Terunnanse v. Menike - [1-nlr-200] Naker v. Sinatti - [1860-ramanathan-reports-75] Banda v. Banda - [44-nlr-302] David v. Gnanawathie - [2000/v2-sri-lr-352] Abdulla v. Junaid - [44-clw-84] Leslin Jayasinghe v. Illangaratne - [2006/v2-sri-lr-39] Muttu Caruppen v. Rankira - [13-nlr-326] Analysis Issue 1: Identification of the cause of action Rule applied: The court examined whether the High Court of Civil Appeal correctly interpreted the nature of the action as one based on prescriptive title. Court's reasoning: The court noted that the Defendant's own submissions acknowledged the action was for a declaration of title based on prescription. The High Court of Civil Appeal explicitly identified the Plaintiff's case as an appeal against the dismissal of a prescriptive claim. There was no evidence of misdirection by the High Court in its characterization of the legal dispute. Finding: The High Court of Civil Appeal correctly identified the cause of action; the question is answered in the negative. Issue 2: Establishment of prescriptive right Rule applied: Section 3 of the Prescription Ordinance and Section 41 of the Civil Procedure Code. Court's reasoning: The court rejected the argument that prescription is only a "shield" and not a "sword," citing Banda v. Banda to confirm that a plaintiff may use the Prescription Ordinance to vindicate title. Regarding Section 41 of the Civil Procedure Code, the court distinguished David v. Gnanawathie, noting that strict plan requirements apply to servitudes like rights of way. In this case, the property was sufficiently described by assessment number, extent, and boundaries, which, combined with the Plaintiff's evidence of exclusive occupation of a specific portion, allowed for an executable decree. Finding: The Plaintiff established prescriptive title and sufficiently identified the property; the question is answered in the negative. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing. By correctly identifying the cause of action as a claim for prescriptive title, the High Court of Civil Appeal properly applied the substantive law of prescription. This legal foundation allowed the court to evaluate the sufficiency of the property description under Section 41 of the Civil Procedure Code, concluding that the description was adequate to support the prescriptive claim. Conclusion The Supreme Court affirmed the judgment of the High Court of Civil Appeal dated 24th October 2017. The appeal of the Defendant is dismissed with costs awarded to the Plaintiff. Keywords Prescriptive title — Adverse possession — Section 41 of the Civil Procedure Code — Cause of action — High Court of Civil Appeal — District Court — Section 3 of the Prescription Ordinance — Right of way		SC/APPEAL/125/2018	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_125_2018.pdf
sc-appeal-32-2020	19 October 2020	Karunarathne v. Attorney General	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Special Leave to Appeal — Excessive sentence — Principles of sentencing — Theories of punishment — Fair trial — Impartial judge — Atmosphere of judicial calm — Plea of guilty — Withdrawal of plea — Role of the High Court Judge — Role of the State Counsel — Role of the assigned counsel — Constitutional guarantees — ICCPR Act	Facts The Accused-Appellant was indicted before the High Court of Anuradhapura on six counts, comprising three counts of kidnapping under Section 354 of the Penal Code and three counts of rape under Section 364(2) of the Penal Code. These offences were alleged to have occurred between December 2011 and February 2012. The trial commenced on November 5, 2013, with the victim testifying. On December 18, 2013, the Accused changed his plea to guilty on all counts. The High Court Judge imposed the maximum sentence for each count, ordering them to run consecutively, resulting in a total of 81 years of rigorous imprisonment. The Court of Appeal subsequently dismissed the Accused's appeal. The Accused then sought special leave to appeal to the Supreme Court. Although the application was technically out of time, the court accepted the appeal due to the Accused's demonstrated intent to appeal and the Attorney General's decision not to raise a technical objection. Issues Whether the imposition of a cumulative sentence of 81 years of rigorous imprisonment was excessive and contrary to sentencing principles. Whether the Accused was denied a fair trial due to procedural irregularities, including the lack of evidence to support certain counts and the failure of assigned counsel to provide effective representation. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 4, 13(3), 127, 128 Penal Code, Sections 354, 364(2) Code of Criminal Procedure Act No. 15 of 1979, Sections 183, 196, 197(1) International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (ICCPR) Act No. 56 of 2007, Section 4(1) Supreme Court (Conduct of and Etiquette for Attorneys-at-Law) Rules 1988, Rule 15, Rule 52 Cases Referred to: Zahira Habibullah Sheikh v. State of Gujarat - [Appeal (crl.) 446-449] Velupillai v. Chairman, Urban District Council - [39-nlr-464] R.J. Henderson v. T.G. Morgan - [426-us-637] Smith v. O'Grady - [312-us-329] McMann v. Richardson - [397-us-759] The Attorney-General v. Segulebbe Latheef - [2008/v1-sri-lr-225] Somawathie v. Madawela - [1983/v2-sri-lr-15] Jennison v. Backer - [1972-1-all-er-1006] Sri Lanka Ports Authority v. Pieris - [1981/v1-sri-lr-101] Albert v. Veeriahpillai - [1981/v1-sri-lr-110] Analysis Issue 1: Excessiveness of Sentence Rule applied: Principles of sentencing and the statutory maximums provided under Sections 354 and 364(2) of the Penal Code. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the High Court imposed the maximum possible sentence for each of the six counts and ordered them to run consecutively. The court found that the Accused had pleaded guilty, which should have been a mitigating factor. Furthermore, the court determined that the evidence did not support all counts, rendering the total 81-year sentence manifestly excessive and disproportionate. Finding: The sentences were set aside as excessive. Issue 2: Right to a Fair Trial Rule applied: Article 13(3) of the Constitution; Section 4(1) of the ICCPR Act No. 56 of 2007; Rule 52 of the Supreme Court Rules. Court's reasoning: The court found that the trial judge failed to ensure a fair trial by allowing a guilty plea without verifying the Accused's understanding, especially when the prosecution's own evidence contradicted several charges. The court noted that the assigned counsel failed to cross-examine witnesses or challenge the lack of evidence, and the prosecutor failed to bring exculpatory evidence to the court's attention. The court held that it has an inherent duty under Article 127 to correct such miscarriages of justice. Finding: The convictions on counts 1, 2, 3, and 4 were quashed due to a failure to establish the charges and a denial of a fair trial. Interaction of Issues The findings on the fair trial issue directly informed the resolution of the sentencing issue. Because the court determined that the convictions on four of the six counts were legally unsustainable due to a lack of evidence and procedural unfairness, the total sentence was necessarily reduced to reflect only the two counts that were properly established. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The convictions and sentences on counts 1, 2, 3, and 4 are quashed. The convictions on counts 5 and 6 are affirmed, and the sentences are reduced and ordered to run concurrently. Keywords Special Leave to Appeal — Excessive sentence — Principles of sentencing — Theories of punishment — Fair trial — Impartial judge — Atmosphere of judicial calm — Plea of guilty — Withdrawal of plea — Role of the High Court Judge — Role of the State Counsel — Role of the assigned counsel — Constitutional guarantees — ICCPR Act		SC/APPEAL/32/2020	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_32_2020.pdf
sc-fr-369-2013	21 October 2020	Anurdha and Others v. Head Quarter Inspector of Police and Others	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Article 11 — Torture — Cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment — Police misconduct — Arrest — Investigation — Ayurvedic treatment — Medical examination — Escape from custody — Duty of police officers — Human dignity	Facts Nandasenage Lalantha Anurdha Nandasena, the Petitioner, alleged that he was arrested on 25th September 2013 by the 2nd and 3rd Respondents. The Petitioner claimed that he was hung on the rear door of a police jeep and subjected to severe assault by the 3rd and 4th Respondents along with other officers, resulting in a fractured left arm and loss of consciousness. Following the incident, the Petitioner was taken to an Ayurvedic practitioner and subsequently detained at the Anuradhapura Police Station. The Petitioner further alleged that on 26th September 2013, the 2nd Respondent threatened him with a revolver to prevent him from disclosing the assault. The Respondents denied the allegations of torture. They contended that the Petitioner was arrested on 26th September 2013 in connection with a housebreaking and theft complaint. The Respondents asserted that while transporting the Petitioner by jeep, the vehicle skidded due to a steep incline, causing the rear doors to open. They claimed the Petitioner attempted to escape, jumped from the vehicle, and sustained injuries to his left shoulder upon landing. The Petitioner was produced before the Magistrate Court of Anuradhapura on 27th September 2013, where his counsel informed the court of the assault. The Magistrate cancelled an identification parade and ordered the Petitioner to be admitted to the Anuradhapura Teaching Hospital. Medical records from the hospital noted swelling, a fracture of the left humerus, and left wrist drop. Issues Whether the Petitioner was subjected to torture in violation of Article 11 of the Constitution. Whether the Respondents failed to comply with mandatory procedural requirements regarding the treatment and documentation of injuries sustained by a person in police custody. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 11 Code of Criminal Procedure Act No. 15 of 1979, Section 23 Convention against Torture and Other Cruel, Inhuman or Degrading Treatment or Punishment Act No. 22 of 1994, Section 12 Cases Referred to: Ansalin Fernando v Sarath Perera - [(1992) 2 SLR 411] Amal Sudath Silva v Kodituwakku - [(1987) 2 SLR 119] Velmurugu v Attorney General - [(1981) 1 SLR 406] Bandula Samarasekara v Vijith Alwis - [(2009) 1 SLR 213] Senthilnayagam v Seneviratne - [(1981) 2 SLR 187] State of Uttar Pradesh v Ram Sagar Yadav - [1985 AIR 416] Analysis Issue 1: Violation of Article 11 Rule applied: Article 11 of the Constitution, which prohibits torture or cruel, inhuman, or degrading treatment or punishment. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the Respondents failed to provide a credible explanation for the Petitioner's severe injuries, which included a fractured humerus. The court noted that the Respondents' version—that the injuries resulted from an escape attempt—was inconsistent with their failure to follow mandatory procedures, such as producing the Petitioner to a government hospital for a medico-legal examination. The court emphasized that police officers, as repositories of state power, must be held to a higher standard of accountability. Relying on the principle that torture is a non-derogable right, the court found the Petitioner's account of the assault to be factual. Finding: The Petitionerâ€™s fundamental right under Article 11 was violated by the 2nd and 3rd Respondents. Issue 2: Procedural Non-compliance Rule applied: Departmental Order No. A 20, Departmental Order No. E 6, and IG Circular No. 2328/2011. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Respondents failed to report the alleged escape and the resulting injuries to higher authorities, such as the Assistant Superintendent of Police, as required by Departmental Order No. E 6. Furthermore, the court highlighted that the Respondents failed to obtain a Medico-Legal Report (MLR) as mandated by IG Circular No. 2328/2011. The court noted that taking an injured suspect to an Ayurvedic practitioner instead of a government hospital was highly irregular and indicative of an attempt to conceal the truth. Finding: The Respondents failed to adhere to established police procedures and circulars regarding the protection and medical treatment of suspects in custody. Interaction of Issues The findings on procedural non-compliance directly reinforced the conclusion that the Petitioner was subjected to torture. The court reasoned that the Respondents' failure to follow mandatory reporting and medical examination protocols suggested a deliberate attempt to suppress evidence of the assault, thereby strengthening the Petitioner's claim of a violation of Article 11. Conclusion The court held that the fundamental rights of the Petitioner under Article 11 were violated by the 2nd and 3rd Respondents and the State. The 2nd and 3rd Respondents were ordered to pay Rs. 50,000 each as compensation, and the 5th Respondent (the State) was ordered to pay Rs. 100,000 to the Petitioner. The application was allowed. Keywords Fundamental rights — Article 11 — Torture — Cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment — Police misconduct — Arrest — Investigation — Ayurvedic treatment — Medical examination — Escape from custody — Duty of police officers — Human dignity		SC/FR/369/2013	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_369_2013.pdf
sc-fr-256-2017	10 December 2020	Wijerathna v. Sri Lanka Ports Authority and Others	Yasantha Kodagoda, PC, J.	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.; Yasantha Kodagoda, PC, J.	Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Scheme of recruitment — Arbitrary decision-making — Public sector employment — Station Officer appointment	Facts The Petitioner, W.P.S. Wijerathna, joined the Sri Lanka Ports Authority in 1987 as a Fireman and later served as a Landing and Delivery Clerk. In 2012, the Petitioner applied for the post of Station Officer of the Fire Brigade. Although he met the eligibility criteria, the Respondents failed to appoint him, citing his lack of continuous service in the Fire Brigade, a requirement not found in the recruitment scheme. Following an audit by the Auditor Generalâ€™s Department, which concluded that the Petitioner had been treated unfairly, the Petitioner was eventually called for an interview in 2016. Despite the interview panel recommending his appointment, the Acting Chief Human Resources Manager recommended cancelling the interview results and calling for fresh applications. The Petitioner challenged these actions, alleging that the failure to appoint him and the subsequent decision to re-advertise the post were arbitrary and violated his fundamental rights. Issues Whether the non-appointment of the Petitioner to the post of Station Officer following the 2016 interview and the subsequent decision to re-advertise the post constituted an infringement of the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental right to equality under Article 12(1) of the Constitution. Whether the non-appointment of the Petitioner constituted an infringement of the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental right to engage in a lawful occupation under Article 14(1)(g) of the Constitution. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Articles 12(1), 14(1)(g), 15(5), 15(7), 126 Sri Lanka Ports Authority Act No. 51 of 1979 Cases Referred to: Elmore Perera v. Major Montague Jayawickrema, Minister of Public Administration and Plantation Industries - [1985/v1-sri-lr-285] E.P. Royappa v. State of Tamil Nadu - [AIR 1974 SC 555] Ramuppillai v. Festus Perera, Minister of Public Administration, Provincial Councils and Home Affairs - [1991/v1-sri-lr-11] Kanapathipilli v. Sri Lanka Broadcasting Corporation - [2009/v1-sri-lr-406] Wickremasinghe v. Ceylon Petroleum Corporation - [2001/v2-sri-lr-409] Weligodapola v. Secretary, Ministry of Womens Affairs - [1989/v2-sri-lr-63] Gunaratne v. Ceylon Petroleum Corporation - [1996/v1-sri-lr-315] Jayawardena v. Dharani Wijayatilake, Secretary, Ministry of Justice and Constitutional Affairs - [2001/v1-sri-lr-132] W.M.K. De Silva v. Chairman, Ceylon Fertilizer Corporation - [1989/v2-sri-lr-393] Analysis Issue 1: Infringement of Article 12(1) Rule applied: Article 12(1) of the Constitution, which guarantees equality before the law and equal protection of the law, prohibiting arbitrary and unreasonable administrative action. Court's reasoning: The court found that the recruitment process was marred by arbitrary decision-making. The Respondents failed to follow the established scheme of recruitment and imposed non-existent requirements, such as continuous service in the Fire Brigade. The decision to cancel the 2016 interview, despite a positive recommendation from the panel, was found to be without justification and capricious. The court emphasized that public authorities must act in accordance with objective criteria and that arbitrariness is the "sworn enemy" of equality. Finding: The non-appointment of the Petitioner and the decision to re-advertise the post infringed the Petitioner's fundamental right to equality under Article 12(1). Issue 2: Infringement of Article 14(1)(g) Rule applied: Article 14(1)(g) of the Constitution, which guarantees the freedom to engage in any lawful occupation, profession, trade, business, or enterprise. Court's reasoning: The court held that while Article 14(1)(g) protects the general right to engage in a chosen profession, it does not confer a fundamental right to hold a specific employment position or to be employed by a particular employer. The Petitionerâ€™s grievance was specifically about not being appointed to a particular post within the Sri Lanka Ports Authority, rather than being deprived of the right to engage in his profession generally. Finding: The Respondents did not infringe the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental right under Article 14(1)(g). Interaction of Issues The findings are distinct; while the court found that the administrative process was arbitrary and violated the right to equality (Article 12(1)), this did not extend to a violation of the broader freedom of occupation (Article 14(1)(g)). The resolution of the Article 12(1) claim provided the Petitioner with the necessary relief, rendering the Article 14(1)(g) claim secondary. Conclusion The application is allowed. The court declares that the Petitionerâ€™s fundamental rights under Article 12(1) were infringed and directs the Sri Lanka Ports Authority to appoint the Petitioner to the post of Station Officer, backdated to 1st July 2012, without back wages. The Notice dated 19th June 2017 is quashed, and compensation is awarded to the Petitioner. Keywords Fundamental rights — Equality before the law — Administrative action — Scheme of recruitment — Arbitrary decision-making — Public sector employment — Station Officer appointment		SC/FR/256/2017	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_fr_256_2017.pdf
sc-appeal-154-2014	13 December 2020	Attorney General v. Perera and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J. (Majority); Preethi Padman Surasena, J. (Dissenting), J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; L.T.B.
