Dehideniya, J.; Preethi Padman Surasena, J	Procuration — Penal Code — Section 360A(1) — Procurement of a prostitute — Consent — Evidence — Court of Appeal — Special Leave to Appeal	Facts The Accused-Appellant was indicted for the offence of procuration under Section 360A(1) of the Penal Code. Following information received, officers of the Colombo Crime Division conducted a raid at an apartment in the Liberty Plaza Shopping Complex. A police constable acting as a decoy paid the Accused-Appellant Rs. 3000 to obtain the services of a woman for sexual intercourse. The Accused-Appellant presented eight women, from whom the decoy selected one, later identified as Priyangika. The Accused-Appellant led the decoy and Priyangika to a separate room. Upon a pre-arranged signal, police officers entered the premises and arrested the Accused-Appellant, Priyangika, seven other women, and six men. The High Court convicted the Accused-Appellant, and the Court of Appeal subsequently affirmed the conviction and sentence. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal misdirected itself in law by holding that "procuring" under Section 360A(1) of the Penal Code includes procuring a person who is already a prostitute. Whether the prosecution is required to call the person alleged to have been procured as a witness to establish that she was not a prostitute at the time of the alleged procuration. Whether it is essential for the prosecution to prove that the person alleged to have been procured in fact engaged in prostitution as a result of the procuration. Applicable Law Statutes: Penal Code (as amended by Act No. 22 of 1995) Vagrants Ordinance No. 4 Brothels Ordinance No. 5 Evidence Ordinance Cases Referred to: R v. Joseph James Broadfoot - [64-cr-app-r-71] Attorney Generals Reference (No. 1 of 1975) - [1975/v1-q-b-773] Regina v. Morris-Lowe - [1985-1-wlr-29] R v. Ubolcharoen (Phanda) and Thonarin (Buppha) - [2009/ewca-crim-3263] Shaw Wallace and Hedges Ltd v. Palmerstone Tea Co. Ltd and Others - [1982/v2-sri-lr-427] Ramesh v. The State of Maharashtra - [1962-air-1908] Canada (Attorney General) v. Bedford - [2012-onca-186] Regina v. Johnson (Gerald) - [1964-2-qb-404] Rex v. Christian and Another - [23-cox-c-c-541] Analysis Issue 1: Scope of "Procuring" and "To Become" Rule applied: Section 360A(1) of the Penal Code. Court's reasoning: The majority held that the term "to become" implies a change of status or condition. Relying on the principle that the offence targets the luring of persons not previously engaged in prostitution, the court reasoned that if a person is already a prostitute, they cannot "become" one. The court rejected the lower court's view that the provision applies to those already in the profession. Finding: The Court of Appeal misdirected itself; the offence cannot be committed against a person who is already a prostitute. Issue 2: Necessity of Victim Testimony Rule applied: Section 134 of the Evidence Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court held that while the prosecution bears the burden of proving the victim was not a prostitute, there is no legal requirement that this be established solely through the victim's testimony. The prosecution may rely on other direct or circumstantial evidence. Requiring the victim to testify would create an insurmountable barrier to prosecution. Finding: The prosecution is not legally required to call the victim as a witness. Issue 3: Requirement of Actual Engagement in Prostitution Rule applied: Section 360A(1) of the Penal Code. Court's reasoning: The court noted that the statute criminalizes the act of "procuring or attempting to procure." However, the majority concluded that the legislative intent was to protect those not yet in the profession. If the person is already a prostitute, the act of offering them for sexual services does not constitute "procuring to become" a prostitute. Finding: It is essential to establish that the person was not a prostitute prior to the act of procuration. Interaction of Issues The findings are interdependent: the court's interpretation of "to become" (Issue 1) necessitates that the prosecution prove the victim's prior status (Issue 3). Because the majority held that the offence only applies to those not already engaged in prostitution, the failure of the prosecution to prove that Priyangika was not a prostitute rendered the conviction unsustainable. Conclusion The Supreme Court allowed the appeal, set aside the conviction and sentence imposed by the High Court, and acquitted the Accused-Appellant. The Court held that the offence of procuration under Section 360A(1) of the Penal Code does not apply to persons already engaged in prostitution. Keywords Procuration — Penal Code — Section 360A(1) — Procurement of a prostitute — Consent — Evidence — Court of Appeal — Special Leave to Appeal		SC/APPEAL/154/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_154_2014.pdf
sc-appeal-110-2015	13 December 2020	Attorney General v. Sadath	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J	Buwaneka Aluwihare PC, J; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Importation of Heroin — Trafficking of drugs — Possession of drugs — Burden of proof — Mens rea — Conscious possession — Section 54A of the Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance — Section 69 and 72 of the Penal Code — Knowledge of contents	Facts The Accused-Appellant traveled from Chennai to Colombo on September 1, 2001. Upon arrival at the Katunayake International Airport, Customs officers inspected his baggage and discovered two parcels containing 1.384 kilograms of pure Heroin concealed within a travelling bag. The Accused-Appellant did not dispute the detection of the drugs but contended that he had no knowledge of the contents of the bag. He testified that he had agreed to carry the bag for a couple he met at the Chennai airport to assist them with excess baggage weight in exchange for a small payment. The High Court rejected this version of events, convicted the Accused-Appellant of charges under Section 54A of the Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance, and sentenced him accordingly. The Court of Appeal subsequently affirmed the conviction and sentence. Issues Whether the Court of Appeal misdirected itself on the law regarding the burden of proof by holding that the Prosecution need not establish the mental element of conscious possession in a charge under Section 54A of the Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance. Whether the Court of Appeal erred in law by considering Sections 69 and 72 of the Penal Code and casting a burden on the defense without first determining if the Prosecution had discharged its burden of proof. Whether mere possession is sufficient to convict an accused on charges of trafficking under the Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance. Applicable Law Statutes: Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance (as amended by Act No. 13) Penal Code (Sections 69, 72, 394) Cases Referred to: Van der Hultes v. Attorney General - [1989/v1-sri-lr-204] Warner v. Metropolitan Police Commissioner - [1968-52-crim-appl-r-373] Jayaseelan Sathyanathan v. Attorney General - [ca-188/96] Sarala Natarajan Setti v. Attorney General - [ca-209/96] Sumanawathie Perera v. Attorney General - [1998/v2-sri-lr-20] Shanmugarajah v. The Republic - [1990/v2-sri-lr-57] Nirmakumar Bhowmik v. Emperor - [air-1938-cal] Analysis Issue 1: Conscious possession and burden of proof Rule applied: Section 54A of the Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance and the principle of mens rea. Court's reasoning: The court held that mens rea is an essential ingredient for an offence under Section 54A. However, the Prosecution can discharge its burden of establishing this mental element presumptively by adducing the circumstances under which the substance was recovered. Once the Prosecution establishes a strong prima facie case, the evidentiary burden shifts to the Accused to create a reasonable doubt regarding their knowledge. The court found the Accused-Appellant's explanation for carrying the bag highly improbable and noted his failure to substantiate his claims. Finding: The Court of Appeal did not misdirect itself; the Prosecution successfully established the requisite knowledge through circumstantial evidence. Issue 2: Application of Penal Code sections Rule applied: Sections 69 and 72 of the Penal Code. Court's reasoning: The court clarified that Section 69 is inapplicable to these facts. Regarding Section 72, the court noted that it serves as a general exception, and the onus is on the party seeking to rely on it to prove "good faith," which requires "due care and attention." The trial judge did not improperly cast a burden on the Accused but rather evaluated the evidence and rejected the Accused-Appellant's version as a matter of fact. Finding: The Court of Appeal did not err in its reference to these sections, as it correctly identified the legal requirements for the defense to negate knowledge. Issue 3: Sufficiency of possession for trafficking Rule applied: Section 54A of the Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The court determined that while possession and trafficking both require knowledge, trafficking requires an additional act (e.g., transporting or distributing). Possession alone is insufficient for a trafficking conviction; the Prosecution must provide circumstantial evidence indicating the drugs were for commercial purposes rather than personal use. Finding: The issue is answered in the affirmative; however, the Accused-Appellant remains liable because the Prosecution established his knowledge and the nature of his conduct. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected because the resolution of the mens rea issue (Issue 1) and the evidentiary burden (Issue 2) directly support the conclusion that the Accused-Appellant's possession was not innocent. By failing to provide a credible explanation for his possession, the Accused-Appellant failed to rebut the presumption of knowledge, which in turn allowed the court to uphold the conviction for trafficking (Issue 3) based on the quantity and circumstances of the transport. Conclusion The Supreme Court answered the first and second questions of law in the negative and the third question in the affirmative. The conviction and sentence imposed on the Accused-Appellant were affirmed, and the appeal was dismissed. Keywords Importation of Heroin — Trafficking of drugs — Possession of drugs — Burden of proof — Mens rea — Conscious possession — Section 54A of the Poisons, Opium and Dangerous Drugs Ordinance — Section 69 and 72 of the Penal Code — Knowledge of contents		SC/APPEAL/110/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_110_2015.pdf
sc-appeal-151-2016	13 December 2020	Seneviratne v. Viyannalage and Others	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J	Buwaneka Aluwihare, PC. J; Priyantha Jayawardena, PC. J; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.	Conciliation Boards Act — Jurisdiction of Conciliation Boards — Settlement of disputes — Partition action — Immovable property — District Court decree — Conciliation Board area — Wakirigala Conciliation Board — Land demarcation — Bona fide purchaser	Facts The Plaintiff-Appellant-Respondent (Plaintiff) initiated an action in the District Court of Mawanella seeking a declaration of title to a one-acre portion of land known as Nayawatuna Hena and the ejectment of the Defendants. The land was originally transferred to the Plaintiffâ€™s wife, Jane Nona, by one Malida via a conditional transfer in 1963, which became an absolute transfer in 1965. Following disputes regarding the boundaries, Jane Nona sought the assistance of the Wakirigala Conciliation Board. The Board facilitated a settlement in 1976, which was filed of record in the District Court of Kegalle. A licensed surveyor subsequently demarcated the one-acre portion. The Defendants, who were successors to the original transferor Malida, challenged the Plaintiff's title through various legal proceedings, including a failed partition action. The District Court of Mawanella initially dismissed the Plaintiff's action, but the High Court of Civil Appeals set aside that judgment, affirming the Plaintiff's title based on the Conciliation Board settlement. The Defendants appealed to the Supreme Court, contending that the Conciliation Board lacked jurisdiction to partition land and that its settlement could not acquire the force of a court decree. Issues Whether the High Court of Civil Appeals erred in law by holding that the determination of the Conciliation Board in case No. 295/75 acquired the force of a decree entered by the District Court of Kegalle. Whether the Conciliation Board exceeded its jurisdiction by effectively partitioning land, given that partition actions are governed by specific legislation. Applicable Law Statutes: Conciliation Boards Act No. 10 Partition Act No. 16 Partition Law Civil Procedure Code Cases Referred to: R. Arnolis v. R. Hendrick - [75-nlr-532] Selenchi Appuhamy v. Livinia - [9-nlr-59] Angela Fernando v. Devadeepthi Fernando - [2006/v2-sri-lr-188] Analysis Issue 1: Force of the Conciliation Board Settlement Rule applied: Section 13 of the Conciliation Boards Act No. 10. Court's reasoning: The Court observed that under Section 13(3)(a) of the Act, once a settlement reached before a Conciliation Board is filed of record in the District Court, it is deemed to be a decree of that court. The Court found that the settlement in question was duly filed in the District Court of Kegalle. Consequently, the settlement acquired the legal status of a court decree, binding the parties and their successors. Finding: The High Court did not err in holding that the settlement acquired the force of a decree. Issue 2: Jurisdiction of the Conciliation Board Rule applied: Section 6 of the Conciliation Boards Act No. 10. Court's reasoning: The Court clarified that while Conciliation Boards cannot perform a judicial partition with in rem effect, they possess jurisdiction to resolve disputes regarding immovable property. The Court distinguished between a formal partition action and an amicable settlement of a boundary dispute between parties. Since the evidence showed a subsisting dispute regarding the possession and boundaries of the land, the Board acted within its mandate to mediate that dispute. The Court noted that the settlement did not attempt to create a title against the whole world, but merely resolved the conflict between the specific parties involved. Finding: The Conciliation Board acted within its jurisdiction to resolve a dispute regarding immovable property. Interaction of Issues The findings on these issues are interdependent; the validity of the settlement as a decree (Issue 1) is contingent upon the Board having the jurisdiction to entertain the matter (Issue 2). By determining that the Board had jurisdiction to resolve a property dispute, the Court confirmed that the resulting settlement was a valid instrument capable of being filed as a decree under Section 13 of the Act. Conclusion The Supreme Court answered the question of law in the negative, affirming the judgment of the High Court of Civil Appeals. The appeal was dismissed with costs. Keywords Conciliation Boards Act — Jurisdiction of Conciliation Boards — Settlement of disputes — Partition action — Immovable property — District Court decree — Conciliation Board area — Wakirigala Conciliation Board — Land demarcation — Bona fide purchaser		SC/APPEAL/151/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_151_2016.pdf
sc-appeal-20-2010	17 December 2020	Lokubanda v. Chandrasena and Menike	E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Jayantha Jayasuriya, PC, J; P. Padman Surasena, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Rei vindicatio — Entitlement to possession — Land Development Ordinance — Permit holder — Validity of permit — Cause of action — Fundamental rights — Administrative action	Facts The Plaintiff, A.M. Lokubanda, instituted an action in the District Court of Polonnaruwa seeking to recover possession of a paddy field, claiming he was the valid permit holder under the Land Development Ordinance. The Plaintiff alleged that the Defendant, B.R. Chandrasena, was in unlawful possession of the land. The Defendant contended that the land had been transferred to him by one Henaka Ralalage Punchimenika, who he claimed was the rightful permit holder. The Defendant further argued that the Plaintiffâ€™s action was prescribed, that there was a misjoinder of parties, and that the Plaintiffâ€™s permit was obtained through unlawful means. During the trial, the Plaintiff produced evidence, including testimony from a land officer, to establish that he was granted the land in the early 1970s after a formal inquiry. The Defendant relied on a report marked X and various receipts to assert his right to possession. The District Court ruled in favor of the Plaintiff, finding that he held a valid permit and that the Defendant was an unauthorized possessor. The Civil Appellate High Court subsequently dismissed the Defendantâ€™s appeal. The Defendant then appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in holding that an action for rei vindicatio could be maintained if the permit marked P1 was a nullity. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court failed to consider vital evidence contained in the report marked X and its annexures. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in law by relying on document P1, which was issued after the institution of the action. Applicable Law Statutes: Constitution of the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka, Article 127(2) High Court of the Provinces (Special Provisions) (Amendment) Act No. 54 of 2006 Land Development Ordinance Civil Procedure Code, Sections 5, 6, 35, 188, 217 Evidence Ordinance, Section 116 Cases Referred to: Pathirana v. Jayasundara - [58-nlr-169] Luwis Singho v. Ponnamperuma - [1996/v2-sri-lr-320] Palisena v. Perera - [56-nlr-407] Haniffi v. Nallamma - [1998/v1-sri-lr-73] Dharmasiri v. Wickramatunga - [2002/v2-sri-lr-218] Attanayake v. Aladin - [1997/v3-sri-lr-386] T.B. Jayasinghe v. Kiriwanegedara Tikiri Banda - [1988/ii-calr-24] Lowe v. Fernando - [16-nlr-398] Analysis Issue 1: Maintainability of the action Rule applied: Sections 5, 6, 35, and 217 of the Civil Procedure Code; Land Development Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Court observed that while the lower court labeled the case a rei vindicatio action, the trial proceeded on the basis of entitlement to possession via a permit rather than strict ownership. The Court held that even if an action does not strictly qualify as rei vindicatio, a permit holder under the Land Development Ordinance has a statutory right to possess that can be enforced against a trespasser. The Plaintiffâ€™s failure to pray for a declaration of title does not bar the remedy of ejectment if the entitlement is proven. Finding: The action is maintainable as an action for recovery of possession based on the Plaintiff's statutory right as a permit holder. Issue 2: Consideration of evidence Rule applied: Rules of evidence regarding official documents and the Land Development Ordinance. Court's reasoning: The Court reviewed the report marked X and its annexures. It found that these documents actually supported the Plaintiffâ€™s position, confirming that the land was allocated to him in 1974 following a proper inquiry. The Defendant failed to produce any evidence of a valid permit in his name or his predecessor's name, nor did he prove that the Plaintiffâ€™s permit was lawfully cancelled under Chapter VIII of the Ordinance. Finding: The High Court did not err; the evidence in report X reinforces the Plaintiff's claim. Issue 3: Reliance on document P1 Rule applied: Law of evidence regarding secondary proof of documents. Court's reasoning: P1 was issued in 1986 as a certified copy of the original permit granted in 1974. The Plaintiff testified that the original was destroyed by fire. The Court found that the Plaintiff was the permit holder from 1974, well before the action was instituted in 1983. The issuance of P1 merely formalized the existing status of the Plaintiff in the official ledgers. Finding: P1 is not a nullity, and the Plaintiffâ€™s status as a permit holder was established prior to the institution of the action. Interaction of Issues The findings are mutually reinforcing: the determination that the Plaintiff held a valid permit since 1974 (Issue 3) confirms his legal standing to maintain the action (Issue 1). Furthermore, the evidence contained in report X (Issue 2) corroborates the validity of the Plaintiff's permit, thereby negating the Defendant's claim that the permit was a nullity. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed with costs. The Plaintiff is confirmed as the valid permit holder entitled to the possession of the land in dispute. Keywords Rei vindicatio — Entitlement to possession — Land Development Ordinance — Permit holder — Validity of permit — Cause of action — Fundamental rights — Administrative action		SC/APPEAL/20/2010	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_20_2010.pdf
sc-appeal-129-2014	17 December 2020	Fernando and Others v. Silva and Others	E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.; E.A.G.R. Amarasekara, J.	Prescriptive user — Right of way — Servitude — Dominant and servient tenement — Necessity — Abandonment — District Court judgment — High Court judgment — Plan No. 5196 — Plan No. 2618 — Plan No. 3465	Facts The Plaintiffs-Appellants instituted an action in the District Court of Negombo seeking a declaration of a right of way over a 10-foot wide roadway allegedly situated on the western boundary of the Defendants-Respondents' land. The Plaintiffs claimed this right based on prescriptive user and/or as a way of necessity. The Defendants contested the claim, arguing that the Plaintiffs had no such right over their land, that the Plaintiffs had alternative access, and that the purported roadway was not within the Defendants' land as described in the second schedule to the plaint. The trial court and the Civil Appellate High Court dismissed the Plaintiffs' action, finding that the Plaintiffs failed to prove the existence of a defined track or the necessity for the claimed right of way. The Plaintiffs subsequently appealed to the Supreme Court, which granted leave to appeal on specific questions of law regarding the entitlement to a servitude by necessity and the potential restriction of the right of way to a width of 8.8 feet. Issues Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in law by failing to consider the Plaintiffs' entitlement to a servitude by way of necessity when no alternative road was available. Whether the Civil Appellate High Court erred in law by failing to consider whether the Plaintiffs could restrict their claim to an 8.8-foot wide right of way. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code, Section 41 Prescription Ordinance Cases Referred to: Velupillai v. Subasinghe - [58-nlr-385] David v. Gunawathie - [2000/v2-sri-lr-135] Hanaffi v. Nallamma - [1998/v1-sri-lr-73] Karunaratne v. Gabriel Appuhamy - [15-nlr-257] Kandaiah v. Seenitamby - [17-nlr-29] Fernando v. Fernando - [31-nlr-126] Morgappa v. Casie Chetty - [17-nlr-31] Hendrick v. Saranelis - [17-clw-87] Marasinghe v. Samarasinghe - [73-nlr-433] Thambapillai v. Nagamanipillai - [52-nlr-225] Saparamadu v. Melder - [2004/v3-sri-lr-148] Fernando v. de Silva - [30-nlr-56] Amarasuriya v. Perera - [45-nlr-348] Chandrasiri v. Wickramasinghe - [70-nlr-15] Paramount Investment Limited v. Cader - [1986/v2-sri-lr-309] Dayawathie v. Dias - [The Bar Association Law Journal 2013 Vol. XX] Analysis Issue 1: Entitlement to servitude by necessity Rule applied: The law requires the claimant to prove the necessity of a cartway and the absence of a reasonable alternative. Court's reasoning: The court observed that the Plaintiffs failed to prove a "fair need" for a vehicle-accessible road. Evidence indicated that the Plaintiffs only required access for infrequent tasks, and the Defendants successfully demonstrated the existence of an alternative foot path. The court noted that the burden of proof lies on the party claiming the right of way of necessity, and the Plaintiffs failed to establish special circumstances justifying a cartway over the Defendants' land. Finding: The court found no error in the lower courts' conclusion that the Plaintiffs failed to prove the necessity of a cartway. Issue 2: Restriction of right of way to 8.8 feet Rule applied: A prescriptive right of way must be based on the user of a strictly defined track over the servient tenement. Court's reasoning: The court found that the Plaintiffs' claim was inconsistent and lacked a defined track. The evidence regarding the 8.8-foot width was based on a misinterpretation of a survey plan that did not actually show a roadway. Furthermore, the Plaintiffs' own surveyor testified that trees on the purported strip made it unusable as a road. The court held that the Plaintiffs failed to define the servient tenement with the precision required by Section 41 of the Civil Procedure Code. Finding: The court rejected the argument, concluding that there was no reliable evidence of a defined track of any width (10 feet, 8.8 feet, or otherwise) over the Defendants' land. Interaction of Issues The findings on both issues reinforce the conclusion that the Plaintiffs' action was fundamentally flawed. The failure to define the servient tenement and the lack of evidence for a specific, used track (Issue 2) directly undermined the claim for a right of way by necessity (Issue 1), as the court could not grant a servitude over an ill-defined or non-existent path. Conclusion The appeal is dismissed with costs. The Plaintiffs failed to establish the existence of a servitutal right of way by prescription or necessity. Keywords Prescriptive user — Right of way — Servitude — Dominant and servient tenement — Necessity — Abandonment — District Court judgment — High Court judgment — Plan No. 5196 — Plan No. 2618 — Plan No. 3465		SC/APPEAL/129/2014	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_129_2014.pdf
sc-appeal-210-2015	17 December 2020	Dharmasena v. Malani and Others	Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, J.; Vijith K. Malalgoda, PC, J.; Murdu Fernando, PC, J.	Appeal — Revisionary jurisdiction — Exceptional circumstances — Partition action — Land case — Mutually exclusive Judgements — Execution of decree — District Court Order — High Court Order	Facts The Plaintiff initiated a land case (D.C. Pugoda Case No. 327/L) against the 1st and 2nd defendants regarding a development agreement and against the 3rd and 4th defendants for ejectment from specific lots. The District Court dismissed the Plaintiff's claim for declaration of title and ejectment on 25-04-2002, instead ordering the Plaintiff to convey the lots to the 3rd and 4th defendants upon payment of specified sums. The Plaintiff did not appeal this judgment. Separately, the Plaintiff filed a partition action (D.C. Pugoda Case No. 317/P) concerning a larger land corpus. The 3rd and 4th defendants were named as parties in possession but were not allotted shares. The partition action concluded with a final decree. The 3rd and 4th defendants unsuccessfully attempted to incorporate the judgment from the land case into the partition action through various appellate proceedings. On 28-07-2010, the 3rd and 4th defendants moved to execute the decree in the land case. On 18-08-2011, the District Court ordered the execution of the deeds in favor of the 3rd and 4th defendants, stipulating that this should occur after the Plaintiff obtained possession of his allotment from the partition action. The Plaintiff challenged this order in the High Court via revision. The High Court set aside the District Court's order, holding that the partition decree was final and the land case judgment could not interfere with it. The 3rd and 4th defendants appealed to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the High Court erred in law by exercising revisionary jurisdiction in the absence of exceptional circumstances. Whether the High Court erred in law by failing to recognize that the land case (327/L) and the partition action (317/P) are distinct, mutually exclusive proceedings. Applicable Law Statutes: Civil Procedure Code Partition Law No. 21 of 1977 Cases Referred to: Rustom v. Hapangama and Co. - [1979/v1-sri-lr-352] Hotel Galaxy (Pvt) Ltd. and others v. Mercantile Hotels Management Ltd. - [1987/v1-sri-lr-5] Perera v. Peoples Bank - [1995/v2-sri-lr-84] Sikander Abdul Samadh v. Musajee - [1988/v2-calr-147] Caderamanpulle v. Ceylon Paper Sacks Ltd - [2001/v3-sri-lr-112] Dharmaratne and another v. Palm Paradise Cabanas Ltd and others - [2003/v3-sri-lr-24] Wijesinghe v. Tharmaratnam - [Sri-LR-IV] Bank of Ceylon v. Kaleel and others - [2004/v1-sri-lr-284] Atukorale v. Samynathan - [41-nlr-165] Rasheed Ali v. Mohammed Ali - [1981/v2-sri-lr-29] Attorney General v. Gunawardena - [1996/v2-sri-lr-149] Jayaratne and another v. Premadasa and others - [2004/v1-sri-lr-340] De Costa and others v. De Costa and others - [1998/v1-sri-lr-107] Latheef and another v. Mansoor and another - [2011-blj-189] Ittapana v. Hemawathie - [1981/v2-sri-lr-476] Madurasnghe v. Madurasinghe - [1988/v2-sri-lr-142] Finance Land Sales Ltd v. Perera - [2005/v1-sri-lr-79] Rodge v. Comptoir D Escomple de Paris - [1871-3-pc-465] Sirinatha v. Sirisena and others - [1998/v3-sri-lr-19] Sirisoma and others v. Saranelis Appuhamy - [51-nlr-337] B. Sillie Fernando v. W. Silman Fernando and others - [64-nlr-401] Abeyratne v. Rosalin - [2001/v3-sri-lr-308] Abusali Sithi Fareeda v. Mohamed Noor and another - [sc-appeal-134/2013] Subaseris v. Prolis - [16-nlr-393] Analysis Issue 1: Revisionary Jurisdiction Rule applied: The principle that revisionary jurisdiction is a discretionary, extraordinary remedy requiring "exceptional circumstances" or a failure of justice that "shocks the conscience of the court." Court's reasoning: The Court noted that the Plaintiff had already exercised a right of appeal against the District Court order. The High Court failed to address preliminary objections regarding the maintainability of the revision application. The Supreme Court found no evidence of exceptional circumstances or manifest error that would justify bypassing the ordinary appellate process. Finding: The High Court erred in exercising its revisionary jurisdiction as no exceptional circumstances existed. Issue 2: Distinctness of Proceedings Rule applied: The legal distinction between a partition action (in rem) and a land case (in personam/contractual). Court's reasoning: The Court held that the partition action determined the shares of the corpus, while the land case concerned specific contractual obligations and ownership of defined lots. The land case judgment did not challenge the partition decree; it merely provided for the conveyance of specific lots. The District Court's order to execute the land case decree after the partition decree was a pragmatic approach to avoid conflict. Finding: The two cases are distinct and mutually exclusive; the land case does not assail the partition decree. Interaction of Issues The improper exercise of revisionary jurisdiction (Issue 1) allowed the Plaintiff to collaterally attack a final judgment in the land case that had not been appealed. Because the High Court failed to recognize the distinct nature of the two proceedings (Issue 2), it erroneously concluded that the land case judgment interfered with the partition decree, leading to a failure of justice. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The order of the High Court is set aside, and the District Court order dated 18-08-2011 is affirmed. Keywords Appeal — Revisionary jurisdiction — Exceptional circumstances — Partition action — Land case — Mutually exclusive Judgements — Execution of decree — District Court Order — High Court Order		SC/APPEAL/210/2015	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_210_2015.pdf
sc-appeal-58-2016	17 December 2020	Institute of Data Management (Private) Limited v. IDM Nations Campus (Pvt) Ltd and Others	S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Buwaneka Aluwihare, J.; L.T.B. Dehideniya, J.; S. Thurairaja, PC, J.	Companies Act — Oppression — Mismanagement — Shareholder rights — Interim orders — Laches — Fraudulent documents — Removal of director — Company secretary — Corporate governance — Articles of association	Facts The Petitioner, Institute of Data Management (Private) Limited, is a 50% shareholder of the 1st Respondent, IDM Nations Campus (Pvt) Ltd. The 2nd and 3rd Respondents are directors of the 1st Respondent, and the 4th Respondent is the Company Secretary. The Petitioner alleged that its representative on the Board of Directors, Dr. Bandusena Ranasinghe, was unlawfully removed through a fraudulent Form 20 filed with the Registrar of Companies. The Petitioner further alleged that the 2nd Respondent incorporated a competing company, the 5th Respondent, and diverted business from the 1st Respondent. The Petitioner filed an action in the Commercial High Court seeking relief for oppression and mismanagement under Sections 224, 225, and 521 of the Companies Act No. 07 of 2007. The High Court refused to grant interim orders, citing laches due to a five-month delay in filing the petition and the Petitioner's failure to utilize the remedy under Section 483 of the Companies Act. The Petitioner appealed this decision to the Supreme Court. Issues Whether the High Court misdirected itself by failing to recognize that a claim for oppression and mismanagement requires a series of acts rather than an isolated incident, and whether the Petitioner established a prima facie case. Whether the High Court erred in dismissing the petition on the grounds of laches for a five-month delay. Whether the court has jurisdiction to grant interim orders under Sections 224, 225, and 521 of the Companies Act to regulate the affairs of the company. Applicable Law Statutes: Companies Act No. 07 of 2007 Companies (Amendment) Act No. 15 Cases Referred to: Scottish Co-op Society v. Meyers - [1959 AC 324] Needle Industries (India) Ltd v. Needle Industries Newey (India) Holding Ltd. - [AIR 1981 SC 1298] S. P. Jain v. Kalinga Tubes Ltd - [AIR 1965 1535] Ratnam v. Jayathileka - [2002/v1-sri-lr-409] Re Harmer Ltd - [1958/v3-all-er-689] Re Bright Pine Mills Pty Ltd - [1969/vr-1002] A Brahmaraj v. Sivakumar Spinning Mills Pvt. Ltd. - [1984/v1-mlj-376] Shri Raj Kumar Gupta v. D.P Gupta & Co. (P) Ltd. - [2008/v84-cla-390] S. Sukhdeep Singh Jhikka v. S. Ajit Singh Deogan - [2009/v93-scl-212] Re a company (No 005134) - [1989/bclc-383] Burland v. Earle - [1902/ac-83] Lee v. Chou Wen Hsein - [1985/bclc-45] Unipak Ltd. v. Amarasena - [2002/v3-sri-lr-173] Re The Langham Skating Rink Company - [1877/36-ct-605] Analysis Issue 1: Prima facie case of oppression and mismanagement Rule applied: Sections 224 and 225 of the Companies Act No. 07 of 2007. Court's reasoning: The court held that while an isolated act is insufficient, a series of acts amounting to a "continuing story" of oppression or mismanagement satisfies the threshold. The Petitioner presented evidence of a potentially fraudulent Form 20, the failure to appoint a replacement director, and the incorporation of a competing 5th Respondent. These events, taken collectively, constitute a prima facie case of oppressive conduct and mismanagement. Finding: The Petitioner established a prima facie case. Issue 2: Laches and delay Rule applied: Sections 224, 225, and 521 of the Companies Act No. 07 of 2007. Court's reasoning: The court noted that the Companies Act does not prescribe a specific time limit for filing such applications. Laches should only be applied in cases of "unjustifiable, unreasonable, or abnormal extensive delay." A five-month delay is not excessive in the context of complex corporate litigation where parties may attempt to resolve disputes outside of court. Finding: The Petitioner was not guilty of laches. Issue 3: Jurisdiction to grant interim orders Rule applied: Sections 224(3), 225(3), and 521 of the Companies Act No. 07 of 2007. Court's reasoning: The court affirmed that it has the power to intervene in internal company affairs when there is a lack of fairness or equity that threatens the company or its stakeholders. Following the precedent in Unipak Ltd. v. Amarasena, the court held that intervention is justified when strong grounds exist, such as the unlawful removal of a director and the diversion of business to a competing entity. Finding: The court has the jurisdiction to grant interim orders. Interaction of Issues The findings are interconnected: because the Petitioner established a prima facie case of a continuing series of oppressive acts (Issue 1), the delay in filing was not unreasonable (Issue 2), thereby justifying the court's intervention through interim orders (Issue 3) to protect the company's interests. Conclusion The appeal is allowed. The Supreme Court set aside the High Court's order and granted interim orders setting aside the resignation of Dr. Bandusena Ranasinghe and restraining the 5th Respondent from engaging in business activities in competition with the 1st Respondent. Keywords Companies Act — Oppression — Mismanagement — Shareholder rights — Interim orders — Laches — Fraudulent documents — Removal of director — Company secretary — Corporate governance — Articles of association		SC/APPEAL/58/2016	https://supremecourt.lk/wp-content/uploads/judgements/sc_appeal_58_2016.pdf
